extensive rule, such as is also attributed to Godrich and Havelok, may
point to the title of <i>Bretwalda</i>, which Æthelberht so long
coveted, and at last obtained. Our poet, in describing Birkabeyn,
repeats this character so exactly, and makes the circumstances of the
deaths of Athelwold and Birkabeyn so similar, that they are almost
indistinguishable; a fault which he doubles by repeating the character
of Godrich in describing that of Godard. Both of these answer to
Laȝamon’s Æluric, who was “the wickedest of all kings” (Laȝ. iii. 195).
So far, perhaps, the connection of the various stories is not very
evident, but I will now mention an obvious coincidence. The quarrel and
reconciliation between Athelbert and Edelfrid, as told in the Eulogium
Historiarum, &amp;c., exactly answers to the quarrel and reconciliation
between Cadwan and Æluric as told in Laȝamon (vol. iii. p. 205);
where Cadwan has come forward in place of Æthelbert, who has by this
time dropped out of Laȝamon’s narrative. Again, the Gunter or Gurmond
who was Havelok’s father reminds us of the Gurmund of Laȝamon
(p. 156), who is curiously described as king of Africa; but the
name is Danish. The character of Grim is fairly paralleled by that of
Brian, who makes sea-voyages, and goes about as a merchant (Laȝamon,
iii. 232). In several respects Havelok may have been drawn from
Cadwalan, whose gallant attempts to gain the king of Northumberland are
recorded in Laȝamon (iii. 216-254); his opponent being Edwin, who has
replaced Ethelfrid as Laȝamon’s narrative proceeds. At last he
overthrows him and slays him in the great battle of Heathfield or
Hatfield, which took place, according to the Saxon Chronicle, <span class="smallroman">A.D.</span> 633. This great battle resembles the
decisive one between Havelok and Godrich. As Cadwalan was well supported
by his liegeman Penda (Laȝamon, iii. 251), so was Havelok by Ubbe.
Again, Cadwalan marries Helen, whom he found at</p>
<div class="verse">
<p class="indent">—þan castle of Deoure</p>
<p>on þere sæ oure;</p>
</div>
<p class="author">(Laȝamon, iii. 250),</p>
<span class="pagenum">xxxii</span>
<a id="intro32"> </a>
<p>which reminds us of Havelok’s wife Goldborough, who was imprisoned
at</p>
<div class="verse">
<p class="indent3">—doure</p>
<p>þat standeth on þe seis oure;</p>
</div>
<p class="author">(l. 320).</p>
<p>The very name Helen, though not the name of Havelok’s wife, was that
of his mother, who was killed by the pirates. For the connection between
Laȝamon’s Helen and pirates, see Sir F. Madden’s note, vol. iii.
p. 428. There is a most curious contradiction in the English lay
about Havelok’s religion; in l. 2520 he is a devout Christian, but
in l. 2580 Godrich speaks of him as being a cruel pagan. Now it was
just about this very time that Paulinus preached in Lindsey, “where the
first that believed was a powerful man called <i>Blecca</i>, with all
his followers” (A.S. Chron. ed. Thorpe, vol. ii. p. 21; <span class="smallroman">A.D.</span> 627). Havelok, according to some, was buried
at Stonehenge; but so was Constantine (Laȝamon, iii. 151). A dearth in
mentioned in the English lay (l. 824); cf. Laȝamon, iii. 279. And I
may here add another coincidence, of an interesting but certainly of a
very circuitous nature. A close examination of the Lay of King Horn
shews that there is no real connection between the story therein
contained and that of Havelok. Yet there is a connection after a sort.
Though by different authors, and in different metre, both lays are found
in English in the same MS.; both versions belong to the same date; both
are from French versions, written by Englishmen from British sources;
and now, if we compare King Horn with the very part of Laȝamon now under
consideration, there is at once seen to be a most exact resemblance in
one point. The story of the ring given by Horn to Rymenhild
(K. Horn, ed. Lumby, ll. 1026-1210) is remarkably like that of the
ring whereby Brian is recognized by his sister (Laȝamon, iii. 234-238).
But it is hardly worth while to pursue the subject further. It may
suffice to suppose that the period of the existence of Havelok and Grim
may be referred to the times of Æthelberht of Kent and Æthelfrith and
Eadwine of Northumbria.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note30" id="tag30">30</a> It is exceedingly probable that Havelok was never
more than a chief or a petty prince, and
<span class="pagenum">xxxiii</span>
<a id="intro33"> </a>
whether he was a Danish or only a British enemy of the Angles is not of
very great importance. If, however, more exact dates be required, they
may be found in “The Conquest of Britain by the Saxons,” by Daniel P.
Haigh, London, 8vo, 1861, pp. 363-367; where the following dates
are suggested. Havelok’s father slain, <span class="smallroman">A.D.</span> 487; his expedition to Denmark, <span class="smallroman">A.D.</span> 507; his reign in England, <span class="smallroman">A.D.</span> 511-531, or a little later. These dates follow
a system which is here about 16 years earlier than the dates in the A.S.
Chronicle. His results are obtained from totally different
considerations. On the whole, let us place Havelok in the <i>sixth</i>
century, at <i>some</i> period of his life.</p>
<p class="section">
<a id="pref_24">§ 24.</a> It is, perhaps, worthy of a
passing remark that some of the circumstances in the Lay may have been
suggested by the romantic story of Eadwine of Northumbria, who was also
born at the close of the sixth century. For he it was who really married
the <i>daughter of Æthelberht</i>, and it was the <i>archbishop of
York</i>, Paulinus, who performed the ceremony. The relation of how
Eadwine was persecuted by <i>Æthelfrith</i>, how he fled and was
protected by Rædwald, king of the East Angles, how he saw a vision of an
angel who promised his restoration to the throne and that his rule
should exceed that of his predecessors, how, with the assistance of
Rædwald, he overthrew and <i>slew Æthelfrith</i> in a terrible battle
beside the river Idle, may be found in Beda’s Ecclesiastical History,
bk. II. ch. 9-16.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note31" id="tag31">31</a> In the last of these chapters there is again mention of
<i>Blecca, the governor of the city of Lincoln</i>. Sir F. Madden, in
his note to l. 45, speaks of the extraordinary proofs of the
peaceable state of the country in the reign of Ælfred; but Beda uses
similar language in speaking of the reign of Eadwine; and the earlier
instance is even more remarkable. “It is reported that there was then
such perfect peace in Britain, wheresoever the dominion of King Edwin
extended, that, as <i>is still proverbially said</i>, a woman with her
new-born babe might walk throughout the island, from sea to sea, without
receiving any harm. That king took such care for the good of his nation,
that in several places where he had seen clear springs near the
highways, he caused stakes to be fixed, with brass dishes hanging
<span class="pagenum">xxxiv</span>
<a id="intro34"> </a>
at them, for the conveniency of travellers; nor durst any man touch them
for any other purpose than that for which they were designed, either
through the dread they had of the king, or for the affection which they
bore him, &amp;c.”<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note32" id="tag32">32</a> Readers who are acquainted with the pleasing poem of
“Edwin of Deira,” by the late Alexander Smith, will remember his
adventures; and it may be noted, as an instance of the manner in which
poets alter names at pleasure, that Mr Smith gives to Æthelfrith the
name of Ethelbert, to Eadwine’s wife Æthelburh, that of Bertha, and to
his father Ælle, that of Egbert. My theory of the Lay of Havelok is then
simply this, that I look upon it as the general result of various
narratives connected with the history of Northumbria and Lindesey at the
close, or possibly the beginning, of the sixth century, gathered round
some favourite local (i.e. Lincolnshire) tradition as a nucleus. A
similar theory may be true of the Lay of Horn.</p>
<h5 class="section">
<a id="pref_25">§ 25.</a> <span class="smallcaps">On the names “Curan” and “Havelok.”</span></h5>
<p>The French version tells us that <i>Coaran</i>, <i>Cuaran</i>, or
<i>Cuheran</i> is the British word for a scullion. This etymology has
not hitherto been traced, but it may easily have been perfectly true.
A glance at Armstrong’s Gaelic Dictionary shews us that the Gaelic
<i>cearn</i> (which answers very well to the Old English <i>hirne</i>, a
corner) has the meaning of a <i>corner</i>, and, secondly, of a
<i>kitchen</i>; and that <i>cearnach</i> is an adjective meaning <i>of
or belonging to a kitchen</i>. But we may come even nearer than this;
for by adding the diminutive ending <i>-an</i> to the Gaelic
<i>cocaire</i>, a cook, we see that <i>Cuheran</i> may really have
conveyed the idea of <i>scullion</i> to a British ear, and this probably
further gave rise to the story of Havelok’s degradation. It is a common
custom—one which true etymologists must always deplore—to
invent a story to account for a derivation; and such a practice is
invariably carried out with greater boldness and to a greater extent if
the said derivation chances to be false. For it is possible that Curan
may be simply the Gaelic <i>curan</i>, a brave man, and the Irish
<i>curanta</i>, brave. The derivation of Havelok is certainly puzzling.
<span class="pagenum">xxxv</span>
<a id="intro35"> </a>
Professor Rask declared it to have no meaning in Danish. It bears,
however, a remarkable resemblance to the Old English <i>gavelok</i>,
which occurs in Weber’s <i>Kyng Alisaunder</i>, l. 1620, and which
is the A.S. <i>gafeluc</i>, Icel. <i>gaflak</i>, Welsh <i>gaflach</i>, a
spear, dart, or javelin. This is an appropriate name for a warrior, and
possibly reappears in the instance of Hugh <i>Kevelock</i>, earl of
Chester (Bp. Percy’s Folio MS., ed. Hales and Furnivall, i. 128). It is
remarkable that the Gaelic and Irish <i>corran</i> has the same sense,
that of <i>a spear</i>, whilst <i>curan</i>, as above-mentioned, means
<i>a brave man</i>. It is best, perhaps, to stop here; for etymology,
when pursued too far, is wont to beguile the pursuer into every possible
quagmire of absurdity.</p>
<h5 class="section">
<a id="pref_26">§ 26.</a> <span class="smallcaps">Description of the MS.</span>, &amp;c.</h5>
<p>The MS. from which the present poem is printed is in the Laudian
collection in the Bodleian Library, where its old mark is K 60, and
its present one Misc. 108. Being described in the old printed catalogue
merely as <i>Vitæ Sanctorum</i>, the romance was in consequence for a
long time overlooked. The Lives of the Saints occupy a large portion of
the volume, and are probably to be ascribed to the authorship of Robert
of Gloucester. “These Lives or Festivals,” says Sir F. Madden, “are
[here] 61 in number, written in long Alexandrine verse. Then succeed the
Sayings of St Bernard and the Visions of St Paul, both in six-line
stanzas; the <i>Disputatio inter Corpus et Animam</i>, the English
Romance of Havelok, the Romance of Kyng Horn, and some additions in a
hand of the 15th century, including the lives of St Blaise, St Cecilia,
and St Alexius, and an alliterative poem intitled <i>Somer Soneday</i>,
making in all the Contents of the Volume to amount to 70 pieces.” The
lays of Havelok and Horn are written out in the same handwriting, of an
early date, certainly not later than the end of the thirteenth century.
The Havelok begins on fol. 204, and is written in double columns, each
column containing 45 lines. A folio is lost between fol. 211 and 212,
but no notice of this has been taken in numbering the folios; hence the
catchword which should have been found at the bottom of fol.
215 <i>b</i>, appears at the bottom of fol. 214 <i>b</i> (see
l. 2164). The poem terminates at the
<span class="pagenum">xxxvi</span>
<a id="intro36"> </a>
27th line on fol. 219 <i>b</i>, and is immediately followed by Kyng Horn
in the same column. The character of the handwriting is bold and square,
but the words are very close together. The initial letter of every line
is written a little way apart from the rest, as in William of Palerne,
and other MSS. Both the long and short <i>s</i>
(ſ <i>and</i> s) are used. The long <i>s</i> is in general
well distinguished from <i>f</i>, and on this account I have taken the
liberty of printing both <i>esses</i> alike, as my experience in
printing the Romans of Partenay proved that the difficulty of avoiding
misprints is greater than the gain of representing the difference
between them. The chief point of interest is that, as in <i>early</i>
MSS., the long <i>s</i> is sometimes found at the <i>end</i> of a word,
as in “uſ” in l. 22, and “iſ” in l. 23. The following are all
the examples of the use of this letter in the first 26 lines; ſo (4),
wicteſte (9), ſtede (10), criſt, ſchilde (16), Kriſt, ſo (17), ſo (19),
ſchal (21), Kriſt, uſ (22), iſ (23), ſtalworþi (24), ſtalworþeſte (25),
ſtede (26). With this exception, the present reprint is a faithful
representation of the original; for, as the exact fidelity of a text is
of the first importance, I have been careful to compare the proof-sheets
with the MS. twice throughout; besides which, the original edition is
itself exceedingly correct, and had been re-read by Sir F. Madden with
the MS. His list of errata (nearly all of them of minor importance)
agreed almost exactly with my own. A great difficulty is caused by the
use of the Saxon letter <i>w</i> (ƿ). This letter, the thorn-letter (þ),
and <i>y</i>, are all three made very nearly alike. In general, the
<i>y</i> is dotted, but the dot is occasionally omitted. Wherever the
letter really appears to be a <i>w</i>, I have denoted it by printing
the <i>w</i> as an italic letter. The following are, I believe, the
<i>only</i> examples of it. <i>W</i>it-drow = withdrew, l. 502;
<i>w</i>e, 1058; <i>w</i>as, 1129 (cf. “him was ful wa,” <i>Sir
Tristr.</i> f. iii. st. 43); ber<i>w</i>en, 1426 (written “berwen” in
l. 697); <i>w</i>at = known, 1674; <i>w</i>e, miswritten for
<i>w</i>o = who, 1914; to which perhaps we may add <i>w</i>it, 997. This
evidence is interesting as shewing that this letter was then fast going
out of use, and I think that we may safely date the final disappearance
of this letter from MSS. at about the year 1300. As regards the
<i>th</i>, we may remark that at the end of a word both þ and <i>th</i>
are used, as in “norþ and suth,”
<span class="pagenum">xxxvii</span>
<a id="intro37"> </a>
l. 434; sometimes <i>th</i> occurs in the middle of a word, as “sithen,”
l. 1238, which is commonly written “siþen,” as in l. 399. The
words þe, þat, þer, &amp;c., are hardly ever written otherwise. But the
reader will remark many instances in which <i>th</i> final seems to have
the hard sound of <i>t</i>, as in <i>brouth</i>, 57, <i>nouth</i>, 58,
<i>lith</i>, 534, <i>þouth</i>, 1190, &amp;c.; cf. § 27. The letter
<i>t</i> is sometimes shortened so as nearly to resemble <i>c</i>, and
<i>c</i> is sometimes lengthened into <i>t</i>. The letters <i>n</i> and
<i>u</i> are occasionally alike, but the difference between them is
commonly well marked. The <i>i</i> has a long stroke over it when
written next to <i>m</i> or <i>n</i>. On the whole, the writing is very
clear and distinct, after a slight acquaintance with it. The poem is
marked out into paragraphs by the use of large letters. I have
introduced a slight space at the end of each paragraph, to shew this
more clearly.</p>
<h5 class="section">
<a id="pref_27">§ 27.</a> <span class="smallcaps">On the grammatical forms occurring in the poem.</span></h5>
<p>The following peculiarities of spelling may be first noted. We
frequently find <i>h</i> prefixed to words which it is usual to spell
without one. Examples are: <i>holde</i> for <i>old</i>, <i>hete</i> for
<i>ete</i> (eat), <i>het</i> for <i>et</i> (ate), <i>heuere</i> for
<i>euere</i>, <i>Henglishe</i> for <i>Englishe</i>, &amp;c.; see the
Glossary, under the letter H. This enables us to explain some words
which at first appear puzzling; thus <i>her</i> = <i>er</i>, ere;
<i>hayse</i> = <i>ayse</i>, ease; <i>helde</i> = <i>elde</i>, old age;
<i>hore</i> = <i>ore</i>, grace; <i>hende</i> = <i>ende</i>, which in
one passage means <i>end</i>, but in another <i>a duck</i>. The forms
<i>hof</i>, <i>hus</i>, <i>hure</i>, for <i>of</i>, <i>us</i>,
<i>ure</i> are such as we should hardly have expected to find. On the
other hand, <i>h</i> is omitted in the words <i>auelok</i>,
<i>aueden</i>, <i>osed</i>, and in <i>is</i> for <i>his</i>
(l. 2254). These instances, and other examples such as follow, may
readily be found by help of the Glossarial Index. Again, <i>d</i> final
after <i>l</i> or <i>n</i> was so slightly sounded as to be omitted even
in writing. Examples are: <i>lon</i> for <i>lond</i>, <i>hel</i> for
<i>held</i>, <i>bihel</i> for <i>biheld</i>, <i>shel</i> for
<i>sheld</i>, <i>gol</i> for <i>gold</i>. But a more extraordinary
omission is that of <i>r</i> final in <i>the</i>, <i>neythe</i>,
<i>othe</i>, <i>douthe</i>, which does not seem to be satisfactorily
explained even by the supposition that the scribe may have omitted the
small upward curl which does duty for <i>er</i> so frequently in MSS.
For we further find the omission of <i>l</i> final, as in <i>mike</i>
for <i>mikel</i>, <i>we</i> for <i>wel</i>, and of <i>t</i> final, as in
<i>bes</i> for <i>best</i>; from which
<span class="pagenum">xxxviii</span>
<a id="intro38"> </a>
instances we should rather infer some peculiarity of pronunciation
rendering final letters indistinct, of which there are numerous
examples, as <i>fiel</i> for <i>field</i>, in modern provincial English.
Cf. <i>il</i> for <i>ilk</i>, in ll. 818, 1740; and <i>twel</i> for
<i>twelf</i>. “From the same license,” says Sir F. Madden, “arises the
frequent repetition of such rhythm as <i>riden</i> and <i>side</i>,
where the final <i>n</i> seems to have been suppressed in pronunciation.
Cf. ll. 29, 254, 957, 1105, 1183, 2098, &amp;c, and hence we perceive
how readily the infinitive verbal Saxon termination glided into its
subsequent form. The broad pronunciation of the dialect in which the
poem was written is also frequently discernible, as in <i>slawen</i>,
l. 2676, and <i>knaue</i>, l. 949, which rhyme to <i>Rauen</i>
and <i>plawe</i>.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note33" id="tag33">33</a> So likewise, <i>bothe</i> or <i>bethe</i> is, in sound,
equivalent to <i>rede</i>, ll. 360, 694, 1680.” Other peculiarities will
be noticed in discussing the Metre. Observe also the Anglo-Saxon
<i>hw</i> for the modern <i>wh</i>, exemplified by <i>hwo</i>, 368,
<i>hwan</i>, 474, <i>hweþer</i>, 294, <i>hwere</i>, 549, <i>hwil</i>,
301; compare also <i>qual</i>, <i>qui</i>, <i>quan</i>, meaning
<i>whale</i>, <i>why</i>, <i>when</i>.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note34" id="tag34">34</a> The letter <i>w</i> (initial) is the
modern provincial <i>’oo</i>, as in <i>wlf</i>, <i>wluine</i>,
<i>wman</i>; cf. <i>hw</i>, <i>w</i>, both forms of <i>how</i>; and
<i>lowerd</i> for <i>louerd</i>. In particular, we should notice the
hard sound of <i>t</i> denoted by <i>th</i> in the words <i>with</i>,
<i>rithe</i>, <i>brouth</i>, <i>nouth</i>, <i>ricth</i>, <i>knicth</i>,
meaning <i>white</i>, <i>right</i>, <i>brought</i>, <i>naught</i>,
<i>right</i>, <i>knight</i>; so too <i>douther</i>, daughter,
<i>neth</i>, a net, <i>uth</i>, out, <i>woth</i>, wot, <i>leth</i>, let,
<i>lauthe</i> (<i>laught</i>), caught, <i>nither-tale</i>
(<i>nighter-tale</i>), night-time.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note35" id="tag35">35</a> On the other hand, <i>t</i> stands for
<i>th</i> in <i>hauet</i>, 564, <i>seyt</i>, 647, <i>herknet</i>, 1,
<i>wit</i>, 100. When <i>th</i> answers to the modern sound, it seems
equivalent to A.S. ð rather than to A.S. þ; examples are <i>mouth</i>,
433, <i>oth</i>, 260, <i>loth</i>, 261. <i>Y</i> and <i>g</i> are
interchangeable, as in <i>yaf</i>, <i>gaf</i>, <i>youen</i>,
<i>gouen</i>; <i>g</i> even occurs for <i>k</i>, as in <i>rang</i>,
2561. In MSS., <i>e</i> is not uncommonly written by
<span class="pagenum">xxxix</span>
<a id="intro39"> </a>
mistake for <i>o</i>; this may perhaps account for <i>helde</i>, 2472,
<i>meste</i>, 233, <i>her</i>, 1924, which should rather be
<i>holde</i>, 30, <i>moste</i>, and <i>hor</i>, 235; there is a like
confusion of <i>weren</i> and <i>woren</i>; and perhaps <i>grotinde</i>
should be <i>gretinde</i>.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note36" id="tag36">36</a> The vowel <i>u</i> is replaced by the modern
<i>ou</i> in the words <i>prud</i>, 302, <i>suth</i>, 434, <i>but</i>,
1040, <i>hus</i>, 740, <i>spusen</i>, 1123; cf. <i>hws</i> in
l. 1141. Mr Ellis shews, in his Early English Pronunciation,
chap. v, that in pure specimens of the <i>thirteenth</i> century,
there is no <i>ou</i> in such words, and in the <i>fourteenth</i>
century, no simple <i>u</i>. This furnishes a ready explanation of the
otherwise difficult <i>sure</i>, in l. 2005; it is merely the
adverb of <i>sour</i>, <i>sourly</i> being used in the sense of
<i>bitterly</i>; to <i>bye it bitterly</i>, or <i>bye it bittre</i>, is
a common phrase in Piers Plowman. Other spellings worth notice occur in
<i>ouerga</i>, 314, <i>stra</i>, 315 (spelt <i>strie</i> in
l. 998), <i>hawe</i>, 1188, <i>plawe</i>, 950, <i>sal</i>, 628
(commonly spelt <i>shal</i>). Note also <i>arum</i> for <i>arm</i>,
<i>harum</i> for <i>harm</i>, <i>boren</i> for <i>born</i>, 1878, and
<i>koren</i> for <i>corn</i>, 1879. There are several instances of words
joined together, as <i>haui</i>, 2002, <i>biddi</i>, 484; <i>shaltu</i>,
2186, <i>wiltu</i>, 905, <i>wenestu</i>, 1787; <i>wilte</i>, 528,
<i>thenkeste</i>, 578, <i>shaltou</i>, 1800; <i>thouthe</i>, 790,
<i>hauedet</i>, <i>youenet</i>, <i>hauenet</i>; <i>sawe</i>, 338;
<i>latus</i>, 1772; where the personal pronouns <i>i</i>, <i>þu</i>,
<i>he</i>, <i>it</i>, <i>we</i>, <i>us</i> are added to the verb. Hence,
in l. 745, it is very likely that <i>calleth</i> is written for
<i>callet</i>, i.e. call it; and on the same principle we can explain
<i>dones</i>; see <i>Es</i> in the Glossary. In like manner
<i>goddot</i> is contracted from <i>God wot</i>; and <i>þerl</i> from
<i>þe erl</i>.</p>
<p><i>Nouns.</i> As regards the nouns employed, I may remark that the
final <i>e</i> is perhaps always sounded in the oblique cases, and
especially in the dative case; as in <i>nedè</i>, <i>stedè</i>, &amp;c.
(see ll. 86-105), <i>willè</i>, 85, <i>gyuè</i>, 357, <i>blissè</i>,
2187, <i>cricè</i>, 2450; cf. the adjectives <i>longè</i>, 2299,
<i>wisè</i>, 1713; also the nominatives <i>rosè</i>, 2919, <i>newè</i>,
2974. <i>Frend</i> is a pl. form; cf. <i>hend</i>, which is both a
plural (2444) and a dat. sing. (505). In the plural, the final <i>e</i>
is fully pronounced in the adjectives <i>allè</i>, 2, <i>hardè</i>, 143,
<i>starkè</i>, 1015, <i>fremdè</i>, 2277, <ins class="mycorr" title="è conjectural: printed text unclear"><i>bleikè</i></ins> 470, and in
many others; cf. the full form <i>boþen</i>, 2223. Not only does the
phrase <i>none kines</i>, of no kind, occur in ll. 861, 1140, but we
find the unusual phrase <i>neuere kines</i>, of
<span class="pagenum">xl</span>
<a id="intro40"> </a>
never a kind, in l. 2691. Among the numerals, we find not only
<i>þre</i>, but <i>þrinne</i>.</p>
<p><i>Pronouns.</i> The first personal pronoun occurs in many forms in
the nominative, as <i>i</i>, <i>y</i>, <i>hi</i>, <i>ich</i>, <i>ic</i>,
<i>hic</i>, and even <i>ihc</i>; the oblique cases take the form
<i>me</i>. For the second person, we have <i>þu</i>, <i>þou</i>, in the
nominative, and also <i>tu</i>, when preceded by <i>þat</i>, as in
l. 2903. We may notice also <i>hijs</i> for <i>his</i>, l. 47;
<i>he</i> for <i>they</i>; <i>sho</i>, 112, <i>scho</i>, 126,
<i>sche</i>, 1721, for <i>she</i>; and, in particular, the dual form
<i>unker</i>, of you two, 1882. The most noteworthy possessive pronouns
are <i>minè</i>, pl. 1365, <i>þinè</i>, pl. 620; <i>his</i> or
<i>hise</i>, pl. <i>hisè</i>, 34; <i>ure</i>, 606; <i>youres</i>, 2800;
<i>hirè</i>, 2918, with which cf. the dat. sing. <i>hirè</i> of the
personal pronoun, 85, 300. <i>þis</i> is plural, and means <i>these</i>,
in l. 1145. As in other old English works, <i>men</i> is frequently
an impersonal pronoun, answering to the French <i>on</i>, and is
followed by a singular verb; as in <i>men ringes</i>, 390, <i>men seyt
and suereth</i>, 647, <i>men fetes</i>, 2341, <i>men nam</i>, 900,
<i>men birþe</i>, 2101, <i>men dos</i>, 2434; cf. <i>folk sau</i>, 2410;
but there are a few instances of its use with a plural verb, as <i>men
haueden</i>, 901, <i>men shulen</i>, 747. The former is the more usual
construction.</p>
<p><i>Verbs.</i> The infinitives of verbs rarely have <i>y-</i>
prefixed; two examples are <i>y-lere</i>, 12, <i>y-se</i>, 334. Nor is
the same prefix common before past participles; yet we find
<i>i-gret</i>, 163, <i>i-groten</i>, 285, and <i>i-maked</i>, 5, as well
as <i>maked</i>, 23. Infinitives end commonly in <i>-en</i> or
<i>-e</i>, as <i>riden</i>, 26, <i>y-lere</i>; also in <i>-n</i>, as
<i>don</i>, 117, <i>leyn</i>, 718; and even in <i>-o</i>, as <i>flo</i>,
612, <i>slo</i>, 1364. The present singular, 3rd person, of the
indicative, ends both in <i>-es</i> or <i>-s</i>, and <i>-eth</i> or
<i>-th</i>, the former being the more usual. Examples are <i>longes</i>,
396, <i>leues</i>, 1781, <i>haldes</i>, 1382, <i>fedes</i>, 1693,
<i>bes</i>, 1744, <i>comes</i>, 1767, <i>glides</i>, 1851,
<i>þarnes</i>, 1913, <i>haues</i>, 1952, <i>etes</i>, 2036, <i>dos</i>,
1913; also <i>eteth</i>, 672, <i>haueth</i>, 804, <i>bikenneth</i>,
1269, <i>doth</i>, 1876, <i>liþ</i>, 673. The full form of the 2nd
person is <i>-est</i>, as <i>louest</i>, 1663; but it is commonly cut
down to <i>-es</i>, as <i>weldes</i>, 1359, <i>slepes</i>, 1283,
<i>haues</i>, 688, <i>etes</i>, 907, <i>getes</i>, 908; cf. <i>dos</i>,
2390, <i>mis-gos</i>, 2707, <i>slos</i>, 2706. The same dropping of the
<i>t</i> is observable in the past tense, as in <i>reftes</i>, 2394,
<i>feddes and claddes</i>, 2907. Still more curious is the ending in
<i>t</i> only, as in <i>þu bi-hetet</i>, 677, <i>þou mait</i>, 689; cf.
ll. 852, 1348. In the subjunctive mood the <i>-st</i> disappears as in
Anglo-Saxon,
<span class="pagenum">xli</span>
<a id="intro41"> </a>
and hence the forms <i>bute þou gonge</i>, 690, <i>þat þu fonge</i>,
856, &amp;c.; cf. <i>bede</i>, 668. In the 3rd person, present tense, of
the same mood, we have the <i>-e</i> fully pronounced, as in
<i>shildè</i>, 16, <i>yeuè</i>, 22, <i>leuè</i>, 334, <i>redè</i>, 687;
and in l. 544, <i>wreken</i> should undoubtedly be <i>wrekè</i>,
since the <i>-en</i> belongs to the plural, as in <i>moten</i>, 18. The
plural of the indicative present ends in <i>-en</i>, as, <i>we
hauen</i>, 2798, <i>ye witen</i>, 2208, <i>þei taken</i>, 1833; or, very
rarely, in <i>-eth</i>, as <i>ye bringeth</i>, 2425, <i>he</i> (they)
<i>strangleth</i>, 2584. Sometimes the final <i>-n</i> is lost, as in
<i>we haue</i>, 2799, <i>ye do</i>, 2418, <i>he</i> (they)
<i>brenne</i>, 2583. There is even a trace of the plural in <i>-es</i>,
as in <i>haues</i>, 2581. The <i>present</i> tense has often a
<i>future</i> signification, as in <i>etes</i>, 907, <i>eteth</i>, 672,
<i>getes</i>, 908.</p>
<p><i>Past tense.</i> Of the third person singular and plural of the
past tense the following are selected examples. <span class="smallcaps">Weak Verbs</span>: <i>hauede</i>, 770, <i>sparedè</i>, 898,
<i>yemedè</i>, 975, <i>semedè</i>, 976, <i>sparkëdè</i>, 2144,
<i>þankedè</i>, 2189; pl. <i>loueden</i>, 955, <i>leykeden</i>, 954,
<i>woundeden</i>, 2429, <i>stareden</i>, 1037, <i>yemede</i> (rather
read <i>yemeden</i>), 2277, <i>makeden</i>, 554, <i>sprauleden</i>, 475;
also <i>calde</i>, 2115, <i>gredde</i>, 2417, <i>herde</i>, 2410,
<i>kepte</i>, 879, <i>fedde</i>, 786, <i>ledde</i>, 785, <i>spedde</i>,
756, <i>clapte</i>, 1814, <i>kiste</i>, 1279; pl. <i>herden</i>,
<i>brenden</i>, 594, <i>kisten</i>, 2162, <i>ledden</i>, 1246; and,
thirdly, of the class which change the vowel, <i>aute</i>, 743,
<i>laute</i>, 744, <i>bitauhte</i>, 2212. Compare the past participles
<i>osed</i>, 971, <i>mixed</i>, 2533, <i>parred</i>, 2439,
<i>gadred</i>, 2577; <i>reft</i>, 1367, <i>wend</i>, 2138, <i>hyd</i>,
1059; <i>told</i>, 1036, <i>sold</i>, 1638, <i>wrouth</i> =
<i>wrout</i>, 1352. There are also at least two past participles in
<i>-et</i>, as <i>slenget</i>, 1923, <i>grethet</i>, 2615, to which add
<i>weddeth</i>, <i>beddeth</i>, 1127, In l. 2057, <i>knawed</i>
seems put for <i>knawen</i>, for the rime’s sake.</p>
<p><span class="smallcaps">Strong Verbs</span>: third person singular,
past tense, <i>bar</i>, 815, <i>bad</i>, 1415, <i>yaf</i>, or
<i>gaf</i>, <i>spak</i>; <i>kam</i>, 766 (spelt <i>cham</i>, 1873),
<i>nam</i>, <i>kneu</i>, <i>hew</i>, 2729, <i>lep</i>, 1777, <i>let</i>,
2447 (spelt <i>leth</i>, 2651), <i>slep</i>, 1280, <i>wex</i>, 281:
<i>drou</i>, 705, <i>for</i>, 2943, <i>low</i>, 903, <i>slow</i>, 1807,
<i>hof</i>, 2750, <i>stod</i>, 986, <i>tok</i>; 751, <i>wok</i>, 2093;
pl. <i>beden</i>, 2774, <i>youen</i>, or <i>gouen</i>; <i>comen</i>,
1017 (spelt <i>keme</i>, 1208), <i>nomen</i>, 2790 (spelt <i>neme</i>,
1207), <i>knewen</i>, 2149, <i>lopen</i>, 1896, <i>slepen</i>, 2128;
<i>drowen</i>, 1837, <i>foren</i>, 2380, <i>lowen</i>, 1056,
<i>slowen</i>, 2414, &amp;c. And secondly, of the class which more
usually change the vowel in the <i>plural</i> of the preterite, we find
the singular forms <i>bigan</i>, 1357, <i>barw</i>, 2022, <i>karf</i>,
471, <i>swank</i>, 788, <i>warp</i>, 1061, <i>shon</i>, 2144,
<i>clef</i>, 2643, <i>sau</i>, 2409, <i>grop</i>, 1965, <i>drof</i>,
725, <i>shof</i>,
<span class="pagenum">xlii</span>
<a id="intro42"> </a>
892; pl. <i>bigunnen</i>, 1011, <i>sowen</i>, 1055, <i>gripen</i>, 1790,
<i>driue</i>, for <i>driuen</i>, 1966; also <i>bunden</i>, 2436,
<i>scuten</i>, 2431 (spelt <i>schoten</i>, 1864, <i>shoten</i>, 1838),
<i>leyen</i>, 2132, &amp;c. Compare the past participles <i>boren</i>,
1878, <i>youen</i> or <i>gouen</i>, <i>cumen</i>, 1436, <i>nomen</i>,
2265 (spelt <i>numen</i>, 2581), <i>laten</i>, 1925, <i>waxen</i>, 302,
<i>drawen</i>, 1925, <i>slawen</i>, 2000, which two last become
<i>drawe</i>, <i>slawe</i> in ll. 1802, 1803.</p>
<p>We should also observe the past tenses <i>spen</i>, 1819,
<i>stirt</i>, 812, <i>fauth</i> for <i>faut</i> or <i>fauht</i>, 1990,
<i>citte</i>, 942, <i>bere</i>, 974, <i>kipte</i>, 1050, <i>flow</i>,
2502, <i>plat</i>, 2755; and the past participles <i>demd</i> for
<i>demed</i>, 2488, <i>giue</i> for <i>giuen</i>, 2488, <i>henged</i>,
1429, <i>keft</i>, 2005.</p>
<p><i>Imperative Mood.</i> Examples of the imperative mood singular, 2nd
person, are <i>et</i>, <i>sit</i>, 925, <i>nim</i>, 1336, <i>yif</i>,
674; in the plural, the usual ending is <i>-es</i>, as in <i>liþes</i>,
2204, <i>comes</i>, 1798, <i>folwes</i>, 1885, <i>lokes</i>, 2292,
<i>bes</i>, 2246, to which set belong <i>slos</i>, 2596, <i>dos</i>,
2592; but there are instances of the ending <i>-eth</i> also, as in
<i>cometh</i>, 1885, <i>yeueþ</i>, 911, to which add <i>doth</i>, 2037,
<i>goth</i>, 1780. Indeed both forms occur in one line, as in <i>Cometh
swiþe, and folwes me</i> (1885). Instead of <i>-eth</i> we even find
<i>-et</i>, as in <i>herknet</i>, 1. These variations afford a good
illustration of the unsettled state of the grammar in some parts of
England at this period; we need not suppose the scribe to be at fault in
all cases where there is a want of uniformity.</p>
<p>Of reflexive verbs, we meet with <i>me dremede</i>, 1284, <i>me
met</i>, 1285, <i>me þinkes</i>, 2169, <i>him hungrede</i>, 654, <i>him
semede</i>, 1652, <i>him stondes</i>, 2983, <i>him rewede</i>, 503. The
present participles end most commonly in <i>-inde</i>, as
<i>fastinde</i>, 865, <i>grotinde</i> (? <i>gretinde</i>), 1390,
<i>lauhwinde</i>, 946, <i>plattinde</i>, 2282, <i>starinde</i>, 508; but
we also find <i>gangande</i>, 2283, <i>driuende</i>, 2702. Compare the
nouns <i>tiþande</i>, 2279, <i>offrende</i>, 1386, which are Norse
forms, <i>tíðindi</i> (pl.) being the Icelandic for <i>tidings</i>, and
<i>offrandi</i> the present participle of <i>offra</i>, to offer. But
the true Icelandic equivalent of the substantive <i>an offering</i> is
<i>offran</i>, and the old Swedish is <i>offer</i>; and hence we see at
how very early a date the confusion between the noun-ending and the
ending of the present participle arose; a confusion which has bewildered
many generations of Englishmen. Yet this very poem in other places has
<i>-ing</i> as a noun-ending <i>only</i>, never (that I remember) for
the present participle. Examples of it are
<span class="pagenum">xliii</span>
<a id="intro43"> </a>
<i>greting</i>, 166, <i>dreping</i>, i.e. slaughter, 2684,
<i>buttinge</i>, <i>skirming</i>, <i>wrastling</i>, <i>putting</i>,
<i>harping</i>, <i>piping</i>, <i>reding</i>; see ll. 2322-2327. Such
words are frequently called <i>verbal nouns</i>, but the term is very
likely to mislead. I have found that many suppose it to imply <i>present
participles used as nouns</i>, instead of <i>nouns of verbal
derivation</i>. If such nouns could be called by some new name, such as
<i>nouns of action</i>, or by any other title that can be conventionally
restricted to signify them, it would, I think, be a gain. Amongst the
auxiliary verbs, may be noted the use of <i>cone</i>, 622, as the
subjunctive form of <i>canst</i>; <i>we mone</i>, 840, as the
subjunctive of <i>mowen</i>; cf. <i>ye mowen</i>, 11; but especially we
should observe the use of the comparatively rare verbs <i>birþe</i>, it
behoves, pt. t. <i>birde</i>, it behoved, and <i>þurte</i>, he
need, the latter of which is fully explained in the Glossary to William
of Palerne, s.v. <i>þort</i>.</p>
<p>The prefix <i>to-</i> is employed in <i>both</i> senses, as explained
in the same Glossary, s.v. <i>To-</i>. In <i>to-brised</i>,
<i>to-deyle</i>, &amp;c., it is equivalent to the German <i>zer-</i> and
Mœso-Gothic <i>dis-</i>; of its <i>other</i> and <i>rarer</i> use,
wherein it answers to the German <i>zu-</i> and Mœso-Gothic <i>du-</i>,
there is but <i>one</i> instance, viz. in the word <i>to-yede</i>, 765,
which signifies <i>went to</i>; cf. Germ. <i>zugehen</i>, to go to,
<i>zugang</i> (A.S. <i>to-gang</i>), access, approach. There are some
curious instances of a peculiar syntax, whereby the infinitive mood
active partakes of a passive signification, as in <i>he made him kesten,
and in feteres festen</i>, he caused him to be cast in prison (<i>or
perhaps</i>, overthrown), and to be fastened in fetters; l. 81. But
it is probable that this is to be explained by considering it as a
phrase in which we should <i>now</i> supply the word <i>men</i>, and
that we may interpret it by “he caused [men] to cast him in prison, and
to fasten him with fetters;” for in ll. 1784, 1785, the phrase is
repeated in a less ambiguous form. See also l. 86. So also, in ll.
2611, 2612, I consider <i>keste</i>, <i>late</i>, <i>sette</i>, to
be in the infinitive mood. Such a construction is at once understood by
comparing it with the German <i>er liess ihn binden</i>, he caused him
to be bound. In l. 2352, appears the most unusual form
<i>ilker</i>, which is literally <i>of each</i>, and hence,
<i>apiece</i>; cf. <i>unker</i>, which also is a genitive plural. It
will be observed that the verb following is in the plural, the real
nominative to it being <i>þei þre</i>. In l. 2404, the expression
<i>þat þer þrette</i>, “that there threat,” recalls a colloquialism
<span class="pagenum">xliv</span>
<a id="intro44"> </a>
which is still common. The word <i>þrie</i>, 730, is, apparently, the
O.E. adverb <i>thrie</i>, thrice; <i>liues</i>, 509, is an adverb ending
in <i>-es</i>, originally a genitive case. <i>Þus-gate</i> is, according
to Mr Morris, unknown to the Southern dialect; it occurs in ll. 785,
2419, 2586. I may add that Havelok contains as many as five expressions,
which seem to refer to <i>proverbs</i> current at the time of writing
it. See ll. 307, 648, 1338, 1352, 2461.</p>
<h5 class="section">
<a id="pref_28">§ 28.</a> <span class="smallcaps">On the Metre of Havelok.</span></h5>
<p>The poem is written in the familiar rhythm of which I have already
spoken elsewhere, viz. at p. xxxvii of the Preface to Mr Morris’s
edition of Genesis and Exodus. The metre of Havelok is rather more
regular, but many of the remarks there made apply to it. The chief rule
is that every line shall contain four accents,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note37" id="tag37">37</a> the two principal types
being afforded (1) by the eight-syllable and nine-syllable
lines—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>(<i>a</i>) For hém | ne yé|dë góld | ne fé, 44;</p>
<p>(<i>b</i>) It wás | a kíng | bi á|rë dáwës, 27;</p>
</div>
<p>and (2) by the seven-syllable and eight-syllable lines—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>(<i>c</i>) Hérk|net tó | me gó|dë men, 1;</p>
<p>(<i>d</i>) Al|lë thát | he mícth|ë fyndë, 42.</p>
</div>
<p>To one of these four forms every line can be reduced, by the use of
that slighter utterance of less important syllables which is so very
common in English poetry. It is not the number of <i>syllables</i>, but
of <i>accents</i>, that is essential. In <i>every</i> line throughout
the poem there are four accents, with only two or three exceptions,
<span class="pagenum">xlv</span>
<a id="intro45"> </a>
viz. ll. 1112, 1678, &amp;c, which are defective. In a similar manner,
we may readily scan any of the lines, as e.g. ll. 2-4;</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>(<i>c</i>) Wi|uës, mayd|nës, and al|lë men</p>
<p>(<i>b</i>) Of a ta|lë þat | ich you | wile tellë<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note38" id="tag38">38</a></p>
<p>(<i>b</i>) Wo-so | ’t wil’ her’ | and þer|to duellë, &amp;c.</p>
</div>
<p>Here the syllables <i>-nes and</i> in <ins class="mycorr" title="error for ‘l. 2’">l. 3</ins>, <i>of a</i> in <ins class="mycorr" title="error for ‘l. 3’">l. 4</ins>, and <i>it wile</i> in <ins class="mycorr" title="error for ‘l. 4’">l. 5</ins>, are so rapidly
pronounced as to occupy only the room of one unaccented syllable in
lines of the strict type. However awkward this appears to be in theory,
it is very easy in practice, as the reciter readily manages his voice so
as to produce the right rhythmical effect; and, indeed, this variation
of arrangement is a real improvement, preventing the recitation from
becoming monotonous. Those who have a good ear for rhythm will readily
understand this, and it seems unnecessary to dwell upon it more at
length. But it may be remarked, that the three lines above quoted are
rather <i>more irregular than usual</i>, and that the metre is such as
to enable us to fix the instances in which the final <i>-e</i> is
pronounced with great accuracy, on which account I shall say more about
this presently. I would, however, first enumerate the rimes which seem
to be more or less inexact or peculiar, or otherwise instructive.</p>
<p>I. <i>Repetitions.</i> Such are <i>men, men</i>; <i>holden,
holde</i>, 29;<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note39" id="tag39">39</a> <i>erþe, erþe</i>, 739; <i>heren, heren</i>, 1640;
<i>nithes, knithes</i>, 2048; <i>youres, youres</i>, 2800. To this class
belong also <i>longe, londe</i>, 172, <i>heye, heie</i>, 1151, 2544;
where <i>longe, londe</i> is, however, only an assonance.</p>
<p>II. <i>Assonant rimes.</i> Here the rime is in the vowel-sound; the
consonantal endings differ. Such are <i>rym, fyn</i>, 21; <i>yeme,
quene</i>, 182; <i>shop, hok</i>, 1101 (where <i>shop</i> is probably
corrupt); <i>odrat, bad</i>, 1153; <i>fet, ek</i>, 1303; <i>yer,
del</i>, 1333; <i>maked, shaped</i>, 1646; <i>beþe, rede</i>, 1680;
<i>riche, chinche</i>, 1763, 2940; <i>feld, swerd</i>, 1824, 2634;
<i>seruede, werewed</i>, 1914; <i>wend, gent</i>, 2138; <i>þank,
rang</i>, 2560; <i>boþen, ut-drowen</i>, 2658. To the same class belong
<i>name</i>, <i>rauen</i>,
<span class="pagenum">xlvi</span>
<a id="intro46"> </a>
1397, <i>grauen, name</i>, 2528; <i>slawen, rauen</i>, 2676. <i>Henged,
slenget</i>, 1922, should rather be called an imperfect rime.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note40" id="tag40">40</a> There is also
found the exact opposite to this, viz., an agreement or
<i>consonance</i> at the end, preceded by an apparent diversity in the
vowel; as <i>longe, gange</i>, 795 (but see <i>longe, gonge</i>, 843),
<i>bidde, stede</i>, 2548, <i>open, drepen</i>, 1782, <i>gres, is</i>,
2698, <i>boþe, rathe</i>, 2936 (but see <i>rathe, bathe</i>, 1335,
2542), <i>fet</i> (long <i>e</i>), <i>gret</i>, 2158; and not unlike
these are some instances of loose rimes, as <i>beþe, rede</i>, 360,
<i>knaue, plawe</i>, 949, <i>sawe, hawe</i> (where <i>hawe</i> is
written for <i>haue</i>), 1187, <i>sawe, wowe</i>, 1962 (but see
<i>wowe, lowe</i>, 2078, <i>lowe, sawe</i>, 2142, <i>wawe, lowe</i>,
2470). Observe also <i>bouth, oft</i> (read <i>vt</i> or <i>ut</i> =
<i>out</i>?), 883, <i>tun, barun</i>, 1001 (cf. <i>toun, brun</i>, 1750,
<i>champiouns, barouns</i>, 1032); <i>plattinde, gangande</i>, 2282,
&amp;c. <i>Eir, toþer</i>, 410, <i>harde, crakede</i>, 567, are probably
due to mistakes.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note41" id="tag41">41</a></p>
<p>III. Rimes which shew that the final <i>-en</i> was pronounced so
slightly as to be nearly equivalent to <i>-e</i>. Examples: <i>holden,
holde</i>, 29; <i>gongen, fonge</i>, 855; <i>bringe, ringen</i>, 1105;
<i>mouthen, douthe</i>, 1183; <i>riden, side</i>, 1758; <i>wesseylen,
to-deyle</i>, 2098; <i>slawen, drawe</i>, 2476. In the same way
<i>hon</i> rimes to <i>lond</i>, 1341, owing to the slight pronunciation
of the final <i>d</i>.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note42" id="tag42">42</a></p>
<p>IV. Rimes which appear imperfect, but may be perfect. <i>Riche</i>
answers to <i>like</i>, 132, but the true spelling is <i>rike</i>,
answering to <i>sike</i>, 290. <i>Mithe</i>, 196, should probably be
<i>moucte</i>, as in l. 257, and it would thus rime with
<i>þoucte</i>. <i>Blinne</i>, 2670, should certainly be <i>blunne</i>;
cf. A.S. <i>blinnan</i>, pt. t. s. <i>ic blan</i>, pt. t. pl. <i>we
blunnon</i>; and thus it rimes to <i>sunne</i>. <i>Misdede</i>, 993, is
clearly an error for
<span class="pagenum">xlvii</span>
<a id="intro47"> </a>
<i>misseyde</i>, as appears from the parallel passage in ll. 49, 50; and
it then rimes with <i>leyde</i>. So in l. 1736, for <i>deled</i>
read <i>deyled</i>, as in l. 2098. <i>Boþe</i>, 430, has no line
answering to it, and a line may have been lost. <i>Nicth, lict</i>, 575,
is a perfect rime. <i>Halde, bolde</i>, 2308, may also be perfect.
<i>For-sworen</i> answers to <i>for-lorn</i> (pronounced
<i>for-loren</i>), 1423; <i>bitawte</i> to <i>authe</i> (pronounced
<i>aute</i>), 1409; <i>yemede</i> (pronounced <i>yem-dè</i>) is not an
improper rime to <i>fremde</i>, 2276; <i>anon</i> rimes with
<i>iohan</i> (if pronounced <i>ion</i> or <i>John</i>, as indicated by
the spelling <i>ion</i> in l. 177), 2562, 2956. Yet in another
instance it seems to be two syllables, <i>Jo-han</i>; see <i>wimman,
iohan</i>, 1720.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note43" id="tag43">43</a> Speche should be <i>speke</i>, and thus rimes to
<i>meke</i>, 1065. <i>Stareden</i> should perhaps be <i>stradden</i>, or
some such form, rightly riming to <i>ladden</i>, 1037. Under this head
we may notice some rimes which throw, possibly, some light on the
pronunciation. Thus, for the sound of <i>ey, ei</i>, observe <i>hayse,
preyse</i>, 60; <i>leyke, bleike</i>, 469; <i>laumprei, wei</i>, 771;
<i>deye</i> rimes to <i>preye</i>, 168; <i>day</i> to <i>wey</i>, 663;
<i>seyd</i> to <i>brayd</i>, 1281; but we also find <i>hey, fri</i>,
1071; <i>hey, sley</i>, 1083, <i>heye, heie</i>, 1151; <i>heye, eie</i>,
2544; <i>leye, heye</i>, 2010; <i>heye, fleye</i>, 2750. <i>Fram</i>
rimes to <i>sham</i>, 55; yet the latter word is really <i>shame</i>,
83; <i>gange</i> is also spelt <i>gonge</i>, <i>halde</i> rimes with
<i>bolde</i>, 2308. The pronunciation of <i>ware</i>, <i>were</i>, or
<i>wore</i>, seems ambiguous; we find <i>sore, wore</i>, 236; <i>wore,
more</i>, 258; <i>ware, sare</i>, 400; <i>wore, sore</i>, 414; <i>were,
þere</i>, 741; <i>more, þore</i>, 921. For the sound of <i>e</i>,
observe <i>suere, gere</i>, 388; <i>suereth, dereth</i>, 648; <i>eten,
geten</i>, 930; <i>yet, fet</i>, 1319; <i>stem, bem</i>, 592; <i>glem,
bem</i>, 2122; also <i>yeue, liue</i>, 198; <i>liue, gyue</i>, 356;
<i>lyue, yeue</i>, 1217; <i>her, ther</i>, 1924; <i>fishere, swere</i>,
2230. For that of <i>i</i>,
<span class="pagenum">xlviii</span>
<a id="intro48"> </a>
observe <i>cri, merci</i>, 270; <i>sire, swire</i>, 310; <i>swiþe,
vnbliþe</i>, 140; <i>fir, shir</i>, 587; <i>sire, hire</i>, 909;
<i>rise, bise</i>, 723; <i>fyr, shir</i>, 915; <i>lye, strie</i>, 997;
<i>hey, fri</i>, 1071; <i>for-þi, merci</i>, 2500. For that of <i>o</i>,
observe <i>two, so</i>, 350; <i>do, so</i>, 713; <i>shon, on</i>, 969;
<i>hom, grom</i>, 789; <i>lode, brode</i>, 895; <i>anon, ston</i>, 927;
<i>ston, won</i>, 1023; <i>do, sho</i> (shoe), 1137; <i>do, sho</i>
(she), 1231; <i>stod, mod</i>, 1702; <i>ilkon, ston</i>, 1842;
<i>shon</i> (shoon), <i>ston</i>, 2144; <i>croud, god</i>, 2338; <i>don,
bon</i>, 2354; <i>sone</i> (soon), <i>bone</i>, 2504; <i>bole, hole</i>,
2438.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note44" id="tag44">44</a> Only in a few of these instances would the words rime
in modern standard English. For the <i>ou</i> and <i>u</i> sounds,
observe <i>couþe, mouþe</i>, 112; <i>yow, now</i>, 160; <i>wolde,
fulde</i>, 354; <i>yw, nou</i>, 453; <i>bounden, wnden</i>, 545;
<i>sowel, couel</i>, 767; <i>low, ynow</i>, 903; <i>sowen, lowe</i>,
957; <i>strout, but</i>, 1039; <i>þou, nou</i>, 1283; <i>doun, tun</i>,
1630; <i>crus, hous</i>, 1966; <i>wounde, grunde</i>, 1978; <i>bowr,
tour</i>, 2072; <i>spuse, huse</i>, 2912. <i>Lowe</i>, 1291, 2431, 2471,
should rather be <i>lawe</i>, as in l. 2767. These hints will
probably suffice for the guidance of those who wish to follow up the
subject. It is evident that full dependence cannot be placed upon the
<i>exactness</i> of the rimes.</p>
<h5 class="section">
<a id="pref_29">§ 29.</a> <span class="smallcaps">On the final -e</span>, &amp;c.</h5>
<p>There can be little doubt that the final <i>-e</i> is, in general,
fully pronounced in this poem wherever it is written, with but a very
few exceptions; but at the same time it is liable to be elided when
followed by a vowel or (sometimes) by the letter <i>h</i>, as is usual
in old English poetry. In the following remarks, I shall use an
apostrophe to signify that <i>e</i> is <i>written, but not
pronounced</i>; thus “wil’” signifies that “wile” is the MS. form, but
“wil” the apparent pronunciation. I shall use an italic <i>e</i> to
signify that the <i>e</i> is elided because followed by a vowel or
<i>h</i>, as “cupp<i>e</i>” (l. 14); and in the same way,
“rid<i>en</i>,” “lit<i>el</i>,” &amp;c, signify that the syllables
<i>-en</i>, <i>-el</i> are slurred over in a like manner. It will be
seen that such syllables are, in general, slurred over when they occur
before a vowel or <i>h</i>; under the same circumstances, that is, as
the final <i>-e</i>. When I simply write the word in the form “gode” as
in the MS., I mean that the <i>-e</i> is <i>fully pronounced</i>; so
that “gode” stands for “godë.”</p>
<span class="pagenum">xlix</span>
<a id="intro49"> </a>
<p>The following, then, are instances. I follow the order in Mr Morris’s
Introduction to Chaucer’s Prologue, &amp;c. (Clarendon Press
Series).</p>
<p class="inset1">
(<i>A</i>) In nouns and adjectives (of A.S. origin) the final <i>-e</i>
represents one of the final vowels <i>a</i>, <i>u</i>, <i>e</i>, and
hence is fully sounded even in the nominative case in such instances.
Examples; gome (A.S. <i>goma</i>), 7, blome (A.S. <i>bloma</i>), 63,
trewe (A.S. <i>treowe</i>), 179, knaue (A.S. <i>cnafa</i>), 308, 450,
sone (A.S. <i>sunu</i>), 394.</p>
<p class="inset1">
(<i>B</i>) In words of French origin it is sounded as in French verse.
Such words are scarce in Havelok. Examples: hayse, 59, beste, 279,
mirácle, 500, rose, 2919, curtesye (<i>miswritten</i> curteyse), 2876,
cf. 194, drurye, 195, male, 48, large, 97, noble, 1263.</p>
<p class="inset1">
(<i>C</i>) It is a remnant of various grammatical inflexions:—</p>
<p class="inset2">
<ins class="mycorr" title="paragraph break added by transcriber">(1)</ins>
it is a sign of the <i>dative</i> case in nouns;
as, nede, 9, stede, 10, trome, 8, wronge, 72, stede, 142, dede (not
elided, because of the cæsura), 167, arke, 222, erþe, 248, lite þrawe,
276. It also sometimes marks the accusative, or the genitive of feminine
nouns: <i>accusatives</i>, cupp<i>e</i>, 14, wede, 94, brede, 98,
shrede, 99, mede, 102, quiste, 219, sorwe, 238 (cf. sorw’ in
l. 240), son<i>e</i>, 308, knaue, 308, sone, 350, wille, 441:
<i>genitives</i>, messe, 186, 188, helle, 405.</p>
<p class="inset2">
(2) In adjectives it marks—</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>a</i>) the <i>definite form</i> of the adjective; as, þe meste, 233,
þe riche (not elided<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note45" id="tag45">45</a>), 239, te beste, 87, þe hexte [man], 1080, þat wicke,
1158, þat foule, 1158, þe firste, 1333, þe rede, 1397. This rule is most
often violated in the case of <i>dissyllabic</i> superlatives; as, þe
wictest’, 8, þe fairest, þe strangest, 1081, 1110; cf. 199, 200.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>b</i>) the <i>plural</i> number. Examples abound, as, gode, 1, alle,
2, are, 27, yung = yung<i>e</i>, 30, holde, 30, gode, 34, 55, harde,
143, gren<i>e</i>, 470, bleike, 470, halte, 543, doumbe, 543,
&amp;c.</p>
<p class="inset3 continue">
The same use is often extended to possessive pronouns; we find the
plurals mine, 385, 514 (but min’, 392), þine, 620, hise, 34, 67, hure,
1231; and even the singulars hire, 84, 85, hure, 338, yure, 171. But the
personal pronoun feminine is often hir’, 172, 209; yet see
l. 316.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>c</i>) the <i>vocative</i> case, as, dere, 839, 2170; leue, 909.</p>
<span class="pagenum">l</span>
<a id="intro50"> </a>
<p class="inset2">
(3) In verbs it marks—</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>a</i>) the infinitive mood; as, telle, 3, duelle, 4, falle, 39,
bey<i>e</i>, 53, swere, 254, be-bedde, 421, ber<i>e</i>, 549, &amp;c. On
this point there cannot be a moment’s doubt, for the form <i>-en</i> is
found quite as often, and they rime together, as in 254, 255, cf. 29,
30. But it is well worth remarking that <i>-en</i> is slurred over
exactly where <i>-e</i> would be, with much regularity. Examples are:
rid<i>en</i>, 10, biginn<i>en</i>, 21, mak<i>en</i>, 29, heng<i>en</i>,
43, lurk<i>en</i>, 68, crep<i>en</i>, 68, rid<i>en</i>, 88,
hau<i>en</i>, 270. Other examples are very numerous. But we sometimes
find <i>-en</i> not slurred over, as, drinken, 15; and the same is true
even of <i>-e</i>, but such cases are exceptional and rare.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>b</i>) the gerund; as, to preyse, 60.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>c</i>) the past participle of a strong verb; as, drawe, 1802, slawe,
1803. But these are rare, as they are commonly written drawen, slawen,
2224.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>d</i>) the past tense of weak verbs, where the <i>-e</i> follows
<i>-ed</i>, <i>-t</i>, or <i>-d</i>. Examples are very numerous; as,
louede = lov’de, 30, 35 (not elided), 37, hauede = hav’de, 343; cf.
haued = havd’, 336; þurte, 10, durst<i>e</i>, 65, reft<i>e</i>, 94;
dede, 29, sende, 136, seyde, 228, herde, 286. Observe hated =
hated<i>e</i>, <ins class="mycorr" title="period missing">40.</ins>
The plurals of these tenses are rarely in <i>-e</i>, generally in
<i>-en</i>, as, haueden, 241, ded<i>en</i>, 242, sprauleden =
spraul’den, 475.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>e</i>) the subjunctive or optative mood, or the 3rd person of the
imperative mood, which is really the 3rd person of the subjunctive. This
rule seems to be carefully observed. Examples are yeue, 22, thaue, 296,
yerne, 299, leue, 406, were, 513, wit<i>e</i>, 517, &amp;c. So for the
<i>first</i> person, as, lat<i>e</i>, 509, lepe (not elided), 2009,
spek<i>e</i>, 2079; and for the <i>second</i> person, as, understonde,
1159, fare, 2705, cone, 622, 623.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>f</i>) other parts of a <i>few</i> verbs; thus, the 1st person
singular present, as, liue, 301, ete, 793, rede, 1660, wille, 388, where
<i>wille</i> is equivalent to <i>wish</i>.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>g</i>) present participles: thus, plattínde, 2282, is a half-rime to
gangánde. In other places, the author is careful to place them before a
vowel, as gretind<i>e</i>, 1390, lauhwind<i>e</i>, 946, starind<i>e</i>,
508, driuend<i>e</i>, 2702, fastind<i>e</i>, 865.</p>
<p class="inset2">
(4) In adverbs the final <i>-e</i> denotes—</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>a</i>) an older vowel-ending; as, son<i>e</i> (A.S. <i>sóna</i>),
136, sone, 218,
<span class="pagenum">li</span>
<a id="intro51"> </a>
251, yete (A.S. <i>géta</i>, as well as <i>gét</i>), 495, ofte (Swed.
<i>ofta</i>, Dan. <i>ofte</i>), 227.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>b</i>) an adverb as distinguished from its corresponding adjective,
as, yerne, 153, loude, 96, longe, 241, more, 301, softe, 305, heye, 335,
swiþe, 455, harde, 639. Hence, in l. 640, we should read
<i>neye</i>.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>c</i>) an older termination in <i>-en</i> or <i>-an</i>; as,
þer-hinne, 322, 709, 712, henne, 843, inne, 855. Cf. A.S. <i>heonan</i>,
<i>innan</i>.</p>
<p class="inset3">
(<i>d</i>) It is also sounded in the termination <i>-like</i>, as,
sikerlike, 422. Hence, in baldelike, 53, <i>both</i> the <i>ees</i> are
sounded; cf. feblelike, 418. When the final <i>-e</i> is slurred over
before an <i>h</i> in <i>Chaucer</i>, <i>h</i> is found commonly to
begin the pronoun <i>he</i>, or its cases, the possessive pronouns
<i>his</i>, <i>hire</i>, or their cases, a part of the verb to
<i>have</i>, or else the adverbs <i>how</i> or <i>heer</i>. The same
rule seems to hold in <i>Havelok</i>. Observe, that <i>e</i> often forms
a syllable in the <i>middle</i> of a word, as, bondeman, 32, engelondes,
63, pourelik<i>e</i>, 322.</p>
<p>With regard to the final <i>-en</i>, it is most commonly slurred over
before a vowel or the <i>h</i> in <i>he</i> or <i>haue</i>, not only
when it is the termination of the infinitive mood, but in <i>many other
cases</i>. One striking example may suffice:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>He gret<i>en</i> and gouled<i>en</i> and gou<i>en</i> hem ille,
164.</p>
</div>
<p>A still more striking peculiarity is that <i>the same rule often
holds</i> for the ending <i>-es</i>. We find it, of course, forming a
distinct syllable in plurals; as, limes, 86; and in adverbs, as, liues,
509. But observe such instances as maydn<i>es</i>, 2, prest<i>es</i>,
33, vtlaw<i>es</i>, 41, siþ<i>es</i>, 213, &amp;c.</p>
<p>In the same way, when rapid final syllables such as <i>-el</i>,
<i>-er</i>, <i>-ere</i>, &amp;c., are slurred over, it will
<i>generally</i> be found <i>that a vowel or</i> h <i>follows them</i>.
Examples: lit<i>el</i>, 6, won<i>eth</i>, 105, bed<i>els</i>, 266,
bod<i>i</i>, 345, deu<i>el</i>, 446, hung<i>er</i>, 449. Compare
ou<i>er</i>al, 38, 54. There are many other peculiarities which it would
take long to enumerate, such as, that <i>sworn</i> is pronounced
<i>sworen</i>, 204; that the final <i>-e</i> is sometimes preserved
before a vowel, as in <i>dedë am</i>, 167; that the word <i>ne</i> is
very frequently not counted, as it were, in the scansion, as in 57, 113,
220, 419, the second <i>ne</i> in l. 547, and in several other
places. But it must suffice to state merely, that when the above rules
(with allowance of a few exceptions)
<span class="pagenum">lii</span>
<a id="intro52"> </a>
are carefully observed, it will be found that the metre of Havelok is
<i>very regular</i>, and <i>valuable on account of its
regularity</i>.</p>
<p>It would therefore be easy to correct the text in many places by help
of an exact analysis of the rhythm. But this, except in a very few
places, has not been attempted, because the imperfect, but unique, MS.
copy is more instructive as it stands. In l. 19, e.g. <i>wit</i>
should be <i>wite</i>; in l. 47, <i>red</i> should be <i>rede</i>;
in l. 74, <i>his soule</i> should be <i>of his soule</i>, &amp;c.
The importance of attending to the final <i>-e</i> may be exemplified by
the lines—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Allë greten swiþë sore, 236;</p>
<p>But sonë dedë hirë fetë, 317;</p>
<p>þinë cherlës, þinë hinë, 620<ins class="mycorr" title="printed . for ;">; </ins></p>
<p>Grimës sonës allë þre, 1399;</p>
<p>Hisë sistres herë lif, 2395.</p>
</div>
<p>Mr Ellis writes— “These final examples suggested to me to
compose the following German epitaph, which contains just as many final
<i>e</i>’s, and which I think no German would find to have anything
peculiar in the versification:</p>
<h5 class="left">GRABSCHRIFT.</h5>
<div class="verse">
<p>Diese alte reiche Frau</p>
<p>Hasste jede eitle Schau,</p>
<p>Preiste Gottes gute Gabe,</p>
<p>Mehrte stets die eig’ne Habe,</p>
<p>Liegt hier unbeweint im Grabe.</p>
</div>
<p>I think Havelok may be well compared with Goethe’s ballad,</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Es war ein Kön<i>ig in Thu</i>le,</p>
<p class="indent">Gar treu bis an das Grab,</p>
<p>Dem, sterbend, seine Buhle</p>
<p class="indent"><i>Einen gold</i>enen Becher gab.</p>
<p class="stanza">
Es ging ihm nichts darüber,</p>
<p class="indent">Er leert’ ihn jeden Schmaus,</p>
<p>Die Augen gin<i>gen ihm ü</i>ber</p>
<p class="indent">So oft er trank daraus.</p>
<p class="stanza">
Und als er kam zu sterben,</p>
<p class="indent">Zählt’ er <i>seine Städt’</i> im Reich,</p>
<p>Gönnt’ alles seinem Erben,</p>
<p class="indent">Den Becher nicht zugleich:—</p>
</div>
<span class="pagenum">liii</span>
<a id="intro53"> </a>
<p>and the end:—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Die Augen thä<i>ten ihm sin</i>ken,</p>
<p class="indent">Trank nie <i>einen Trop</i>fen mehr.</p>
</div>
<p>The <i>italicised</i> trisyllabic measures are fine. Observe also the
elisions of final <i>-e</i> before a following vowel (<i>Städt’</i>
being very unusual), and the omission of the dative <i>-e</i> in <i>im
Reich</i>, to rhyme with <i>zugleich</i>.”</p>
<p>I have only to add that my special thanks are due to Sir F. Madden
for his permission to make use of his valuable notes, glossary, and
preface, and for his assistance; as also to Mr Ellis for his notes,
which, however, reached me only at the last moment, when much alteration
of the proofs was troublesome. There are many things probably which Mr
Ellis does not much approve of in this short popular sketch of the
metre, in which attention is drawn only to some of the <i>principal</i>
points. In particular, he disapproves of the term <i>slurring over</i>,
though I believe that I mean precisely the same thing aa he does, viz.
that these light syllables are really <i>fully pronounced</i>, and not
in any way forcibly suppressed; but that, owing to their being light
syllables, and occurring before vowel sounds, the full pronunciation of
them does not cause the verse to halt, but merely imparts to it an
agreeable vivacity. As I have already said elsewhere<a class="tag pginternal" href="#note46" id="tag46">46</a>— “A poet’s
business is, in fact, to take care that the syllables which <i>are</i>
to be rapidly pronounced are such as easily <i>can</i> be so; and that
the syllables which are to be heavily accented are naturally those that
<i>ought</i> to be. If he gives attention to this, it does not much
matter whether each foot has <i>two</i> or <i>three</i> <ins class="mycorr" title="text reads ‘sylalbles’">syllables</ins> in it.”</p>
<hr class="small">

<p><a href="#tag1" id="note1" class="pginternal">1.</a>
In particular, we find there a complete proof, supported by some fifty
examples, that, <i>as</i> can be traced, through the forms <i>ase</i>,
<i>als</i>, <i>alse</i>, <i>also</i>, to the A.S. <i>eall-swa</i>; a
proof, that in the difficult phrase <i>lond and lithe</i>, the word
<i>lithe</i> [also spelt <i>lede</i>, <i>lude</i>] is equivalent to the
French <i>tenement</i>, <i>rente</i>, or <i>fe</i>; and, thirdly, a
complete refutation of Mr Singer’s extraordinary notion that the adverb
<i>swithe</i> means <i>a sword</i>!</p>

<p><a href="#tag6" id="note6" class="pginternal">6.</a>
“The Chronicler writes of him, f. 6. ‘Il feu le plus beau bacheleir
q<i>e</i> vnqes reigna en Engleterre, <i>ceo dit le Bruit</i>, par quoy
ly lays ly apellerunt <i>King Adelstane with gilden kroket</i>, pour ce
q’il feu si beaus.’ We have here notice of another of those curious
historical poems, the loss of which can never be sufficiently deplored.
The term <i>crocket</i> (derived by Skinner from the Fr. <i>crochet</i>,
uncinulus) points out the period of the poem’s composition, since the
fashion alluded to of wearing those large rolls of hair so called, only
arose at the latter end of Hen. III. reign, and continued through the
reign of Edw. I. and part of his successor’s.”</p>

<p><a href="#tag18" id="note18" class="pginternal">18.</a>

<p><a href="#tag21" id="note21" class="pginternal">21.</a>

<p><a href="#tag22" id="note22" class="pginternal">22.</a>

<p><a href="#tag24" id="note24" class="pginternal">24.</a>

<p><a href="#tag28" id="note28" class="pginternal">28.</a>

<p><a href="#tag30" id="note30" class="pginternal">30.</a>

spoken of in the Lay may have had names somewhat similar to these, which
may have been replaced by the more familiar names here mentioned.</p>
<p><a href="#tag31" id="note31" class="pginternal">31.</a>
Cf. Lappenberg’s History of England, tr. by Thorpe, vol. i.
pp. 145-154.</p>
<p><a href="#tag32" id="note32" class="pginternal">32.</a>

<p><a href="#tag33" id="note33" class="pginternal">33.</a>
“Cf. K. Horn, 1005, where <i>haue</i> rhymes with plawe.” —M. Mr
A. J. Ellis would consider <i>slawen</i>, <i>knaue</i>, &amp;c., as
assonances— “Do not think of the pronunciation of modern
<i>drawen</i>. Read <i>sla-wen</i>, <i>kna-ue</i>, an assonance.
<i>Beþe</i> does <i>not</i> rhyme to <i>reden</i>; it is only an
assonance.” —Ellis. On the other hand, we find the spellings
<i>rathe</i>, <i>rothe</i> instead of <i>rede</i> in ll. 1335 and
2817.</p>
<p><a href="#tag34" id="note34" class="pginternal">34.</a>
“<i>Qual</i> = <i>quhal</i>, the aspirate being omitted; and
<i>quhal</i> = <i>whal</i>.” —Ellis.</p>
<p><a href="#tag35" id="note35" class="pginternal">35.</a>
The use of <i>th</i> for <i>t</i> is not uncommon. In the <i>Romans of
Partenay</i>, we have <i>thown</i>, <i>thaken</i>, <i>thouchyng</i>,
&amp;c., for <i>town</i>, <i>taken</i>, <i>touching</i>; see Preface,
p. xvi. In the copy of Piers Plowman in MS. Camb. Univ. Lib. Dd
l. 17, I have observed several similar examples. Cf. Eng.
<i>tea</i>, Ital. <i>tè</i>, Span. <i>té</i>, with Fr. <i>thé</i>, Swed.
<i>the</i>, G. Du. Dan. <i>thee</i>.</p>
<p><a href="#tag36" id="note36" class="pginternal">36.</a>
“Is <i>e</i> for <i>o</i> a mistake, or may it be compared with
<i>preue</i> for <i>prove</i>, &amp;c.?” —Ellis. I would observe
that <i>greting</i> is the spelling of the <i>substantive</i> in
l. 166.</p>
<p><a href="#tag37" id="note37" class="pginternal">37.</a>
“This <i>four accents</i> I consider to be a wrong way of stating the
fact. . . The metre consists of four measures, each generally,
not always, of <i>two</i> syllables, the first often <i>one</i>
syllable, the others often of <i>three</i> syllables, and each measure
has generally more stress on the last than on any other, but the accents
or principal stresses in the verse are usually 2, sometimes 3, perhaps
never 4.” —A. J. Ellis. I need hardly add that such a
statement is more exact, and that I here merely use the word
<i>accent</i> in the loose sense it often bears, viz. as denoting the
“stress,” more or less heavy, and sometimes imperceptible, which is
popularly supposed to belong to the last syllable in a measure. I must
request the reader to remember that this present sketch of the metre is
very slight and imperfect, and worded in the usual not very correct
popular language. For more strict and careful statements the reader is
referred to Mr A. J. Ellis’s work on Early English Pronunciation.
Until readers have made themselves acquainted with that work, they will
readily understand what I <i>here</i> mean by “accents;” afterwards,
they can easily adopt a stricter idea of its meaning.</p>
<p><a href="#tag38" id="note38" class="pginternal">38.</a>
“You cannot scan this line in any way. This method of doing it is quite
impossible; it is a mere chopping to make a verse like this. The line is
corrupt. Omit <i>þat</i>, and you have</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Of | a tal’ | ich you | wile telle</p>
</div>
<p>or better,</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Of | a tal’ | ich wil|e telle.”</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
Ellis.</p>
<p><a href="#tag39" id="note39" class="pginternal">39.</a>
The number is that of the <i>first</i> line of the pair.</p>
<p><a href="#tag40" id="note40" class="pginternal">40.</a>
“You have omitted the curious <i>harde, krakede</i>, 567, here; it is
only an assonance, not a mistake, I believe.” —Ellis. But see
<a href="#textnote567" class="pginternal">note to l. 567</a>.</p>
<p><a href="#tag41" id="note41" class="pginternal">41.</a>
“On <i>i, e</i> rhymes, see p. 271, last line and following, of my Chap.
IV. The <i>o, a</i> depend on a provincialism, and this applies to
<i>sawe</i>, <i>wowe</i><ins class="mycorr" title="all ; changed from , for consistency">;
</ins><i>beþe</i>, <i>rede</i>; <i>knaue</i>,
<i>plawe</i>; <i>sawe</i>, <i>hawe</i>; &amp;c. <i>Bouth, oft</i> is a
case of assonance, <i>bouth</i> being <i>bought</i>, where properly the
<i>ugh</i> is the voiced sound of Scotch <i>quh</i>, and easily passes
into <i>f</i>. The assonance is therefore nearly a rhyme. <i>Plattinde,
gangande</i> is probably a scribal error. <i>Eir, toþer</i> is certainly
a mistake; read</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Swanborow, helfled, his sistres fair.”</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
Ellis.</p>
<p>We may then perhaps alter <i>gangande</i> to <i>ganginde</i>. I do
not quite like writing the modern form <i>fair</i> instead of the old
plural <i>fayre</i> in order to gain a rime to <i>eir</i>. Cf. ll. 1095,
2300, 2538, 2768.</p>
<p><a href="#tag42" id="note42" class="pginternal">42.</a>
“<i>Hon, lond</i> may arise from a Danism, or from an English custom at
that time of not pronouncing <i>d</i> after <i>n</i> in <i>nd</i> final;
Danish <i>Mand</i> and German <i>Mann</i> are identical.” —Ellis.
I prefer to call it Danish; we English, now at least, often <i>add</i> a
<i>d</i>, as in <i>sound, gownd</i>, from <i>soun, gown</i>.</p>
<p><a href="#tag43" id="note43" class="pginternal">43.</a>
“<i>Johan</i> is almost <i>Jon</i> in Chaucer, however written, but
l. 177 wants a measure; read—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Bi [Jhesu] crist, and bi seint ion.</p>
</div>
<p>In l. 1720 also the verse is defective; omit <i>al</i>, and
read—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>In denemark nis wimman [non]</p>
<p>So fayr so sche, bi <i>seint</i> Johan,</p>
</div>
<p>where <i>seint</i> is a dissyllable; see p. 264 of my Early
English Pronunciation. <i>Hey, fri</i>, 1071, is an error; read
<i>hy</i>, and see p. 285 of my book. The other instances of
<i>ei</i>, <i>ai</i> are all regular, the confusion of <i>ei</i>,
<i>ai</i> being perfect in the thirteenth century. <i>Shame</i>,
l. 88, is dative, and would prove nothing, but <i>shame</i> in
Orrmin is conclusive. Hence in <i>sham’</i>, 56, we have an <i>e</i>
omitted; compare p. 323 of my book, and the German <i>Ruh’</i>.”
—Ellis. In other places, the spelling <i>heye</i> occurs, rather
than <i>hy</i>: see ll. 719, 987, 1071, 1083, 1289<ins class="mycorr" title="comma invisible">, </ins>1685, 2431, 2471, 2544, 2724, 2750,
2945, &amp;c.</p>
<p><a href="#tag44" id="note44" class="pginternal">44.</a>
“The instances of <i>o</i> are all regular, except <i>croud, god</i>,
2338, which is a false rhyme altogether; <i>ou</i> = modern <i>oo</i>.”
—Ellis.</p>
<p><a href="#tag45" id="note45" class="pginternal">45.</a>
<i>Riche</i> being both <span class="smallroman">A.S.</span> and
French, has the <i>e</i> even when indefinite; a riche king, 841; a
riche man, 373.</p>
<p><a href="#tag46" id="note46" class="pginternal">46.</a>
Preface to Mr Morris’s Genesis and Exodus, p. xxxviii.</p>
</div>
<span class="pagenum">liv</span>
<a id="intro54"> </a>
<h3> <a id="emendations">EMENDATIONS,
ETC.</a></h3>
<hr class="tiny">
<p class="mynote">
This section is shown as printed. The editor’s corrections were
variously handled. Minor changes to the primary text are shown in
brackets and marked with <ins class="edcorr" title="Corrected by author from ‘red’">mouse-hover popups</ins>. The more complicated or
tentative emendations are given as supple­mentary footnotes,
separately <span class="addition">outlined</span>. Additions to the
Glossary are marked in the same way. The following paragraph is part of
the original text.</p>
<p><span class="firstword">Some</span> emendations have been made in
the text by inserting letters and words within square brackets. A few
more may be noticed here.</p>
<p>p. 2, l. 47. The MS. has <i>red</i>; but it should be
<i>rede</i>.</p>
<p>p. 3, l. 66. For the MS. reading <i>here</i> Mr Garnett proposed to
read <i>othere</i>, which is clearly right.</p>
<p>p. 3, l. 74. For <i>his soule</i> (as in the MS.) we should probably
read <i>of his soule</i>.</p>
<p>p. 3, l. 79. For <i>wo diden</i> (as in the MS.) we should read <i>wo
so dide</i>.</p>
<p>p. 6, l. 177. <i>Read</i>— “Bi [ihesu] crist,” &amp;c, to fill
up; but this is doubtful; see l. 1112.</p>
<p>p. 18, l. 560. For <i>with</i>, Mr Garnett proposed to read
<i>wilt</i>.</p>
<p>p. 20, l. <ins class="mycorr" title="text reads ‘60’">640</ins>.
For <i>ney</i> (as in MS.) read <i>neye</i>, the adverbial form.</p>
<p>p. 21, l. 660. Perhaps there should be a comma after <i>Slep</i>,
making the sense to be <i>sleep, son</i>, not <i>sleep soon</i>.</p>
<p>p. 23, l. 746. For <i>alle</i>, Mr Garnett proposed to read
<i>shalle</i>.</p>
<p>p. 24, l. 784. Perhaps we should, however, read <i>se-weren</i>, and
the note on the line (p. 93) may be wrong. See <i>Weren</i> in the
Glossary.
<span class="mynote">
See <a href="#endnote" class="pginternal">Endnote</a>.</span></p>
<p>p. 32, l. 1037. For <i>stareden</i> we should perhaps read
<i>stradden</i>; see the Glossary.</p>
<p>p. 33, l. 1080. For <i>hexte</i> we should rather read <i>hexte</i>
[<i>man</i>]; cf. l. 199.</p>
<span class="pagenum">lv</span>
<a id="intro55"> </a>
<p>p. 38, l. 1233. Mr Garnett suggested that <i>cloþen</i> may mean
<i>clothes</i>. If so, dele the comma after it.</p>
<p>p. 43, l. 1420. For <i>wolde</i> we should rather read [<i>he</i>]
<i>wolde</i>.</p>
<p>p. 46, l. 1687. <i>þarned</i> is an error of the scribe for
<i>þoled</i>; see the Glossary.</p>
<p>p. 47, l. 1720. Perhaps we should rather read—<i>is womman</i>
[<i>non</i>].</p>
<p>p. 47, l. 1733. <i>Bidde</i> must mean <i>offer</i>, rather than
<i>bid</i> (as in the Glossary); unless it be miswritten for <i>bide</i>
= tarry.</p>
<p>p. 47, l. 1736. The MS. reading <i>deled</i> should be <i>deyled</i>;
cf. l. 2099.</p>
<p>p. 76, l. 2670. The MS. reading <i>blinne</i> should clearly be
<i>blunne</i>. A few other suggestions of emendations will be found in
the Glossarial Index. See the words <i>Arwe</i>, <i>Birþe</i>,
<i>Felde</i>, <i>Sor</i>, <i>Tauhte</i>, <i>Þenne</i>, <i>Thit</i>,
<i>Werewed</i>, <i>Wreken</i>, &amp;c. See also the suggestions in the
preface, pp. xxxix, xli, xlvi, xlvii.</p>
<p>p. 132, s.v. <i>Loken</i>. The reference to the Ancren Riwle is to
MS. Titus D 18, fol. 17; cf. the edition by Morton (Camd. Soc.
1853), p. 56.</p>
<p>In the Glossary, <i>Dunten</i> is wrongly placed after
<i>Dint</i>.</p>
<p class="mynote">
See <a href="#endnote" class="pginternal">Endnote</a> for following items.</p>
<p>Also, <i>Greting</i> is wrongly placed before <i>Gres</i>.</p>
<p><i>Hal</i>, more probably, is shortened from <i>half</i>, like
<i>twel</i> from <i>twelue</i>.</p>
<p><ins class="mycorr" title="spelled ‘Schoten’"><i>Shoten</i></ins>,
in l. 1838, means <i>rushed</i>, <i>darted</i>, <i>flew</i>.</p>
<p><i>Teyte</i> may mean <i>lively</i>. My explanation is not generally
accepted.</p>
<p><i>Bise</i> occurs in l. 724.</p>
<a id="intro56"> </a>
</div>
<p> </p>
<p class="illustration">
<span class="pagenum">1</span>
<a id="page1"> </a><br>
<a id="havelok"> </a><br>
<img alt="Incipit vita Hauelok, quondam Rex Anglie et Denemarchie." height="74" src="images/incipit.png" title="Incipit vita Hauelok, quondam Rex Anglie et Denemarchie." width="364" id="img_images_incipit.png"></p>
<hr class="tiny">
<div class="havelok">
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 204, col. 1.]<br>
Hearken!</p>
<p class="section">
Herknet to me, gode men,</p>
<p>Wiues, maydnes, and alle men,</p>
<p>Of a tale þat ich you wile telle,</p>
<span class="linenum">4</span>
<p>Wo so it wile here, and þer-to duelle.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
I will tell you the tale of Havelok,</p>
<p>Þe tale is of hauelok i-maked;</p>
<p>Wil he was litel he yede ful naked:</p>
<p>Hauelok was a ful god gome,</p>
<span class="linenum">8</span>
<p>He was ful god in eueri trome,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote9" id="texttag9">9</a>
<p class="sidenote">
a wight man at need.</p>
<p>He was þe wicteste ma<i>n</i> at nede,</p>
<p>Þat þurte ride<i>n</i> on ani stede.</p>
<p>Þat ye mowen nou y-here,</p>
<span class="linenum">12</span>
<p>And þe tale ye mowen y-lere.</p>
<p>At the begi<i>n</i>ning<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note1" id="poem_tag1">1</a> of vre tale,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
First, fill me a cup of ale.</p>
<p>Fil me a cuppe of ful god ale;</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote15" id="texttag15">15</a>
<p>And [y] wile dri<i>n</i>ken her y spelle,</p>
<span class="linenum">16</span>
<p>Þat crist vs shilde alle fro helle!</p>
<p>Krist late vs heu<i>er</i>e so for to do,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Christ grant we may do right!</p>
<p>Þat we moten comen him to,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote19" id="texttag19">19</a>
<p>And wit[e]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note2" id="poem_tag2">2</a> þat it mote ben so!</p>
<span class="linenum">20</span>
<p><i>Benedicamus domino!</i></p>
<p>Here y schal bigi<i>n</i>nen a rym,</p>
<p>Krist us yeue wel god fyn!</p>
<span class="pagenum">2</span>
<a id="page2"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
The rime is about Havelok.</p>
<p>The rym is maked of hauelok,</p>
<span class="linenum">24</span>
<p>A stalworþi man i<i>n</i> a flok;</p>
<p>He was þe stalworþeste ma<i>n</i> at nede,</p>
<p>Þat may riden on ani stede.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag1" id="poem_note1" class="pginternal">1</a>
MS. Begi<i>n</i>nig.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag2" id="poem_note2" class="pginternal">2</a>
<i>See</i> ll. 517, 1316.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
THE GOOD KING ATHELWOLD   IS KING OVER ALL ENGLAND.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
There was once a king who made good laws.</p>
<p class="section">
IT was a king bi are dawes,</p>
<span class="linenum">28</span>
<p>That i<i>n</i> his time were gode lawes</p>
<p>He dede maken, an ful wel holden;</p>
<p>Hym louede yu<i>n</i>g, him louede holde,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote31" id="texttag31">31</a>
<p>Erl and barun, dreng and kayn,</p>
<span class="linenum">32</span>
<p>Knict, bondema<i>n</i>, and swain,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
All loved him.</p>
<p>Wydues, maydnes, p<i>re</i>stes and clerkes,</p>
<p>And al for hise gode werkes.</p>
<p>He louede god with al his micth,</p>
<span class="linenum">36</span>
<p>And holi kirke, and soth, ant ricth;</p>
<p>Ricth-wise<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note3" id="poem_tag3">3</a> man he louede alle,</p>
<p>And ou<i>er</i>al made hem forto calle;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He hated traitors and robbers.</p>
<p>Wreieres and wrobberes made he falle,</p>
<span class="linenum">40</span>
<p>And hated he<i>m</i> so ma<i>n</i> doth galle;</p>
<p>Vtlawes and theues made he bynde,</p>
<p>Alle that he micthe fynde,</p>
<p>And heye he<i>n</i>gen on galwe-tre;</p>
<span class="linenum">44</span>
<p>For hem ne yede gold ne fe.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote45" id="texttag45">45</a>
<p class="sidenote">
At that time, men could carry gold about safely,</p>
<p>In that time a man þat bore</p>
<p>[Wel fyfty pund, y woth, or more,]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note4" id="poem_tag4">4</a></p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 204, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Of <ins class="edcorr" title="corrected by editor from ‘red’">red[e]</ins> gold up-on hijs bac,</p>
<span class="linenum">48</span>
<p>In a male with or blac,</p>
<p>Ne funde he non that him misseyde,</p>
<p>N[e] with iuele on [him] hond leyde.</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne micthe chapme<i>n</i> fare</p>
<span class="linenum">52</span>
<p>Þuruth englond wit here ware,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and boldly buy and sell.</p>
<p>And baldelike beye and selle<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>Oueral þer he wilen dwellen,</p>
<span class="pagenum">3</span>
<a id="page3"> </a>
<p>In gode burwes, and þer-fram</p>
<span class="linenum">56</span>
<p>Ne funde<i>n</i> he non þat dede he<i>m</i> sham,</p>
<p>Þat he ne were<i>n</i> sone to sorwe brouth,</p>
<p>An pou<i>er</i>e maked, and browt to nouth.</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne was engelond at hayse;<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note5" id="poem_tag5">5</a></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Then was England at ease.</p>
<span class="linenum">60</span>
<p>Michel was svich a king to preyse,</p>
<p>Þat held so eng[e]lond in grith!</p>
<p>Krist of heuene was him with.</p>
<p>He was engelondes blome;</p>
<span class="linenum">64</span>
<p>Was non so bold lond to rome,</p>
<p>Þat durste upon his [menie] bringhe</p>
<p>Hunger, ne here<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note5a" id="poem_tag5a">*</a> wicke þinghe.</p>
<p>Hwan he felede hise foos,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The king made his foes hide themselves.</p>
<span class="linenum">68</span>
<p>He made he<i>m</i> lurken, and crepe<i>n</i> in wros:</p>
<p>Þe hidde<i>n</i> he<i>m</i> alle, and helde<i>n</i> he<i>m</i>
stille,</p>
<p>And diden al his herte wille.</p>
<p>Ricth he louede of alle þinge,</p>
<span class="linenum">72</span>
