<p>To wronge micht hi<i>m</i> no man bri<i>n</i>ge,</p>
<p>Ne for siluer, ne for gold:—</p>
<p>So was he his soule<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note5b" id="poem_tag5b">†</a> hold.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He befriended the fatherless.</p>
<p>To þe faderles was he rath,</p>
<span class="linenum">76</span>
<p>Wo so dede hem wrong or lath,</p>
<p>Were it clerc, or were it knicth,</p>
<p>He dede hem sone to haue<i>n</i> ricth;</p>
<p>And wo [so] dide<i>n</i><a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note5c" id="poem_tag5c">‡</a> widuen wrong,</p>
<span class="linenum">80</span>
<p>Were he neure knicth so strong,</p>
<p>Þat he ne made hi<i>m</i> sone kesten,</p>
<p>And in fet<i>er</i>es ful faste festen;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Them who wrought shame he punished.</p>
<p>And wo so dide maydne shame</p>
<span class="linenum">84</span>
<p>Of hire bodi, or brouth i<i>n</i> blame,</p>
<p>Bute it were bi hire wille,</p>
<p>He<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note6" id="poem_tag6">6</a> made hi<i>m</i> sone of limes spille.</p>
<p>He was te<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note7" id="poem_tag7">7</a> beste knith at nede,</p>
<span class="linenum">88</span>
<p>Þat heuere micthe ride<i>n</i> on stede,</p>
<p>Or wepne wagge, or folc vt lede;</p>
<span class="pagenum">4</span>
<a id="page4"> </a>
<p>Of knith ne hauede he neu<i>er</i>e drede,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote91" id="texttag91">91</a>
<p>Þat he ne spro<i>n</i>g forth so sparke of glede,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 204<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<span class="linenum">92</span>
<p>And lete him [knawe] of hise hand-dede,</p>
<p>Hw he couþe with wepne spede;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He made his foes cry for mercy.</p>
<p>And oþer he refte hi<i>m</i> hors or wede,</p>
<p>Or made him sone ha<i>n</i>des sprede,</p>
<span class="linenum">96</span>
<p>And “louerd, me<i>r</i>ci!” loude grede.</p>
<p>He was large, and no wicth gnede;</p>
<p>Hauede he non so god brede,</p>
<p>Ne on his bord no<i>n</i> so god shrede,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He fed the poor.</p>
<span class="linenum">100</span>
<p>Þat he ne wolde þorwit fede,</p>
<p>Poure þat on fote yede;</p>
<p>Forto haue<i>n</i> of him þe mede</p>
<p>Þat for vs wolde on rode blede,</p>
<span class="linenum">104</span>
<p>Crist, that al kan wisse and rede,</p>
<p>Þat euere woneth i<i>n</i> ani þede.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag3" id="poem_note3" class="pginternal">3</a>
MS. “Rirth wise.”</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag4" id="poem_note4" class="pginternal">4</a>
Supplied from conjecture. Cf. v. 653, 787. A few more instances will be
found where a similar liberty has been taken, for the purpose of
completing the sense.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag5" id="poem_note5" class="pginternal">5</a>
MS. athayse.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag5a" id="poem_note5a" class="pginternal">*</a>
For the MS. reading <i>here</i> Mr Garnett proposed to read
<i>othere</i>, which is clearly right.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag5b" id="poem_note5b" class="pginternal">†</a>
For <i>his soule</i> (as in the MS.) we should probably read <i>of his
soule</i>.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag5c" id="poem_note5c" class="pginternal">‡</a>
For <i>wo diden</i> (as in the MS.) we should read <i>wo so
dide</i>.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag6" id="poem_note6" class="pginternal">6</a>
MS. Ke.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag7" id="poem_note7" class="pginternal">7</a>
MS. Ke waste.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
ATHELWOLD IS ABOUT TO DIE.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
His name was Athelwold.</p>
<p class="space">
¶ Þe ki<i>n</i>g was hoten aþelwold,</p>
<p>Of word, of wepne he was bold;</p>
<span class="linenum">108</span>
<p>In engeland was neure knicth,</p>
<p>Þat bet<i>er</i>e hel þe lond to ricth.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote110" id="texttag110">110</a>
<p class="sidenote">
He had but a young daughter to succeed him.</p>
<p>Of his bodi ne hauede he eyr</p>
<p>Bute a mayden swiþe fayr,</p>
<span class="linenum">112</span>
<p>Þat was so yung þat sho ne couþe</p>
<p>Gon on fote, ne speke wit mouþe.</p>
<p>Þan him tok an iuel strong,</p>
<p>Þat he we[l] wiste, and under-fong,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He feels he is dying, and says,</p>
<span class="linenum">116</span>
<p>Þat his deth was come<i>n</i> him on:</p>
<p>And seyde, “c<i>ri</i>st, wat shal y don!</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote118" id="texttag118">118</a>
<p>Louerd, wat shal me to rede!</p>
<p>I woth ful wel ich haue mi mede.</p>
<span class="linenum">120</span>
<p>W shal nou mi douhter fare?</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“I am in trouble about her.</p>
<p>Of hire haue ich michel kare;</p>
<p>Sho is mikel in mi þouth,</p>
<p>Of me self is me rith nowt.</p>
<span class="linenum">124</span>
<p>No selcouth is, þou me be wo;<span class="pagenum">5</span></p>
<a id="page5"> </a>
<p>Sho ne ka<i>n</i> speke, ne sho kan go.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Were she but of age,</p>
<p>Yif scho couþe on horse ride,</p>
<p>And a thousa<i>n</i>de me<i>n</i> bi hire syde;</p>
<span class="linenum">128</span>
<p>And sho were com<i>en</i> intil helde,</p>
<p>And engelond sho couþe welde;</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote130" id="texttag130">130</a>
<p>And don hem of þar hire were q<i>ue</i>me,</p>
<p>An hire bodi couþe yeme;</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote132" id="texttag132">132</a>
<p>No wolde me neu<i>er</i>e iuele like</p>
<p class="sidenote">
I would not care for myself.”</p>
<p>Me þou ich were i<i>n</i> heuene-riche!”</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HE SUMMONS HIS LORDS TO WINCHESTER.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Qua<i>n</i>ne he hauede þis plei<i>n</i>te maked,</p>
<p>Þer-after stro<i>n</i>glike [he] quaked.</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote136" id="texttag136">136</a>
<p>He sende writes sone on-on</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 204<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<p>After his erles eu<i>er</i>e-ich on;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He summons his lords, from Roxburgh to Dover.</p>
<p>And after hise baru<i>n</i>s, riche and poure,</p>
<p>Fro rokesburw al i<i>n</i>to douere,</p>
<span class="linenum">140</span>
<p>That he shulde<i>n</i> comen swiþe</p>
<p>Til him, that was ful vnbliþe;</p>
<p>To þat stede þe[r] he lay,</p>
<p>In harde bondes, nicth and day.</p>
<span class="linenum">144</span>
<p>He was so faste wit yuel fest,</p>
<p>Þat he ne mouthe haue<i>n</i> no rest;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He can no longer eat.</p>
<p>He ne mouthe no mete hete,</p>
<p>Ne he ne mouchte no lyþe gete;</p>
<span class="linenum">148</span>
<p>Ne non of his iuel þat couþe red;</p>
<p>Of him ne was nouth bute<i>n</i> ded.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
All sadly obey his summons.</p>
<p class="section">
Alle þat the writes herden,</p>
<p>Sorful an sori til him ferde<i>n</i>;</p>
<span class="linenum">152</span>
<p>He wru<i>n</i>gen ho<i>n</i>des, and wepe<i>n</i> sore,</p>
<p>And yerne preyde<i>n</i> cristes hore,</p>
<p>Þat he [wolde] t<i>ur</i>nen him</p>
<p>Vt of þat yuel þat was so grim!</p>
<span class="linenum">156</span>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne he weren comen alle</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They come to Winchester.</p>
<p>Bifor þe king i<i>n</i>to the halle,</p>
<p>At wi<i>n</i>chestre þer he lay:</p>
<span class="pagenum">6</span>
<a id="page6"> </a>
<p>“Welcome,” he seyde, “be ye ay!</p>
<span class="linenum">160</span>
<p>Ful michel þank[e] kan [y] yow</p>
<p>That ye aren come<i>n</i> to me now!”</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
ATHELWOLD SELECTS EARL GODRICH   TO BE HIS DAUGHTER’S
GUARDIAN.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
They all mourn and lament.</p>
<p class="section">
Quanne he were<i>n</i> alle set,</p>
<p>And þe king aueden i-gret,</p>
<span class="linenum">164</span>
<p>He grete<i>n</i>, and goulede<i>n</i>, and goue<i>n</i> he<i>m</i>
ille,</p>
<p>And he bad he<i>m</i> alle ben stille;</p>
<p>And seyde, “þat greti<i>n</i>g helpeth nouth,</p>
<p>For al to dede am ich brouth.</p>
<span class="linenum">168</span>
<p>Bute nov ye sen þat i shal deye,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He prays them to tell him who can guard his daughter best.</p>
<p>Nou ich wille you alle preye</p>
<p>Of mi douther þat shal be</p>
<p>Yure leuedi after me,</p>
<span class="linenum">172</span>
<p>Wo may yeme<i>n</i> hire so longe,</p>
<p>Boþen hire and engelonde,</p>
<p>Til þat she [mowe] winan of helde,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote175" id="texttag175">175</a>
<p>And þa she mowe yeme<i>n</i> and welde?”</p>
<span class="linenum">176</span>
<p>He ansuered<i>en</i>, and seyd<i>en</i> an-on,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They answer, “Earl Godrich of Cornwall.”</p>
<p>Bi crist<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note7a" id="poem_tag7a">*</a> and bi seint ion,</p>
<p>That þerl Godrigh of cornwayle</p>
<p>Was trewe ma<i>n</i>, wit-ute<i>n</i> faile;</p>
<span class="linenum">180</span>
<p>Wis ma<i>n</i> of red, wis ma<i>n</i> of dede,</p>
<p>And me<i>n</i> hauede<i>n</i> of hi<i>m</i> mikel drede.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 205, col. 1.]</p>
<p>“He may hire alþer-best[e] yeme,</p>
<p>Til þat she mowe wel be<i>n</i> quene.”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag7a" id="poem_note7a" class="pginternal">*</a>
<i>Read</i>— “Bi [ihesu] crist,” &amp;c, to fill up; but this is
doubtful; see l. 1112.</p>
</div>
<span class="linenum">184</span>
<p class="section">
Þe king was payed of that Rede;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The king sends for chalice and paten,</p>
<p>A wol fair cloth bri<i>n</i>ge<i>n</i> he dede,</p>
<p>And þer-on leyde þe messebok,</p>
<p>Þe caliz, and þe pateyn ok,</p>
<span class="linenum">188</span>
<p>Þe corporaus, þe messe-gere;</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote189" id="texttag189">189</a>
<p>Þer-on he garte þe erl suere,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
for the earl to swear upon.</p>
<p>Þat he sholde yemen hire wel,</p>
<p>With-ute<i>n</i> lac, wit-ute<i>n</i> tel,</p>
<span class="linenum">192</span>
<p>Til þat she were tuelf<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note8" id="poem_tag8">8</a> wi<i>n</i>ter hold,<span class="pagenum">7</span></p>
<a id="page7"> </a>
<p>And of speche were bold;</p>
<p>And þat she covþe of curteysye,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
His daughter is to marry the best and fairest man that can be found.</p>
<p>Gon, and speken of luue-drurye;</p>
<span class="linenum">196</span>
<p>And til þat she louen þoucte,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note9" id="poem_tag9">9</a></p>
<p>Wom so hire to gode thoucte;</p>
<p>And þat he shulde hire yeue</p>
<p>Þe beste ma<i>n</i> that micthe liue,</p>
<span class="linenum">200</span>
<p>Þe beste, fayreste, the stra<i>n</i>gest ok:—</p>
<p>Þat dede he him sweren on þe bok.</p>
<p>And þa<i>n</i>ne shulde he engelond</p>
<p>Al bitechen in-to hire hond.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag8" id="poem_note8" class="pginternal">8</a>
<i>Qu.</i> tuenti. Cf. v. 259.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag9" id="poem_note9" class="pginternal">9</a>
MS. mithe. But see l. 257.</p>
</div>
<span class="linenum">204</span>
<p class="section">
Quanne<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note10" id="poem_tag10">10</a> þat was sworn on his wise,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He gives up all England to the earl, to keep for her.</p>
<p>Þe ki<i>n</i>g dede þe mayde<i>n</i> arise,</p>
<p>And þe erl hire bitaucte,</p>
<p>And al the lond he euere awcte;</p>
<span class="linenum">208</span>
<p>Engelonde eueri del;</p>
<p>And preide, he shulde yeme hire wel.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag10" id="poem_note10" class="pginternal">10</a>
MS. Ouanne. And perhaps “his” should have been “þis.”</p>
<p class="section">
Þe king ne mowcte don no more,</p>
<p>But yerne preyede godes ore;</p>
<span class="linenum">212</span>
<p>And dede him hosle<i>n</i> wel and shriue,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The king does penance.</p>
<p>I woth, fif hu<i>n</i>dred siþes and fiue;</p>
<p>An ofte dede him sore swinge,</p>
<p>And wit hondes smerte di<i>n</i>ge;</p>
<span class="linenum">216</span>
<p>So þat þe blod ran of his fleys,</p>
<p>Þat tendre was, and swiþe neys.</p>
<p><a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note11" id="poem_tag11">11</a>And sone gaf it euere-il del;</p>
<p>He made his quiste swiþe wel.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He makes his will.</p>
<span class="linenum">220</span>
<p>Wan it was goue<i>n</i>, ne micte me<i>n</i> finde</p>
<p>So mikel me<i>n</i> micte hi<i>m</i> in winde,</p>
<p>Of his i<i>n</i> arke, ne in chiste,</p>
<span class="pagenum">8</span>
<a id="page8"> </a>
<p>In engelond þat noma<i>n</i> wiste:</p>
<span class="linenum">224</span>
<p>For al was youe<i>n</i>, faire and wel,</p>
<p>Þat him was leued no catel.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag11" id="poem_note11" class="pginternal">11</a>
Some lines appear to be wanting here, such as—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>“He þoucte his quiste þan to make,</p>
<p>His catel muste he wel bitake,” &amp;c.</p>
</div>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
KING ATHELWOLD DIES.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Þa<i>n</i>ne he hauede be<i>n</i> ofte swngen,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 205, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Ofte shriue<i>n</i>, and ofte dungen,</p>
<span class="linenum">228</span>
<p>“<i>In man</i>us <i>tuas</i>, lou[<i>er</i>]de,” he seyde,</p>
<p>Her þat he þe speche leyde.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The king dies.</p>
<p>To ihe<i>s</i>u crist bigan to calle,</p>
<p>And deyede biforn his heyme<i>n</i> alle.</p>
<span class="linenum">232</span>
<p>Þan he was ded, þere micte me<i>n</i> se</p>
<p>Þe meste sorwe that micte be;</p>
<p>Þer was sobbing, siking, and sor,</p>
<p>Handes wri<i>n</i>gi<i>n</i>g, and drawi<i>n</i>g bi hor.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
All mourn for him.</p>
<span class="linenum">236</span>
<p>Alle greten swiþe sore,</p>
<p>Riche and poure þat þere wore;</p>
<p>An mikel sorwe hauede<i>n</i> alle,</p>
<p>Leuedyes i<i>n</i> boure, knictes i<i>n</i> halle.</p>
<span class="linenum">240</span>
<p class="section">
Quan þat sorwe was somdel laten,</p>
<p>And he hauede<i>n</i> longe graten,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Masses are sung for him.</p>
<p>Belles dede<i>n</i> he sone ri<i>n</i>gen,</p>
<p>Monkes and p<i>re</i>stes messe singen;</p>
<span class="linenum">244</span>
<p>And saut<i>er</i>es deden he manie reden,</p>
<p>Þat god self shulde his soule lede<i>n</i></p>
<p>Into heuene, biforn his sone,</p>
<p>And þer wit-uten hende wone.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He is buried and the earl takes possession,</p>
<span class="linenum">248</span>
<p>Þan he was to þe erþe brouth,</p>
<p>Þe riche erl ne foryat nouth,</p>
<p>Þat he ne dede al engelond</p>
<p>Sone sayse i<i>n</i>til his hond;</p>
<span class="linenum">252</span>
<p>And in þe castels leth he<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note12" id="poem_tag12">12</a> do</p>
<p>Þe knictes he micte triste<i>n</i> to;</p>
<p>And alle þe englis dede he swere[<i>n</i>],</p>
<span class="pagenum">9</span>
<a id="page9"> </a>
<p>Þat he shulde<i>n</i> him ghod fey baren;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
till the maiden is twenty years old.</p>
<span class="linenum">256</span>
<p>He yaf alle me<i>n</i>, þat god þoucte,</p>
<p>Liuen and deyen til þat him moucte,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note13" id="poem_tag13">13</a></p>
<p>Til þat þe kinges dowter wore</p>
<p>Tuenti wint<i>er</i> hold, and more.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag12" id="poem_note12" class="pginternal">12</a>
Sir F. Madden printed “lechhe”; but the MS. may be read “leth he.”</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag13" id="poem_note13" class="pginternal">13</a>
So in MS. But the sense requires</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>“He gaf alle men, þat god <i>him</i> þouchte,</p>
<p>Liuen and deyen til þat <i>he</i> mouete,” &amp;c.</p>
</div>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
EARL GODRICH TAKES POSSESSION.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">260</span>
<p class="section">
Þa<i>n</i>ne he hauede take<i>n</i> þis oth</p>
<p>Of erles, baru<i>n</i>s, lef and loth,</p>
<p>Of knictes, cherles, fre and þewe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Earl Godrich appoints justices, sheriffs, &amp;c.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote263" id="texttag263">263</a>
<p>Justises dede he maken newe,</p>
<span class="linenum">264</span>
<p>Al engelond to faren þorw,</p>
<p>Fro douere i<i>n</i>to rokesborw.</p>
<p>Schireues he sette, bedels, and g<i>re</i>yues,</p>
<p>Grith-sergea<i>n</i>s, wit lo<i>n</i>ge gleyues,</p>
<span class="linenum">268</span>
<p>To yeme<i>n</i> wilde wodes and paþes</p>
<p>Fro wicke me<i>n</i>, that wolde don scaþes;</p>
<p>And forto haue<i>n</i> alle at his cri,</p>
<p>At his wille, at his merci;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 205<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<span class="linenum">272</span>
<p>Þat non durste be<i>n</i> him ageyn,</p>
<p>Erl ne baron, knict ne sweyn.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He grows very rich,</p>
<p>Wislike for soth, was him wel</p>
<p>Of folc, of wepne, of catel,</p>
<span class="linenum">276</span>
<p>Soþlike, in a lite þrawe</p>
<p>Al engelond of him stod [in] awe;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and all England fears him.</p>
<p>Al engelond was of him adrad,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note14" id="poem_tag14">14</a></p>
<p>So his þe beste fro þe gad.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag14" id="poem_note14" class="pginternal">14</a>
MS. “adred,” altered to “adrad.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The maiden grows up very fair.</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote280" id="texttag280">280</a>
<p class="section">
Þe ki<i>n</i>ges douther bigan þriue,</p>
<p>And wex þe fayrest wma<i>n</i> on liue.</p>
<p>Of alle þewes w[as] she wis,</p>
<p>Þat gode weren, and of pris.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Her name is Goldborough.</p>
<span class="linenum">284</span>
<p>Þe mayden Goldeboru was hoten;</p>
<p>For hire was mani a ter igroten.</p>
<span class="pagenum">10</span>
<a id="page10"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODRICH PLOTS AGAINST HIS WARD.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Quanne the Erl godrich him herde</p>
<p>Of þat mayde<i>n</i>, hw we[l s]he ferde;</p>
<span class="linenum">288</span>
<p>Hw wis sho was, w chaste, hw fayr,</p>
<p>And þat sho was þe rithe eyr</p>
<p>Of engelond, of al þe rike:—</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich is vexed.</p>
<p>Þo bigan godrich to sike,</p>
<span class="linenum">292</span>
<p>And seyde, “weþer she sholde be</p>
<p>Quen and leuedi ouer me?</p>
<p>Hweþer sho sholde al engelond,</p>
<p>And me, and mine, haue<i>n</i> in hire hond?</p>
<span class="linenum">296</span>
<p>Daþeit hwo it hire thaue!</p>
<p>Shal sho it neu<i>er</i>e more haue.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“Shall I give England to a fool, a girl?</p>
<p>Sholde ic yeue a fol, a þerne,</p>
<p>Engelond, þou sho it yerne?</p>
<span class="linenum">300</span>
<p>Daþeit hwo it hire yeue,</p>
<p>Euere more hwil i liue!</p>
<p>Sho is waxen al to prud,</p>
<p>For gode metes, and noble shrud,</p>
<span class="linenum">304</span>
<p>Þat hic haue youen hire to offte;</p>
<p>Hic haue yemed hire to softe.</p>
<p>Shal it nouth ben als sho þenkes,</p>
<p>‘Hope maketh fol ma<i>n</i> ofte ble<i>n</i>kes.’</p>
<p class="sidenote">
My son shall have England<ins class="mycorr" title="close quote missing">. </ins></p>
<span class="linenum">308</span>
<p>Ich haue a sone, a ful fayr knaue,</p>
<p>He shal e<i>n</i>gelond al haue.</p>
<p>He shal [ben] ki<i>n</i>g, he shal ben sire,</p>
<p>So brouke i eu<i>er</i>e mi blake swire!”</p>
<span class="linenum">312</span>
<p class="section">
Hwan þis t<i>r</i>ayson was al þouth,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He lets his oath go for nothing.</p>
<p>Of his oth ne was him nouth.</p>
<p>He let his oth al ouer-ga,</p>
<p>Þerof ne yaf he nouth a stra;</p>
<span class="linenum">316</span>
<p>But sone dede hire fete,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 205<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Er he wolde hete<i>n</i> ani mete,</p>
<p>Fro wi<i>n</i>chestre þer sho was,</p>
<p>Also a wicke t<i>r</i>aytur iudas;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He sends the maiden to Dover.</p>
<span class="linenum">320</span>
<p>And dede lede<i>n</i> hire to doure,<span class="pagenum">11</span></p>
<a id="page11"> </a>
<p>Þat standeth on þe seis oure;</p>
<p>And þerhinne dede hire fede</p>
<p>Pourelike in feble wede,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He shuts her up in the castle.</p>
<span class="linenum">324</span>
<p>Þe castel dede he yemen so,</p>
<p>Þat non ne micte come<i>n</i> hire to</p>
<p>Of hire frend, with [hire] to speke<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>Þat heuere micte hire bale wreke<i>n</i>.</p>
<span class="linenum">328</span>
<p class="section">
Of Goldeb<i>oru</i> shul we nou laten,</p>
<p>Þat nouth ne bli<i>n</i>neth forto g<i>ra</i>te<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>Þet sho liggeth in p<i>ri</i>soun:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
May Christ release Goldborough from prison!</p>
<p>Ihe<i>s</i>u c<i>r</i>ist, that lazarun</p>
<span class="linenum">332</span>
<p>To liue broucte fro dede bondes,</p>
<p>He lese hire wit hise hondes;</p>
<p>And leue sho mo him y-se</p>
<p>Heye ha<i>n</i>gen on galwe tre,</p>
<span class="linenum">336</span>
<p>Þat hire haued i<i>n</i> sorwe brouth,</p>
<p>So as sho ne misdede nouth!</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
BIRKABEYN IS KING OF DENMARK.</span></p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote338" id="texttag338">338</a>
<p class="section">
Sawe nou forth i<i>n</i> hure spelle;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
At that time there was a king of Denmark, called Birkabeyn.</p>
<p>In þat time, so it bifelle,</p>
<span class="linenum">340</span>
<p>Was in þe lon of denemark</p>
<p>A riche king, and swyþe stark.</p>
<p>Þ[e] name of him was birkabeyn,</p>
<p>He hauede mani knict and sueyn;</p>
<span class="linenum">344</span>
<p>He was fayr man, and wicth,</p>
<p>Of bodi he was þe beste knicth</p>
<p>Þat eu<i>er</i>e micte lede<i>n</i> uth here,</p>
<p>Or stede onne ride, or ha<i>n</i>dlen spere,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He had three children.</p>
<span class="linenum">348</span>
<p>Þre children he hauede bi his wif,</p>
<p>He hem louede so his lif.</p>
<p>He hauede a sone [and] douhtres two,</p>
<p>Swiþe fayre, as fel it so.</p>
<span class="linenum">352</span>
<p>He þat wile non forbere,</p>
<p>Riche ne poure; king ne kaysere,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Death came upon him.</p>
<p>Deth him tok þa<i>n</i> he bes[t] wolde</p>
<span class="pagenum">12</span>
<a id="page12"> </a>
<p>Liuen, but hyse dayes were fulde;</p>
<span class="linenum">356</span>
<p>Þat he ne moucte no more liue,</p>
<p>For gol ne siluer, ne for no gyue.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He sends for the priests.</p>
<p class="section">
Hwa<i>n</i> he þat wiste, raþe he sende</p>
<p>After p<i>re</i>stes fer an hende,</p>
<span class="linenum">360</span>
<p>Chanounes gode, and mo<i>n</i>kes beþe,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note15" id="poem_tag15">15</a></p>
<p>Him for to<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note16" id="poem_tag16">16</a> wisse, and to Rede;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 206, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Him for to hoslon, an forto shriue,</p>
<p>Hwil his bodi were on liue.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag15" id="poem_note15" class="pginternal">15</a>
MS. “boþe.” But “beþe” rimes to “Rede”; see l. 694.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag16" id="poem_note16" class="pginternal">16</a>
MS. fort<i>hm</i> to, the <i>hm</i> being expuncted.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
BIRKABEYN SELECTS EARL GODARD   TO BE GUARDIAN OF HIS THREE
CHILDREN.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">364</span>
<p class="section">
Hwa<i>n</i> he was hosled and shriue<i>n</i>,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote365" id="texttag365">365</a>
<p>His q<i>ui</i>ste maked, and for him gyue<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>His knictes dede he alle site,</p>
<p>For þorw hem he wolde wite,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He asks who will guard his children?</p>
<span class="linenum">368</span>
<p>Hwo micte yeme hise childre<i>n</i> yunge,</p>
<p>Til þat he kouþen speke<i>n</i> wit tu<i>n</i>ge;</p>
<p>Speke<i>n</i> and gangen, on horse riden,</p>
<p>Knictes an sweynes bi here side<i>n</i>.</p>
<span class="linenum">372</span>
<p>He spoken þer-offe, and chosen sone</p>
<p>A riche man was under mone,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He chooses Godard.</p>
<p>Was þe trewest þat he wende,</p>
<p>Godard, þe kinges oune frende;</p>
<span class="linenum">376</span>
<p>And seyden, he Moucthe he<i>m</i> best loke,</p>
<p>Yif þat he hem vndertoke,</p>
<p>Til hise sone Mouthe bere</p>
<p>Helm on heued, and lede<i>n</i> vt here,</p>
<span class="linenum">380</span>
<p>In his hand a sp<i>e</i>re stark,</p>
<p>And king ben maked of denemark.</p>
<p>He wel trowede þat he seyde,</p>
<p>And on Godard handes leyde;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He commends the children to Godard.</p>
<span class="linenum">384</span>
<p>And seyde, “Here bi-teche i þe</p>
<p>Mine childre<i>n</i> alle þre,</p>
<p>Al denemark, and al mi fe,</p>
<p>Til þat mi sone of helde be;</p>
<span class="pagenum">13</span>
<a id="page13"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
He makes him swear to take care of them,</p>
<span class="linenum">388</span>
<p>But þat ich wille, þat þo[u] suere</p>
<p>On auter, and on messe-gere,</p>
<p>On þe belles þat men ri<i>n</i>ges,</p>
<p>On messe-bok þe prest on singes,</p>
<span class="linenum">392</span>
<p>Þat þou mine children shalt we[l] yeme,</p>
<p>Þat hire kin be ful wel queme,</p>
<p>Til mi sone mowe ben knicth,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and to give up the kingdom to the boy.</p>
<p>Þanne biteche him þo his Ricth,</p>
<span class="linenum">396</span>
<p>Denemark, and þat þertil lo<i>n</i>ges,</p>
<p>Casteles and tunes, wodes and wo<i>n</i>ges.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godard swears to do so.</p>
<p class="section">
Godard stirt up, an swor al þat</p>
<p>Þe king him bad, and siþen sat</p>
<span class="linenum">400</span>
<p>Bi the knictes, þat þer ware,</p>
<p>Þat wepen alle swiþe sare</p>
<p>For þe king þat deide sone:</p>
<p>Ih<i>es</i>u c<i>ri</i>st, that makede mone</p>
<span class="linenum">404</span>
<p>On þe mirke nith to shine,</p>
<p>Wite his soule fro helle pine;</p>
<p>And leue þat it mote wone</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 206, col. 2.]</p>
<p>In heuene-riche with godes sone!</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODARD IMPRISONS THE THREE CHILDREN.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godard shuts up the children, Havelok, Swanborough, and Helfled, in a
castle.</p>
<span class="linenum">408</span>
<p class="section">
Hwan birkabeyn was leyd i<i>n</i> g<i>ra</i>ue,</p>
<p>Þe erl dede sone take þe knaue,</p>
<p>Hauelok, þat was þe eir,</p>
<p>Swanborow, his sister, helfled, þe toþer,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note17" id="poem_tag17">17</a></p>
<span class="linenum">412</span>
<p>And in þe castel dede he hem do,</p>
<p>Þer non ne micte hem come<i>n</i> to</p>
<p>Of here kyn, þer þei sperd wore;<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note18" id="poem_tag18">18</a></p>
<p>Þer he greten ofte sore,</p>
<span class="linenum">416</span>
<p>Boþe for hunger and for kold,</p>
<p>Or he weren þre wint<i>er</i> hold.</p>
<p>Feblelike he gaf he<i>m</i> cloþes,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He cares not for his oaths.</p>
<p>He ne yaf a note of hise oþes;</p>
<span class="pagenum">14</span>
<a id="page14"> </a>
<span class="linenum">420</span>
<p>He hem [ne] cloþede rith, ne fedde,</p>
<p>Ne hem ne dede richelike be-bedde.</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne godard was sikerlike</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He is a traitor.</p>
<p>Vnder god þe moste swike,</p>
<span class="linenum">424</span>
<p>Þat eure in erþe shaped was,</p>
<p>With-uten on, þe wike Iudas.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
May he be accursed!</p>
<p>Haue he þe malisun to-day</p>
<p>Of alle þat eure speke<i>n</i> may!</p>
<span class="linenum">428</span>
<p>Of p<i>at</i>riark, and of pope!</p>
<p>And of prest with loke<i>n</i> kope!</p>
<p>Of monekes, and h<i>er</i>mites boþe!<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note19" id="poem_tag19">19</a></p>
<p>And of þe leue holi rode,</p>
<span class="linenum">432</span>
<p>Þat god him-selue ran on blode!</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote433" id="texttag433">433</a>
<p class="sidenote">
Cursed be he by north and south!</p>
<p>Crist warie him with his mouth!</p>
<p>Waried wrthe he of norþ and suth!</p>
<p>Offe alle man, þat speke<i>n</i> kunne!</p>
<span class="linenum">436</span>
<p>Of c<i>ri</i>st, þat made<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note20" id="poem_tag20">20</a> mone and su<i>n</i>ne!</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne he hauede of al þe lond</p>
<p>Al þe folk tilled in-til his hond,</p>
<p>And alle haueden swore<i>n</i> him oth,</p>
<span class="linenum">440</span>
<p>Riche and poure, lef and loth,</p>
<p>Þat he sholden hise wille freme,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He plots against the children.</p>
<p>And þat he shulde[<i>n</i>] him nouth g<i>re</i>me,</p>
<p>He þouthe a ful strong trechery,</p>
<span class="linenum">444</span>
<p>A trayson, and a felony,</p>
<p>Of þe children forto make:</p>
<p>Þe deuel of helle him sone take!</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag17" id="poem_note17" class="pginternal">17</a>
Corrupt? Lines 410, 411 do not rime well together.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag18" id="poem_note18" class="pginternal">18</a>
MS. were. But see l. 237.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag19" id="poem_note19" class="pginternal">19</a>
Lines 430, 431, 432 rime together. NB. The words <i>holi rode</i> are
written over an erasure.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag20" id="poem_note20" class="pginternal">20</a>
MS. mande.</p>
</div>
<p class="sidenote">
He goes to the tower where they are.</p>
<p class="section">
Hwa<i>n</i> þat was þouth, onon he ferde</p>
<span class="linenum">448</span>
<p>To þe tour þer he wore<i>n</i> sp<i>er</i>de,</p>
<p>Þer he grete<i>n</i> for hunger and cold:</p>
<p>Þe knaue þat was su<i>m</i>del bold,</p>
<p>Ka<i>m</i> him ageyn, on knes him sette,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 206<i>b</i>, col<ins class="mycorr" title="period missing">.
</ins>1.]</p>
<span class="linenum">452</span>
<p>And godard ful feyre he fer grette;</p>
<p>And Godard seyde, “Wat is yw?</p>
<span class="pagenum">15</span>
<a id="page15"> </a>
<p>Hwi grete ye and goulen nou?”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok says they are hungry.</p>
<p>“For us hu<i>n</i>greth swiþe sore:”—</p>
<span class="linenum">456</span>
<p>Seyden he wolde<i>n</i> [haue] more,</p>
<p>“We ne haue to hete, ne we ne haue</p>
<p>Herinne neyther knith ne knaue</p>
<p>Þat yeueth us drinke<i>n</i>, ne no mete,</p>
<span class="linenum">460</span>
<p>Halue<i>n</i>del þat we moun ete.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“Alas, that we were born!”</p>
<p>Wo is us þat we weren born!</p>
<p>Weilawei! nis it no korn,</p>
<p>Þat men micte make<i>n</i> of bred?</p>
<span class="linenum">464</span>
<p>Vs<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note21" id="poem_tag21">21</a> hungreth, we aren ney ded.”</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag21" id="poem_note21" class="pginternal">21</a>
MS. þs; of. l. 455.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODARD KILLS SWANBORGUGH AND HELFLED.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godard cares not.</p>
<p class="section">
Godard herde here wa,</p>
<p>Ther-offe yaf he nouth a stra,</p>
<p>But tok þe maydnes bothe same<i>n</i>,</p>
<span class="linenum">468</span>
<p>Al-so it were up-on hiis game<i>n</i>;</p>
<p>Al-so he wolde with he<i>m</i> leyke,</p>
<p>Þat were<i>n</i> for hunger g<i>re</i>ne and bleike.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He cuts the throats of the two girls.</p>
<p>Of boþen he karf on two here þrotes,</p>
<span class="linenum">472</span>
<p>And siþen [karf] he<i>m</i> alto grotes.</p>
<p>Þer was sorwe, we so it sawe!</p>
<p>Hwan þe children bi þ[e]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note22" id="poem_tag22">22</a> wawe</p>
<p>Leyen and spraulede<i>n</i> in þe blod:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok sees it, and is afraid.</p>
<span class="linenum">476</span>
<p>Hauelok it saw, and þe[r] bi stod.</p>
<p>Ful sori was þat seli knaue,</p>
<p>Mikel dred he mouthe haue,</p>
<p>For at hise herte he saw a knif,</p>
<span class="linenum">480</span>
<p>For to reuen him hise lyf.</p>
<p>But þe knaue,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note23" id="poem_tag23">23</a> þat litel was,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He begs Godard to spare him,</p>
<p>He knelede bifor þat iudas,</p>
<p>And seyde, “louerd, m<i>er</i>ci nov!</p>
<span class="linenum">484</span>
<p>Ma<i>n</i>rede, louerd, biddi you!</p>
<p>Al denemark i wile you yeue,</p>
<p>To þat forward þu late me liue;</p>
<p>Here hi wile on boke swere,</p>
<span class="linenum">488</span>
<p>Þat neure more ne shal i bere<span class="pagenum">16</span></p>
<a id="page16"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODARD SPARES HAVELOK FOR A TIME.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
offering never to oppose him,</p>
<p>Ayen þe, louerd, shel ne spere,</p>
<p>Ne oþer wepne<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note24" id="poem_tag24">24</a> that may you dere.</p>
<p>Louerd, haue m<i>er</i>ci of me!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and to flee from Denmark.</p>
<span class="linenum">492</span>
<p>To-day i wile fro denemark fle,</p>
<p>Ne neu<i>er</i>e more comen ageyn:</p>
<p>Swere<i>n</i> y wole, þat bircabein</p>
<p>Neu<i>er</i>e yete me ne gat:”—</p>
<span class="linenum">496</span>
<p>Hwan þe deuel he[r]de<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note25" id="poem_tag25">25</a> that,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 206<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Sum-del bigan him forto rewe;</p>
<p>With-drow þe knif, þat was lewe</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godard has pity on him.</p>
<p>Of þe seli children blod;</p>
<span class="linenum">500</span>
<p>Þer was miracle fair and god!</p>
<p>Þat he þe knaue nouth ne slou,</p>
<p>But fo[r] rewnesse him <i>w</i>it-drow.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note26" id="poem_tag26">26</a></p>
<p>Of auelok rewede him ful sore,</p>
<span class="linenum">504</span>
<p>And þoucte, he wolde þat he ded wore,</p>
<p>But on þat he nouth wit his hend</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote506" id="texttag506">506</a>
<p>Ne drepe him nouth,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note27" id="poem_tag27">27</a> þat fule fend!</p>
<p>Þoucte he, als he him bi stod,</p>
<span class="linenum">508</span>
<p>Starinde als he were wod:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
But he reflects</p>
<p>“Yif y late him liues go,</p>
<p>He micte me wirchen michel wo.</p>
<p>Grith ne get y neu<i>er</i>e mo,</p>
<span class="linenum">512</span>
<p>He may [me] waite<i>n</i> for to slo;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
that, were Havelok dead, his children would be the heirs.</p>
<p>And yf he were brouct of liue,</p>
<p>And mine childre<i>n</i> wolde<i>n</i> thriue,</p>
<p>Lou<i>er</i>di<i>n</i>ges after me</p>
<span class="linenum">516</span>
<p>Of al denemark micten he be.</p>
<p>God it wite, he shal ben ded,</p>
<p>Wile i take<i>n</i> non oþer red;</p>
<span class="pagenum">17</span>
<a id="page17"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODARD TELLS GRIM TO DROWN HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
He determines to drown him.</p>
<p>I shal do caste<i>n</i> hi<i>m</i> in þe se,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note28" id="poem_tag28">28</a></p>
<span class="linenum">520</span>
<p>Þer i wile þat he drench[ed] be;</p>
<p>Aboute<i>n</i> his hals an anker god,</p>
<p>Þat he ne flete in the flod.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He sends for a fisherman, and says to him,</p>
<p>Þer anon he dede sende</p>
<span class="linenum">524</span>
<p>After a fishere þat he wende,</p>
<p>Þat wolde al his wille do,</p>
<p>And sone anon he seyde him to:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“Grim, I will make you free.</p>
<p>“Grim, þou wost þu art mi þral,</p>
<span class="linenum">528</span>
<p>Wilte don mi wille al,</p>
<p>Þat i wile bidde<i>n</i> þe,</p>
<p>To-morwen [i] shal make<i>n</i> þe fre,</p>
<p>And aucte þe yeuen, and riche make,</p>
<span class="linenum">532</span>
<p>With-þa<i>n</i> þu wilt þis child[e] take,</p>
<p>And lede<i>n</i> him with þe to-nicht,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Throw this child into the sea”.</p>
<p>Þan þou sest se<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note29" id="poem_tag29">29</a> Mone lith,</p>
<p>In-to þe se, and don him þer-i<i>n</i>ne,</p>
<span class="linenum">536</span>
<p>Al wile [i] take<i>n</i> on me þe sinne.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim binds the child.</p>
<p>Grim tok þe child, and bo<i>n</i>d hi<i>m</i> faste,</p>
<p>Hwil þe bondes micte laste;</p>
<p>Þat weren of ful stro<i>n</i>g line:—</p>
<span class="linenum">540</span>
<p>Þo was hauelok i<i>n</i> ful stro<i>n</i>g pine.</p>
<p>Wiste he neu<i>er</i>e her wat was wo:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 207, col. 1.]</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Christ wreak thee of Godard, Havelok!</p>
<p>Ih<i>es</i>u c<i>ri</i>st, þat makede to go</p>
<p>Þe halte, and þe doumbe speke<i>n</i>,</p>
<span class="linenum">544</span>
<p>Hauelok, þe of Godard wreken!</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag22" id="poem_note22" class="pginternal">22</a>
MS. biþ; of. l. 2470.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag23" id="poem_note23" class="pginternal">23</a>
MS. kaue.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag24" id="poem_note24" class="pginternal">24</a>
MS. “wepne bere,” where “bere” is redundant.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag25" id="poem_note25" class="pginternal">25</a>
MS. hede.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag26" id="poem_note26" class="pginternal">26</a>
Printed thus in the former edition:— “But to rewnesse him thit
drow.” But the MS. has <i>fo</i>, not <i>to</i>, where <i>fo</i> is
corruptly written for <i>for</i>, as in l. 1318; and the initial
letter of the last syllable but one may be read as a Saxon <i>w</i> (ƿ),
not a thorn-letter (þ). It merely repeats the idea in ll. 497, 498.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag27" id="poem_note27" class="pginternal">27</a>
Qu. mouth.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag28" id="poem_note28" class="pginternal">28</a>
MS. she.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag29" id="poem_note29" class="pginternal">29</a>
<i>So in</i> MS. <i>Qu.</i> þe.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK IS TAKEN TO GRIM’S COTTAGE.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Hwan grim hi<i>m</i> hauede faste bou<i>n</i>de<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>And siþe<i>n</i> in an eld cloth wnden</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim gags the child.</p>
<p>A keuel of clutes, ful, un-wraste,</p>
<span class="linenum">548</span>
<p>Þat he [ne] mouthe speke, ne fnaste,</p>
<p>Hwere he wolde him bere or lede.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote550" id="texttag550">550</a>
<p>Hwan he hauede don þat dede,</p>
<p>Hwa<i>n</i><a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note30" id="poem_tag30">30</a> þe swike him hauede hethede,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note31" id="poem_tag31">31</a></p>
<span class="pagenum">18</span>
<a id="page18"> </a>
<span class="linenum">552</span>
<p>Þat he shulde him forth [lede]</p>
<p>And him drinchen in þe se;</p>
<p>Þat forwarde makeden he.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He puts him in a bag, and takes him on his back.</p>
<p>In a poke, ful and blac,</p>
<span class="linenum">556</span>
<p>Sone he caste him on his bac,</p>
<p>Ant bar him hom to hise cleue,</p>
<p>And bi-taucte hi<i>m</i> dame leue,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He puts him in charge of his wife.</p>
<p>And seyde, “wite þou þis knaue,</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote560" id="texttag560">560</a>
<p>Al-so thou with<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note31a" id="poem_tag31a">*</a> mi lif haue;</p>
<p>I shal dreinche<i>n</i> him i<i>n</i> þe se,</p>
<p>For him shole we ben maked fre,</p>
<p>Gold haue<i>n</i> ynou, and oþer fe;</p>
<span class="linenum">564</span>
<p>Þat hauet mi louerd bihote<i>n</i> me.”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag30" id="poem_note30" class="pginternal">30</a>
We should rather read “<i>þan</i>.”</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag31" id="poem_note31" class="pginternal">31</a>
MS. he þede.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag31a" id="poem_note31a" class="pginternal">*</a>
For <i>with</i>, Mr Garnett proposed to read <i>wilt</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GRIM SEES THAT HAVELOK IS THE KING’S SON.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
She throws down Havelok violently.</p>
<p class="section">
Hwan dame [leue] herde þat,</p>
<p>Vp she stirte, and nouth ne sat,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote567" id="texttag567">567</a>
<p>And caste þe knaue adoun so harde,</p>
<span class="linenum">568</span>
<p>Þat hise croune he þer crakede</p>
<p>Ageyn a gret ston, þer it lay:</p>
<p>Þo hauelok micte sei, “weilawei!</p>
<p>Þat eu<i>er</i>e was i kinges bern!”</p>
<span class="linenum">572</span>
<p>Þat him ne hauede grip or ern,</p>
<p>Leoun or wlf, wluine or bere,</p>
<p>Or oþer best, þat wolde him dere.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The child lies there till midnight.</p>
<p>So lay þat child to middel nicth,</p>
<span class="linenum">576</span>
<p>Þat grim bad leue bringe<i>n</i> lict,</p>
<p>For to don on [him] his cloþes:</p>
<p>“Ne the<i>n</i>keste nowt of mine oþes</p>
<p>Þat ich haue mi louerd swore<i>n</i>?</p>
<span class="linenum">580</span>
<p>Ne wile i nouth be forloren.</p>
<p>I shal beren him to þe se,</p>
<p>Þou wost þat [bi-]houes me;</p>
<p>And i shal drenchen him þer-inne;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim tells his wife to light the fire and a candle.</p>
<span class="linenum">584</span>
<p>Ris up swiþe, an go þu binne,</p>
<p>And blou þe fir, and lith a kandel:”</p>
<p>Als she shulde hise cloþes handel</p>
<span class="pagenum">19</span>
<a id="page19"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 207, col. 2.]</p>
<p>On forto don, and blawe þe<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note32" id="poem_tag32">32</a> fir,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
She sees a light shining round the lad.</p>
<span class="linenum">588</span>
<p>She saw þer-inne a lith ful shir,</p>
<p>Also brith so it were day,</p>
<p>Aboute þe knaue þer he lay.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote591" id="texttag591">591</a>
<p>Of hise mouth it stod a stem,</p>
<span class="linenum">592</span>
<p>Als it were a su<i>n</i>nebem;</p>
<p>Also lith was it þer-inne,</p>
<p>So þer brenden cerges i<i>n</i>ne:<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note33" id="poem_tag33">33</a></p>
<p>“Ih<i>es</i>u cr<i>i</i>st!” wat dame leue,</p>
<span class="linenum">596</span>
<p>“Hwat is þat lith in vre cleue!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
She bids Grim come and see.</p>
<p>Sir<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note34" id="poem_tag34">34</a> up grim, and loke wat it menes,</p>
<p>Hwat is þe lith as þou wenes?”</p>
<p>He stirte<i>n</i> boþe up to the knaue,</p>
<span class="linenum">600</span>
<p>For ma<i>n</i> shal god wille haue,</p>
<p>Vnkeuelede<i>n</i> him, and swiþe unbou<i>n</i>de<i>n</i>,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They find a mark on his shoulder.</p>
<p>And sone anon [upon] him fu<i>n</i>den,</p>
<p>Als he tirnede<i>n</i> of his serk,</p>
<span class="linenum">604</span>
<p>On his rith shuldre a kyne merk;</p>
<p>A swiþe brith, a swiþe fair:</p>
<p>“Goddot!” quath grim, “þis [is] ure eir</p>
<p>Þat shal [ben] louerd of denemark,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim says the lad is to be king.</p>
<span class="linenum">608</span>
<p>He shal be<i>n</i> king strong and stark;</p>
<p>He shal haue<i>n</i> in his hand</p>
<p>A[l] denemark and engeland;</p>
<p>He shal do godard ful wo,</p>
<span class="linenum">612</span>
<p>He shal him hangen, or quik flo;</p>
<p>Or he shal him al quic g<i>ra</i>ue,</p>
<p>Of him shal he no m<i>er</i>ci haue.”</p>
<p>Þus seide grim, and sore gret,</p>
<span class="linenum">616</span>
<p>And sone fel him to þe fet,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He prays Havelok to forgive him.</p>
<p>And seide, “louerd, haue m<i>er</i>ci</p>
<p>Of me, and leue, that is me bi!</p>
<p>Louerd, we aren boþe þine,</p>
<span class="linenum">620</span>
<p>Þine cherles, þine hine.<span class="pagenum">20</span></p>
<a id="page20"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GRIM AND HIS WIFE FEED HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p>Lowerd, we shole<i>n</i> þe wel fede,</p>
<p>Til þat þu cone ride<i>n</i> on stede,</p>
<p>Til þat þu cone ful wel bere</p>
<span class="linenum">624</span>
<p>Helm on heued, sheld and sp<i>er</i>e.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godard shall never know about this.</p>
<p>He ne shal neuere wite, sikerlike,</p>
<p>Godard, þat fule swike.</p>
<p>Þoru oþer ma<i>n</i>, louerd, tha<i>n</i> þoru þe,</p>
<span class="linenum">628</span>
<p>Sal i neu<i>er</i>e frema<i>n</i> be.</p>
<p>Þou shalt me, louerd, fre make<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>For i shal yemen þe, and wake<i>n</i>;</p>
<p>Þoru þe wile i fredom haue:”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 207<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<span class="linenum">632</span>
<p>Þo was haueloc a bliþe knaue.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok is glad, and asks for bread.</p>
<p>He sat him up, and crauede bred.</p>
<p>And seide, “ich am [wel] ney ded,</p>
<p>Hwat for hunger, wat for bondes</p>
<span class="linenum">636</span>
<p>Þat þu leidest on min hondes;</p>
<p>And for [þe] keuel at þe laste,</p>
<p>Þat in mi mouth was þrist faste.</p>
<p>y was þe[r]-with so harde prangled,</p>
<span class="linenum">640</span>
<p>Þat i was þe[r]-with <ins class="edcorr" title="changed by editor from ‘ney’">ney[e]</ins> strangled.”</p>
<p>“Wel is me þat þu mayth hete:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Dame Leve brings him bread and cheese, butter, &amp;c.</p>
<p>Goddoth!” quath leue, “y shal þe fete</p>
<p>Bred an chese, butere and milk,</p>
<span class="linenum">644</span>
<p>Pastees and flaunes, al with suilk</p>
<p>Shole we sone þe wel fede,</p>
<p>Louerd, in þis mikel nede,</p>
<p>Soth it is, þ<i>a</i>t me<i>n</i> seyt and suereth:</p>
<span class="linenum">648</span>
<p>‘Þer god wile helpe<i>n</i>, nouth no dereth.’”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag32" id="poem_note32" class="pginternal">32</a>
MS. þer.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag33" id="poem_note33" class="pginternal">33</a>
<i>Qu.</i> þrinne. See ll. 716, 761, 2125.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag34" id="poem_note34" class="pginternal">34</a>
<i>Qu.</i> stir, <i>or</i> stirt.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GRIM SAYS HE HAS DROWNED HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Þa<i>n</i>ne sho hauede brouth þe mete,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok eats all up greedily.</p>
<p>Haueloc anon biga<i>n</i> to ete</p>
<p>Grundlike, and was ful bliþe;</p>
<span class="linenum">652</span>
<p>Couþe he nouth his hunger Miþe.</p>
<p>A lof he het, y woth, and more,</p>
<p>For him hungrede swiþe sore.</p>
<p>Þre dayes þer-biforn, i wene,</p>
<span class="pagenum">21</span>
<a id="page21"> </a>
<span class="linenum">656</span>
<p>Et he no mete, þat was wel sene.</p>
<p>Hwan he hauede eten, and was fed,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim puts him to bed.</p>
<p>Grim dede make<i>n</i> a ful fayr bed;</p>
<p>Vncloþede him, and dede hi<i>m</i> þer-i<i>n</i>ne,</p>
<span class="linenum">660</span>
<p>And seyde, “Slep<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note34a" id="poem_tag34a">*</a> sone, with michel
wi<i>n</i>ne;</p>
<p>Slep wel faste, and dred þe nouth,</p>
<p>Fro sorwe to ioie art þu brouth.”</p>
<p>Sone so it was lith of day,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim tells Godard he has killed Havelok,</p>
<span class="linenum">664</span>
<p>Grim it under-tok þe wey</p>
<p>To þe wicke traitour godard,</p>
<p>Þat was denemak a<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note35" id="poem_tag35">35</a> stiward,</p>
<p>And seyde, “louerd, don ich haue</p>
<span class="linenum">668</span>
<p>Þat þou me bede of þe knaue;</p>
<p>He is drenched i<i>n</i> þe flod,</p>
<p>Aboute<i>n</i> his hals an anker god;</p>
<p>He is witer-like ded,</p>
<span class="linenum">672</span>
<p>Eteth he neure more bred;</p>
<p>He liþ drenched in þe se:—</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and asks for his reward.</p>
<p>Yif me gold [and] oþer fe,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note36" id="poem_tag36">36</a></p>
<p>Þat y mowe riche be;</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote676" id="texttag676">676</a>
<p>And with þi chartre make [me] fre,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 207<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<p>For þu ful wel bi-hetet me,</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne i last[e] spak with þe.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godard bids him go home, and remain a thrall;</p>
<p>Godard stod, and lokede on him</p>
<span class="linenum">680</span>
<p>Þoruth-like, with eyne grim;</p>
<p>And seyde, “Wiltu [nou] ben erl?</p>
<p>Go hom swiþe, fule drit, cherl;</p>
<p>Go heþen, and be euere-more</p>
<span class="linenum">684</span>
<p>Þral and cherl, als þou er wore.</p>
<p>Shal [þou] haue non oþer mede;</p>
<p>For litel i [shal]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note37" id="poem_tag37">37</a> do þe lede</p>
<p>To þe galues, so god me rede!</p>
<span class="pagenum">22</span>
<a id="page22"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
for he has done wickedly.</p>
<span class="linenum">688</span>
<p>For þou haues don a wicke dede.</p>
<p>Þou Mait stonde<i>n</i> her to longe,</p>
<p>Bute þou swiþe eþen gonge.”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag34a" id="poem_note34a" class="pginternal">*</a>
Perhaps there should be a comma after <i>Slep</i>, making the sense to
be <i>sleep, son</i>, not <i>sleep soon</i>.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag35" id="poem_note35" class="pginternal">35</a>
<i>Qu.</i> Denemarkes.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag36" id="poem_note36" class="pginternal">36</a>
Cf. l. 1225.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag37" id="poem_note37" class="pginternal">37</a>
The MS. has “ig,” but the <i>g</i> is expuncted; and it omits
“shal.”</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GRIM SETS SAIL FOR ENGLAND.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Grim thoucte to late þat he ran</p>
<span class="linenum">692</span>
<p>Fro þat t<i>ra</i>ytour, þa wicke man;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim fears that both himself and Havelok will be hung.</p>
<p>And þoucte, “wat shal me to rede?</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote694" id="texttag694">694</a>
<p>Wite he him onliue, he wile beþe</p>
<p>Heye hangen on galwe-tre:</p>
<span class="linenum">696</span>
<p>Betere us is of londe to fle,</p>
<p>And berwen boþen ure liues,</p>
<p>And mine childre<i>n</i>, and mine wiues.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim sells his live stock.</p>
<p>Grim solde sone al his corn,</p>
<span class="linenum">700</span>
<p>Shep wit wolle, neth wit horn,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote701" id="texttag701">701</a>
<p>Hors, and swin, [and gate] wit berd,</p>
<p>Þe gees, þe he<i>n</i>nes of þe yerd;</p>
<p>Al he solde, þat outh douthe,</p>
<span class="linenum">704</span>
<p>That he eure selle moucte,</p>
<p>And al he to þe peni drou:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He fits up his ship carefully.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote706" id="texttag706">706</a>
<p>Hise ship he g<i>re</i>yþede wel inow,</p>
<p>He dede it tere, an ful wel pike,</p>
<span class="linenum">708</span>
<p>Þat it ne doutede sond ne krike;</p>
<p>Þer-inne dide a ful god mast,</p>
<p>Stronge kables, and ful fast,</p>
<p>Ores god, an ful god seyl,</p>
<span class="linenum">712</span>
<p>Þer-i<i>n</i>ne wantede nouth a nayl,</p>
<p>Þat eu<i>er</i>e he sholde þer-i<i>n</i>ne do:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He takes with him his wife, his three sons, his two daughters, and
Havelok.</p>
<p>Hwan he hauedet greyþed so,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote715" id="texttag715">715</a>
<p>Hauelok þe yunge he dide þer-inne,</p>
<span class="linenum">716</span>
<p>Him and his wif, hise sones þrinne,</p>
<p>And hise two doutres, þat faire wore,</p>
<p>And sone dede he leyn in an ore,</p>
<p>And drou him to þe heye se,</p>
<span class="linenum">720</span>
<p>Þere he mith alþer-best[e] fle.</p>
<p>Fro londe wore<i>n</i> he bote a mile,</p>
<span class="pagenum">23</span>
<a id="page23"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 208, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Ne were neuere but ane hwile,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A north wind arises, called the <i>bise</i>, and drives them to
England.</p>
<p>Þat it ne bigan a wind to Rise</p>
<span class="linenum">724</span>
<p>Out of þe north, me<i>n</i> calleth ‘bise’</p>
<p>And drof he<i>m</i> intil engelond,</p>
<p>Þat al was siþen in his hond,</p>
<p>His, þat hauelok was þe name;</p>
<span class="linenum">728</span>
<p>But or he hauede michel shame,</p>
<p>Michel sorwe, and michel tene,</p>
<p>And þrie he gat it al bidene;</p>
<p>Als ye shule<i>n</i> nou forthwar lere,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note38" id="poem_tag38">38</a></p>
<span class="linenum">732</span>
<p>Yf that ye wile<i>n</i> þer-to here.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag38" id="poem_note38" class="pginternal">38</a>
MS. here; <i>read</i> lere. Cf. ll. 12, 1640.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GRIM FOUNDS THE TOWN OF GRIMSBY.</span></p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote733" id="texttag733">733</a>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim went up the Humber to Lindesey.</p>
<p class="section">
IN humb<i>er</i> grim biga<i>n</i> to lende,</p>
<p>In lindeseye, Rith at þe north ende.</p>
<p>Þer sat is ship up-on þe sond,</p>
<span class="linenum">736</span>
<p>But grim it drou up to þe lond;</p>
<p>And þere he made a litel cote,</p>
<p>To him and to hise flote.</p>
<p>Biga<i>n</i> he þere for to erþe,</p>
<span class="linenum">740</span>
<p>A litel hus to maken of erþe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
There he built a house.</p>
<p>So þat he wel þore were</p>
<p>Of here herboru h<i>er</i>borwed þere;</p>
<p>And for þat grim þat place aute,</p>
<span class="linenum">744</span>
<p>Þe stede of grim þe name laute;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
That place was called Grimsby, after Grim.</p>
<p>So þat [hit] grimesbi calleth alle</p>
<p>Þat þer-offe speken alle,<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note38a" id="poem_tag38a">*</a></p>
<p>And so shule<i>n</i> me<i>n</i> calle<i>n</i> it ay,</p>
<span class="linenum">748</span>
<p>Bituene þis and domesday.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag38a" id="poem_note38a" class="pginternal">*</a>
For <i>alle</i>, Mr Garnett proposed to read <i>shalle</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK DOES NOT LIKE BEING IDLE.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim was a good fisherman.</p>
<p class="section">
Grim was fishere swiþe god,</p>
<p>And mikel couþe on the flod;</p>
<p>Mani god fish þer-inne he tok,</p>
<span class="linenum">752</span>
<p>Boþe with neth, and with hok.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote753" id="texttag753">753</a>
<p class="sidenote">
He caught sturgeons, turbot, &amp;c.</p>
<p>He tok þe sturgiun, and þe qual,</p>
<p>And þe turbut, and lax with-al,</p>
<span class="pagenum">24</span>
<a id="page24"> </a>
<p>He tok þe sele, and þe hwel;</p>
<span class="linenum">756</span>
<p>He spedde ofte swiþe wel:</p>
<p>Keling he tok, and tu<i>m</i>berel,</p>
<p>Hering, and þe makerel,</p>
<p>Þe Butte, þe schulle, þe þornebake:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He had four panniers made for himself and his sons.</p>
<span class="linenum">760</span>
<p>Gode paniers dede he make</p>
<p>Ontil him, and oþer þrinne,</p>
<p>Til hise sones to bere<i>n</i> fish inne,</p>
<p>Vp o-londe to selle and fonge;</p>
<span class="linenum">764</span>
<p>Forbar he neyþe[r] tun, ne gronge,</p>
<p>Þat he ne to-yede with his ware;</p>
<p>Kam he neu<i>er</i>e hom hand-bare,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 208, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Þat he ne broucte bred and sowel,</p>
<span class="linenum">768</span>
<p>In his shirte, or in his couel;</p>
<p>In his poke benes and korn:—</p>
<p>Hise swink ne hauede he nowt forlorn.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He used to sell lampreys at Lincoln,</p>
<p>And hwan he tok þe grete laumprei,</p>
<span class="linenum">772</span>
<p>Ful we[l] he couþe þe rithe wei</p>
<p>To lincolne, þe gode boru;</p>
<p>Ofte he yede it þoru and þoru,</p>
<p>Til he hauede wol<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note39" id="poem_tag39">39</a> wel sold,</p>
<span class="linenum">776</span>
<p>And þer-fore þe penies told.</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne he com, þe<i>n</i>ne he were bliþe,</p>
<p>For hom he brouthe fele siþe</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and bring home simnels, meal, meat, and hemp.</p>
<p>Wastels, simenels with þe horn,</p>
<span class="linenum">780</span>
<p>Hise pokes fulle of mele an korn,</p>
<p>Netes flesh, shepes, and swines,</p>
<p>And hemp to make<i>n</i> of gode lines;</p>
<p>And stronge ropes to hise netes,</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote784" id="texttag784">784</a>
<p>In þe se were<i>n</i> he ofte setes.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note40" id="poem_tag40">40</a>
<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note40a" id="poem_tag40a">*</a></p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag39" id="poem_note39" class="pginternal">39</a>
<i>Qu.</i> ful <i>or</i> al.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag40" id="poem_note40" class="pginternal">40</a>
<i>Sic</i> in MS.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag40a" id="poem_note40a" class="pginternal">*</a>
Perhaps we should, however, read <i>se-weren</i>, and the <a href="#textnote784" class="pginternal">note</a> on the line (p. 93) may be wrong. See
<a href="#gloss_Weren" class="pginternal"><i>Weren</i></a> in the Glossary.
<span class="mynote">
See <a href="#endnote" class="pginternal">Endnote</a>.</span></p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK SELLS FISH LIKE THE BEST.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Thus they lived for 12 years.</p>
<p class="section">
Þus-gate grim him fayre ledde.</p>
<p>Him and his genge wel he fedde</p>
<p>Wel twelf wint<i>er</i>, oþer more:</p>
<span class="linenum">788</span>
<p>Hauelok was war þat gri<i>m</i> swa<i>n</i>k sore<span class="pagenum">25</span></p>
<a id="page25"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok thinks he eats too much to be idle.</p>
<p>For his mete, and he lay at hom:</p>
<p>Thouthe, “ich am nou no grom;</p>
<p>Ich am wel waxe<i>n</i>, and wel may ete<i>n</i></p>
<span class="linenum">792</span>
<p>More þa<i>n</i> euere Grim may geten.</p>
<p>Ich ete more, bi god on liue,</p>
<p>Þan grim an hise children fiue!</p>
<p>It ne may nouth ben þus lo<i>n</i>ge,</p>
<span class="linenum">796</span>
<p>Goddot! y wile with þe gange,</p>
<p>For to leren su<i>m</i> god to gete;</p>
<p>Swinken ich wolde for mi mete.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
It is no shame for a man to work.</p>
<p>It is no shame forto swinken;</p>
<span class="linenum">800</span>
<p>Þe ma<i>n</i> þat may wel ete<i>n</i> and dri<i>n</i>ke<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>Þat nouth ne haue but on swi<i>n</i>k lo<i>n</i>g,</p>
<p>To ligge<i>n</i> at hom it is ful strong.</p>
<p>God yelde him þer i ne<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note41" id="poem_tag41">41</a> may,</p>
<span class="linenum">804</span>
<p>Þat haueth me fed to þis day!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He determines to carry about panniers like the rest.</p>
<p>Gladlike i wile þe paniers bere;</p>
<p>Ich woth, ne shal it me nouth dere,</p>
<p>Þey þer be i<i>n</i>ne a birþene gret,</p>
<span class="linenum">808</span>
<p>Al so heui als a neth.</p>
<p>Shal ich neuere lengere dwelle,</p>
<p>To morwe<i>n</i> shal ich forth pelle.”</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag41" id="poem_note41" class="pginternal">41</a>
MS. ine.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK GOES TO LINCOLN TO GET WORK.</span></p>
<p class="section">
On þe morwen, hwa<i>n</i> it was day,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 208<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<span class="linenum">812</span>
<p>He stirt up sone, and nouth ne lay;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He carries a pannier full of fish,</p>
<p>And cast a panier on his bac,</p>
<p>With fish giueled als a stac;</p>
<p>Also michel he bar him one,</p>
<span class="linenum">816</span>
<p>So he foure, bi mine mone!<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note42" id="poem_tag42">42</a></p>
<p class="sidenote">
and sells them.</p>
<p>Wel he it bar, and solde it wel,</p>
<p>Þe siluer he brouthe hom il del;</p>
<p>Al þat he þer-fore tok</p>
<span class="linenum">820</span>
<p>With-held he nouth a ferþinges nok.</p>
<p>So yede he forth ilke day,</p>
<p>Þat he neuere at home lay.</p>
<span class="pagenum">26</span>
<a id="page26"> </a>
<p>So wolde he his mester lere;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A great dearth arises.</p>
<span class="linenum">824</span>
<p>Bifel it so a strong dere</p>
<p>Bigan to rise of korn of bred,</p>
<p>That grim ne couþe no god red,</p>
<p>Hw he sholde his meine fede;</p>
<span class="linenum">828</span>
<p>Of hauelok hauede he michel drede:</p>
<p>For he was strong, and wel mouthe ete</p>
<p>More þa<i>n</i>ne heuere mouthe he gete;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They have not enough to eat.</p>
<p>Ne he ne mouthe on þe se take</p>
<span class="linenum">832</span>
<p>Neyþer le<i>n</i>ge, ne þorn[e]bake,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note43" id="poem_tag43">43</a></p>
<p>Ne non oþer fish þat douthe</p>
<p>His meyne fede<i>n</i> with he[r]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note44" id="poem_tag44">44</a> mouthe.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Grim is sorry for Havelok.</p>
<p>Of hauelok he hauede kare,</p>
<span class="linenum">836</span>
<p>Hwilgat þat he micthe fare;</p>
<p>Of his childre<i>n</i> was him nouth,</p>
<p>On hauelok was al hise þouth,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote839" id="texttag839">839</a>
<p>And seyde, “hauelok, dere sone,</p>
<span class="linenum">840</span>
<p>I wene that we deye mone</p>
<p>For hu<i>n</i>ger, þis dere is so strong,</p>
<p>And hure mete is uten long.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He advises him to go to Lincoln,</p>
<p>Betere is þat þu he<i>n</i>ne gonge,</p>
<span class="linenum">844</span>
<p>Þan þu here dwelle longe;</p>
<p>Heþen þow mayt ga<i>n</i>ge<i>n</i> to late;</p>
<p>Thou canst ful wel þe ricthe gate</p>
<p>To lincolne, þe gode borw,</p>
<span class="linenum">848</span>
<p>Þou hauest it gon ful ofte þoru;</p>
<p>Of me ne is me nouth a slo,</p>
<p>Bet<i>er</i>e is þat þu þider go,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and work there.</p>
<p>For þer is mani god ma<i>n</i> inne,</p>
<span class="linenum">852</span>
<p>Þer þou mayt þi mete winne.</p>
<p>But wo is me! þou art so naked,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He makes him a coat of an old sail.</p>
<p>Of mi seyl y wolde þe were maked</p>
<p>A cloth, þou mithest i<i>n</i>ne gongen,</p>
<span class="linenum">856</span>
<p>Sone, no cold þat þu ne fonge.”</p>
<span class="pagenum">27</span>
<a id="page27"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HE HELPS THE EARL OF CORNWALL’S COOK.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 208<i>b</i>. col. 2.]</p>
<p class="section">
He tok þe sh[e]res<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note45" id="poem_tag45">45</a> of þe nayl,</p>
<p>And made him a couel of þe sayl,</p>
<p>And hauelok dide it sone on;</p>
<span class="linenum">860</span>
<p>Hauede neyþer hosen ne shon,</p>
<p>Ne none kines oþe[r] wede;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok goes to Lincoln barefoot.</p>
<p>To lincolne barfot he yede.</p>
<p>Hwan he kam þe[r], he was ful wil,</p>
<span class="linenum">864</span>
<p>Ne hauede he no frend to gange<i>n</i> til;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He fasts for two days.</p>
<p>Two dayes þer fastinde he yede,</p>
<p>Þat non for his werk wolde him fede;</p>
<p>Þe þridde day herde he calle:</p>
<span class="linenum">868</span>
<p>“Bermen, bermen, hider forth alle!”</p>
<p>[Poure þat on fote yede]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note46" id="poem_tag46">46</a></p>
<p>Sprongen forth so sparke on glede.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok becomes the earl’s cook’s porter.</p>
<p>Hauelok shof dun nyne or ten,</p>
<span class="linenum">872</span>
<p>Rith amidewarde þe fen,</p>
<p>And stirte forth to þe kok,</p>
<p>[Þer the herles mete he tok,]</p>
<p>Þat he bouthe at þe brigge:</p>
<span class="linenum">876</span>
<p>Þe berme<i>n</i> let he alle ligge,</p>
<p>And bar þe mete to þe castel,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He gets a farthing cake.</p>
<p>And gat him þere a ferþing wastel.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag42" id="poem_note42" class="pginternal">42</a>
Cf. ll. 1711, 1972.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag43" id="poem_note43" class="pginternal">43</a>
See l. 759.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag44" id="poem_note44" class="pginternal">44</a>
<i>Qu.</i> her, <i>i.e.</i> their. MS. he.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag45" id="poem_note45" class="pginternal">45</a>
<i>Qu.</i> sheres. MS. shres.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag46" id="poem_note46" class="pginternal">46</a>
Cf. ll. 91, 101. Here and below an additional line seems requisite.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK IS HIRED BY THE EARL’S COOK.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Another day, he watches the earl’s cook,</p>
<p class="section">
Þet oþer day kepte he ok</p>
<span class="linenum">880</span>
<p>Swiþe yerne þe erles kok,</p>
<p>Til þat he say him on þe b[r]igge,</p>
<p>And bi him mani fishes ligge.</p>
<p>Þe herles mete hauede he bouth</p>
<span class="linenum">884</span>
<p>Of cornwalie, and kalde oft:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
who calls for a porter.</p>
<p>“Berme<i>n</i>, bermen, hider swiþe!”</p>
<p>Hauelok it herde, and was ful bliþe,</p>
<p>Þat he herde “berme<i>n</i>” calle;</p>
<span class="linenum">888</span>
<p>Alle made he hem dun falle<span class="pagenum">28</span></p>
<a id="page28"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok upsets 16 lads.</p>
<p>Þat in his gate yeden and stode,</p>
<p>Wel sixtene laddes gode.</p>
<p>Als he lep þe kok [vn-]til,</p>
<span class="linenum">892</span>
<p>He shof hem alle upon an hyl;</p>
<p>Astirte til him with his rippe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He catches up the cook’s fish,</p>
<p>And bigan þe fish to kippe.</p>
<p>He bar up wel a carte lode</p>
<span class="linenum">896</span>
<p>Of segges, laxes, of playces brode,</p>
<p>Of grete laumprees, and of eles;</p>
<p>Sparede he neyþer tos ne heles,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and carries them to the castle.</p>
<p>Til þat he to þe castel cam,</p>
<span class="linenum">900</span>
<p>Þat me<i>n</i> fro him his birþene nam.</p>
<p>Þan me<i>n</i> hauede<i>n</i> holpe<i>n</i> him doun</p>
<p>With þe birþene of his croun,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote903" id="texttag903">903</a>
<p>Þe kok [bi] stod, and on him low,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 209, col. 1.]</p>
<span class="linenum">904</span>
<p>And þoute him stalworþe ma<i>n</i> ynow,</p>
<p>And seyde, “wiltu ben wit me?</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The cook takes him into his service.</p>
<p>Gladlike wile ich feden þe;</p>
<p>Wel is set þe mete þu etes,</p>
<span class="linenum">908</span>
<p>And þe hire þat þu getes.”</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HE CUTS WOOD, AND DRAWS WATER.</span></p>
<p class="section">
“Goddot!”<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note47" id="poem_tag47">47</a> quoth he, “leue sire,</p>
<p>Bidde ich you non oþer hire;</p>
<p>But yeueþ me inow to ete,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok tells the cook what he can do.</p>
<span class="linenum">912</span>
<p>Fir and wat<i>er</i> y wile yow fete,</p>
<p>Þe fir blowe, an ful wele make<i>n</i>;</p>
<p>Stickes kan ich breken and krake<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>And kindlen ful wel a fyr,</p>
<span class="linenum">916</span>
<p>And make<i>n</i> it to brennen shir;</p>
<p>Ful wel kan ich cleue<i>n</i> shides,</p>
<p>Eles to-turnen<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note48" id="poem_tag48">48</a> of here hides;</p>
<p>Ful wel kan ich dishes swilen,</p>
<span class="linenum">920</span>
<p>And don al þat ye eu<i>er</i>e wilen.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The cook is content to hire him.</p>
<p>Quoth þe kok, “wile i no more;</p>
<span class="pagenum">29</span>
<a id="page29"> </a>
<p>Go þu yunder, and sit þore,</p>
<p>And y shal yeue þe ful fair bred,</p>
<span class="linenum">924</span>
<p>And make þe broys i<i>n</i> þe led.</p>
<p>Sit now doun and et ful yerne:</p>
<p>Daþeit hwo þe mete werne!”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag47" id="poem_note47" class="pginternal">47</a>
Soddot, MS.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag48" id="poem_note48" class="pginternal">48</a>
MS. to turuen; <i>but the <b>u</b> and <b>n</b> are almost
indistinguishable.</i> Cf. l. 603; and <i>William of Palerne</i>,
2590.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK GROWS VERY TALL AND STRONG.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok eats a good dinner.</p>
<p class="section">
Hauelok sette him dun anon,</p>
<span class="linenum">928</span>
<p>Also stille als a ston,</p>
<p>Til he hauede ful wel eten;</p>
<p>Þo hauede hauelok fayre gete<i>n</i>.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He fills a large tub with water for the kitchen.</p>
<p>Hwa<i>n</i> he hauede ete<i>n</i> inow,</p>
<span class="linenum">932</span>
<p>He ka<i>m</i> to þe welle, wat<i>er</i> up-drow,</p>
<p>And filde þe[r] a michel so;</p>
<p>Bad he non ageyn him go,</p>
<p>But bi-twen his hondes he bar it in,</p>
<span class="linenum">936</span>
<p>A[l] him one to þe kichin.</p>
<p>Bad he non him wat<i>er</i> to fete,</p>
<p>Ne fro b[r]igge to bere þe mete,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote939" id="texttag939">939</a>
<p>He bar þe turues, he bar þe star,</p>
<span class="linenum">940</span>
<p>Þe wode fro the brigge he bar;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He draws water, and cuts wood.</p>
<p>Al that euere shulde<i>n</i> he nytte,</p>
<p>Al he drow, and al he citte;</p>
<p>Wolde he neu<i>er</i>e haue<i>n</i> rest,</p>
<span class="linenum">944</span>
<p>More þa<i>n</i> he were a best.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote945" id="texttag945">945</a>
<p class="sidenote">
He is always laughing and blithe.</p>
<p>Of alle me<i>n</i> was he mest meke,</p>
<p>Lauhwinde ay, and bliþe of speke;</p>
<p>Eu<i>er</i>e he was glad and bliþe,</p>
<span class="linenum">948</span>
<p>His sorwe he couþe ful wel miþe.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 209, col. 2.]</p>
<p>It ne was non so litel knaue,</p>
<p>For to leyken, ne forto plawe,</p>
<p>Þat he ne wo[l]de with him pleye:</p>
<span class="linenum">952</span>
<p>Þe children that y[e]de<i>n</i> in þe weie</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Children play with him.</p>
<p>Of him he deden al he[r] wille,</p>
<p>And with him leykeden here fille.</p>
<p>Him louede<i>n</i> alle, stille and bolde,</p>
<span class="linenum">956</span>
<p>Knictes, childre<i>n</i>, yunge and holde;<span class="pagenum">30</span></p>
<a id="page30"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
All like him.</p>
<p>Alle him louede<i>n</i> þat him sowen,</p>
<p>Boþen heyemen and lowe.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote959" id="texttag959">959</a>
<p>Of him ful wide þe word sprong,</p>
<span class="linenum">960</span>
<p>Hw he was mike, hw he was stro<i>n</i>g,</p>
<p>Hw fayr ma<i>n</i> god him hauede maked,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He has nothing to wear but the old sail.</p>
<p>But on þat he was almest naked:</p>
<p>For he ne hauede nouth to shride,</p>
<span class="linenum">964</span>
<p>But a kouel ful unride,</p>
<p>Þat [was] ful, and swiþe wicke,</p>
<p>Was it nouth worth a fir sticke.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The cook buys him new clothes.</p>
<p>Þe cok biga<i>n</i> of him to rewe,</p>
<span class="linenum">968</span>
<p>And bouthe him cloþes, al spa<i>n</i>newe;</p>
<p>He bouthe him boþe hose<i>n</i> and shon,</p>
<p>And sone dide him dones on.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He looks very well in his new suit.</p>
<p>Hwan he was cloþed, osed, and shod,</p>
<span class="linenum">972</span>
<p>Was no<i>n</i> so fayr under god,</p>
<p>Þat euere yete in erþe were,</p>
<p>Non þat eu<i>er</i>e moder bere;</p>
<p>It was neu<i>er</i>e ma<i>n</i> þat yemede</p>
<span class="linenum">976</span>
<p>In kinneriche, þat so wel semede</p>
<p>King or cayser forto be,</p>
<p>Þan he was shrid, so semede he;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok is the tallest man in Lincoln,</p>
<p>For þa<i>n</i>ne he weren alle same<i>n</i></p>
<span class="linenum">980</span>
<p>At lincolne, at þe game<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>And þe erles men woren al þore,</p>
<p>Þan was hauelok bi þe shuldre<i>n</i> more</p>
<p>Þan þe meste þat þer kam:</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote984" id="texttag984">984</a>
<p>In armes him noma<i>n</i> [ne] nam,</p>
<p>Þat he doune sone ne caste;</p>
<p>Hauelok stod ouer he<i>m</i> als a mast.</p>
<p>Als he was heie, al<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note49" id="poem_tag49">49</a> he was long,</p>
<span class="linenum">988</span>
<p>He was boþe stark and strong;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and the strongest in England.</p>
<p>In engelond [was] non hise per</p>
<p>Of strengþe þat eu<i>er</i>e ka<i>m</i> him ner.</p>
<p>Als he was strong, so was he softe;</p>
<span class="pagenum">31</span>
<a id="page31"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HE SEES SOME MEN “PUTTING THE STONE.”</span></p>
<span class="linenum">992</span>
<p>Þey a man him misdede ofte,</p>
<p>Neuere more he him misdede,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 209<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Ne hond on him with yuele leyde.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He is good-natured and pure.</p>
<p>Of bodi was he mayden clene,</p>
<span class="linenum">996</span>
<p>Neuere yete in game, ne in grene,</p>
<p>Þit<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note50" id="poem_tag50">50</a> hire ne wolde leyke ne lye,</p>
<p>No more þan it were a strie.</p>
<p>In þat time al hengelond</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich summons a parliament at Lincoln.</p>
<span class="linenum">1000</span>
<p>Þerl Godrich hauede in his hond,</p>
<p>And he gart kome<i>n</i> i<i>n</i>to þe tun</p>
<p>Mani erl, and mani barun;</p>
<p>And alle [men] þat liues were</p>
<span class="linenum">1004</span>
<p>In eng[e]lond, þa<i>n</i>ne wer þere,</p>
<p>Þat þey haueden after sent,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1006" id="texttag1006">1006</a>
<p>To ben þer at þe parleme<i>n</i>t.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Some champions begin to contend in games.</p>
<p>With hem com mani cha<i>n</i>bioun,</p>
<span class="linenum">1008</span>
<p>Mani with ladde, blac and brown;</p>
<p>An fel it so, þat yunge men,</p>
<p>Wel aboute<i>n</i> nine or ten,</p>
<p>Bigu<i>n</i>ne<i>n</i> þe[r] for to layke:</p>
<span class="linenum">1012</span>
<p>Þider kome<i>n</i> bothe stro<i>n</i>ge and wayke;</p>
<p>Þider kome<i>n</i> lesse and more,</p>
<p>Þat in þe borw þa<i>n</i>ne were<i>n</i> þore;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Strong lads and bondmen are there.</p>
<p>Chaunpiouns, and starke laddes,</p>
<span class="linenum">1016</span>
<p>Bondeme<i>n</i> with here gaddes,</p>
<p>Als he comen fro þe plow;</p>
<p>Þere was sembling i-now!</p>
<p>For it ne was non horse-knaue,</p>
<span class="linenum">1020</span>
<p>Þo þei sholden in honde haue,</p>
<p>Þat he ne kam þider, þe leyk to se:</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1022" id="texttag1022">1022</a>
<p>Biforn here fet þa<i>n</i>ne lay a tre,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They begin to “put the stone.”</p>
<p>And putte<i>n</i><a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note51" id="poem_tag51">51</a> with a mikel ston</p>
<span class="linenum">1024</span>
<p>Þe starke laddes, ful god won.<span class="pagenum">32</span></p>
<a id="page32"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HE “PUTS THE STONE” FURTHER THAN ANY.</span></p>
<p>Þe ston was mikel, and ek greth,</p>
<p>And al so heui so a neth;</p>
<p>Grund stalwrthe ma<i>n</i> he sholde be,</p>
<span class="linenum">1028</span>
<p>Þat mouthe lifte<i>n</i> it to his kne;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Few can lift it.</p>
<p>Was þer neyþer clerc, ne prest,</p>
<p>Þat mithe liften it to his brest:</p>
<p>Þerwit putte<i>n</i> the chaunpiouns,</p>
<span class="linenum">1032</span>
<p>Þat þider come<i>n</i> with þe barouns.</p>
<p>Hwo so mithe putten þore</p>
<p>Biforn a-noþer, an inch or more,</p>
<p>Wore ye yung, [or] wore he hold,</p>
<span class="linenum">1036</span>
<p>He was for a ke<i>m</i>pe told.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Whilst this is going on,</p>
<p>Al-so þe[i] stoden, an ofte starede<i>n</i>,<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note51a" id="poem_tag51a">*</a></p>
<p>Þe chaunpiouns, and ek the ladde<i>n</i>,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 209<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<p>And he maden mikel strout</p>
<span class="linenum">1040</span>
<p>Abouten þe alþerbeste but,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok looks on at them.</p>
<p>Hauelok stod, and lokede þer-til;</p>
<p>And of putti<i>n</i>gge he was ful wil,</p>
<p>For neu<i>er</i>e yete ne saw he or</p>
<span class="linenum">1044</span>
<p>Putte<i>n</i> the stone, or þa<i>n</i>ne þor.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
His master tells him to try.</p>
<p>Hise mayst<i>er</i> bad him gon þer-to,</p>
<p>Als he couþe þer-with do.</p>
<p>Þo hise mayst<i>er</i> it him bad,</p>
<span class="linenum">1048</span>
<p>He was of him sore adrad;</p>
<p>Þerto he stirte sone anon,</p>
<p>And kipte up þat heui ston,</p>
<p>Þat he sholde puten wiþe;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He puts the stone 12 feet beyond the rest.</p>
<span class="linenum">1052</span>
<p>He putte at þe firste siþe,</p>
<p>Ouer alle þat þer wore,</p>
<p>Twel fote, and su<i>m</i>del more.</p>
<p>Þe chaunpiouns þat [þat] put sowen,</p>
<span class="linenum">1056</span>
<p>Shuldrede<i>n</i> he ilc oþer, and lowen;</p>
<p>Wolde<i>n</i> he no more to putti<i>n</i>g gange,</p>
<p>But seyde, “<i>w</i>e<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note52" id="poem_tag52">52</a> dwelle<i>n</i> her to
longe!”</p>
<span class="pagenum">33</span>
<a id="page33"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODRICH HEARS OF HAVELOK’S STRENGTH.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
This feat is everywhere talked about.</p>
<p>Þis selkouth mithe nouth be<i>n</i> hyd,</p>
<span class="linenum">1060</span>
<p>Ful sone it was ful loude kid</p>
<p>Of hauelok, hw he warp þe ston</p>
<p>Ouer þe laddes euerilkon;</p>
<p>Hw he was fayr, hw he was long,</p>
<span class="linenum">1064</span>
<p>Hw he was with, hw he was stro<i>n</i>g;</p>
<p>Þoruth england yede þe speke,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note53" id="poem_tag53">53</a></p>
<p>Hw he was strong, and ek meke;</p>
<p>In the castel, up in þe halle,</p>
<span class="linenum">1068</span>
<p>Þe knithes speke<i>n</i> þer-of alle,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich hears the knights talking of it.</p>
<p>So that Godrich it herde wel</p>
<p>Þe[r] speke<i>n</i> of hauelok, eueri del,</p>
