<p>Hw he was stro<i>n</i>g man and hey,</p>
<span class="linenum">1072</span>
<p>Hw he was stro<i>n</i>g and ek fri,</p>
<p>And þouthte godrich, “þoru þis knaue</p>
<p>Shal ich engelond al haue,</p>
<p>And mi sone after me;</p>
<span class="linenum">1076</span>
<p>For so i wile þat it be.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1077" id="texttag1077">1077</a>
<p class="sidenote">
“Athelwold said I was to marry his daughter to the strongest man
alive.</p>
<p>The king aþelwald me dide swere</p>
<p>Vpon al þe messe-gere,</p>
<p>Þat y shu[l]de his douthe[r] yeue</p>
<span class="linenum">1080</span>
<p>Þe hexte <ins class="edcorr" title="word added by editor">[man]</ins> þat mithe liue,</p>
<p>Þe beste, þe fairest, þe stra<i>n</i>gest ok;</p>
<p>Þat gart he me swere<i>n</i> on þe bok.</p>
<p>Hwere mithe i finden ani so hey</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 210, col. 1.]</p>
<span class="linenum">1084</span>
<p>So hauelok is, or so sley?</p>
<p>Þou y southe heþen in-to ynde,</p>
<p>So fayr, so strong, ne mithe y finde.</p>
<p>Hauelok is þat ilke knaue,</p>
<span class="linenum">1088</span>
<p>Þat shal goldeborw haue.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
That is Havelok.”</p>
<p>Þis þouthe [he] with trechery,</p>
<p>With t<i>ra</i>ysoun, and wit felony;</p>
<p>For he wende, þat hauelok wore</p>
<span class="linenum">1092</span>
<p>Sum cherles sone, and no more;</p>
<p>Ne shulde he haue<i>n</i> of engellond</p>
<span class="pagenum">34</span>
<a id="page34"> </a>
<p>Onlepi forw in his hond,</p>
<p>With hire, þat was þerof eyr,</p>
<span class="linenum">1096</span>
<p>Þat boþe was god and swiþe fair.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He thought Havelok was only a thrall.</p>
<p>He wende, þat hauelok wer a þral,</p>
<p>Þer-þoru he we<i>n</i>de haue<i>n</i> al</p>
<p>In engelond, þat hire rith was;</p>
<span class="linenum">1100</span>
<p>He was werse þan sathanas,</p>
<p>Þat ih<i>es</i>u c<i>ri</i>st in erþe shop:<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note54" id="poem_tag54">54</a></p>
<p>Hanged worþe he on an hok!</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag49" id="poem_note49" class="pginternal">49</a>
<i>Qu.</i> so; see l. 991.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag50" id="poem_note50" class="pginternal">50</a>
<i>Qu.</i> wit = with: miswritten owing to confusion of þ with ƿ
(<i>w</i>)?</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag51" id="poem_note51" class="pginternal">51</a>
MS. pulten. But see ll. 1031, 1033, 1044, 1051, &amp;c.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag51a" id="poem_note51a" class="pginternal">*</a>
For <i>stareden</i> we should perhaps read <i>stradden</i>; see the
<a href="#gloss_Stareden" class="pginternal">Glossary</a>.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag52" id="poem_note52" class="pginternal">52</a>
In the former edition— “ye”. But the <i>y</i> is not dotted, and
it may be “ƿe.”</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag53" id="poem_note53" class="pginternal">53</a>
MS. speche. Read “speke,” as in l. 946.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag54" id="poem_note54" class="pginternal">54</a>
<i>Qu.</i> shok <i>or</i> strok.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GOLDBOROUGH IS SENT FOR TO LINCOLN.</span></p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1103" id="texttag1103">1103</a>
<p class="sidenote">
He sends for Goldborough to Lincoln.</p>
<p class="section">
After goldebo[r]w sone he sende,</p>
<span class="linenum">1104</span>
<p>Þat was boþe fayr and hende,</p>
<p>And dide hire to lincolne bringe,</p>
<p>Belles dede he ageyn hire ringen,</p>
<p>And ioie he made hire swiþe mikel,</p>
<span class="linenum">1108</span>
<p>But neþeles he was ful swikel.</p>
<p>He seyde, þat he sholde hire yeue</p>
<p>Þe fayrest ma<i>n</i> that mithe liue.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
She says she will marry none but a king.</p>
<p>She answerede, and seyde anon,</p>
<span class="linenum">1112</span>
<p>Bi crist, and bi seint iohan,</p>
<p>Þat hire sholde noma<i>n</i> wedde,</p>
<p>Ne noma<i>n</i> bringen to hire<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note55" id="poem_tag55">55</a> bedde,</p>
<p>But he were king, or ki<i>n</i>ges eyr,</p>
<span class="linenum">1116</span>
<p>Were he neuere ma<i>n</i> so fayr.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag55" id="poem_note55" class="pginternal">55</a>
<i>Qu.</i> hise.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODRICH ASKS HAVELOK IF HE WILL MARRY.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich is wrath at this.</p>
<p class="section">
Godrich þe erl was swiþe wroth,</p>
<p>Þat she swore swilk an oth,</p>
<p>And seyde, “hwor þou wilt be</p>
<span class="linenum">1120</span>
<p>Quen and leuedi ouer me?</p>
<p>Þou shalt haue<i>n</i> a gadeling,</p>
<p>Ne shalt þou haue<i>n</i> non oþer ki<i>n</i>g;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He says she shall marry his cook’s servant.</p>
<p>Þe shal spusen mi cokes knaue,</p>
<span class="linenum">1124</span>
<p>Ne shalt þou non oþer lou<i>er</i>d haue.</p>
<p>Daþeit þat þe oþer yeue</p>
<p>Eu<i>er</i>e more hwil i liue!</p>
<span class="pagenum">35</span>
<a id="page35"> </a>
<p>To-mo[r]we ye shole<i>n</i> ben weddeth,</p>
<span class="linenum">1128</span>
<p>And, maugre þin, to-gidere beddeth.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 210, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Goldeborw gret, and <i>w</i>as<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note56" id="poem_tag56">56</a> hire ille,</p>
<p>She wolde ben ded bi hire wille.</p>
<p>On the morwen, hwa<i>n</i> day was spru<i>n</i>ge<i>n</i>,</p>
<span class="linenum">1132</span>
<p>And day-belle at kirke rungen,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He sends next day for Havelok, and says,</p>
<p>After hauelok sente þat iudas,</p>
<p>Þat werse was þa<i>n</i>ne sathanas:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“Master, wilt wive?”</p>
<p>And seyde, “mayst<i>er</i>, wilte wif?”</p>
<span class="linenum">1136</span>
<p>“Nay,” quoth, hauelok, “bi my lif!</p>
<p>Hwat sholde ich with wif do?</p>
<p>I ne may hire fede, ne cloþe, ne sho.</p>
<p>Wider sholde ich wimma<i>n</i> bringe?</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok refuses.</p>
<span class="linenum">1140</span>
<p>I ne haue none kines þinge.</p>
<p>I ne haue hws, y ne haue cote,</p>
<p>Ne i ne<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note57" id="poem_tag57">57</a> haue stikke, y ne haue sprote,</p>
<p>I ne haue neyþer bred ne sowel,</p>
<span class="linenum">1144</span>
<p>Ne cloth, but of an hold with couel.</p>
<p>Þis cloþes, þat ich onne haue,</p>
<p>Aren þe kokes, and ich his knaue.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich beats him, and threatens to hang him.</p>
<p>Godrich stirt up, and on him dong</p>
<span class="linenum">1148</span>
<p>[With dintes swiþe hard and strong,]</p>
<p>And seyde, “But þou hire take,</p>
<p>Þat y wole yeue<i>n</i> þe to make,</p>
<p>I shal hange<i>n</i> þe ful heye,</p>
<span class="linenum">1152</span>
<p>Or y shal þristen vth þin heie.”</p>
<p>Hauelok was one, and was odrat,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok consents.</p>
<p>And grauntede hi<i>m</i> al þat he bad.</p>
<p>Þo sende he after hire sone,</p>
<span class="linenum">1156</span>
<p>Þe fayrest wymman under mone;</p>
<p>And seyde til hire, [false]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note58" id="poem_tag58">58</a> and slike,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich next threatens Goldborough.</p>
<p>Þat wicke þral, þat foule swike:</p>
<p>“But þu þis ma<i>n</i> under-stonde,</p>
<span class="pagenum">36</span>
<a id="page36"> </a>
<span class="linenum">1160</span>
<p>I shal fleme<i>n</i> þe of londe;</p>
<p>Or þou shal to þe galwes re<i>n</i>ne,</p>
<p>And þer þou shalt i<i>n</i> a fir bre<i>n</i>ne.”</p>
<p>Sho was adrad, for he so þrette,</p>
<span class="linenum">1164</span>
<p>And durste nouth þe spusi<i>n</i>g lette,</p>
<p>But þey hire likede swiþe ille,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
She consents, thinking it is God’s will.</p>
<p>Þouthe it was godes wille:</p>
<p>God, þat makes to growe<i>n</i> þe korn,</p>
<span class="linenum">1168</span>
<p>Formede hire wi<i>m</i>ma<i>n</i> to be born.</p>
<p>Hwan he hauede don him for drede,</p>
<p>Þat he sholde hire spuse<i>n</i>, and fede,</p>
<p>And þat she sholde til him holde,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A dowry is given her.</p>
<span class="linenum">1172</span>
<p>Þer were<i>n</i> penies þicke tolde,</p>
<p>Mikel plente upon þe bok:</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1174" id="texttag1174">1174</a>
<p>He ys hire yaf, and she as tok.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 210<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p>He weren spused fayre and wel,</p>
<span class="linenum">1176</span>
<p>Þe messe he deden eueridel,</p>
<p>Þat fel to spusing, and god cle[r]k,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The archbishop of York marries them.</p>
<p>Þe erchebishop uth of yerk,</p>
<p>Þat kam to þe p<i>ar</i>leme<i>n</i>t,</p>
<span class="linenum">1180</span>
<p>Als god him hauede þider sent.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag56" id="poem_note56" class="pginternal">56</a>
The first letter of this word is either <i>þ</i> or a Saxon <i>w</i>
(ƿ). I read it as the latter.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag57" id="poem_note57" class="pginternal">57</a>
MS. ine.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag58" id="poem_note58" class="pginternal">58</a>
Both sense and metre require this word.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK RESOLVES TO GO TO GRIMSBY.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Hwan he were<i>n</i> togydere i<i>n</i> godes lawe,</p>
<p>Þat þe folc ful wel it sawe,</p>
<p>He ne wiste<i>n</i> hwat he mouthe<i>n</i>,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok knows not what to do.</p>
<span class="linenum">1184</span>
<p>Ne he ne wiste<i>n</i> wat hem douthe;</p>
<p>Þer to dwelle<i>n</i>, or þe<i>n</i>ne to gonge,</p>
<p>Þer ne wolde<i>n</i> he dwelle<i>n</i> lo<i>n</i>ge,</p>
<p>For he wiste<i>n</i>, and ful wel sawe,</p>
<span class="linenum">1188</span>
<p>Þat godrich he<i>m</i> hatede, þe deuel hi<i>m</i> hawe!</p>
<p>And yf he dwellede<i>n</i> þer outh—</p>
<p>Þat fel hauelok ful wel on þouth—</p>
<p>Men sholde don his leman shame,</p>
<span class="linenum">1192</span>
<p>Or elles bringe<i>n</i> i<i>n</i> wicke blame.</p>
<p>Þat were him leuere to ben ded,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He determines to go to Grimsby.</p>
<p>For-þi he toke<i>n</i> anoþer red,</p>
<span class="pagenum">37</span>
<a id="page37"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GRIM’S CHILDREN WELCOME HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p>Þat þei sholden þenne fle</p>
<span class="linenum">1196</span>
<p>Til grim, and til hise sones þre;</p>
<p>Þer we<i>n</i>den he alþer-best to spede,</p>
<p>Hem forto cloþe, and for to fede.</p>
<p>Þe lond he token under fote,</p>
<span class="linenum">1200</span>
<p>Ne wisten he non oþer bote,</p>
<p>And helden ay the riþe [sti]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note59" id="poem_tag59">59</a></p>
<p>Til he kome<i>n</i> to grimesby.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1203" id="texttag1203">1203</a>
<p class="sidenote">
He finds that Grim is dead, but his five children are alive.</p>
<p>Þanne he kome<i>n</i> þere, þanne was gri<i>m</i> ded,</p>
<span class="linenum">1204</span>
<p>Of him ne hauede<i>n</i> he no red;</p>
<p>But hise childre<i>n</i> alle fyue</p>
<p>Alle weren yet on liue;</p>
<p>Þat ful fayre ayen he<i>m</i> neme,</p>
<span class="linenum">1208</span>
<p>Hwan he wiste<i>n</i> þat he keme,</p>
<p>And made<i>n</i> ioie swiþe mikel,</p>
<p>Ne were<i>n</i> he neuere ayen he<i>m</i> fikel.</p>
<p>On knes ful fayre he hem setten,</p>
<span class="linenum">1212</span>
<p>And hauelok swiþe fayre grette<i>n</i>,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They welcome Havelok very kindly.</p>
<p>And seyde<i>n</i>, “welkome, louerd dere!</p>
<p>And welkome be þi fayre fere!</p>
<p>Blessed be þat ilke þrawe,</p>
<span class="linenum">1216</span>
<p>Þat þou hire toke in godes lawe!</p>
<p>Wel is hus we sen þe on lyue,</p>
<p>Þou mithe us boþe selle and yeue;</p>
<p>Þou mayt us boþe yeue and selle,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 210<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<span class="linenum">1220</span>
<p>With þat þou wilt here dwelle.</p>
<p>We haue<i>n</i>, louerd, alle gode,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They beg him to stay with them.</p>
<p>Hors, and neth, and ship on flode,</p>
<p>Gold, and siluer, and michel auchte,</p>
<span class="linenum">1224</span>
<p>Þat grim ute fader us bitawchte.</p>
<p>Gold, and siluer, and oþer fe</p>
<p>Bad he us bi-taken þe.</p>
<p>We hauen shep, we hauen swin,</p>
<span class="linenum">1228</span>
<p>Bi-leue her, louerd, and al be þin;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They will serve him and his wife.</p>
<p>Þo shalt ben louerd, þou shalt be<i>n</i> syre,</p>
<span class="pagenum">38</span>
<a id="page38"> </a>
<p>And we sholen s<i>er</i>ue<i>n</i> þe and hire;</p>
<p>And hure sistres shole<i>n</i> do</p>
<span class="linenum">1232</span>
<p>Al that euere biddes sho;</p>
<p>He shole<i>n</i> hire cloþe<i>n</i>,<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note59a" id="poem_tag59a">*</a>
washe<i>n</i>, and wri<i>n</i>ge<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>And to hondes water bringe<i>n</i>;</p>
<p>He sholen bedde<i>n</i> hire and þe,</p>
<span class="linenum">1236</span>
<p>For leuedi wile we þat she be.”</p>
<p>Hwan he þis ioie hauede<i>n</i> maked,</p>
<p>Sithen stikes broke<i>n</i> and kraked,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They make a fire, and spare neither goose nor hen.</p>
<p>And þe fir brouth on bre<i>n</i>ne,</p>
<span class="linenum">1240</span>
<p>Ne was þer spared gos ne he<i>n</i>ne,</p>
<p>Ne þe hende, ne þe drake,</p>
<p>Mete he deden plente make;</p>
<p>Ne wantede þere no god mete,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They fetch wine and ale.</p>
<span class="linenum">1244</span>
<p>Wyn and ale dede<i>n</i> he fete,</p>
<p>And made[n] hem [ful] glade and bliþe,</p>
<p>Wesseyl ledde<i>n</i> he fele siþe.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag59" id="poem_note59" class="pginternal">59</a>
A word is here erased; but see l. 2618.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag59a" id="poem_note59a" class="pginternal">*</a>
Mr Garnett suggested that <i>cloþen</i> may mean <i>clothes</i>. If so,
dele the comma after it.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GOLDBOROUGH SEES THE WONDROUS LIGHT.</span></p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1247" id="texttag1247">1247</a>
<p class="sidenote">
At night Goldborough lies down sorrowful.</p>
<p class="section">
On þe nith, als goldeborw lay,</p>
<span class="linenum">1248</span>
<p>Sory and sorwful was she ay,</p>
<p>For she wende she were bi-swike,</p>
<p>Þat sh[e w]ere<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note60" id="poem_tag60">60</a> yeue<i>n</i> un-kyndelike.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
She sees a great light.</p>
<p>O nith saw she þer-inne a lith,</p>
<span class="linenum">1252</span>
<p>A swiþe fayr, a swiþe bryth,</p>
<p>Al so brith, al so shir,</p>
<p>So it were a blase of fir.</p>
<p>She lokede no[r]þ,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note61" id="poem_tag61">61</a> and ek south,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
It comes out of Havelok’s mouth.</p>
<span class="linenum">1256</span>
<p>And saw it come<i>n</i> ut of his mouth,</p>
<p>Þat lay bi hire in þe bed:</p>
<p>No ferlike þou she were adred.</p>
<p>Þouthe she, “wat may this bi-mene!</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote1260" id="texttag1260">1260</a>
<p>He beth heyma<i>n</i> yet, als y wene,</p>
<p>He beth heyma<i>n</i> er he be ded:”—</p>
<p class="sidenote">
She sees a red cross on his shoulder, and hears an angel, saying,</p>
<p>On hise shuldre, of gold red</p>
<p>She saw a swiþe noble croiz,</p>
<span class="pagenum">39</span>
<a id="page39"> </a>
<span class="linenum">1264</span>
<p>Of an angel she herde a uoyz:</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag60" id="poem_note60" class="pginternal">60</a>
MS. shere, <i>evidently miswritten for</i> she were.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag61" id="poem_note61" class="pginternal">61</a>
MS. noþ.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK TELLS HER HIS STRANGE DREAM.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 211, col. 1.]<br>
“Goldborough, be not sad.</p>
<p class="section">
“Goldeborw, lat þi sorwe be,</p>
<p>For hauelok, þat haueþ spuset þe,</p>
<p>He<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note62" id="poem_tag62">62</a> kinges sone, and ki<i>n</i>ges eyr,</p>
<span class="linenum">1268</span>
<p>Þat bike<i>n</i>neth þat croiz so fayr.</p>
<p>It<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note63" id="poem_tag63">63</a> bikenneth more, þat he shal</p>
<p>Denemark haue<i>n</i>, and englond al;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok shall be a king,</p>
<p>He shal ben king strong and stark</p>
<span class="linenum">1272</span>
<p>Of engelond and denemark;</p>
<p>Þat shal þu wit þin eyne sen,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and thou, queen.”</p>
<p>And þo shalt quen and leuedi ben!”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag62" id="poem_note62" class="pginternal">62</a>
<i>Qu.</i> Is.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag63" id="poem_note63" class="pginternal">63</a>
MS. Iit.</p>
</div>
<p class="section">
Þanne she hauede herd the steuene</p>
<span class="linenum">1276</span>
<p>Of þe angel uth of heuene,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
She rejoices, and kisses Havelok.</p>
<p>She was so fele siþes blithe,</p>
<p>Þat she ne mithe hire ioie mythe;</p>
<p>But hauelok sone anon she kiste,</p>
<span class="linenum">1280</span>
<p>And he slep, and nouth ne wiste.</p>
<p>Hwan þat aungel hauede seyd,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He awakes, and says he has had a dream.</p>
<p>Of his slep a-non he brayd,</p>
<p>And seide, “le<i>m</i>ma<i>n</i>, slepes þou?</p>
<span class="linenum">1284</span>
<p>A selkuth drem dremede me nou.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
SHE SAYS HAVELOK WILL BE A GREAT KING.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Herkne nou hwat me haueth met:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He dreamt he was on a high hill in Denmark,</p>
<p>Me þouthe y was i<i>n</i> denemark set,</p>
<p>But on on þe moste hil</p>
<span class="linenum">1288</span>
<p>Þat eu<i>er</i>e yete kam i til.</p>
<p>It was so hey, þat y wel mouthe</p>
<p>Al þe werd se, als me þouthe.</p>
<p>Als i sat up-on þat lowe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and began to possess all that country.</p>
<span class="linenum">1292</span>
<p>I bigan denemark for to awe,</p>
<p>Þe borwes, and þe castles stronge;</p>
<p>And mine armes were<i>n</i> so lo<i>n</i>ge,</p>
<p>That i fadmede, al at ones,</p>
<span class="pagenum">40</span>
<a id="page40"> </a>
<span class="linenum">1296</span>
<p>denemark, with mine lo<i>n</i>ge bones;</p>
<p>And þa<i>n</i>ne y wolde mine armes drawe</p>
<p>Til me, and hom for to haue,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
All things in Denmark cleaved to his arms.</p>
<p>Al that euere in denemark liueden</p>
<span class="linenum">1300</span>
<p>On mine armes faste clyueden;</p>
<p>And þe stronge castles alle</p>
<p>On knes bigunne<i>n</i> for to falle,</p>
<p>Þe keyes felle<i>n</i> at mine fet:—</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He also dreamt that he went to England,</p>
<span class="linenum">1304</span>
<p>Anoþer drem dremede me ek,</p>
<p>Þat ich fley ouer þe salte se</p>
<p>Til engeland, and al with me</p>
<p>Þat euere was in denemark lyues,</p>
<span class="linenum">1308</span>
<p>But bo<i>n</i>deme<i>n</i>, and here wiues,</p>
<p>And þat ich kom til engelond,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 211, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Al closede it i<i>n</i>til min hond,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and that became his too.</p>
<p>And, goldeborw, y gaf [it] þe:—</p>
<span class="linenum">1312</span>
<p>Deus! le<i>m</i>man, hwat may þis be?”</p>
<p>Sho answerede, and seyde sone:</p>
<p>“Ih<i>es</i>u c<i>ri</i>st, þat made mone,</p>
<p>Þine dremes t<i>ur</i>ne to ioye;</p>
<span class="linenum">1316</span>
<p>Þat wite þw that sittes i<i>n</i> trone!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
She says, he will be king of England and Denmark.</p>
<p>Ne non stro<i>n</i>g ki<i>n</i>g, ne caysere,</p>
<p>So þou shalt be, fo[r] þou shalt bere</p>
<p>In engelond corune yet;</p>
<span class="linenum">1320</span>
<p>Denemark shal knele to þi fet;</p>
<p>Alle þe castles þat aren þer-inne,</p>
<p>Shal-tow, le<i>m</i>man, ful wel winne.</p>
<p>I woth, so wel so ich it sowe,</p>
<span class="linenum">1324</span>
<p>To þe shole come<i>n</i> heye and lowe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“All men in Denmark shall come to thee.</p>
<p>And alle þat in denemark wone,</p>
<p>Em and broþer, fader and sone,</p>
<p>Erl and baroun, dreng an kayn,</p>
<span class="linenum">1328</span>
<p>Knithes, and burgeys, and sweyn;</p>
<p>And mad king heyelike and wel,</p>
<p>Denemark shal be þin euere-ilc del.</p>
<span class="pagenum">41</span>
<a id="page41"> </a>
<p>Haue þou nouth þer-offe douthe</p>
<span class="linenum">1332</span>
<p>Nouth þe worth, of one nouthe;</p>
<p>Þer-offe with-i<i>n</i>ne þe firste yer</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Thou shalt be king within the year.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1334" id="texttag1334">1334</a>
<p>Shalt þou ben king, of euere-il del.</p>
<p>But do nou als y wile rathe,</p>
<span class="linenum">1336</span>
<p>Nim in with þe to denema[r]k baþe,</p>
<p>And do þou nouth onfrest þis fare,</p>
<p>Lith and selthe felawes are.</p>
<p>For shal ich neuere bliþe be</p>
<span class="linenum">1340</span>
<p>Til i with eyen denemark se;</p>
<p>For ich woth, þat al þe lond</p>
<p>Shalt þou haue<i>n</i> in þin hon[d].</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Pray Grim’s sons to go with you to Denmark.</p>
<p>Prey grimes sones alle þre,</p>
<span class="linenum">1344</span>
<p>That he wenden forþ with þe;</p>
<p>I wot, he wilen þe nouth werne,</p>
<p>With þe wende shule<i>n</i> he yerne,</p>
<p>For he loue<i>n</i> þe herte-like,</p>
<span class="linenum">1348</span>
<p>Þou maght til he aren quike,</p>
<p>Hwore so he o worde aren;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Go at once.</p>
<p>Þere ship þou do he<i>m</i> swithe yare<i>n</i>,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Delays are dangerous.”</p>
<p>And loke þat þou dwelle<i>n</i> nouth:</p>
<span class="linenum">1352</span>
<p>Dwelling haueth ofte scaþe wrouth.”</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK PRAYS FOR VENGEANCE ON GODARD.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Hwan Hauelok herde þat she radde,</p>
<p>Sone it was day, sone he him cladde,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 211<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p>And sone to þe kirke yede,</p>
<span class="linenum">1356</span>
<p>Or he dide ani oþer dede,</p>
<p>And bifor þe rode biga<i>n</i> falle,</p>
<p>Croiz and crist bi[gan] to kalle,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok prays for success,</p>
<p>And seyde, “louerd, þat al weldes,</p>
<span class="linenum">1360</span>
<p>Wind and wat<i>er</i>, wodes and feldes,</p>
<p>For the holi milce of you,</p>
<p>Haue m<i>er</i>ci of me, louerd, nou!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and for vengeance on his foe,</p>
<p>And wreke me yet on mi fo,</p>
<span class="linenum">1364</span>
<p>Þat ich saw biforn min eyne slo</p>
<p>Mine sistres, with a knif,</p>
<span class="pagenum">42</span>
<a id="page42"> </a>
<p>And siþen wolde me mi lyf</p>
<p>Haue reft, for in the [depe] se</p>
<span class="linenum">1368</span>
<p>Bad he grim haue drenched me.</p>
<p>He [hath] mi lond with mikel vn-Rith,</p>
<p>With michel wro<i>n</i>g, with mikel plith,</p>
<p>For i ne<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note64" id="poem_tag64">64</a> misdede him neu<i>er</i>e nouth,</p>
<span class="linenum">1372</span>
<p>And haued me to sorwe brouth.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
who had caused him to be a beggar.</p>
<p>He haueth me do mi mete to þigge,</p>
<p>And ofte in sorwe and pine ligge.</p>
<p>Louerd, haue m<i>er</i>ci of me,</p>
<span class="linenum">1376</span>
<p>And late [me] wel passe þe se,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He prays for a fair passage across the sea.</p>
<p>Þat <ins class="mycorr" title="see Glossary">ihc</ins> haue
ther-offe douthe and kare,</p>
<p>With-uten stormes ouer-fare,</p>
<p>Þat y ne dre<i>n</i>ched [be] þer-ine,</p>
<span class="linenum">1380</span>
<p>Ne forfaren for no sinne.</p>
<p>And bringge me wel to þe lond,</p>
<p>Þat godard haldes in his hond;</p>
<p>Þat is mi Rith, eueri del:</p>
<span class="linenum">1384</span>
<p>Ih<i>es</i>u c<i>ri</i>st, þou wost it wel!”</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag64" id="poem_note64" class="pginternal">64</a>
MS. ine.</p>
<p class="section">
Þa<i>n</i>ne he hauede his bede seyd,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He leaves his offering on the altar.</p>
<p>His offrende on þe auter leyd,</p>
<p>His leue at ih<i>es</i>u c<i>ri</i>st he tok,</p>
<span class="linenum">1388</span>
<p>And at his suete moder ok,</p>
<p>And at þe croiz, þat he biforn lay,</p>
<p>Siþe<i>n</i> yede sore grotinde awey.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK ADDRESSES GRIM’S THREE SONS.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
He finds Grim’s sons ready to fish.</p>
<p class="section">
Hwa<i>n</i><a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note65" id="poem_tag65">65</a> he com hom, he wore yare,</p>
<span class="linenum">1392</span>
<p>Grimes sones, forto fare</p>
<p>In-to þe se, fishes to gete,</p>
<p>Þat hauelok mithe wel of ete.</p>
<p>But auelok þouthe al anoþer,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok calls Grim’s three sons.</p>
<span class="linenum">1396</span>
<p>First he ka[l]de þe heldeste broþer,</p>
<p>Roberd þe rede, bi his name,</p>
<span class="pagenum">43</span>
<a id="page43"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HE ASKS THEM TO GO WITH HIM TO DENMARK.</span></p>
<p>Wiliam wenduth, and h[uwe r]aue<i>n</i>,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note66" id="poem_tag66">66</a></p>
<p>Grimes sones alle þre,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 211<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<span class="linenum">1400</span>
<p>And sey[d]e, “liþes nou alle to me,</p>
<p>Lou<i>er</i>dinges, ich wile you sheue,</p>
<p>A þing of me þat ye wel knewe.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He says, “My father was king of Denmark.</p>
<p>Mi fader was king of denshe lond,</p>
<span class="linenum">1404</span>
<p>Denemark was al in his hond</p>
<p>Þe day þat he was quik and ded;</p>
<p>But þa<i>n</i>ne hauede he wicke red,</p>
<p>Þat he me, and denemark al,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He left me and my sisters in charge of a foul fiend,</p>
<span class="linenum">1408</span>
<p>And mine sistres bi-tawte a þral:</p>
<p>A deueles lime [he] hus bitawte,</p>
<p>And al his lond, and al hise authe.</p>
<p>For y saw that fule fend</p>
<span class="linenum">1412</span>
<p>Mine sistres slo with hise hend;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
who slew my sisters,</p>
<p>First he shar a-two here þrotes,</p>
<p>And siþen [karf] hem al to grotes,</p>
<p>And siþen bad [he] in þe se</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and bade Grim drown me.</p>
<span class="linenum">1416</span>
<p>Grim, youre fader, dre<i>n</i>che<i>n</i> me.</p>
<p>Deplike dede he him swere</p>
<p>On bok, þat he sholde me bere</p>
<p>Vnto þe se, an dre<i>n</i>chen ine,</p>
<span class="linenum">1420</span>
<p>And <ins class="edcorr" title="word added by editor">[he]</ins>
wolde take<i>n</i> on him þe sinne.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
But Grim was wise.</p>
<p>But grim was wis, and swiþe hende,</p>
<p>Wolde he nouth his soule shende;</p>
<p>Leuere was him to be for-sworen,</p>
<span class="linenum">1424</span>
<p>Þan dre<i>n</i>chen me, and ben for-lorn;</p>
<p>But sone biga<i>n</i> he forto fle</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He fled from Denmark with me,</p>
<p>Fro denemark, forto ber<i>w</i>en<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note67" id="poem_tag67">67</a> me,</p>
<p>For yif<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note68" id="poem_tag68">68</a> ich hauede þer ben fu<i>n</i>den,</p>
<span class="linenum">1428</span>
<p>Hauede ben slayn, or harde bunde<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>And heye ben henged on a tre,</p>
<span class="pagenum">44</span>
<a id="page44"> </a>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1430" id="texttag1430">1430</a>
<p>Hauede go for him gold ne fe.</p>
<p>For-þi fro denemark hider he fledde,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and took care of me.</p>
<span class="linenum">1432</span>
<p>And me ful fayre and ful wel fedde,</p>
<p>So þat vn-to þis [ilke] day,</p>
<p>Haue ich ben fed and fostred ay.</p>
<p>But nou ich am up to þat helde</p>
<span class="linenum">1436</span>
<p>Cume<i>n</i>, that ich may wepne welde,</p>
<p>And y may grete di<i>n</i>tes yeue,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
And now, I must go to Denmark.</p>
<p>Shal i neuere hwil ich lyue</p>
<p>Ben glad, til that ich denemark se;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Go with me, and I will make you rich men.”</p>
<span class="linenum">1440</span>
<p>I preie you þat ye wende with me,</p>
<p>And ich may mak you riche men,</p>
<p>Ilk of you shal haue castles ten,</p>
<p>And þe lond þat þor-til longes,</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote1444" id="texttag1444">1444</a>
<p>Borwes, tunes, wodes and wo<i>n</i>ges.”<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note69" id="poem_tag69">69</a></p>
<p><span class="gap"> *****</span></p>
<p><span class="gap"> *****</span></p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag65" id="poem_note65" class="pginternal">65</a>
In the MS. the Capital letter is prefixed to the next line.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag66" id="poem_note66" class="pginternal">66</a>
MS. haue<i>n</i>. Cf. ll. 1868, 2528. Only an assonance, not a rime,
seems intended.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag67" id="poem_note67" class="pginternal">67</a>
MS. berƿen, <i>the A.S. <b>w</b> being used here</i>. Cf.
l. 697.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag68" id="poem_note68" class="pginternal">68</a>
MS. yif.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag69" id="poem_note69" class="pginternal">69</a>
A folio has here been cut out of the MS., containing 180 lines. The
missing portion must have been to this effect. “To this they gladly
assented; and Havelok, accompanied by his wife Goldeborw and the sons of
Grim, set sail for Denmark. Disembarking, they travel till they reach
the castle of a great Danish earl, named Ubbe, who had formerly been a
close friend to king Birkabeyn. Havelok begs that he will allow him to
live in that part of the country, and to gain a livelihood by
trading.”</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK GIVES UBBE A GOLD RING.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 212, col. 1.]</p>
<p>“With swilk als ich byen shal:</p>
<p>Þer-of bi-seche you nou leue;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok asks Ubbe to give him leave to buy and sell there.</p>
<p>Wile ich speke with non oþer reue,</p>
<span class="linenum">1628</span>
<p>But with þe, þat iustise are,</p>
<p>Þat y mithe seke<i>n</i><a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note70" id="poem_tag70">70</a> mi ware</p>
<p>In gode borwes up and doun,</p>
<p>And faren ich wile fro tun to tun.”</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote1632" id="texttag1632">1632</a>
<p>A gold ring drow he forth anon,</p>
<p>An hundred pu<i>n</i>d was worth þe ston,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He gives Ubbe a gold ring.</p>
<p>And yaf it ubbe for to spede:—</p>
<p>He was ful wis þat first yaf mede,</p>
<span class="linenum">1636</span>
<p>And so was hauelok ful wis here,<span class="pagenum">45</span></p>
<a id="page45"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
Dearly he sells it, all the same.</p>
<p>He solde his gold ring ful dere,</p>
<p>Was neuere non so dere sold,</p>
<p>For chapme<i>n</i>, neyþer yung ne old:</p>
<span class="linenum">1640</span>
<p>Þat sholen<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note71" id="poem_tag71">71</a> ye forthward ful wel here<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>Yif þat ye wile þe storie heren.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag70" id="poem_note70" class="pginternal">70</a>
<i>Qu.</i> sellen.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag71" id="poem_note71" class="pginternal">71</a>
MS. shoren.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
UBBE INVITES HAVELOK TO A FEAST.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe takes the ring,</p>
<p class="section">
Hwa<i>n</i> ubbe hauede þe gold ring,</p>
<p>Hauede he youenet for no þing,</p>
<span class="linenum">1644</span>
<p>Nouth for þe borw euere-il del:—</p>
<p>Hauelok bi-hel he swiþe wel,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1646" id="texttag1646">1646</a>
<p class="sidenote">
admires Havelok’s make and strength,</p>
<p>Hw he was wel of bones maked,</p>
<p>Brod in þe sholdres, ful wel schaped,</p>
<span class="linenum">1648</span>
<p>Þicke in þe brest, of bodi long;</p>
<p>He semede wel to ben wel strong.</p>
<p>“Deus!” hwat ubbe, “qui ne were he knith?</p>
<p>I woth, þat he is swiþe with!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and thinks he ought to be a knight, not a pedlar.</p>
<span class="linenum">1652</span>
<p>Bet<i>er</i>e semede him to bere</p>
<p>Helm on heued, sheld and sp<i>er</i>e,</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne to beye and selle ware.</p>
<p>Allas! þat he shal þer-with fare.</p>
<span class="linenum">1656</span>
<p>Goddot! wile he trowe me,</p>
<p>Chaffare shal he late be.”</p>
<p>Neþeles he seyde sone:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“Havelok, bring your wife, and come and eat with me.”</p>
<p>“Hauelok, haue [þou] þi bone,</p>
<span class="linenum">1660</span>
<p>And y ful wel rede þ[e]</p>
<p>Þat þou come, and ete with me</p>
<p>To-day, þou, and þi fayre wif,</p>
<p>Þat þou louest also þi lif.</p>
<span class="linenum">1664</span>
<p>And haue þou of hire no drede,</p>
<p>Shal hire no man shame bede.</p>
<p>Bi þe fey that y owe to þe,</p>
<p>Þerof shal i me serf-borw be.”</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
UBBE TAKES A GREAT FANCY TO HAVELOK.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">1668</span>
<p class="section">
Hauelok herde þat he bad,</p>
<p>And thow was he ful sore drad,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 212, col. 2.]</p>
<p>With him to ete, for hise wif;</p>
<span class="pagenum">46</span>
<a id="page46"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok fears ill may come of it.</p>
<p>For him wore leuere þat his lif</p>
<span class="linenum">1672</span>
<p>Him wore reft, þan she i<i>n</i> blame</p>
<p>Felle, or lauthe ani shame.</p>
<p>Hwa<i>n</i>ne he hauede his wille <i>w</i>at,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note72" id="poem_tag72">72</a></p>
<p>Þe stede, þat he onne sat,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
But Ubbe rides away, saying,</p>
<span class="linenum">1676</span>
<p>Smot ubbe with spures faste,</p>
<p>And forth awey, but at þe laste,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1678" id="texttag1678">1678</a>
<p>Or he fro him ferde,</p>
<p>Seyde he, þat his folk herde:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“Mind that you come.”</p>
<span class="linenum">1680</span>
<p>“Loke þat ye come<i>n</i> beþe,</p>
<p>For ich it wile, and ich it rede.”</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag72" id="poem_note72" class="pginternal">72</a>
MS. <i>either</i> þat <i>or</i> ƿat.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
UBBE SENDS HAVELOK TO BERNARD.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok dares not refuse.</p>
<p class="section">
Hauelok ne durste, þe he were adrad,</p>
<p>Nouth with-sitte<i>n</i> þat ubbe bad;</p>
<span class="linenum">1684</span>
<p>His wif he dide with him lede,</p>
<p>Vn-to þe heye curt he y[e]de.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note73" id="poem_tag73">73</a></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Robert the Red leads Goldborough.</p>
<p>Roberd hire ledde, þat was red,</p>
<p>Þat hau[ed]e þarned<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note73a" id="poem_tag73a">*</a> for hire þe ded</p>
<span class="linenum">1688</span>
<p>Or ani hauede hire misseyd,</p>
<p>Or hand with iuele onne leyd.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
William Wendut is on the other side of her.</p>
<p>Willam wendut was þat oþer</p>
<p>Þat hire ledde, roberdes broþer,</p>
<span class="linenum">1692</span>
<p>Þat was with at alle nedes:</p>
<p>Wel is him þat god ma<i>n</i> fedes!</p>
<p>Þan he were<i>n</i> come<i>n</i> to þe halle,</p>
<p>Biforen ubbe, and hise me<i>n</i> alle,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe starts up to welcome them.</p>
<span class="linenum">1696</span>
<p>Vbbe stirte hem ageyn,</p>
<p>And mani a knith, and mani a sweyn,</p>
<p>Hem for to se, and forto shewe;</p>
<p>Þo stod hauelok als a lowe</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok is a head taller than any of them.</p>
<span class="linenum">1700</span>
<p>Aboven [þo] þat þer-i<i>n</i>ne wore,</p>
<p>Rith al bi þe heued more</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne ani þat þer-inne stod:</p>
<p>Þo was ubbe bliþe of mod,</p>
<span class="linenum">1704</span>
<p>Þat he saw him so fayr and he<i>n</i>de,<span class="pagenum">47</span></p>
<a id="page47"> </a>
<p>Fro him ne mithe his herte we<i>n</i>de,</p>
<p>Ne fro him, ne fro his wif;</p>
<p>He louede hem sone so his lif.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe loves Havelok better than any one else.</p>
<span class="linenum">1708</span>
<p>Were<i>n</i> non i<i>n</i> denemark, þat him þouthe,</p>
<p>Þat he so mikel loue mouthe;</p>
<p>More he louede hauelok one,</p>
<p>Þan al denemark, bi mine wone!</p>
<span class="linenum">1712</span>
<p>Loke nou, hw god helpen kan</p>
<p>O mani wise wif and man.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag73" id="poem_note73" class="pginternal">73</a>
MS. yde.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag73a" id="poem_note73a" class="pginternal">*</a>
<i>þarned</i> is an error of the scribe for <i>þoled</i>; see the
<a href="#gloss_Tharne" class="pginternal">Glossary</a>.</p>
</div>
<p class="section">
Hwan it was come<i>n</i> time to ete,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 212<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Hise wif dede ubbe sone in fete,</p>
<span class="linenum">1716</span>
<p>And til hire seyde, al on game<i>n</i>:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe’s wife is to eat with Havelok, and Goldborough with Ubbe.</p>
<p>“Dame, þou and hauelok shule<i>n</i> ete same<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>And goldeboru shal ete wit me,</p>
<p>Þat is so fayr so flour on tre;</p>
<span class="linenum">1720</span>
<p>In al denemark nis<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note74" id="poem_tag74">74</a>
<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note74a" id="poem_tag74a">*</a> wimma<i>n</i></p>
<p>So fayr so sche, bi seint iohan!”</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1722" id="texttag1722">1722</a>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne [he] were set, and bord leyd,</p>
<p>And þe beneysun was seyd,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
There were cranes, swans, venison, fish, and wines.</p>
<span class="linenum">1724</span>
<p>Biforn he<i>m</i> com þe beste mete</p>
<p>Þat king or cayser wolde ete;</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1726" id="texttag1726">1726</a>
<p>Kranes, swa<i>n</i>nes, ueneysun,</p>
<p>Lax, lampreys, and god sturgun,</p>
<span class="linenum">1728</span>
<p>Pyme<i>n</i>t to drinke, and god clare,</p>
<p>Win hwit and red, ful god plente.</p>
<p>Was þer-inne no page so lite,</p>
<p>Þat euere wolde ale bite.</p>
<span class="linenum">1732</span>
<p>Of þe mete forto tel,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
No need to tell it all.</p>
<p>Ne of þe metes<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note75" id="poem_tag75">75</a> bidde<a class="emend pginternal" href="#poem_note75a" id="poem_tag75a">†</a> i nout
dwelle;</p>
<p>Þat is þe storie for to lenge,</p>
<p>It wolde anuye þis fayre genge.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
When the feast is over,</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote1736" id="texttag1736">1736</a>
<p>But hwan he haueden þe kiwing<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note76" id="poem_tag76">76</a> <ins class="edcorr" title="changed by editor from ‘deled’">de[y]led</ins>,</p>
<p>And fele siþes hauede<i>n</i> wosseyled,</p>
<p>And with gode drinkes sete<i>n</i> longe,</p>
<span class="pagenum">48</span>
<a id="page48"> </a>
<p>And it was time for to gonge,</p>
<span class="linenum">1740</span>
<p>Il man to þer he cam fro,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe thinks he must let them have an escort.</p>
<p>Þouthe ubbe, “yf I late hem go,</p>
<p>Þus one foure, with-uten mo,</p>
<p>So mote ich brouke finger or to,</p>
<span class="linenum">1744</span>
<p>For þis wi<i>m</i>ma<i>n</i> bes mike wo!</p>
<p>For hire shal me<i>n</i> hire louerd slo.”</p>
<p>He tok sone knithes ten,</p>
<p>And wel sixti oþer men,</p>
<span class="linenum">1748</span>
<p>Wit gode bowes, and with gleiues,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1749" id="texttag1749">1749</a>
<p>And sende him unto þe greyues,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He sends them to Bernard Brown, and bids him take care of them till next
day.</p>
<p>Þe beste ma<i>n</i> of al þe toun,</p>
<p>Þat was named b<i>er</i>nard brun;</p>
<span class="linenum">1752</span>
<p>And bad him, als he louede his lif,</p>
<p>Hauelok wel y[e]men,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note77" id="poem_tag77">77</a> and his wif,</p>
<p>And wel do wayten al þe nith,</p>
<p>Til þe oþer day, þat it were lith.</p>
<span class="linenum">1756</span>
<p>Bernard was trewe, and swiþe with,</p>
<p>In al þe borw ne was no knith</p>
<p>Þat betere couþe on stede riden,</p>
<p>Helm on heued, ne swerd bi side.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 212<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Bernard provides a rich supper for Havelok.</p>
<span class="linenum">1760</span>
<p>Hauelok he gladlike under-stod,</p>
<p>With mike loue, and herte god,</p>
<p>And dide greyþe a super riche,</p>
<p>Also he was no with chinche,</p>
<span class="linenum">1764</span>
<p>To his bihoue euer-il del,</p>
<p>Þat he mithe supe swiþe wel.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag74" id="poem_note74" class="pginternal">74</a>
MS. is.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag74a" id="poem_note74a" class="pginternal">*</a>
Perhaps we should rather read—<i>is womman</i> [<i>non</i>].</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag75" id="poem_note75" class="pginternal">75</a>
<i>Qu.</i> win.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag75a" id="poem_note75a" class="pginternal">†</a>
<i>Bidde</i> must mean <i>offer</i>, rather than <i>bid</i> (as in the
<a href="#gloss_Bidd" class="pginternal">Glossary</a>); unless it be miswritten for
<i>bide</i> = tarry.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag76" id="poem_note76" class="pginternal">76</a>
Uncertain in MS. See <a href="#textnote1736" class="pginternal">note</a>.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag77" id="poem_note77" class="pginternal">77</a>
MS. ymen.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
A GANG OF THIEVES BESET BERNARD’S HOUSE.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
At suppertime sixty-one thieves come to the house,</p>
<p class="section">
Also he seten, and sholde soupe,</p>
<p>So comes a ladde in a ioupe,</p>
<span class="linenum">1768</span>
<p>And with him sixti oþer stronge,</p>
<p>With swerdes drawen, and kniues lo<i>n</i>ge,</p>
<p>Ilkan i<i>n</i> hande a ful god gleiue,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and bid Bernard open the door.</p>
<p>And seyde, “undo, b<i>er</i>nard þe greyue!</p>
<span class="linenum">1772</span>
<p>Vndo swiþe, and latus<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note78" id="poem_tag78">78</a> in,<span class="pagenum">49</span></p>
<a id="page49"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
THREE OF THE THIEVES ATTACK HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p>Or þu art ded, bi seint austin!”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Bernard starts up, arms himself,</p>
<p>Bernard stirt up, þat was ful big,</p>
<p>And caste a brinie up-on his rig,</p>
<span class="linenum">1776</span>
<p>And grop an ax,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note79" id="poem_tag79">79</a> þat was ful god,</p>
<p>Lep to þe dore, so he wore wod,</p>
<p>And seyde, “hwat are ye, þat are þer-oute,</p>
<p>Þat þus bigi<i>n</i>ne<i>n</i> forto stroute?</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and tells them to go away.</p>
<span class="linenum">1780</span>
<p>Goth he<i>n</i>ne swiþe, fule þeues,</p>
<p>For, bi þe louerd, þat ma<i>n</i> on leues,</p>
<p>Shol ich casten þe dore open,</p>
<p>Su<i>m</i>me of you shal ich drepen!</p>
<span class="linenum">1784</span>
<p>And þe oþre shal ich kesten</p>
<p>In fet<i>er</i>es, and ful faste festen!”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They defy him.</p>
<p>“Hwat haue ye seid,” quoth a ladde,</p>
<p>“Wenestu þat we ben adradde?</p>
<span class="linenum">1788</span>
<p>We shole at þis dore gonge</p>
<p>Maugre þin, carl, or outh longe.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They break the door open with a boulder.</p>
<p>He gripe<i>n</i> sone a bulder ston,</p>
<p>And let it fleye, ful god won,</p>
<span class="linenum">1792</span>
<p>Agen þe dore, þat it to-rof:</p>
<p>Auelok it saw, and þider drof,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok seizes the bar of the door, and says,</p>
<p>And þe barre sone vt-drow,</p>
<p>Þat was unride, and gret ynow,</p>
<span class="linenum">1796</span>
<p>And caste þe dore open wide,</p>
<p>And seide, “her shal y now abide:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“Come here to me.”</p>
<p>Comes swiþe vn-to me!<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note80" id="poem_tag80">80</a></p>
<p>Datheyt hwo you he<i>n</i>ne fle!”</p>
<span class="linenum">1800</span>
<p>“No,” quodh on, “þat shaltou coupe,”</p>
<p>And bigan til him to loupe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Three men attack Havelok.</p>
<p>In his hond is swerd ut-drawe,</p>
<p>Hauelok he wende þore haue slawe;</p>
<span class="linenum">1804</span>
<p>And with [him] comen oþer two,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 213, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Þat him wolde of liue haue do.</p>
<span class="pagenum">50</span>
<a id="page50"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK SLAYS SEVEN OF THE THIEVES.</span></p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1806" id="texttag1806">1806</a>
<p>Hauelok lifte up þe dore-tre,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He kills them all.</p>
<p>And at a dint he slow he<i>m</i> þre;</p>
<span class="linenum">1808</span>
<p>Was non of hem þat his h<i>er</i>nes</p>
<p>Ne lay þer-ute ageyn þe sternes.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A fourth he knocks down with a blow on the head.</p>
<p>Þe ferþe þat he siþen mette,</p>
<p>Wit þe barre so he him grette,</p>
<span class="linenum">1812</span>
<p>Bifor þe heued, þat þe rith eye</p>
<p>Vt of þe hole made he fleye,</p>
<p>And siþe clapte him on þe crune,</p>
<p>So þat he stan-ded fel þor dune.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A fifth he hits between the shoulders.</p>
<span class="linenum">1816</span>
<p>Þe fifte þat he ouer-tok,</p>
<p>Gaf he a ful sor dint[e] ok,</p>
<p>Bitwen þe sholdres, þer he stod,</p>
<p>Þat he spen his herte blod.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A sixth he smites on the neck.</p>
<span class="linenum">1820</span>
<p>Þe sixte wende for to fle,</p>
<p>And he clapte him with þe tre</p>
<p>Rith in þe fule necke so,</p>
<p>Þat he smot hise necke on to.</p>
<span class="linenum">1824</span>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne þe sixe weren doun feld,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A seventh aims at Havelok’s eye.</p>
<p>Þe seuenþe brayd ut his swerd,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1826" id="texttag1826">1826</a>
<p>And wolde hauelok Riht i<i>n</i> the eye;</p>
<p>And haue<i>lok</i> le[t þe]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note81" id="poem_tag81">81</a> barre fleye,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok kills him.</p>
<span class="linenum">1828</span>
<p>And smot him sone ageyn þe brest,</p>
<p>Þat hauede he neu<i>er</i>e sch[r]ifte of p<i>re</i>st;</p>
<p>For he was ded on lesse hwile,</p>
<p>Þan me<i>n</i> mouthe re<i>n</i>ne a mile.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The rest divide into two parties,</p>
<span class="linenum">1832</span>
<p>Alle þe oþere were<i>n</i> ful kene,</p>
<p>A red þei taken hem bi-twene,</p>
<p>þat he sholde him bi-halue,</p>
<p>And brise<i>n</i> so, þat wit no salue</p>
<span class="linenum">1836</span>
<p>Ne sholde him helen leche non:</p>
<p>Þey drowen ut swerdes, ful god won,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1838" id="texttag1838">1838</a>
<p class="sidenote">
and rush at him like dogs at a bear.</p>
<p>And shoten on him, so don on bere</p>
<p>Dogges, þat wolden him to-tere,</p>
<span class="pagenum">51</span>
<a id="page51"> </a>
<span class="linenum">1840</span>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne men doth þe bere beyte:</p>
<p>Þe laddes were kaske and teyte,</p>
<p>And vn-bi-yeden him ilkon,</p>
<p>Su<i>m</i> smot with tre, and su<i>m</i> wit ston;</p>
<span class="linenum">1844</span>
<p>Su<i>m</i>me putte<i>n</i> with gleyue, i<i>n</i> bac and side,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They wound Havelok in twenty places.</p>
<p>And yeue<i>n</i> wundes longe and wide;</p>
<p>In twenti stedes, and wel mo,</p>
<p>Fro þe croune til the to.</p>
<span class="linenum">1848</span>
<p>Hwan he saw þat, he was wod,</p>
<p>And was it ferlik hw he stod,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 213, col. 2.]</p>
<p>For the blod ran of his sides</p>
<p>So water þat fro þe welle glides;</p>
<span class="linenum">1852</span>
<p>But þa<i>n</i>ne bigan he for to mowe</p>
<p>With the barre, and let hem shewe,</p>
<p>Hw he cowþe sore smite,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He at last succeeds in killing twenty of them.</p>
<p>For was þer non, long ne lite,</p>
<span class="linenum">1856</span>
<p>Þat he Mouthe ouer-take,</p>
<p>Þat he ne garte his croune krake;</p>
<p>So þat on a litel stund,</p>
<p>Felde he twenti to þe grund.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag78" id="poem_note78" class="pginternal">78</a>
<i>Sic</i> in MS.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag79" id="poem_note79" class="pginternal">79</a>
MS. ar; <i>but see</i> l. 1894.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag80" id="poem_note80" class="pginternal">80</a>
MS. vnto me datheit,—evidently the repetition of the first word in
the succeeding line.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag81" id="poem_note81" class="pginternal">81</a>
<i>Qu.</i> Hauelok let the. MS. “haue le.”</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
THE THIEVES SHOOT AT HIM FROM A DISTANCE.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">1860</span>
<p class="section">
Þo bigan gret dine to rise,</p>
<p>For þe laddes on ilke wise</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They throw stones at him.</p>
<p>Him asaylede<i>n</i> wit grete dintes,</p>
<p>Fro fer he stoden, him with flintes</p>
<span class="linenum">1864</span>
<p>And gleyues schote<i>n</i> him fro ferne,</p>
<p>For drepen him he wolden yerne;</p>
<p>But durste<i>n</i> he newhe<i>n</i> him no more,</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne he bor or leu<i>n</i> wore.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HUGH AND THE REST COME TO THE RESCUE.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Hugh Raven hears the noise,</p>
<span class="linenum">1868</span>
<p class="section">
Huwe raue<i>n</i> þat dine herde,</p>
<p>And þowthe wel, þat me<i>n</i> mis-ferde</p>
<p>With his louerd, for his wif,</p>
<p>And grop an ore, and a long knif,</p>
<span class="linenum">1872</span>
<p>And þider drof al so an hert,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and comes to help.</p>
<p>And cham þer on a litel stert,</p>
<span class="pagenum">52</span>
<a id="page52"> </a>
<p>And saw how þe laddes wode</p>
<p>Hauelok his louerd umbistode,</p>
<span class="linenum">1876</span>
<p>And bete<i>n</i> on him so doth þe smith</p>
<p>With þe hamer on þe stith.</p>
<p class="section">
“Allas!” hwat hwe, “þat y was bore<i>n</i>!</p>
<p>Þat eu<i>er</i>e et ich bred of koren!</p>
<span class="linenum">1880</span>
<p>Þat ich here þis sorwe se!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Hugh calls out to Robert and William.</p>
<p>Roberd! willam! hware ar ye?</p>
<p>Gripeth eþer unker a god tre,</p>
<p>And late we nouth þise doges fle,</p>
<span class="linenum">1884</span>
<p>Til ure louerd wreke [we];</p>
<p>Cometh swiþe, and folwes me!</p>
<p>Ich haue in honde a ful god ore:</p>
<p>Datheit wo ne smite sore!”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Robert comes to the rescue,</p>
<span class="linenum">1888</span>
<p>“Ya! leue, ya!” quod roberd sone,</p>
<p>“We haue<i>n</i> ful god lith of þe mone.”</p>
<p>Roberd grop a staf, strong and gret,</p>
<p>Þat mouthe ful wel bere a net,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and William too, and Bernard.</p>
<span class="linenum">1892</span>
<p>And willam wendut grop a tre</p>
<p>Mikel grettere þan his þe,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note82" id="poem_tag82">82</a></p>
<p>And b<i>er</i>nard held his ax ful faste;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 213<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p>I seye, was he nouth þe laste;</p>
<span class="linenum">1896</span>
<p>And lopen forth so he weren wode</p>
<p>To þe laddes, þer he stode,</p>
<p>And yaf hem wundes swiþe grete;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They fight with the thieves.</p>
<p>Þer mithe men wel se boyes bete,</p>
<span class="linenum">1900</span>
<p>And ribbes in here sides breke,</p>
<p>And hauelok on hem wel wreke.</p>
<p>He broken armes, he broken knes,</p>
<p>He broken shankes, he broke<i>n</i> thes.</p>
<span class="linenum">1904</span>
<p>He dide þe blode þere re<i>n</i>ne dune</p>
<p>To þe fet rith fro the crune,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
No head was spared.</p>
<p>For was þer spared heued non:</p>
<p>He leyden on heuedes, ful god won,</p>
<span class="pagenum">53</span>
<a id="page53"> </a>
<span class="linenum">1908</span>
<p>And made croune[s] breke and crake,</p>
<p>Of þe broune, and of þe blake;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He made their backs as soft as their bellies.</p>
<p>He made<i>n</i> here backes al so bloute</p>
<p>Als h[<i>er</i>]e<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note83" id="poem_tag83">83</a> wombes, and made he<i>m</i>
rowte</p>
<span class="linenum">1912</span>
<p>Als he weren kradelbarnes:</p>
<p>So dos þe child þat moder þarnes.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag82" id="poem_note82" class="pginternal">82</a>
MS. þre, <i>the <b>r</b> being caught from the word above.</i> Cf.
l. 1903.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag83" id="poem_note83" class="pginternal">83</a>
<i>Qu.</i> here. MS. he.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
ALL THE SIXTY THIEVES ARE SLAIN.</span></p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1914" id="texttag1914">1914</a>
<p class="section">
Daþeit <i>w</i>o<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note84" id="poem_tag84">84</a> recke! for he it s<i>er</i>uede,</p>
<p>Hwat dide he þore were<i>n</i> he werewed;</p>
<span class="linenum">1916</span>
<p>So longe hauede<i>n</i> he but and bet</p>
<p>With neues under hernes set,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
All sixty assailants are slain.</p>
<p>Þat of þo sixti men and on</p>
<p>Ne wente þer awey liues non.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag84" id="poem_note84" class="pginternal">84</a>
MS. “ƿe,” clearly miswritten for “ƿo” or “wo.” See ll. 2047, 296, 300,
&amp;c.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
UBBE ASKS BERNARD WHAT HAS HAPPENED.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
At morn, there they lay like dogs.</p>
<span class="linenum">1920</span>
<p class="section">
ON þe morwe<i>n</i>, h<i>w</i>an<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note85" id="poem_tag85">85</a> it was day,</p>
<p>Ilc on other wirwed lay,</p>
<p>Als it were dogges þat were<i>n</i> he<i>n</i>ged,</p>
<p>And su<i>m</i>me leye i<i>n</i> dikes slenget,</p>
<span class="linenum">1924</span>
<p>And su<i>m</i>me in gripes bi þe her</p>
<p>Drawen ware, and lat<i>en</i> ther.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote1926" id="texttag1926">1926</a>
<p>Sket cam tiding intil ubbe,</p>
<p>Þat hauelok hauede with a clubbe</p>
<span class="linenum">1928</span>
<p>Of hise slawen sixti and on</p>
<p>Sergaunz, þe beste þat mithe<i>n</i> gon.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe comes to see what is the matter.</p>
<p>“Deus!” quoth ubbe, “hwat may þis be!</p>
<p>Bet<i>er</i>e his i nime<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note86" id="poem_tag86">86</a> miself and se,</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote1932" id="texttag1932">1932</a>
<p>Þat þis baret on hwat is wold,</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne i sende yunge or old.</p>
<p>For yif i sende him un-to,</p>
<p>I wene me<i>n</i> sholde him shame do,</p>
<span class="linenum">1936</span>
<p>And þat ne wolde ich for no þing:<span class="pagenum">54</span></p>
<a id="page54"> </a>
<p>I loue him wel, bi heuene king!</p>
<p>Me wore leuere i wore lame,</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne men dide him ani shame,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 213<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<span class="linenum">1940</span>
<p>Or tok, or onne handes leyde,</p>
<p>Vn-ornelike,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note87" id="poem_tag87">87</a> or same seyde.”</p>
<p>He lep up on a stede lith,</p>
<p>And with him mani a noble knith,</p>
<span class="linenum">1944</span>
<p>And ferde forth un-to þe tun,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He calls for Bernard Brown.</p>
<p>And dide calle b<i>er</i>nard brun</p>
<p>Vt of his hus, wan he þer cam;</p>
<p>And b<i>er</i>nard sone ageyn [him] nam,</p>
<span class="linenum">1948</span>
<p>Al to-tused and al to-torn,</p>
<p>Ner also naked so he was born,</p>
<p>And al to-brised, bac and þe:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe asks who has beaten him about so?</p>
<p>Quoth ubbe, “b<i>er</i>nard, hwat is þe?</p>
<span class="linenum">1952</span>
<p>Hwo haues þe þus ille maked,</p>
<p>Þus to-riue<i>n</i>, and al mad naked?”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag85" id="poem_note85" class="pginternal">85</a>
MS. “hhan,” miswritten for “hƿan,” from which it differs very
slightly.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag86" id="poem_note86" class="pginternal">86</a>
MS. inime.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag87" id="poem_note87" class="pginternal">87</a>
MS. Vn ornelſke; <i>but <b>ſ</b> should certainly be</i> i.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
BERNARD RELATES HAVELOK’S PROWESS.</span></p>
<p class="section">
“Louerd,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note88" id="poem_tag88">88</a> m<i>er</i>ci,” quot he sone,</p>
<p>“To-nicht also ros þe mone</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“Sixty thieves attacked me last night.</p>
<span class="linenum">1956</span>
<p>Come<i>n</i> her mo þan sixti þeues,</p>
<p>With lokene copes, and wide sleues,</p>
<p>Me forto robben, and to pine,</p>
<p>And for to drepe me and mine.</p>
<span class="linenum">1960</span>
<p>Mi dore he broken up ful sket,</p>
<p>And wolde me binden hond and fet.</p>
<p>Wan þe godeme<i>n</i> þat sawe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok and his friends drove them off.</p>
<p>Hauelok, and he þat bi þe wowe</p>
<span class="linenum">1964</span>
<p>Leye, he stirte<i>n</i> up sone on-on,</p>
<p>And su<i>m</i>me grop tre, and su<i>m</i> grop ston,</p>
<p>And driue he<i>m</i> ut, þei he were<i>n</i> crus,</p>
<p>So dogges ut of milne-hous.</p>
<span class="linenum">1968</span>
<p>Hauelok grop þe dore-tre,</p>
<p>And [at] a dint he slow he<i>m</i> thre.</p>
<span class="pagenum">55</span>
<a id="page55"> </a>
<p>He is þe beste ma<i>n</i> at nede,</p>
<p>Þat eu<i>er</i>e mar shal ride stede!</p>
<span class="linenum">1972</span>
<p>Als helpe god, bi mine wone,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He is worth a thousand men.</p>
<p>A þhousend of me<i>n</i> his he worth one!</p>
<p>Yif he ne were, ich were nou ded,</p>
<p>So haue ich don Mi soule red;</p>
<span class="linenum">1976</span>
<p>But it is hof him mikel sinne;</p>
<p>He made<i>n</i> him swilke woundes þri<i>n</i>ne,</p>
<p>Þat of þe alþer-leste wounde</p>
<p>Were a stede brouht to grunde.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He has some bad wounds, more than twenty.</p>
<span class="linenum">1980</span>
<p>He haues a wunde i<i>n</i> the side,</p>
<p>With a gleyue, ful un-ride,</p>
<p>And he haues on þoru his arum,</p>
<p>Þer-of is ful mikel haru<i>m</i>,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 214, col. 1.]</p>
<span class="linenum">1984</span>
<p>And he haues on þoru his þhe,</p>
<p>Þe vn-rideste þat men may se,</p>
<p>And oþe[r] wundes haues he stro<i>n</i>ge,</p>
<p>Mo than twenti swiþe longe.</p>
<span class="linenum">1988</span>
<p>But siþen he hauede lauth þe sor</p>
<p>Of þe wundes, was neuere bor</p>
<p>Þat so fauth so he fauth þa<i>n</i>ne;</p>
<p>Was non þat hauede þe hern-panne</p>
<span class="linenum">1992</span>
<p>So hard, þat he ne dede alto-cruhsse,</p>
<p>And alto-shiuere, and alto-frusshe.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He followed them like a dog does a hare.</p>
<p>He folwede he<i>m</i> so hund dos hare,</p>
<p>Daþeyt on he wolde spare,</p>
<span class="linenum">1996</span>
<p>Þat [he] ne made he<i>m</i> euerilk on</p>
<p>Ligge stille so doth þe ston:</p>
<p>And þer nis he nouth to frie,</p>
<p>For oþer sholde he make hem lye</p>
<span class="linenum">2000</span>
<p>Ded, or þei him hauede slawen,</p>
<p>Or alto-hewen, or al-to-drawen.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag88" id="poem_note88" class="pginternal">88</a>
MS. Iouerd.</p>
<p class="section">
Louerd, haui no more plith</p>
<p>Of þat ich was þus greþed to-nith.</p>
<span class="linenum">2004</span>
<p>Þus wolde þe theues me haue reft,<span class="pagenum">56</span></p>
<a id="page56"> </a>
<p>But god-þank, he hauenet sure keft.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
But I fear Havelok is all but dead.”</p>
<p>But it is of him mikel scaþe:</p>
<p>I woth þat he bes ded ful raþe.”</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
THE OTHERS CONFIRM BERNARD’S STORY.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">2008</span>
<p class="section">
Quoth ubbe, “b<i>er</i>nard, seyst þou soth?”</p>
<p>“Ya, sire, that i ne<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note89" id="poem_tag89">89</a> lepe oth.</p>
<p>Yif y, louerd, a word leye,</p>
<p>To-morwen do me hengen heye.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The rest confirm Bernard’s story.</p>
<span class="linenum">2012</span>
<p>Þe burgeys þat þer-bi stode þore,</p>
<p>Grundlike and grete oþes swore,</p>
<p>Litle and mikle, yunge and holde,</p>
<p>Þat was soth, þat b<i>er</i>nard tolde.</p>
<span class="linenum">2016</span>
<p>Soth was, þat he wolde<i>n</i> him bynde,</p>
<p>And trusse al þat he mithen fynde</p>
<p>Of hise, in arke or in kiste,</p>
<p>Þat he mouthe i<i>n</i> seckes þriste.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“The thieves wanted to steal all he had.</p>
<span class="linenum">2020</span>
<p>“Louerd, he hauede<i>n</i> al awey born</p>
<p>His þing, and him-self alto-torn,</p>
<p>But als god self barw him wel,</p>
<p>Þat he ne tinte no catel.</p>
<span class="linenum">2024</span>
<p>Hwo mithe so mani sto<i>n</i>de ageyn,</p>
<p>Bi nither-tale, knith or swein?</p>
<p>He were<i>n</i> bi tale sixti and ten,</p>
<p>Starke laddes, stalworþi men,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They were led on by one G[r]iffin Gall.”</p>
<span class="linenum">2028</span>
<p>And on, þe mayster of he<i>m</i> alle,</p>
<p>Þat was þe name giffin<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note90" id="poem_tag90">90</a> galle.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 214, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Hwo mouthe agey[<i>n</i>]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note91" id="poem_tag91">91</a> so mani sto<i>n</i>de,</p>
<p>But als þis ma<i>n</i> of f<i>er</i>ne londe</p>
<span class="linenum">2032</span>
<p>Haueth he<i>m</i> slawe<i>n</i> with a tre?</p>
<p>Mikel ioie haue he!</p>
<p>God yeue him mikel god to welde,</p>
<p>Boþe in tun, and ek in felde!</p>
<span class="linenum">2036</span>
<p>We[l]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note92" id="poem_tag92">92</a> is set he etes mete.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe sends for Havelok,</p>
<p>Quoth ubbe, “doth him swiþe fete,</p>
<span class="pagenum">57</span>
<a id="page57"> </a>
<p>Þat y mouthe his woundes se,</p>
<p>Yf that he mouthe<i>n</i> heled<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note93" id="poem_tag93">93</a> be.</p>
<span class="linenum">2040</span>
<p>For yf he mouthe couere yet,</p>
<p>And gange<i>n</i> wel up-on hise fet,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
to dub him knight.</p>
<p>Mi-self shal dubbe him to knith,</p>
<p>For-þi þat he is so with.</p>
<span class="linenum">2044</span>
<p>And yif he liuede, þo foule theues,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2045" id="texttag2045">2045</a>
<p>Þat were<i>n</i> of kaym kin and eues,</p>
<p>He sholden hange bi þe necke;</p>
<p>Of here ded daþeit wo recke,</p>
<span class="linenum">2048</span>
<p>Hwan he yeden þus on nithes</p>
<p>To binde boþe burgme<i>n</i> and knithes.</p>
<p>For bynderes loue ich neuere mo,</p>
<p>Of hem ne yeue ich nouht a slo.”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag89" id="poem_note89" class="pginternal">89</a>
MS. ine.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag90" id="poem_note90" class="pginternal">90</a>
<i>Qu.</i> griffin.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag91" id="poem_note91" class="pginternal">91</a>
MS. agey.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag92" id="poem_note92" class="pginternal">92</a>
Cf. ll. 772, 907.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag93" id="poem_note93" class="pginternal">93</a>
MS. holed. See l. 2058.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
UBBE SENDS FOR HAVELOK AND TAKES HIM TO HIS CASTLE.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok is brought before Ubbe.</p>
<span class="linenum">2052</span>
<p class="section">
Hauelok was bifore ubbe browth,</p>
<p>Þat hauede for him ful mikel þouth,</p>
<p>And mikel sorwe in his herte</p>
<p>For hise wundes, þat we[r] so smerte.</p>
<span class="linenum">2056</span>
<p class="section">
But hwa<i>n</i> his wundes were<i>n</i> shewed,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A leech says he can be healed.</p>
<p>And a leche hauede knawed,</p>
<p>Þat he hem mouthe ful wel hele,</p>
<p>Wel make him gange, and ful wel mele,</p>
<span class="linenum">2060</span>
<p>And wel a palefrey bistride,</p>
<p>And wel up-on a stede ride,</p>
<p>Þo let ubbe al his care</p>
<p>And al his sorwe ouer-fare;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe invites him and Goldborough to his own castle.</p>
<span class="linenum">2064</span>
<p>And seyde, “cu<i>m</i> now forth with me,</p>
<p>And goldeboru, þi wif, with þe,</p>
<p>And þine seriaunz al þre,</p>
<p>For nou wile y youre warant be;</p>
<span class="linenum">2068</span>
<p>Wile y non of here frend</p>
<p>Þat þu slowe with þin hend</p>
<p>Moucte wayte þe [to] slo,</p>
<span class="pagenum">58</span>
<a id="page58"> </a>
<p>Also þou gange to and fro.</p>
<span class="linenum">2072</span>
<p>I shal lene þe a bowr,</p>
<p>Þat is up in þe heye tour,</p>
<p>Til þou mowe ful wel go,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 214<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p>And wel ben hol of al þi wo.</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote2076" id="texttag2076">2076</a>
<p>It ne shal no þing be<i>n</i> bitwene</p>
<p>Þi bour and min, also y wene,</p>
<p>But a fayr firrene wowe;—</p>
<p>Speke y loude, or spek y lowe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2080</span>
<p>Þou shalt<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note94" id="poem_tag94">94</a> ful wel heren me,</p>
<p>And þan þu wilt, þou shalt me se.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He promises to protect Goldborough.</p>
<p>A rof shal hile us boþe o-nith,</p>
<p>Þat none of mine, clerk ne knith,</p>
<span class="linenum">2084</span>
<p>Ne sholen þi wif no shame bede,</p>
<p>No more þan min, so god me rede!”</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag94" id="poem_note94" class="pginternal">94</a>
MS. sahalt; <i>and the second a is expuncted by mistake, instead of the
first</i>.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
UBBE SEES THE MIRACULOUS LIGHT   WHICH ISSUES FROM HAVELOK’S
MOUTH.</span></p>
<p class="section">
HE dide un-to þe borw bringe</p>
<p>Sone anon, al with ioynge,</p>
<span class="linenum">2088</span>
<p>His wif, and his serganz þre,</p>
<p>Þe beste men þat mouthe be.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The first night, about midnight,</p>
<p>Þe firste nith he lay þer-inne,</p>
<p>Hise wif, and his serganz þrinne,</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote2092" id="texttag2092">2092</a>
<p>Aboute þe middel of þe nith</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe wakes and sees a great light.</p>
<p>Wok ubbe, and saw a mikel lith</p>
<p>In þe bour þat hauelok lay,</p>
<p>Also brith so it were day.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe says he must go and see what it means.</p>
<span class="linenum">2096</span>
<p class="section">
“Deus!” quoth ubbe, “hwat may þis be?</p>
<p>Bet<i>er</i>e is i go miself, and se:</p>
<p>Hweþer he sitte<i>n</i> nou, and wesseyle<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>Or of ani shotshipe to-deyle,</p>
<span class="linenum">2100</span>
<p>Þis tid nithes, also foles;</p>
<p>Þan birþe men caste<i>n</i> hem i<i>n</i> poles,</p>
<p>Or in a grip, or in þe fen:</p>
<span class="pagenum">59</span>
<a id="page59"> </a>
<p>Nou ne sitten none but wicke men,</p>
<span class="linenum">2104</span>
<p>Glotuns, reu[e]res, or wicke þeues,</p>
<p>Bi c<i>ri</i>st, þat alle folk onne leues!”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He peeps in, and sees them all asleep.</p>
<p class="section">
He stod, and totede i<i>n</i> at a bord,</p>
<p>Her he spak anilepi word,</p>
<span class="linenum">2108</span>
<p>And saw hem slepen faste ilkon,</p>
<p>And lye stille so þe ston;</p>
<p>And saw al þat mikel lith</p>
<p>Fro hauelok cam, þat was so brith.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The light issues from Havelok’s mouth.</p>
<span class="linenum">2112</span>
<p>Of his mouth it com il del,</p>
<p>Þat was he war ful swiþe wel.</p>
<p>“Deus!” quoth he, “hwat may þis mene!”</p>
<p>He calde boþe arwe me<i>n</i> and kene,</p>
<span class="linenum">2116</span>
<p>Knithes, and serganz swiþe sleie,</p>
<p>Mo þan an hundred, with-ute<i>n</i> leye,</p>
<p>And bad hem alle come<i>n</i> and se,</p>
<p>Hwat þat selcuth mithe be.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
THE LIGHT SHOWS THAT HAVELOK IS THE HEIR.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 214<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<span class="linenum">2120</span>
<p class="section">
Als þe knithes were come<i>n</i> alle,</p>
<p>Þer hauelok lay, ut of þe halle,</p>
<p>So stod ut of his mouth a glem,</p>
<p>Rith al swilk so þe sunne-bem;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The light is like that of 107 candles.</p>
<span class="linenum">2124</span>
<p>Þat al so lith wa[s] þare, bi heuene!</p>
<p>So þer brenden serges seuene,</p>
<p>And an hundred serges ok:</p>
<p>Þat durste hi sweren on a bok.</p>
<span class="linenum">2128</span>
<p>He slepen faste alle fiue,</p>
<p>So he weren brouth of liue;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok and Goldborough are fast asleep.</p>
<p>And hauelok lay on his lift side,</p>
<p>In his armes his brithe bride.</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote2132" id="texttag2132">2132</a>
<p>Bi þe pappes he leyen naked:</p>
<p>So faire two weren neuere maked</p>
<p>In a bed to lyen samen:—</p>
<p>Þe knithes þouth of hem god game<i>n</i>,</p>
<span class="linenum">2136</span>
<p>Hem forto shewe, and loken to.<span class="pagenum">60</span></p>
<a id="page60"> </a>
<p>Rith also he stode<i>n</i> alle so,</p>
<p>And his bac was toward he<i>m</i> we<i>n</i>d,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They see a bright cross on his back, denoting kingship.</p>
<p>So were<i>n</i> he war of a croiz ful gent,</p>
<span class="linenum">2140</span>
<p>On his rith shuldre sw[iþ]e<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note95" id="poem_tag95">95</a> brith,</p>
<p>Brithter þan gold ageyn þe lith.</p>
<p>So þat he wiste heye and lowe,</p>
<p>Þat it was kunrik þat he sawe.</p>
<span class="linenum">2144</span>
<p>It sparkede, and ful brith shon,</p>
<p>So doth þe gode charbucle ston,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
It was light enough to choose a penny by.</p>
<p>Þat men Mouthe se by þe lith,</p>
<p>A peni chesen, so was it brith.</p>
<span class="linenum">2148</span>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne bihelden he him faste,</p>
<p>So þat he knewe<i>n</i> at þe laste,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They know he is Birkabeyn’s son and heir.</p>
<p>Þat he was birkabeynes sone,</p>
<p>Þat was here king, þat was he<i>m</i> wone</p>
<span class="linenum">2152</span>
<p>Wel to yeme, and wel were</p>
<p>Ageynes uten-laddes here.</p>
<p>“For it was neuere yet a broþer</p>
<p>In al denemark so lich anoþer,</p>
<span class="linenum">2156</span>
<p>So þis man þat is so fayr</p>
<p>Als birkabeyn, he is hise eyr.”</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag95" id="poem_note95" class="pginternal">95</a>
MS. swe, <i>for</i> swiþe. Cf. l. 1252.</p>
<p class="section">
He fellen sone at hise fet,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They weep for joy.</p>
<p>Was non of hem þat he ne gret,</p>
<span class="linenum">2160</span>
<p>Of ioie he weren alle so fawen,</p>
<p>So he him haueden of erþe drawe<i>n</i>.</p>
<p>Hise fet he kisten an hundred syþes,</p>
<p>Þe tos, þe nayles, and þe lithes,</p>
<span class="linenum">2164</span>
<p>So þat he bigan to wakne,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note96" id="poem_tag96">96</a></p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 215, col. 1.]</p>
<p>And wit he<i>m</i> ful sore to blakne,</p>
<p>For he wende he wolde<i>n</i> him slo,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok wakes.</p>
<p>Or elles binde him, and do wo.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag96" id="poem_note96" class="pginternal">96</a>
Here follows the catchword— “And wit hem.”</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
UBBE SAYS HAVELOK SHALL BE KING.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">2168</span>
<p class="section">
Quoth ubbe, “louerd, ne dred þe nowth,</p>
<p>Me þinkes that I se þi þouth.</p>
<span class="pagenum">61</span>
<a id="page61"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe offers homage to him,</p>
<p>Dere sone, wel is me,</p>
<p>Þat y þe with eyn[e]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note97" id="poem_tag97">97</a> se.</p>
<span class="linenum">2172</span>
<p>Man-red, louerd, bede y þe,</p>
<p>Þi man auht i ful wel to be,</p>
<p>For þu art come<i>n</i> of birkabeyn,</p>
<p>Þat hauede mani knith and sweyn;</p>
<span class="linenum">2176</span>
<p>And so shalt þou, louerd, haue,</p>
<p>Þou þu be yet a ful yung knaue.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and says he shall be king of Denmark.</p>
<p>Þou shalt be king of al denemark,</p>
<p>Was þer-inne neu<i>er</i>e non so stark.</p>
<span class="linenum">2180</span>
<p>To-morwen shaltu manrede take</p>
<p>Of þe brune and of þe blake;</p>
<p>Of alle þat aren in þis tun,</p>
<p>Boþe of erl, and of barun,</p>
<span class="linenum">2184</span>
<p>And of dreng, and of thayn,</p>
<p>And of knith, and of sweyn.</p>
<p>And so shaltu ben mad knith</p>
<p>Wit blisse, for þou art so with.”</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag97" id="poem_note97" class="pginternal">97</a>
We find <i>eyne</i> in ll. 680, 1273, &amp;c.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok is blithe, and thanks God.</p>
<span class="linenum">2188</span>
<p class="section">
Þo was hauelok swiþe bliþe,</p>
<p>And þankede God ful fele siþe.</p>
<p>On þe morwen, wan it was lith,</p>
<p>And gon was þist<i>er</i>nesse of þe nith,</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote2192" id="texttag2192">2192</a>
<p>Vbbe dide up-on a stede</p>
<p>A ladde lepe, and þider bede</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe summons all his lords.</p>
<p>Erles, barouns, drenges, theynes,</p>
<p>Klerkes, knithes, bu[r]geys,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note98" id="poem_tag98">98</a> sweynes,</p>
<span class="linenum">2196</span>
<p>Þat he sholden comen a-non,</p>
<p>Biforen him sone euerilkon,</p>
<p>Also he loue<i>n</i> here liues,</p>
<p>And here children, and here wiues.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag98" id="poem_note98" class="pginternal">98</a>
MS. bugeyſ.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
UBBE RELATES HAVELOK’S HISTORY.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
All come to receive his orders.</p>
<span class="linenum">2200</span>
<p class="section">
Hise bode ne durste he non at-sitte,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2201" id="texttag2201">2201</a>
<p>Þat he ne neme<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note99" id="poem_tag99">99</a> for to wite</p>
<span class="pagenum">62</span>
<a id="page62"> </a>
<p>Sone, hwat wolde þe iustise:</p>
<p>And [he] bigan anon to rise,</p>
<span class="linenum">2204</span>
<p>And seyde sone, “liþes me,</p>
<p>Alle samen, þeu and fre.</p>
<p>A þing ich wile you here shauwe,</p>
<p>Þat ye<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note100" id="poem_tag100">100</a> alle ful wel knawe.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe tells them about Birkabeyn,</p>
<span class="linenum">2208</span>
<p>Ye wite<i>n</i> wel, þat al þis lond</p>
<p>Was i<i>n</i> birkabeynes hond,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 215, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Þe day þat he was quic and ded;</p>
<p>And how þat he, bi youre red,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
who commended his children to Godard;</p>
<span class="linenum">2212</span>
<p>Bitauhte hise children þre</p>
<p>Godard to yeme, and al his fe.</p>
<p>Hauelok his sone he him tauhte,</p>
<p>And hise two douhtres, and al his auhte,</p>
<span class="linenum">2216</span>
<p>Alle herden ye him swere</p>
<p>On bok, and on messe-gere,</p>
<p>Þat he shulde yeme hem wel,</p>
<p>With-uten lac, with-uten tel.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag99" id="poem_note99" class="pginternal">99</a>
MS. meme; <i>miswritten for</i> neme; <i>see</i> ll. 1207, 1931.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag100" id="poem_note100" class="pginternal">100</a>
MS. he.</p>
</div>
<p class="sidenote">
and how Godard slew the two girls,</p>
<span class="linenum">2220</span>
<p class="section">
He let his oth al ouer-go,</p>
<p>Euere wurþe him yuel and wo!</p>
<p>For<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note101" id="poem_tag101">101</a> þe maydnes here lif</p>
<p>Refte he boþen, with a knif,</p>
<span class="linenum">2224</span>
<p>And him shulde ok haue slawe<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>Þe knif was at his herte drawe<i>n</i>,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
but had pity on the boy;</p>
<p>But god him wolde wel haue saue,</p>
<p>He hauede reunesse of þe knaue,</p>
<span class="linenum">2228</span>
<p>So þat he with his hend</p>
<p>Ne drop him nouth, þat sor[i] fend,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
but afterwards ordered Grim to drown him.</p>
<p>But sone dide he a fishere</p>
<p>Swiþe grete oþes swere,</p>
<span class="linenum">2232</span>
<p>Þat he sholde drenchen him</p>
<p>In þe se, þat was ful brim.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag101" id="poem_note101" class="pginternal">101</a>
<i>Qu.</i> Fro.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
But Grim fled with him to England.</p>
<p class="section">
Hwan grim saw þat he was so fayr,</p>
<p>And wiste he was þe Rith eir,</p>
<span class="pagenum">63</span>
<a id="page63"> </a>
<span class="linenum">2236</span>
<p>Fro denemark ful sone he fledde</p>
<p>In-til englond, and þer him fedde</p>
<p>Mani winter, þat til þis day</p>
<p>Haues he ben fed and fostred ay.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Then Ubbe shows Havelok to them all,</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote2240" id="texttag2240">2240</a>
<p>Lokes, hware he sto<i>n</i>des her:</p>
<p>In al þis werd ne haues he per;</p>
<p>Non so fayr, ne non so long,</p>
<p>Ne non so mikel, ne non so stro<i>n</i>g.</p>
<span class="linenum">2244</span>
<p>In þis middelerd nis no knith</p>
<p>Half so strong, ne half so with.</p>
<p>Bes of him ful glad and bliþe,</p>
<p>And cometh alle hider swiþe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and bids them swear fealty to him.</p>
<span class="linenum">2248</span>
<p>Manrede youre louerd forto make,</p>
<p>Boþe brune and þe blake.</p>
<p>I shal mi-self do first þe gamen,</p>
<p>And ye siþen alle samen.”</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
