UBBE DOES HOMAGE TO HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe swears fealty first.</p>
<span class="linenum">2252</span>
<p class="section">
Oknes ful fayre he him sette,</p>
<p>Mouthe noþing him þer-fro lette,</p>
<p>And bi-cam is man Rith þare,</p>
<p>Þat alle sawe<i>n</i> þat þere ware.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 215<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p class="sidenote">
All the rest do the same.</p>
<span class="linenum">2256</span>
<p class="section">
After him stirt up laddes ten,</p>
<p>And bi-come<i>n</i> hise men;<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note102" id="poem_tag102">102</a></p>
<p>And siþen euerilk a baroun,</p>
<p>Þat euere weren i<i>n</i> al that toun;</p>
<span class="linenum">2260</span>
<p>And siþen drenges, and siþe<i>n</i> thaynes,</p>
<p>And siþen knithes, and siþe<i>n</i> sweynes;</p>
<p>So þat, or þat day was gon,</p>
<p>In al þe tun ne was nouth on</p>
<span class="linenum">2264</span>
<p>Þat it ne was his man bicomen:</p>
<p>Manrede of alle hauede he nomen.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag102" id="poem_note102" class="pginternal">102</a>
A word is added in the MS. after <i>men</i>, apparently <i>beye</i>.
Perhaps we should read: <i>hise heye men</i>.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
ALL SWEAR TO BE FAITHFUL TO HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok makes them swear to be faithful to him always.</p>
<p class="section">
Hwan he hauede of he<i>m</i> alle</p>
<p>Manrede taken, i<i>n</i> the halle,</p>
<span class="pagenum">64</span>
<a id="page64"> </a>
<span class="linenum">2268</span>
<p>Grundlike dide he hem swere,</p>
<p>Þat he sholden him god feyth bere</p>
<p>Ageynes alle þat woren on liue;</p>
<p>Þer-yen ne wolde neuer on striue,</p>
<span class="linenum">2272</span>
<p>Þat he ne made<i>n</i> sone þat oth,</p>
<p>Riche and poure, lef and loth.</p>
<p>Hwan þat was maked, sone he se<i>n</i>de,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe sends for all the sheriffs and constables.</p>
<p>Vbbe, writes fer and hende,</p>
<span class="linenum">2276</span>
<p>After alle þat castel yemede,</p>
<p>Burwes, tunes, sibbe an fremde,</p>
<p>Þat þider sholde<i>n</i> come<i>n</i> swiþe</p>
<p>Til him, and heren tiþandes bliþe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2280</span>
<p>Þat he hem alle shulde telle:</p>
<p>Of hem ne wolde neu<i>er</i>e on dwelle,</p>
<p>Þat he ne come sone plattinde,</p>
<p>Hwo hors ne hauede, com ga<i>n</i>ga<i>n</i>de.</p>
<span class="linenum">2284</span>
<p>So þat with-i<i>n</i>ne a fourtenith,</p>
<p>In al denemark ne was no knith,</p>
<p>Ne conestable, ne shireue,</p>
<p>Þat com of adam and of eue,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They all come.</p>
<span class="linenum">2288</span>
<p>Þat he ne com biforn sire ubbe:</p>
<p>He dredde<i>n</i> him so þhes<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note103" id="poem_tag103">103</a> doth clubbe.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag103" id="poem_note103" class="pginternal">103</a>
<i>Qu.</i> þes, <i>i.e.</i> thighs; or the spelling <i>þhes</i> may be
intentional; see l. 1984. But Sir F. Madden suggests
<i>þeues</i>.</p>
<p class="section">
Hwan he hauede<i>n</i> alle þe ki<i>n</i>g gret,</p>
<p>And he weren alle dun set,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe shows Havelok to them all.</p>
<span class="linenum">2292</span>
<p>Þo seyde ubbe, “lokes here,</p>
<p>Vre louerd swiþe dere,</p>
<p>Þat shal ben king of al þe lond,</p>
<p>And haue us alle under hond.</p>
<span class="linenum">2296</span>
<p>For he is birkabeynes sone,</p>
<p>Þe king þat was vmbe stonde wone</p>
<p>For to yeme, and wel were,</p>
<p>Wit sharp[e]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note104" id="poem_tag104">104</a> swerd, and lo<i>n</i>ge spere.</p>
<span class="pagenum">65</span>
<a id="page65"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 215<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<span class="linenum">2300</span>
<p>Lokes nou, hw he is fayr;</p>
<p>Sikerlike he is hise eyr.</p>
<p>Falles alle to hise fet,</p>
<p>Bicomes hise men ful sket.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
All swear to obey Havelok.</p>
<span class="linenum">2304</span>
<p>He weren for ubbe swiþe adrad,</p>
<p>And dide sone al þat he bad,</p>
<p>And yet dede<i>n</i> he sumdel more,</p>
<p>O bok ful grundlike he swore,</p>
<span class="linenum">2308</span>
<p>Þat he sholde with him halde</p>
<p>Boþe ageynes stille and bolde,</p>
<p>Þat euere wo[l]de his bodi dere:</p>
<p>Þat dide [he] hem o boke swere.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag104" id="poem_note104" class="pginternal">104</a>
See l. 2645 for the final <i>e</i>.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
UBBE DUBS HAVELOK A KNIGHT.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">2312</span>
<p class="section">
Hwan he hauede ma<i>n</i>rede and oth</p>
<p>Taken of lef and of loth,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2314" id="texttag2314">2314</a>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe dubs Havelok a knight,</p>
<p>Vbbe dubbede him to knith,</p>
<p>With a swerd ful swiþe brith,</p>
<span class="linenum">2316</span>
<p>And þe folk of al þe lond</p>
<p>Bitauhte him al in his hond,</p>
<p>Þe cunnriche eu<i>er</i>il del,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and makes him king.</p>
<p>And made him king heylike and wel.</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote2320" id="texttag2320">2320</a>
<p>Hwan he was king, þer mouthe me<i>n</i> se</p>
<p>Þe moste ioie þat mouhte be:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Great joy and many sports.</p>
<p>Buttinge with sharpe sp<i>er</i>es,</p>
<p>Skirming with taleuaces, þat me<i>n</i> beres,</p>
<span class="linenum">2324</span>
<p>Wrastling with laddes, putti<i>n</i>g of ston,</p>
<p>Harping and piping, ful god won,</p>
<p>Leyk of mine, of hasard ok,</p>
<p>Romanz reding on þe bok;</p>
<span class="linenum">2328</span>
<p>Þer mouthe men here þe gestes singe,</p>
<p>Þe gleymen on þe tabour dinge;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
There is baiting of bulls and boars,</p>
<p>Þer mouhte men se þe boles beyte,</p>
<p>And þe bores, with hundes teyte;</p>
<span class="linenum">2332</span>
<p>Þo mouthe men se eueril gleu,</p>
<p>Þer mouthe me<i>n</i> se hw grim greu;</p>
<p>Was neu<i>er</i>e yete ioie more</p>
<span class="pagenum">66</span>
<a id="page66"> </a>
<p>In al þis werd, þan þo was þore.</p>
<span class="linenum">2336</span>
<p>Þer was so mike<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note105" id="poem_tag105">105</a> yeft of cloþes,</p>
<p>Þat þou i swore you grete othes,</p>
<p>I ne wore nouth þer-offe croud:</p>
<p>Þat may i ful wel swere, bi god!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and plenty of meat and wine.</p>
<span class="linenum">2340</span>
<p>Þere was swiþe gode metes,</p>
<p>And of wyn, þat men fer fetes,</p>
<p>Rith al so mik and gret plente,</p>
<p>So it were water of þe se.</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote2344" id="texttag2344">2344</a>
<p>Þe feste fourti dawes sat,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 216, col. 1.]</p>
<p>So riche was neu<i>er</i>e non so þat.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The king makes Robert, William, and Hugh all barons.</p>
<p>Þe king made Roberd þere knith,</p>
<p>Þat was ful strong, and ful with,</p>
<span class="linenum">2348</span>
<p>And willa<i>m</i>, wendut het, his broþer,</p>
<p>And huwe raue<i>n</i>, þat was þat oþer,</p>
<p>And made hem barouns alle þre,</p>
<p>And yaf hem lond, and oþer fe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2352</span>
<p>So mikel, þat ilker twent[i] knihtes</p>
<p>Hauede of genge, dayes and nithes.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag105" id="poem_note105" class="pginternal">105</a>
See l. 2342.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK IS MADE KING OF DENMARK.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Hwan þat feste was al don,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A thousand knights accompany the king.</p>
<p>A thusand knihtes ful wel o bon</p>
<span class="linenum">2356</span>
<p>With-held þe king, with hi<i>m</i> to lede;</p>
<p>Þat ilkan hauede ful god stede,</p>
<p>Helm, and sheld, and brinie brith,</p>
<p>And al þe wepne þat fel to knith.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and five thousand sergeants.</p>
<span class="linenum">2360</span>
<p>With he<i>m</i> fiue thusand gode</p>
<p>Sergaunz, þat were<i>n</i> to fyht wode,</p>
<p>With-held he al of his genge:</p>
<p>Wile I na more þe storie lenge.</p>
<span class="linenum">2364</span>
<p>Yet hwan he hauede of al þe lond</p>
<p>Þe casteles alle i<i>n</i> his hond,</p>
<p>And conestables don þer-inne,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He swears to be avenged of Godard,</p>
<p>He swor, he ne sholde neuer bli<i>n</i>ne,</p>
<span class="pagenum">67</span>
<a id="page67"> </a>
<span class="linenum">2368</span>
<p>Til þat he were of godard wreke<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>Þat ich haue of ofte speken.</p>
<p>Hal hundred knithes dede he calle,</p>
<p>And hise fif thusand sergaunz alle,</p>
<span class="linenum">2372</span>
<p>And dide swere<i>n</i> on the bok</p>
<p>Sone, and on þe auter ok,</p>
<p>Þat he ne sholde neuere blinne,</p>
<p>Ne for loue, ne for sinne,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and to find and bind him.</p>
<span class="linenum">2376</span>
<p>Til þat he haueden godard funde,</p>
<p>And brouth biforn hi<i>m</i> faste bunde.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK GOES TO SEEK OUT GODARD.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Þanne he hauede<i>n</i> swor þis oth,</p>
<p>Ne leten he nouth for lef ne loth,</p>
<span class="linenum">2380</span>
<p>Þat he ne foren swiþe rathe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He goes to meet Godard.</p>
<p>Þer he was unto þe paþe,</p>
<p>Þer he yet on hunti<i>n</i>g for,</p>
<p>With mikel genge, and swiþe stor.</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote2384" id="texttag2384">2384</a>
<p>Robert, þat was of al þe ferd</p>
<p>Mayster, was girt wit a swerd,</p>
<p>And sat up-on a ful god stede,</p>
<p>Þat vnder him Rith wolde wede;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Robert accosts Godard,</p>
<span class="linenum">2388</span>
<p>He was þe firste þat with godard</p>
<p>Spak, and seyde, “hede<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note106" id="poem_tag106">106</a> cauenard!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 216, col. 2.]</p>
<p>Wat dos þu here at þis paþe?</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and tells him to come to the king,</p>
<p>Cu<i>m</i> to þe king, swiþe and raþe.</p>
<span class="linenum">2392</span>
<p>Þat sendes he þe word, and bedes,</p>
<p>Þat þu þenke hwat þu him dedes,</p>
<p>Hwan þu reftes with a knif</p>
<p>Hise sistres here lif,</p>
<span class="linenum">2396</span>
<p>An siþen bede þu in þe se</p>
<p>Drenchen him, þat herde he.</p>
<p>He is to þe swiþe grim:</p>
<p>Cu<i>m</i> nu swiþe un-to him,</p>
<span class="linenum">2400</span>
<p>Þat king is of þis kuneriche.</p>
<p>Þu fule man! þu wicke swike!</p>
<span class="pagenum">68</span>
<a id="page68"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
who will repay him.</p>
<p>And he shal yelde þe þi mede,</p>
<p>Bi c<i>ri</i>st þat wolde on rode blede!”</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag106" id="poem_note106" class="pginternal">106</a>
<i>Qu.</i> helde, <i>i.e.</i> old. Unless it means “heed!”</p>
<span class="linenum">2404</span>
<p class="section">
Hwan godard herde þat þer þrette,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godard and Robert strike each other.</p>
<p>With þe neue he robert sette</p>
<p>Biforn þe teth a dint ful strong.</p>
<p>And robert kipt ut a knif long,</p>
<span class="linenum">2408</span>
<p>And smot him þoru þe rith arum:</p>
<p>Þer-of was ful litel harum.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODARD’S MEN ARE BEATEN.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Hwan his folk þat sau and herde,</p>
<p>Hwou robert with here louerd ferde,</p>
<span class="linenum">2412</span>
<p>He haueden him wel ner browt of liue,</p>
<p>Ne were<i>n</i> his two breþren and oþre fiue</p>
<p>Slowen of here laddes ten,</p>
<p>Of godardes alþer-beste men.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godard’s men flee,</p>
<span class="linenum">2416</span>
<p>Hwan þe oþre sawe<i>n</i> þat, he fledde<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>And godard swiþe loude gredde:</p>
<p>“Mine knithes, hwat do ye?</p>
<p>Sule ye þus-gate fro me fle?</p>
<span class="linenum">2420</span>
<p>Ich haue you fed, and yet shal fede,</p>
<p>Helpe me nu in þis nede,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
but Godard rallies them.</p>
<p>And late ye nouth mi bodi spille,</p>
<p>Ne hauelok don of me hise wille.</p>
<span class="linenum">2424</span>
<p>Yif ye id<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note107" id="poem_tag107">107</a> do, ye do you shame,</p>
<p>And bringeth you-self in mikel blame.”</p>
<p>Hwan he þat herden, he wenten ageyn,</p>
<p>And slowen a knit and<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note108" id="poem_tag108">108</a> a sweyn</p>
<span class="linenum">2428</span>
<p>Of þe kinges oune men,</p>
<p>And woundede<i>n</i> abute<i>n</i> ten.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag107" id="poem_note107" class="pginternal">107</a>
<i>Qu.</i> it.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag108" id="poem_note108" class="pginternal">108</a>
MS. and and.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODARD IS BOUND AND LED TO HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
The king’s men kill all Godard’s men.</p>
<p class="section">
The kinges men hwan he þat sawe,</p>
<p>Scute<i>n</i> on hem, heye and lowe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2432</span>
<p>And euerilk fot of hem slowe,</p>
<p>But godard one, þat he flowe,</p>
<span class="pagenum">69</span>
<a id="page69"> </a>
<p>So þe þef men dos henge,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 216<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Or hund men shole i<i>n</i> dike slenge.</p>
<span class="linenum">2436</span>
<p>He bunde<i>n</i> him ful swiþe faste,</p>
<p>Hwil þe bondes wolden laste,</p>
<p>Þat he rorede als a bole,</p>
<p>Þat he wore parred in an hole,</p>
<span class="linenum">2440</span>
<p>With dogges forto bite and beite:</p>
<p>Were þe bondes nouth to leite.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They bind Godard,</p>
<p>He bounde<i>n</i> hi<i>m</i> so<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note109" id="poem_tag109">109</a> fele sore,</p>
<p>Þat he gan crien godes ore,</p>
<span class="linenum">2444</span>
<p>Þat he sholde of his hend plette,</p>
<p>Wolde<i>n</i> he nouht þer-fore lette,</p>
<p>Þat he ne bounde<i>n</i> hond and fet:</p>
<p>Daþeit þat on þat þer-fore let!</p>
<span class="linenum">2448</span>
<p>But dunte<i>n</i> him so man doth bere,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and cast him on an old mare, to take him to Havelok.</p>
<p>And keste him on a scabbed mere,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2450" id="texttag2450">2450</a>
<p>Hise nese went un-to þe crice:</p>
<p>So ledde<i>n</i> he þat fule swike,</p>
<span class="linenum">2452</span>
<p>Til he was biforn hauelok brouth,</p>
<p>Þat he haue[de] ful wo wrowht,</p>
<p>Boþe with hungre<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note110" id="poem_tag110">110</a> and with cold,</p>
<p>Or he were twel winter old,</p>
<span class="linenum">2456</span>
<p>And with mani heui swink,</p>
<p>With poure mete, and feble drink,</p>
<p>And [with] swiþe wikke cloþes,</p>
<p>For al hise manie grete othes.</p>
<span class="linenum">2460</span>
<p>Nu beyes he his holde blame:</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2461" id="texttag2461">2461</a>
<p class="sidenote">
“<i>Old sin makes new shame.</i>”</p>
<p>‘Old sinne makes newe shame:’</p>
<p>Wan he was [brouht] so shamelike</p>
<p>Biforn<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note111" id="poem_tag111">111</a> þe king, þe fule swike,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The king summons Ubbe and the rest.</p>
<span class="linenum">2464</span>
<p>Þe king dede ubbe swiþe calle</p>
<p>Hise erles, and hise barouns alle,</p>
<p>Dreng and thein, burgeis and knith,</p>
<span class="pagenum">70</span>
<a id="page70"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HE IS CONDEMNED TO BE HUNG.</span></p>
<p>And bad he sholde<i>n</i> demen him rith:</p>
<span class="linenum">2468</span>
<p>For he kneu, þe swike dam,</p>
<p>Euerildel god was him gram.</p>
<p>He sette<i>n</i> hem dun bi þe wawe,</p>
<p>Riche and pouere, heye and lowe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They sit in judgment.</p>
<span class="linenum">2472</span>
<p>Þe helde men, and ek þe grom,</p>
<p>And made þer þe rithe dom,</p>
<p>And seyde<i>n</i> unto þe king anon,</p>
<p>Þat stille sat [al] so þe ston:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
“He is to be flayed, drawn, and hung.”</p>
<span class="linenum">2476</span>
<p>“We deme, þat he be al quic slawen,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note112" id="poem_tag112">112</a></p>
<p>And siþen to þe galwes drawe[<i>n</i>],</p>
<p>At þis foule mere tayl;</p>
<p>Þoru is fet a ful stro<i>n</i>g nayl;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 216<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<span class="linenum">2480</span>
<p>And þore ben henged wit two feteres,</p>
<p>And þare be writen þise leteres:</p>
<p>‘Þis is þe swike þat wende wel,</p>
<p>Þe king haue reft þe lond il del,</p>
<span class="linenum">2484</span>
<p>And hise sistres with a knif</p>
<p>Boþe refte here lif.’</p>
<p>Þis writ shal henge bi him þare;</p>
<p>Þe dom is demd, seye we na more.”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag109" id="poem_note109" class="pginternal">109</a>
MS. fo.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag110" id="poem_note110" class="pginternal">110</a>
MS. hungred.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag111" id="poem_note111" class="pginternal">111</a>
MS. Brouht biforn; <i>but the word <b>brouht</b> clearly belongs to the
preceding line, in which, however, it is omitted</i>.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag112" id="poem_note112" class="pginternal">112</a>
We should perhaps read <i>flawen</i>, as required by the sense.
<i>See</i> ll. 2495, 2502.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODARD IS FLAYED ALIVE AND HUNG.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godard is shriven.</p>
<span class="linenum">2488</span>
<p class="section">
Hwan þe dom was demd and giue,</p>
<p>And he was wit þe p<i>re</i>stes shriue,</p>
<p>And it ne mouhte ben non oþer,</p>
<p>Ne for fader, ne for broþer,</p>
<span class="linenum">2492</span>
<p>Þat he sholde þarne lif;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A lad flays him.</p>
<p>Sket cam a ladde with a knif,</p>
<p>And bigan Rith at þe to</p>
<p>For to ritte, and for to flo,</p>
<span class="linenum">2496</span>
<p>And he bigan for to rore,</p>
<p>So it were grim or gore,</p>
<p>Þat men mithe þeþen a mile</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He roars.</p>
<p>Here him rore, þat fule file.</p>
<span class="linenum">2500</span>
<p>Þe ladde ne let no with for-þi,<span class="pagenum">71</span></p>
<a id="page71"> </a>
<p>Þey he criede ‘m<i>er</i>ci! m<i>er</i>ci!’</p>
<p>Þat [he] ne flow [him] eueril del</p>
<p>With knif mad of grunde<i>n</i> stel.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He is bound on an old mare,</p>
<span class="linenum">2504</span>
<p>Þei garte bringe þe mere sone,</p>
<p>Skabbed<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note113" id="poem_tag113">113</a> and ful iuele o bone,</p>
<p>And bunde<i>n</i> him rith at hire tayl</p>
<p>With a rop of an old seyl,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
drawn over rough ground,</p>
<span class="linenum">2508</span>
<p>And drowe<i>n</i> him un-to þe galwes,</p>
<p>Nouth bi þe gate, But ouer þe falwes;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and hung.</p>
<p>And henge [him] þore Bi þe hals:</p>
<p>Daþeit hwo recke! he was fals.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag113" id="poem_note113" class="pginternal">113</a>
MS. Skabbeb.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODRICH RAISES AN ARMY AGAINST HAVELOK.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">2512</span>
<p class="section">
Þa<i>n</i>ne he was ded, þat sathanas,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2513" id="texttag2513">2513</a>
<p>Sket was seysed al þat his was</p>
<p>In þe kinges hand il del,</p>
<p>Lond and lith, and oþer catel,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok makes Ubbe his steward.</p>
<a class="texttag pginternal" href="#textnote2516" id="texttag2516">2516</a>
<p>And þe king ful sone it yaf</p>
<p>Vbbe i<i>n</i> þe hond, wit a fayr staf,</p>
<p>And seyde, “her ich sayse þe</p>
<p>In al þe lo<i>n</i>d, in al þe fe.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He founds a priory of black monks for Grim’s soul,</p>
<span class="linenum">2520</span>
<p>Þo swor hauelok he sholde make,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2521" id="texttag2521">2521</a>
<p>Al for grim, of monekes blake</p>
<p>A p<i>ri</i>orie to s<i>er</i>uen inne ay</p>
<p>Ih<i>es</i>u c<i>ri</i>st, til domesday,</p>
<span class="linenum">2524</span>
<p>For þe god he haueden him don,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 217, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Hwil he was pou<i>er</i>e and iuel<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note114" id="poem_tag114">114</a> o
bon.</p>
<p>And þer-of held he wel his oth,</p>
<p>For he it made, god it woth!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
in the town of Grimsby.</p>
<span class="linenum">2528</span>
<p>In þe tun þer grim was graue<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>Þat of grim yet haues þe name.</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2530" id="texttag2530">2530</a>
<p>Of grim bidde ich na more spelle.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note115" id="poem_tag115">115</a>—</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich, earl of Cornwall,</p>
<p>But wan godrich herde telle,</p>
<span class="pagenum">72</span>
<a id="page72"> </a>
<span class="linenum">2532</span>
<p>Of cornwayle þat was erl,</p>
<p>(Þat fule traytour, that mixed cherl!)</p>
<p>Þat hauelok was king of denemark,</p>
<p>And ferde with him strong and stark,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
hears that Havelok has invaded England.</p>
<span class="linenum">2536</span>
<p>Comen engelond with-inne,</p>
<p>Engelond al for to winne,</p>
<p>And þat she, þat was so fayr,</p>
<p>Þat was of engelond rith eir,</p>
<span class="linenum">2540</span>
<p>Þat was comen up at grimesbi,</p>
<p>He was ful sorful and sori,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He says he will slay Havelok and his wife.</p>
<p>And seyde, “Hwat shal me to raþe?</p>
<p>Goddoth! i shal do slou hem baþe.</p>
<span class="linenum">2544</span>
<p>I shal don hengen hem ful heye,</p>
<p>So mote ich brouke mi Rith eie!</p>
<p>But yif he of mi lond[e]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note116" id="poem_tag116">116</a> fle;</p>
<p>Hwat? wende<i>n</i> he to desherite me?”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He raises a great army.</p>
<span class="linenum">2548</span>
<p>He dide sone ferd ut bidde,</p>
<p>Þat al þat euere mouhte o stede</p>
<p>Ride, or helm on heued bere,</p>
<p>Brini on bac, and sheld, and sp<i>er</i>e,</p>
<span class="linenum">2552</span>
<p>Or ani oþer wepne bere,</p>
<p>Hand-ax, syþe, gisarm, or sp<i>er</i>e,</p>
<p>Or aunlaz,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note117" id="poem_tag117">117</a> and god long knif,</p>
<p>Þat als he louede leme or lif,</p>
<span class="linenum">2556</span>
<p>Þat þey sholde<i>n</i> comen him to,</p>
<p>With ful god wepne ye ber so,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The army is to meet at Lincoln on the 17th of March.</p>
<p>To lincolne, þer he lay,</p>
<p>Of marz þe seue<i>n</i>tenþe day,</p>
<span class="linenum">2560</span>
<p>So þat he couþe he<i>m</i> god þank;</p>
<p>And yif þat ani were so rang,</p>
<p>That he þa<i>n</i>ne ne come anon,</p>
<p>He swor bi crist, and [bi]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note118" id="poem_tag118">118</a> seint Iohan,</p>
<span class="pagenum">73</span>
<a id="page73"> </a>
<span class="linenum">2564</span>
<p>That he sholde maken him þral,</p>
<p>And al his of-spring forth with-al.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag114" id="poem_note114" class="pginternal">114</a>
The MS. has “we,” which the scribe several times writes instead of
“wel.” But “wel” is a manifest blunder, since “iuel” is meant. Cf.
l. 2505.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag115" id="poem_note115" class="pginternal">115</a>
The author has here omitted to tell us that Havelok, at the desire of
his wife, invades England. See the note.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag116" id="poem_note116" class="pginternal">116</a>
Cf. l. 2599.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag117" id="poem_note117" class="pginternal">117</a>
Printed “alinlaz” in the former edition. The first stroke of the
<i>u</i> is longer than the second, and the tail of the <i>x</i> in the
line above converts the second downstroke of the <i>u</i> into an
apparent <i>i</i>.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag118" id="poem_note118" class="pginternal">118</a>
Cf. l. 1112.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODRICH EXCITES THE ENGLISH AGAINST HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Þe englishe þat herde þat,</p>
<p>Was non þat euere his bode sat,</p>
<span class="linenum">2568</span>
<p>For he him dredde swiþe sore,</p>
<p>So Runci spore, and mikle more.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 217, col. 2.]</p>
<p>At þe day he come sone</p>
<p>Þat he hem sette, ful wel o bone,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
All come to Lincoln on that day.</p>
<span class="linenum">2572</span>
<p>To lincolne, with gode stedes,</p>
<p>And al þe wepne þat knith ledes.</p>
<p>Hwa<i>n</i> he wore come, sket was þe erl yare,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note119" id="poem_tag119">119</a></p>
<p>Ageynes denshe men to fare,</p>
<span class="linenum">2576</span>
<p>And seyde, “lyþes me<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note120" id="poem_tag120">120</a> alle samen,</p>
<p>Haue ich gadred you for no game<i>n</i>,</p>
<p>But ich wile seyen you forþi;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich tells them what Havelok is doing at Grimsby.</p>
<p>Lokes hware here at g<i>ri</i>mesbi,</p>
<span class="linenum">2580</span>
<p>Hise uten-laddes here comen,</p>
<p>And haues nu þe p<i>ri</i>orie numen;</p>
<p>Al þat euere mithen he finde,</p>
<p>He bre<i>n</i>ne kirkes, and p<i>re</i>stes binde;</p>
<span class="linenum">2584</span>
<p>He strangleth mo<i>n</i>kes, and nu<i>n</i>nes boþe:</p>
<p>Wat wile ye, frend, her-offe Rede?</p>
<p>Yif he regne þus-gate longe,</p>
<p>He Moun us alle ouer-gange,</p>
<span class="linenum">2588</span>
<p>He moun vs alle quic henge or slo,</p>
<p>Or þral maken, and do ful wo,</p>
<p>Or elles reue us ure liues,</p>
<p>And ure children, and ure wiues.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He excites them to attack Havelok.</p>
<span class="linenum">2592</span>
<p>But dos nu als ich wile you lere,</p>
<p>Als ye wile be with me dere;</p>
<p>Nimes nu swiþe forth and raþe,</p>
<p>And helpes me and yu-self baþe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2596</span>
<p>And slos up-o[n] þe dogges swiþe:</p>
<p>For shal [i] neuere more be bliþe,</p>
<span class="pagenum">74</span>
<a id="page74"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
THE ENGLISH MARCH TO GRIMSBY.</span></p>
<p>Ne hoseled ben, ne of p<i>re</i>st shriuen,</p>
<p>Til þat he ben of londe driuen.</p>
<span class="linenum">2600</span>
<p>Nime we swiþe, and do hem fle,</p>
<p>And folwes alle faste me,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He will lead them himself.</p>
<p>For ich am he, of al þe ferd,</p>
<p>Þat first shal slo with drawe<i>n</i> swerd.</p>
<span class="linenum">2604</span>
<p>Daþeyt hwo ne stonde faste</p>
<p>Bi me, hwil hise armes laste!”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Earl Gunter and Earl Reyner of Chester support him.</p>
<p>“Ye! lef, ye!”<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note121" id="poem_tag121">121</a> couth þe erl gunter;</p>
<p>“Ya!” quoth þe erl of cestre, reyner.</p>
<span class="linenum">2608</span>
<p>And so dide alle þat þer stode,</p>
<p>And stirte forth so he were wode.</p>
<p>Þo mouthe me<i>n</i> se þe brinies brihte</p>
<p>On backes keste, and late rithe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2612</span>
<p>Þe helmes heye on heued sette;</p>
<p>To armes al so swiþe plette,</p>
<p>Þat þei wore on a litel stunde</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 217<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Grethet, als me<i>n</i> mithe telle a pund,</p>
<span class="linenum">2616</span>
<p>And lopen on stedes sone anon,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They approach Grimsby.</p>
<p>And toward g<i>ri</i>mesbi, ful god won,</p>
<p>He foren softe bi þe sti,</p>
<p>Til he come ney at grimesbi.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag119" id="poem_note119" class="pginternal">119</a>
<i>Or</i> þare; but see l. 2954.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag120" id="poem_note120" class="pginternal">120</a>
MS. mi. Cf. l. 2204.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag121" id="poem_note121" class="pginternal">121</a>
MS. <i>has <b>þe, ƿe</b>, or <b>ye</b> in both places.</i> But see
l. 1888.</p>
</div>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok meets them boldly,</p>
<span class="linenum">2620</span>
<p class="section">
Hauelok, þat hauede spired wel</p>
<p>Of here fare, eueril del,</p>
<p>With al his ferd cam hem a-geyn,</p>
<p>For-bar he noþer knith ne sweyn.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and kills the foremost knight.</p>
<span class="linenum">2624</span>
<p>Þe firste knith þat he þer mette,</p>
<p>With þe swerd so he him grette,</p>
<p>For his heued of he plette,</p>
<p>Wolde he nouth for sinne lette.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Robert kills a second.</p>
<span class="linenum">2628</span>
<p>Roberd saw þat dint so hende,</p>
<p>Wolde he neuere þeþe[<i>n</i>] wende,</p>
<p>Til þat he hauede anoþer slawen,</p>
<p>With þe swerd he held ut-drawen.</p>
<span class="pagenum">75</span>
<a id="page75"> </a>
<p class="sidenote">
William disables a third.</p>
<span class="linenum">2632</span>
<p>Willa<i>m</i> wendut his swerd vt-drow,</p>
<p>And þe þredde so sore he slow,</p>
<p>Þat he made up-on the feld</p>
<p>His lift arm fleye, with the swerd.<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note122" id="poem_tag122">122</a></p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag122" id="poem_note122" class="pginternal">122</a>
Cf. l. 1825. We should otherwise be tempted to read <i>sheld</i>;
especially as the <i>shield</i> is more appropriate to the <i>left</i>
arm.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
COMBAT BETWEEN UBBE AND GODRICH.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
Hugh Raven seizes his sword,</p>
<span class="linenum">2636</span>
<p class="section">
Huwe raue<i>n</i> ne forgat nouth</p>
<p>Þe swerd he hauede þider brouth,</p>
<p>He kipte it up, and smot ful sore</p>
<p>An erl, þat he saw priken þore,</p>
<span class="linenum">2640</span>
<p>Ful noblelike upon a stede,</p>
<p>Þat with him wolde al quic wede.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and cleaves an earl’s head in two.</p>
<p>He smot him on þe heued so,</p>
<p>Þat he þe heued clef a-two,</p>
<span class="linenum">2644</span>
<p>And þat bi þe shu[l]dre-blade</p>
<p>Þe sharpe swerd let [he] wade,</p>
<p>Þorw the brest unto þe herte;</p>
<p>Þe dint bigan ful sore to smerte,</p>
<span class="linenum">2648</span>
<p>Þat þe erl fel dun a-non,</p>
<p>Al so ded so ani ston.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe attacks Godrich.</p>
<p>Quoth ubbe, “nu dwelle ich to lo<i>n</i>ge,”</p>
<p>And leth his stede sone gonge</p>
<span class="linenum">2652</span>
<p>To godrich, with a god spere,</p>
<p>Þat he saw a-noþer bere,</p>
<p>And smoth godrich, and G<i>odrich</i> him,</p>
<p>Hetelike with h<i>er</i>te grim,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Both fall.</p>
<span class="linenum">2656</span>
<p>So þat he boþe felle dune,</p>
<p>To þe erþe first þe croune.</p>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne he wore<i>n</i> falle<i>n</i> dun boþen,</p>
<p>Grundlike here swerdes ut-drowe<i>n</i>,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 217<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<span class="linenum">2660</span>
<p>Þat were<i>n</i> swiþe sharp and gode,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They fight on foot.</p>
<p>And fouhten so þei wore<i>n</i> wode,</p>
<p>Þat þe swot ran fro þe crune</p>
<p>[To the fet rith þere adune.]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note123" id="poem_tag123">123</a></p>
<span class="pagenum">76</span>
<a id="page76"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODRICH DISPLAYS GREAT PROWESS.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">2664</span>
<p>Þer mouthe men se to knithes bete</p>
<p>Ayþer on oþer dintes grete,</p>
<p>So þat with alþer-lest[e] dint</p>
<p>Were al to-shiuered a flint,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The fight lasts from morn to night.</p>
<span class="linenum">2668</span>
<p>So was bi-twene<i>n</i> he<i>m</i> a fiht,</p>
<p>Fro þe morwen ner to þe niht,</p>
<p>So þat þei nouth ne <ins class="edcorr" title="changed by editor from ’blinne’: see footnote">bl[u]nne</ins>,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note123a" id="poem_tag123a">*</a></p>
<p>Til þat to sette bigan þe su<i>n</i>ne.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich wounds Ubbe sorely.</p>
<span class="linenum">2672</span>
<p>Þo yaf godrich þorw þe side</p>
<p>Vbbe a wunde ful un-ride,</p>
<p>So þat þorw þat ilke wounde</p>
<p>Hauede ben brouth to þe grunde,</p>
<span class="linenum">2676</span>
<p>And his heued al of-slawen,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Hugh Raven rescues him.</p>
<p>Yif god ne were, and huwe rauen,</p>
<p>Þat drow him fro godrich awey,</p>
<p>And barw him so þat ilke day.</p>
<span class="linenum">2680</span>
<p>But er he were fro godrich drawe<i>n</i>,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
A thousand knights slain.</p>
<p>Þer were a þousind knihtes slawe<i>n</i></p>
<p>Bi boþe halue, and mo y-nowe,</p>
<p>Þer þe ferdes to-gidere slowe.</p>
<span class="linenum">2684</span>
<p>Þer was swilk dreping of þe folk,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The pools are full of blood.</p>
<p>Þat on þe feld was neu<i>er</i>e a polk</p>
<p>Þat it ne stod of blod so ful,</p>
<p>Þat þe strem ran i<i>n</i>til þe hul.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich attacks the Danes like lightning.</p>
<span class="linenum">2688</span>
<p>Þo tarst<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note124" id="poem_tag124">124</a> bigan godrich to go</p>
<p>Vp-on þe danshe, and faste to slo,</p>
<p>And forth rith also leuin fares,</p>
<p>Þat neu<i>er</i>e kines best ne spares,</p>
<span class="linenum">2692</span>
<p>Þa<i>n</i>ne his [he] gon, for he garte alle</p>
<p>Þe denshe men biforn him falle.</p>
<p>He felde browne, he felde blake,</p>
<p>Þat he mouthe ouer-take.</p>
<span class="linenum">2696</span>
<p>Was neuere non þat mouhte þaue</p>
<p>Hise dintes, noyþer knith ne knaue,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He mows them down like grass.</p>
<p>Þat he felden so dos þe gres</p>
<span class="pagenum">77</span>
<a id="page77"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
COMBAT BETWEEN GODRICH AND HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p>Bi-forn þe syþe þat ful sharp is.</p>
<span class="linenum">2700</span>
<p>Hwan hauelok saw his folk so brittene,</p>
<p>And his ferd so swiþe littene,</p>
<p>He cam driuende up-on a stede,</p>
<p>And bigan til him to grede,</p>
<span class="linenum">2704</span>
<p>And seyde, “godrich, wat is þe</p>
<p>Þat þou fare þus with me?</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 218, col. 1.]</p>
<p>And mine gode knihtes slos,</p>
<p>Siker-like þou mis-gos.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok reproves Godrich,</p>
<span class="linenum">2708</span>
<p>Þou wost ful wel, yif þu wilt wite,</p>
<p>Þat aþelwold þe dide site</p>
<p>On knes, and sweren on messe-bok,</p>
<p>On caliz, and on [pateyn]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note125" id="poem_tag125">125</a> hok</p>
<span class="linenum">2712</span>
<p>Þat þou hise douhter sholdest yelde,</p>
<p>Þan she were wi<i>n</i>na<i>n</i><a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note126" id="poem_tag126">126</a> of
elde,</p>
<p>Engelond eueril del:</p>
<p>Godrich þe erl, þou wost it wel.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and bids him perform his oaths.</p>
<span class="linenum">2716</span>
<p>Do nu wel with-uten fiht,</p>
<p>Yeld hire þe lond, for þat is rith.</p>
<p>Wile ich forgiue þe þe lathe,</p>
<p>Al mi dede and al mi wrathe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2720</span>
<p>For y se þu art so with,</p>
<p>And of þi bodi so god knith.”</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich refuses.</p>
<p>“Þat ne wile ich neu<i>er</i>e mo,”</p>
<p>Quoth erl godrich, “for ich shal slo</p>
<span class="linenum">2724</span>
<p>Þe, and hire for-henge heye.</p>
<p>I shal þrist ut þi rith eye</p>
<p>Þat þou lokes with on me,</p>
<p>But þu swiþe heþen fle.”</p>
<span class="linenum">2728</span>
<p>He grop þe swerd ut sone anon,</p>
<p>And hew on hauelok, ful god won,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He cleaves Havelok’s shield in two.</p>
<p>So þat he clef his sheld on two:</p>
<p>Hwan hauelok saw þat shame do</p>
<span class="pagenum">78</span>
<a id="page78"> </a>
<span class="linenum">2732</span>
<p>His bodi þer bi-forn his ferd,</p>
<p>He drow ut sone his gode swerd,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok smites him down.</p>
<p>And smot him so up-on þe crune,</p>
<p>Þat godrich fel to þe erþe adune.</p>
<span class="linenum">2736</span>
<p>But godrich stirt up swiþe sket,</p>
<p>Lay he nowth longe at hise fet,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Godrich rises, and wounds Havelok in the shoulder.</p>
<p>And smot him on þe sholdre so,</p>
<p>Þat he dide þare undo</p>
<span class="linenum">2740</span>
<p>Of his brinie ri<i>n</i>ges mo,</p>
<p>Þan þat ich kan tellen fro;</p>
<p>And woundede hi<i>m</i> rith i<i>n</i> þe flesh,</p>
<p>Þat tendre was, and swiþe nesh,</p>
<span class="linenum">2744</span>
<p>So þat þe blod ran til his to:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok is enraged,</p>
<p>Þo was hauelok swiþe wo,</p>
<p>Þat he hauede of him drawen</p>
<p>Blod, and so sore him slawen.</p>
<span class="linenum">2748</span>
<p>Hertelike til him he wente,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and cuts off his foe’s hand.</p>
<p>And godrich þer fulike shente;</p>
<p>For his swerd he hof up heye,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 218, col. 2.]</p>
<p>And þe hand he dide of fleye,</p>
<span class="linenum">2752</span>
<p>Þat he smot him with so sore:</p>
<p>Hw mithe he don him shame more?</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag123" id="poem_note123" class="pginternal">123</a>
Cf. l. 1904.</p>
<p class="addition">
<a href="#poem_tag123a" id="poem_note123a" class="pginternal">*</a>
The MS. reading <i>blinne</i> should clearly be <i>blunne</i>. A few
other suggestions of emendations will be found in the <a href="#glossary" class="pginternal">Glossarial Index</a>. See the words <i>Arwe</i>,
<i>Birþe</i>, <i>Felde</i>, <i>Sor</i>, <i>Tauhte</i>, <i>Þenne</i>,
<i>Thit</i>, <i>Werewed</i>, <i>Wreken</i>, &amp;c. See also the
suggestions in the preface, pp. xxxix, xli, xlvi, xlvii.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag124" id="poem_note124" class="pginternal">124</a>
So in MS. <i>Qu.</i> faste, as in next line.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag125" id="poem_note125" class="pginternal">125</a>
MS. <i>here repeats</i> messe, <i>by mistake. Read</i> pateyn. Cf.
l. 187.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag126" id="poem_note126" class="pginternal">126</a>
MS. wīman, <i>i.e.</i> wi<i>n</i>man <i>or</i> wi<i>m</i>man; <i>but we
are sure, from</i> l. 174, <i>that</i> wi<i>n</i>nan <i>is
meant</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODRICH IS BOUND AND FETTERED.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Hwan he hauede him so shamed,</p>
<p>His hand of plat, and yuele lamed,</p>
<span class="linenum">2756</span>
<p>He tok him sone bi þe necke</p>
<p>Als a t<i>ra</i>ytour, daþeyt wo recke!</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He has him bound and fettered,</p>
<p>And dide him binde and fet<i>er</i>e wel</p>
<p>With gode feteres al of stel,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and sends him to the queen.</p>
<span class="linenum">2760</span>
<p>And to þe quen he sende him,</p>
<p>Þat birde wel to him ben grim;</p>
<p>And Bad she sholde don him gete,</p>
<p>And þat non ne sholde him bete,</p>
<span class="linenum">2764</span>
<p>Ne shame do, for he was knith,</p>
<p>Til knithes hauede<i>n</i> demd him Rith.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
When the English find out that Goldborough is the heiress,</p>
<p>Þan þe englishe men þat sawe,</p>
<span class="pagenum">79</span>
<a id="page79"> </a>
<p>Þat þei wisten, heye and lawe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2768</span>
<p>Þat Goldeboru, þat was so fayr,</p>
<p>Was of engeland rith eyr,</p>
<p>And þat þe king hire hauede wedded,</p>
<p>And haueden ben samen bedded,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
they submit to Havelok.</p>
<span class="linenum">2772</span>
<p>He comen alle to crie m<i>er</i>ci,</p>
<p>Vnto þe king, at one cri,</p>
<p>And beden him sone manrede and oth,</p>
<p>Þat he ne sholde<i>n</i>, for lef ne loth,</p>
<span class="linenum">2776</span>
<p>Neuere more ageyn him go,</p>
<p>Ne ride, for wel ne for wo.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
THE ENGLISH SUBMIT TO GOLDBOROUGH.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Þe king ne wolde nouth for-sake,</p>
<p>Þat he ne shulde of hem take</p>
<span class="linenum">2780</span>
<p>Manrede þat he beden, and ok</p>
<p>Hold oþes swere<i>n</i> on þe bok;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok wishes to show Goldborough to the English.</p>
<p>But or bad he, þat þider were brouth</p>
<p>Þe quen, for hem, swilk was his þouth,</p>
<span class="linenum">2784</span>
<p>For to se, and forto shawe,</p>
<p>Yif þat he hire wolde knawe.</p>
<p>Þoruth hem wite<i>n</i> wolde he,</p>
<p>Yif þat she aucte quen to be.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Six earls fetch her in.</p>
<span class="linenum">2788</span>
<p class="section">
Sixe erles weren sone yare,</p>
<p>After hire for to fare.</p>
<p>He nomen on-on, and come<i>n</i> sone,</p>
<p>And brouthe<i>n</i> hire, þat under mone</p>
<span class="linenum">2792</span>
<p>In al þe werd ne hauede per,</p>
<p>Of hende-leik, fer ne ner.</p>
<p>Hwan she was come þider, alle</p>
<p>Þe englishe men bi-gu<i>n</i>ne to falle</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 218<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<span class="linenum">2796</span>
<p>O knes, and grete<i>n</i> swiþe sore,</p>
<p>And seyden, “leuedi, k[r]istes ore,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The English ask her pardon.</p>
<p>And youres! we haue<i>n</i> misdo mikel,</p>
<p>Þat we ayen you haue be fikel,</p>
<span class="linenum">2800</span>
<p>For englond auhte forto ben youres,<span class="pagenum">80</span></p>
<a id="page80"> </a>
<p>And we youre me<i>n</i> and youres.</p>
<p>Is non of us, yung ne old,</p>
<p>Þat we ne wot, þat aþelwold</p>
<span class="linenum">2804</span>
<p>Was king of þis kunerike,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They admit she is heiress.</p>
<p>And ye his eyr, and þat þe swike</p>
<p>Haues it halden with mikel wro<i>n</i>ge:</p>
<p>God leue him sone to honge!”</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
GODRICH IS CONDEMNED TO BE BURNT.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">2808</span>
<p class="section">
Quot<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note127" id="poem_tag127">127</a> hauelok, “hwan þat ye it wite.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok says they must pass judgment on Godrich.</p>
<p>Nu wile ich þat ye doun site,</p>
<p>And after godrich haues wrouht,</p>
<p>Þat haues in sorwe him-self brouth,</p>
<span class="linenum">2812</span>
<p>Lokes þat ye deme<i>n</i> him rith,</p>
<p>For dom ne spared<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note128" id="poem_tag128">128</a> clerk ne knith,</p>
<p>And siþen shal ich under-stonde</p>
<p>Of you, after lawe of londe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2816</span>
<p>Manrede, and holde oþes boþe,</p>
<p>Yif ye it wilen, and ek rothe.”</p>
<p>Anon þer dune he hem sette,</p>
<p>For non þe dom ne durste lette,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They say he is to be bound on an ass’s back,</p>
<span class="linenum">2820</span>
<p>And demden him to binde<i>n</i> faste</p>
<p>Vp-on an asse swiþe un-wraste,</p>
<p>Andelong, nouht ouer-þwert,</p>
<p>His nose went unto þe stert;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
taken to Lincoln,</p>
<span class="linenum">2824</span>
<p>And so to lincolne lede,</p>
<p>Shamelike in wicke wede,</p>
<p>And hwan he cam un-to þe borw,</p>
<p>Shamelike ben led þer-þoru,</p>
<span class="linenum">2828</span>
<p>Bisouþe þe borw, un-to a grene,</p>
<p>Þat þare is yet, als[o] y wene,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
bound to a stake, and burnt.</p>
<p>And þere be bunde<i>n</i> til a stake,</p>
<p>Aboute<i>n</i> him ful gret fir make,</p>
<span class="linenum">2832</span>
<p>And al to dust be brend Rith þere;</p>
<p>And yet demde<i>n</i> he þer more,</p>
<p>Oþer swikes for to warne,</p>
<span class="pagenum">81</span>
<a id="page81"> </a>
<p>Þat hise childre<i>n</i> sulde þarne</p>
<span class="linenum">2836</span>
<p>Euere more þat eritage,</p>
<p>Þat his was, for hise utrage.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag127" id="poem_note127" class="pginternal">127</a>
MS. Guot. Cf. l. 1954.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag128" id="poem_note128" class="pginternal">128</a>
<i>Qu.</i> spares.</p>
</div>
<p class="section">
Hwan þe dom was demd and seyd,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
So he is laid on the ass, and burnt.</p>
<p>Sket was þe swike on þe asse leyd,</p>
<span class="linenum">2840</span>
<p>And [led vn-]til<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note129" id="poem_tag129">129</a> þat ilke grene,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 218<i>b</i>, col. 2.]</p>
<p>And brend til asken al bidene.</p>
<p>Þo was Goldeboru ful bliþe,</p>
<p>She þa<i>n</i>ked god fele syþe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2844</span>
<p>Þat þe fule swike was brend,</p>
<p>Þat wende wel hire bodi haue shend,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Goldborough rejoices.</p>
<p>And seyde, “nu is time to take</p>
<p>Manrede of brune and of blake,</p>
<span class="linenum">2848</span>
<p>Þat ich se ride[<i>n</i>] and go:</p>
<p>Nu ich am wreke[<i>n</i>]<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note130" id="poem_tag130">130</a> of mi fo.”</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag129" id="poem_note129" class="pginternal">129</a>
MS. “And him til,” which is nonsense. See l. 2827.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag130" id="poem_note130" class="pginternal">130</a>
See l. 2992.</p>
</div>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok makes the English swear fealty.</p>
<p class="section">
Hauelok anon ma<i>n</i>rede tok</p>
<p>Of alle englishe, on þe bok,</p>
<span class="linenum">2852</span>
<p>And dide hem grete oþes swere,</p>
<p>Þat he sholde<i>n</i> him god feyth bere</p>
<p>Ageyn alle þat wore<i>n</i> liues,</p>
<p>And þat sholde ben born of wiues.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
THE EARL OF CHESTER MARRIES GUNILD.</span></p>
<span class="linenum">2856</span>
<p class="section">
Þa<i>n</i>ne he hauede<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note131" id="poem_tag131">131</a> sikernesse</p>
<p>Taken of more and of lesse,</p>
<p>Al at hise wille, so dide he calle</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He proposes that Earl Reyner of Chester</p>
<p>Þe erl of cestre, and hise men alle,</p>
<span class="linenum">2860</span>
<p>Þat was yung knith wit-ute<i>n</i> wif,</p>
<p>And seyde, “sire erl, bi mi lif,</p>
<p>And þou wile mi <i>con</i>seyl tro,</p>
<p>Ful wel shal ich with þe do,</p>
<span class="linenum">2864</span>
<p>For ich shal yeue þe to wiue</p>
<p>Þe fairest þing that is oliue.</p>
<span class="pagenum">82</span>
<a id="page82"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK REMEMBERS BERTRAM’S KINDNESS.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
shall marry Gunild, Grim’s daughter;</p>
<p>Þat is gu<i>n</i>nild of grimesby,</p>
<p>Grimes douther, bi seint dauy!</p>
<span class="linenum">2868</span>
<p>Þat me forth broute, and wel fedde,</p>
<p>And ut of denemark with me fledde,</p>
<p>Me for to burwe fro mi ded:</p>
<p>Sikerlike, þoru his red</p>
<span class="linenum">2872</span>
<p>Haue ich liued in-to þis day,</p>
<p>Blissed worþe his soule ay!</p>
<p>I rede þat þu hire take,</p>
<p>And spuse, and curteyse make,</p>
<span class="linenum">2876</span>
<p>For she is fayr, and she is fre,</p>
<p>And al so hende so she may be.</p>
<p>Þertekene she is wel with me,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and he will then always be his friend.</p>
<p>Þat shal ich ful wel shewe þe,</p>
<span class="linenum">2880</span>
<p>For ich giue þe a giue,</p>
<p>Þat euere more hwil ich liue,</p>
<p>For hire shal-tu be with me dere,</p>
<p>Þat wile ich þat þis folc al here.”</p>
<span class="linenum">2884</span>
<p>Þe erl ne wolde nouth ageyn</p>
<p>Þe king[e] be, for knith ne sweyn,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 219, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Ne of þe spusing seyen nay,</p>
<p>But spusede [hire] þat ilke day.</p>
<span class="linenum">2888</span>
<p>Þat spusinge was god time maked,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They are married,</p>
<p>For it ne were neuere clad ne naked,</p>
<p>In a þede samened two</p>
<p>Þat cam to-gidere, liuede so,</p>
<span class="linenum">2892</span>
<p>So þey dide[<i>n</i>] al here liue:</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and have five sons.</p>
<p>He geten same<i>n</i> sones fiue,</p>
<p>Þat were þe beste me<i>n</i> at nede,</p>
<p>Þat mouthe ride<i>n</i> on ani stede.</p>
<span class="linenum">2896</span>
<p>Hwan gu<i>n</i>nild was to cestre brouth,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok remembers Bertram, the earl’s cook<ins class="mycorr" title="comma missing">, </ins></p>
<p>Hauelok þe gode ne for-gat nouth</p>
<p>Bertra<i>m</i>, þat was the erles kok,</p>
<p>Þat he ne dide calle<i>n</i> ok,</p>
<span class="linenum">2900</span>
<p>And seyde, “frend, so god me rede!</p>
<p>Nu shaltu haue riche mede,</p>
<span class="pagenum">83</span>
<a id="page83"> </a>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
BERTRAM MARRIES GRIM’S SECOND DAUGHTER.</span></p>
<p>For wissing, and þi gode dede,</p>
<p>Þat tu me dides in ful gret nede.</p>
<span class="linenum">2904</span>
<p>For þa<i>n</i>ne y yede in mi cuuel,</p>
<p>And ich ne haue[de] bred, ne sowel,</p>
<p>Ne y ne hauede no catel,</p>
<p>Þou feddes and claddes me ful wel.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
and makes him Earl of Cornwall.</p>
<span class="linenum">2908</span>
<p>Haue nu for-þi of cornwayle</p>
<p>Þe erldom ildel, with-uten fayle,</p>
<p>And al þe lond þat godrich held,</p>
<p>Boþe in towne, and ek in feld;</p>
<span class="linenum">2912</span>
<p>And þerto wile ich, þat þu spuse,</p>
<p>And fayre bring hire un-til huse,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He is to marry Levive, Grim’s daughter,</p>
<p>Grimes douther, leuiue þe hende,</p>
<p>For þider shal she with þe wende.</p>
<span class="linenum">2916</span>
<p>Hire semes curteys forto be,</p>
<p>For she is fayr so flour on tre;</p>
<p>Þe heu is swilk in hire ler</p>
<p class="sidenote">
who is as fair as a rose.</p>
<p>So [is] þe rose in roser,</p>
<span class="linenum">2920</span>
<p>Hwan it is fayr sprad ut newe</p>
<p>Ageyn þe su<i>n</i>ne, brith and lewe.”</p>
<p>And girde him sone with þe swerd</p>
<p>Of þe erldom, bi-forn his ferd,</p>
<span class="linenum">2924</span>
<p>And with his hond he made hi<i>m</i> knith,</p>
<p>And yaf him armes, for þat was rith,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
They are married.</p>
<p>And dide him þere sone wedde</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2927" id="texttag2927">2927</a>
<p>Hire þat was ful swete in bedde.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag131" id="poem_note131" class="pginternal">131</a>
MS. hauede<i>n</i>.</p>
<span class="linenum">2928</span>
<p class="section">
After þat he spused wore,</p>
<p>Wolde þe erl nouth dwelle þore,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok and Goldborough lived 100 years, and had many children.</p>
<p>But sone na<i>m</i> until his lond,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 219, col. 2.]</p>
<p>And seysed it al in his hond,</p>
<span class="linenum">2932</span>
<p>And liuede þer-i<i>n</i>ne, he and his wif,</p>
<p>An hundred winter in god lif,<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note132" id="poem_tag132">132</a></p>
<span class="pagenum">84</span>
<a id="page84"> </a>
<p>And gaten mani childre<i>n</i> samen,</p>
<p>And liueden ay in blisse and game<i>n</i>.</p>
<span class="linenum">2936</span>
<p>Hwa<i>n</i> þe maydens were spused boþe,</p>
<p>Hauelok anon bigan ful rathe</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The Danes are enriched.</p>
<p>His denshe men to feste wel</p>
<p>Wit riche landes and catel,</p>
<span class="linenum">2940</span>
<p>So þat he weren alle riche:</p>
<p>For he was large and nouth chinche.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag132" id="poem_note132" class="pginternal">132</a>
Between this line and the next are inserted in the MS. the words: <i>For
he saw þat he</i>, which have been subsequently struck out by the same
hand, and the word <i>vacat</i> affixed.</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
HAVELOK IS CROWNED KING OF ENGLAND.</span></p>
<p class="section">
Þer-after sone, with his here,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok is crowned at London.</p>
<p>For he to lundone, forto bere</p>
<span class="linenum">2944</span>
<p>Corune, so þat [alle] it sawe,</p>
<p>Henglishe ant denshe, heye and lowe,</p>
<p>Hwou he it bar with mikel pride,</p>
<p>For his barnage þat was un-ride.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The feast lasts 40 days.</p>
<span class="linenum">2948</span>
<p class="section">
Þe feste of his coruni[<i>n</i>]g<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note133" id="poem_tag133">133</a></p>
<p>Laste[de] with gret ioying</p>
<p>Fourti dawes, and sumdel mo;</p>
<p>Þo bigu<i>n</i>nen þe denshe to go</p>
<span class="linenum">2952</span>
<p>Vn-to þe king, to aske leue,</p>
<p>And he ne wolde hem nouth g<i>re</i>ue,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
The Danes return home.</p>
<p>For he saw þat he woren yare</p>
<p>In-to denemark for to fare,</p>
<span class="linenum">2956</span>
<p>But gaf hem leue sone anon,</p>
<p>And bitauhte hem seint Johan;</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Ubbe is to rule Denmark.</p>
<p>And bad ubbe, his iustise,</p>
<p>Þat he sholde on ilke wise</p>
<span class="linenum">2960</span>
<p>Denemark yeme and gete so,</p>
<p>Þat no pleynte come him to.</p>
<p class="footnote">
<a href="#poem_tag133" id="poem_note133" class="pginternal">133</a>
MS. corunig.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Havelok remained in England for sixty years.</p>
<p class="section">
Hwan he wore parted alle samen,</p>
<p>Hauelok bi-lefte wit ioie and game<i>n</i></p>
<span class="pagenum">85</span>
<a id="page85"> </a>
<span class="linenum">2964</span>
<p>In engelond, and was þer-i<i>n</i>ne</p>
<p>Sixti winter king with winne,</p>
<p>And Goldeboru quen, þat I wene:</p>
<p>So mikel loue was hem bitwene,</p>
<span class="linenum">2968</span>
<p>Þat al þe werd spak of hem two:</p>
<p>He louede hire, and she him so,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
He and Goldborough were never apart.</p>
<p>Þat neyþer oþe[r] mithe be</p>
<p>For<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note134" id="poem_tag134">134</a> oþer, ne no ioie se,</p>
<span class="linenum">2972</span>
<p>But yf he were to-gidere<a class="tag pginternal" href="#poem_note135" id="poem_tag135">135</a> boþe;</p>
<p>Neuere yete ne were<i>n</i> he wroþe,</p>
<p>For here loue was ay newe,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
[Fol. 219<i>b</i>, col. 1.]</p>
<p>Neuere yete wordes ne grewe</p>
<span class="linenum">2976</span>
<p>Bitwene hem, hwar-of ne lathe</p>
<p>Mithe rise, ne no wrathe.</p>
<div class="footnote">
<p><a href="#poem_tag134" id="poem_note134" class="pginternal">134</a>
<i>Qu.</i> Fro.</p>
<p><a href="#poem_tag135" id="poem_note135" class="pginternal">135</a>
MS. togidede.</p>
</div>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
SUCH IS THE “GESTE” OF HAVELOK.</span></p>
<p class="sidenote">
They had 15 children, all kings and queens.</p>
<p class="section">
He geten childre<i>n</i> hem bi-twene</p>
<p>Sones and douthres rith fiuetene,</p>
<span class="linenum">2980</span>
<p>Hwar-of þe sones were kinges alle,</p>
<p>So wolde god it sholde bifalle;</p>
<p>And þe douhtres alle q<i>ue</i>nes:</p>
<p>Him sto<i>n</i>des wel þat god child strenes.</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Such is the <i>geste</i> of Havelok and Goldborough.</p>
<span class="linenum">2984</span>
<p>Nu haue ye herd þe gest al þoru</p>
<p>Of hauelok and of goldeborw.</p>
<p>Hw he were<i>n</i> born, and hw fedde,</p>
<p>And hwou he wore<i>n</i> with wro<i>n</i>ge ledde</p>
<span class="linenum">2988</span>
<p>In here youþe, with trecherie,</p>
<p>With tresoun, and with felounye,</p>
<a class="texttag ital pginternal" href="#textnote2990" id="texttag2990">2990</a>
<p>And hwou þe swikes hauede<i>n</i> thit</p>
<p>Reuen hem þat was here rith,</p>
<span class="linenum">2992</span>
<p>And hwou he were<i>n</i> wreken wel,</p>
<p>Haue ich sey you eu<i>er</i>ildel;</p>
<p class="headnote"><span class="headnote">
SAY A PATERNOSTER FOR THE AUTHOR.</span></p>
<p>And forþi ich wolde biseken you,</p>
<p>Þat haue<i>n</i> herd þe rim[e] nu,</p>
<p class="sidenote">
Each of you say a <i>pater-noster</i> for the author.</p>
<span class="linenum">2996</span>
<p>Þat ilke of you, with gode wille,<span class="pagenum">86</span></p>
<a id="page86"> </a>
<p>Seye a pat<i>er</i>-nost<i>er</i> stille,</p>
<p>For him þat haueth þe rym[e] maked,</p>
<p>And þer-fore fele nihtes waked;</p>
<span class="linenum">3000</span>
<p>Þat ih<i>es</i>u c<i>ri</i>st his soule bringe</p>
<p>Bi-forn his fader at his endinge.</p>
<p class="center"><b>Amen.</b></p>
</div>
<div class="notes">
<span class="pagenum">87</span>
<a id="page87"> </a>
<h2> <a id="notes">NOTES.</a></h2>
<p class="editor">
[The following notes are abridged from the notes in Sir F. Madden’s
excellent edition, the abridgement being effected almost entirely by
occasional omissions, and with but very slight unimportant changes of a
few words, chiefly in the case of references to later editions of
various works than were existing in 1828. I have added one or two
short notes upon difficult constructions, but these are distinguished by
being enclosed within square brackets. —W. W. S.]</p>
<p class="spacer"> </p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag9" id="textnote9" class="pginternal">9.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>He was the wicteste man at nede</i></p>
<p><i>That thurte riden on ani stede.</i></p>
</div>
<p>This appears to have been a favourite expression of the poet, and to
have comprehended, in his idea, the perfection of those qualifications
required in a knight and hero. He repeats it, with some slight
variation, no less than five times, viz. in ll. 25, 87, 345, 1757, and
1970. The lines, however, are by no means original, but the common
property of all our early poetical writers. We find them in Laȝamon:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>þis wes þe feiruste mon</p>
<p>þe æuere æhte ær þusne kinedom,</p>
<p>þa he mihte beren wepnen,</p>
<p>&amp; his hors wel awilden.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Laȝamon</i>, vol. i. p. 174.</p>
<p>So also in the Romance of <i>Guy of Warwick</i>:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>He was the best knight at neede</p>
<p>That euer bestrode any stede.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
Coll. Garrick, K. 9. sign. Ll. ii.</p>
<p>Again, in the <i>Continuation of Sir Gy</i>, in the Auchinleck MS.,
(ed. for the Abbotsford Club, 1840, 4to; p. 266),</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>The best bodi he was at nede</p>
<p>That ever might bistriden stede,</p>
<p class="indent">And freest founde in fight.</p>
</div>
<p>And again, in the <i>Chronicle of England</i>, published by Ritson
from a copy in the British Museum, MS. Reg. 12. C. <span class="smallroman">XII</span>.</p>
<span class="pagenum">88</span>
<a id="page88"> </a>
<div class="verse">
<p>After him his sone Arthur</p>
<p>Hevede this lond thourh and thourh.</p>
<p>He was the beste kyng at nede</p>
<p>That ever mihte ride on stede,</p>
<p>Other wepne welde, other folk out-lede,</p>
<p>Of mon he hede he never drede.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—l. 261.</p>
<p>The very close resemblance of these lines to those in Havelok, ll.
87-90, would induce a belief that the writer of the <i>Chronicle</i> had
certainly read, and perhaps copied from, the Romance. The MS. followed
by Ritson was undoubtedly written soon after the death of Piers
Gaveston, in 1313, with the mention of which event it concludes; but in
the Auchinleck copy it is continued, by a later hand, to the minority of
Edward III. It only remains to be observed, that the poem in MS. Reg.
12. C. <span class="smallroman">XII.</span> is written by the same
identical hand as the MS. Harl. 2253 (containing <i>Kyng Horn</i>,
&amp;c.), whence some additional light is thrown on the real age of the
latter, respecting which our antiquaries so long differed.</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag15" id="textnote15" class="pginternal">15.</a>
“And I will drink ere I tell my tale.” <i>Her</i> = ere.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag19" id="textnote19" class="pginternal">19.</a>
<i>And wite, &amp;c.</i>, i.e. And ordain that it may be so; cf. ll.
517, 1316. Both metre and grammar require the final <i>e</i>.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag31" id="textnote31" class="pginternal">31.</a>
<i>Erl and barun, <b>dreng</b> and kayn.</i> The appellation of
<i>Dreng</i>, and, in the plural, <i>Drenges</i>, which repeatedly
occurs in the course of this poem, is uniformly bestowed on a class of
men who hold a situation between the rank of <i>Baron</i> and
<i>Thayn</i>. We meet with the term more than once in Doomsday Book, as,
for instance, in Tit. Cestresc: “Hujus manerii [Neuton] aliam terram xv.
hom. quos <i>Drenches</i> vocabant, pro xv. maneriis tenebant.” And in a
Charter of that period we read: “Alger Prior, et totus Conventus
Ecclesiæ S. Cuthberti, Edwino, et omnibus Teignis et <i>Drengis</i>,
&amp;c.” Hence Spelman infers, that the Drengs were military vassals,
and held land by knight’s service, which was called <i>Drengagium</i>.
This is confirmed by a document from the Chartulary of Welbeck, printed
in Dugdale, <i>Mon. Angl.</i> V. <span class="smallroman">II.</span> p. 598, and in Blount, <i>Jocular
Tenures</i>, p. 177, where it is stated, “In eadem villa [Cukeney,
co. Nottingh.] manebat quidam homo qui vocabatur Gamelbere, et fuit
vetus <i>Dreyinghe</i> ante Conquestum.” It appears from the same
document, that this person held two carucates of land of the King <i>in
capite</i>, and was bound to perform military service for the same,
whenever the army went into Wales. In the Epistle also from the Monks of
Canterbury to Henry II. printed by Somner, in his Treatise on Gavelkind,
p. 123, we find: “Quia vero non erant adhuc tempore Regis Willelmi
Milites in Anglia, sed <i>Threnges</i>, præcepit Rex, ut de eis Milites
fierent, ad terram defendendam.” In Laȝamon’s translation of Wace the
term is frequently used in the acceptation of thayn, and spelt either
<i>dringches</i>, <i>drenches</i>, <i>dranches</i>, or <i>dringes</i>.
[Cf. Sw. <i>dräng</i>, a man, servant; Dan. <i>dreng</i>, a boy.] In the
Isl. and Su. Goth. <i>Dreng</i> originally signified <i>vir fortis</i>,
<i>miles strenuus</i>, and hence Olaf, King of Norway, received the
epithet of <i>Goddreng</i>. See Wormii Lex. Run. p. 26. Ihre, Vet.
Cat. Reg.
<span class="pagenum">89</span>
<a id="page89"> </a>
p. 109. Langebek, Script. Rer. Danic. V. <span class="smallroman">I.</span> p. 156. The term subsequently was applied to
persons in a servile condition, and is so instanced by Spelman, as used
in Denmark. In this latter sense it may be found in Hickes, Diction.
Isl., and in Sir David Lyndsay’s Poems,</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Quhilk is not ordanit for <i>dringis</i></p>
<p>But for Duikis, Empriouris, and Kingis.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
V. Pinkerton’s <ins class="mycorr" title="not an error">Scotish</ins> Poems Reprinted, ii. 97.</p>
<p>V. Jamieson, Dict. <i>in voce</i>.</p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag45" id="textnote45" class="pginternal">45.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>In that time a man that bore</i></p>
<p>(<i>Wel fyfty pund, y woth, or more.</i>)</p>
</div>
<p>This insertion receives additional authority from a similar passage
in the Romance of <i>Guy of Warwick</i>, where it is mentioned as a
proof of the rigorous system of justice pursued by Earl Sigard,</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Though a man bore an hundred pound,</p>
<p>Upon him of gold so round,</p>
<p>There n’as man in all this land</p>
<p>That durst him do shame no schonde.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
Ellis, <i>Metr. Rom.</i> V. <span class="smallroman">II.</span> p. 9.
Ed. 1811.</p>
<p>Many of the traits here attributed to Athelwold appear to be borrowed
from the praises so universally bestowed by our ancient historians on
the character of King Alfred, in whose time, as Otterbourne writes,
p. 52, “armillas aureas in bivio stratas vel suspensas, nemo
abripere est ausus.” Cf. <i>Annal. Eccl. Roffens.</i> MS. Cott. Nero, D.
<span class="smallroman">II.</span> The same anecdote is related of
Rollo, Duke of Normandy, by Guillaume de Jumieges, and Dudon de Saint
Quentin.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag91" id="textnote91" class="pginternal">91.</a>
<i>Sprong forth so sparke of glede.</i> Cf. l. 870. It is a very common
metaphor in early English poetry.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>He sprong forð an stede,</p>
<p class="indent">swa sparc ded of fure,</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Laȝamon</i> v. ii. p. 565.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>He sprange als any sparke one glede.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Sir Isumbras</i>, st. 39 (Camd. Soc. 1844)</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>He spronge as sparkle doth of glede,</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>K. of Tars</i>, l. 194.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>And lepte out of the arsoun,</p>
<p class="indent">As sperk thogh out of glede.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Ly Beaus Desconus</i>, l. 623.</p>
<p>Cf. Chaucer, Cant. Tales, l. 13833, and Tyrwhitt’s note.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag110" id="textnote110" class="pginternal">110.</a> <i>Of his bodi</i>, &amp;c. Compare the French
text, l. 208.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Mes entre eus n’eurent enfant</p>
<p>Mes qe vne fille bele;</p>
<p>Argentille out non la pucele.</p>
<p>Rois Ekenbright fut enfermez,</p>
<p>Et de grant mal forment greuez;</p>
<p>Bien siet n’en poet garrir.</p>
</div>
<span class="pagenum">90</span>
<a id="page90"> </a>
<p>[Here <i>Argentille</i> is <i>Goldborough</i>, and <i>Ekenbright</i>
answers to <i>Athelwold</i>. This quotation, and others below, shewing
the passages of the French text which most nearly resemble the English
poem, are from a MS. in the Herald’s College, marked E. D. N. No. 14.
See the Preface.]</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag118" id="textnote118" class="pginternal">118.</a> <i>Wat shal me to rede</i>, lit. what shall be
for a counsel to me. See <i>Rede</i> in the Glossary to <i>William of
Palerne</i>.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag130" id="textnote130" class="pginternal">130.</a> <i>And don hem of þar hire were queme</i>, lit.
and do them off where it should be agreeable to her; i.e. and keep men
at a distance as she pleased. Such seems to me the meaning of this
hitherto unexplained line.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag132" id="textnote132" class="pginternal">132.</a> For <i>me</i> we ought probably to read
<i>hit</i>.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag136" id="textnote136" class="pginternal">136.</a> <i>He sende <b>writes</b> sone onon.</i> We must
here, and in l. 2275, simply understand <i>letters</i>, without any
reference to the official summonses of parliament, which subsequently
were so termed, <span class="greek" title="kat’ exochên">κατ’
εξοχην</span>. The word <i>briefs</i> is used in the same sense by the
old French writers, and in Laȝamon we meet with some lines nearly
corresponding with the present; see ll. 6669-6678.</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag175" id="textnote175" class="pginternal">175.</a> <i>þa</i>. Frequently written for <i>þat</i>. See
<i>William of Palerne</i>.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag189" id="textnote189" class="pginternal">189-203.</a> <i>Ther-on he garte</i>, &amp;c. Compare the
French Romance, ll. 215-228.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Sa fille li ad comandée,</p>
<p>Et sa terre tote liuerée.</p>
<p>Primerement li fet iurer,</p>
<p>Veiant sa gent &amp; affier,</p>
<p>Qe leaument la nurrireit,</p>
<p>Et sa terre lui gardereit,</p>
<p>Tant q’ele fust de tiel age</p>
<p>Qe suffrir porroit mariage.</p>
<p>Quant la pucele seit granz,</p>
<p>Par le consail de ses tenanz,</p>
<p>Au plus fort home la dorroit</p>
<p>Qe el reaume troueroit;</p>
<p>Qu’il li baillast ses citez,</p>
<p>Ses chasteus &amp; ses fermetez.</p>
</div>
<p class="spacer"> </p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag263" id="textnote263" class="pginternal">263.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>Justises dede he maken newe,</i></p>
<p><i>Al Engelond to faren thorw.</i></p>
</div>
<p>The earliest instance produced by Dugdale of the Justices Itinerant,
is in 23 Hen. II. 1176, when by the advice of the Council held at
Northampton, the realm was divided into six parts, and into each were
sent three Justices. <i>Orig. Judic.</i> p. 51. This is stated on
the authority of Hoveden. Dugdale admits however the custom to have been
older, and in Gervasius Dorobernensis, we find, in 1170, certain
persons, called <i>inquisitores</i>, appointed to perambulate England.
Gervase of Tilbury, or whoever was the author of the <i>Dialogus de
Scaccario</i>, calls them <i>deambulantes, vel perlustrantes
judices</i>. See Spelman, <i>in voc</i>. The office continued to the
time of Edward III., when it was superseded by that of the Justices of
Assize.</p>
<span class="pagenum">91</span>
<a id="page91"> </a>
<p><a href="#texttag280" id="textnote280" class="pginternal">280.</a> <i>The kinges douther</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the Fr.
l. 283.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p class="indent3">Argentille,</p>
<p>La meschine qu’ert sa fille,</p>
<p>Que ia estoit creue &amp; grant,</p>
<p>Et bien poeit auoir enfant.</p>
</div>
<p>[<a href="#texttag338" id="textnote338" class="pginternal">338.</a> <i>Sawe</i>, put for “Say we.” Cf. <i>biddi</i>
for “bidde i,” l. 484; <i>hauedet</i> for “hauede it,” 714;
&amp;c.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag365" id="textnote365" class="pginternal">365.</a> <i>His quiste</i>, &amp;c. “His bequest made, and
(things) distributed for him.”]</p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag433" id="textnote433" class="pginternal">433.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>Crist warie him with his mouth!</i></p>
<p><i>Waried wrthe he of north and suth!</i></p>
</div>
<p>So, in the Romance of Merlin, Bishop Brice curses the enemies of
Arthur,</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Ac, for he is king, and king’s son,</p>
<p>Y curse alle, and y dom</p>
<p>His enemies with Christes mouth,</p>
<p>By East, by West, by North, and South!</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
Ellis, <i>Metr. Rom.</i> V. <span class="smallroman">I.</span> p.
260.</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag506" id="textnote506" class="pginternal">506.</a> For <i>nouth</i> we must read <i>mouth</i> or
<i>wolde</i>. The sense is— “He thought that he would he were
dead, except that he might not (<i>or</i> would not) slay him with his
(own) hand.”</p>
<p><a href="#texttag550" id="textnote550" class="pginternal">550.</a> The sense is— “When he had done that deed
(i.e. gagged the child), <i>then</i> the deceiver had commanded him,”
&amp;c.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag560" id="textnote560" class="pginternal">560.</a> <i>with</i> may mean <i>knowest</i>, but this
hardly gives sense. Perhaps we should read <i>wilt</i>, i.e. “As thou
wilt have (preserve) my life.”</p>
<p><a href="#texttag567" id="textnote567" class="pginternal">567.</a> Mr Morris suggests that the riming words are
<i>adoun</i> and <i>croune</i>. We might then read—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>“And caste þe knaue so harde adoun,</p>
<p>þat he crakede þer hise croune.”]</p>
</div>
<p><a href="#texttag591" id="textnote591" class="pginternal">591.</a> <i>Of hise mouth</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the Fr. l.
71. sq.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Totes les houres q’il dormoit,</p>
<p>Vne flambe de lui issoit.</p>
<p>Par la bouche li venoit fors,</p>
<p>Si grant chalur auoit el cors.</p>
<p>La flambe rendoit tiel odour,</p>
<p>Onc ne sentit nul home meillour.</p>
</div>
<p><a href="#texttag676" id="textnote676" class="pginternal">676.</a> <i>And with thi chartre make (me) fre.</i>
Instances of the manumission of villains or slaves by charter may be
found in Hickes, <i>Diss. Epistol.</i> p. 12, Lye’s Dict. <i>ad
calc.</i>, and Madox’s <i>Formulare Anglicanum</i>, p. 750. The
practice was common in the Saxon times, and existed so late as the reign
of Henry VIII.</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag694" id="textnote694" class="pginternal">694.</a> <i>Wite he him onliue</i>, if he knows him (to
be) alive.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag701" id="textnote701" class="pginternal">701.</a> It is evident that the words <i>and gate</i> =
and goats, must be supplied. For the spelling <i>gate</i>, cf. <i>Pricke
of Conscience</i>, ed. Morris, l. 6134, where <i>gayte</i> is used
collectively as a plural.]</p>
<span class="pagenum">92</span>
<a id="page92"> </a>
<p><a href="#texttag706" id="textnote706" class="pginternal">706.</a> <i>Hise ship</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the Fr. l.
89.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Grim fet niefs apparailler,</p>
<p>Et de viande bien charger.</p>
</div>
<p><a href="#texttag715" id="textnote715" class="pginternal">715-720.</a> <i>Hauelok the yunge</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the
Fr. ll. 97-105.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Quant sa nief fut apparaillée,</p>
<p>Dedenz fist entrer sa meisnée,</p>
<p>Ses cheualers &amp; ses serganz,</p>
<p>Sa femme demeine &amp; ses enfanz:</p>
<p>La reyne mist el batel,</p>
<p>Haueloc tint souz son mantel.</p>
<p>Il meismes apres entra,</p>
<p>A Dieu del ciel se comanda,</p>
<p>Del hauene sont desancré,</p>
<p>Car il eurent bon orré.</p>
</div>
<p>Instead of the storm, in the French text Grim’s ship is attacked by
pirates, who kill the whole of the crew, with the exception of himself
and family, whom they spare on the score of his being an old
acquaintance.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag733" id="textnote733" class="pginternal">733-749.</a> <i>In Humber</i>, &amp;c. So in the Fr.
<i>Ceo fut el north</i>, &amp;c. Cf. ll. 122-135.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Tant out nagé &amp; tant siglé,</p>
<p>Q’en vne hauene out parvenu,</p>
<p>Et de la nief a terre issu.</p>
<p>Ceo fut el North, a Grimesbi;</p>
<p>A icel tens qe ieo vus di,</p>
<p>Ni out onques home habité,</p>
<p>Ne cele hauene n’ert pas haunté.</p>
<p>Il i adresca primes maison,</p>
<p>De lui ad Grimesbi a non.</p>
<p>Quant Grim primes i ariua,</p>
<p>En .ii. moitez sa nief trencha,</p>
<p>Les chiefs en ad amont drescé,</p>
<p>Iloec dedenz s’est herbergé.</p>
<p>Pescher aloit sicome il soloit,</p>
<p>Siel vendoit &amp; achatoit.</p>
</div>
<p class="spacer"> </p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag753" id="textnote753" class="pginternal">753.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>He took the sturgiun and the qual,</i></p>
<p><i>And the turbut, and lax withal,</i></p>
<p><i>He tok the sele, and the hwel</i>, &amp;c.</p>
</div>
<p>The list of fish here enumerated may be increased from l. 896, and
presents us with a sufficiently accurate notion of the different species
eaten in the 13th century. Each of the names will be considered
separately in the Glossary, and it is only intended here to make a few
remarks on those, which in the present day appear rather strangely to
have found a place on the tables of our ancestors. The sturgeon is well
known to have been esteemed a dainty, both in England and France, and
specially appropriated to the King’s service, but that the whale, the
seal, and the porpoise
<span class="pagenum">93</span>
<a id="page93"> </a>
should have been rendered palatable, excites our astonishment. Yet that
the whale was caught for that purpose, appears not only from the present
passage, but also from the Fabliau intitled <i>Bataille de Charnage et
de Caresme</i>, written probably about the same period, and printed by
Barbazan. It is confirmed, as we learn from Le Grand, by the French
writers; and even Rabelais, near three centuries later, enumerates the
whale among the dishes eaten by the Gastrolatres. In the list of fish
also published by Le Grand from a MS. of the 13th century, and which
corresponds remarkably with the names in the Romance, we meet with the
<i>Baleigne</i>. See <i>Vie Privée des François</i>, T. <span class="smallroman">II.</span> sect. 8.</p>
<p>Among the articles at Archbishop Nevil’s Feast, 6 Edw. IV., we find,
<i>Porposes and Seales <span class="smallroman">XII.</span></i> and at
that of Archbishop Warham, held in 1504, is an item: <i>De Seales &amp;
Porposs. prec. in gross <span class="smallroman">XXVI.</span> s. <span class="smallroman">VIII.</span> d.</i> Champier asserts that the Seal
was eaten at the Court of Francis I., so that the taste of the two
nations seems at this period to have been nearly the same. For the
courses of fish in England during the 14th and 15th centuries, see
Pegge’s <i>Form of Cury</i>, and Warner’s <i>Antiquitates Culinariæ</i>,
to which we may add MS. Sloane, 1986. [<i>Cf.</i> <i>Babees Book,
&amp;c.</i>, ed. Furnivall, 1868, p. 153.]</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag784" id="textnote784" class="pginternal">784.</a> For <i>setes</i> we should probably read
<i>seten</i> or <i>sette</i>, which would be as good a rime as many
others. The scribe has probably made the rime more perfect than the
sense. It must mean, “In the sea were they oft set.” We cannot here
suppose <i>setes</i> = <i>set es</i> = set them.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag839" id="textnote839" class="pginternal">839.</a> <i>And seyde, Hauelok, dere sone.</i> In the
French, Grim sends Havelok away for quite a different reason, viz.
because he does not understand fishing.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag903" id="textnote903" class="pginternal">903.</a> <i>The kok stod</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the Fr. l.
242.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Et vn keu le roi le retint,</p>
<p>Purceo qe fort le vist &amp; grant,</p>
<p>Et mult le vist de bon semblant.</p>
<p>Merueillous fes poeit leuer,</p>
<p>Busche tailler, ewe porter.</p>
</div>
<p>The last line answers to l. 942 of the English version.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag939" id="textnote939" class="pginternal">939.</a> <i>He bar the turues, he bar the star.</i> The
meaning of the latter term will be best illustrated by a passage in
Moor’s <i>Suffolk Words</i>, where, under the word <i>Bent</i>, he
writes, “<i>Bent</i> or <i>Starr</i>, on the N.W. coast of England, and
especially in Lancashire, is a coarse reedy shrub—like <ins class="mycorr" title="i.e. the kind we have in Suffolk (quoting Moor)">ours</ins>
perhaps—of some importance formerly, if not now,
on the sandy blowing lands of those counties. Its fibrous roots give
some cohesion to the silicious soil. By the 15 and 16 G. II.
c. 33, plucking up and carrying away <i>Starr</i> or Bent, or
having it in possession within five miles of the sand hills, was
punishable by fine, imprisonment, and whipping.” The use stated in the
Act to which the <i>Starr</i> was applied, is, “making of Mats, Brushes,
and Brooms or Besoms,” therefore it might very well be adapted to the
purposes of a kitchen, and from its being coupled with turves in the
poem, was perhaps sometimes burnt for fuel. The origin of the word is
Danish, and still exists in the Dan. <i>Stær</i>, Swed. <i>Starr</i>,
Isl.
<span class="pagenum">94</span>
<a id="page94"> </a>
<ins class="mycorr" title="error for ‘stær’?"><i>staer</i></ins>,
a species of sedge, or broom, called by Lightfoot, p. 560,
