<i>carex cespitosa</i>. Perhaps it is this shrub alluded to in the
Romance of <i>Kyng Alisaunder</i>, and this circumstance will induce us
to assign its author to the district in which the Starr is found.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>The speris craketh swithe thikke,</p>
<p>So doth on hegge <i>sterre-stike</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—l. 4438.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag945" id="textnote945" class="pginternal">945.</a> <i>of alle men</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the Fr. l.
254.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Tant estoit franc &amp; deboneire,</p>
<p>Que tuz voloit lur pleisir fere,</p>
<p>Pur la franchise q’il out.</p>
</div>
<p><a href="#texttag959" id="textnote959" class="pginternal">959.</a> <i>Of him ful wide the word sprong.</i> A phrase
which from the Saxon times occurs repeatedly in all our old writers. A
few examples may suffice.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Beowulf wæs breme,</p>
<p>Blæd wíde sprang.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Beowulf</i>, ed. Thorpe, p. 2.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Welle wide sprong þas eorles word.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Laȝamon</i>, l. 26242.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Of a knight is that y mene,</p>
<p>His name is sprong wel wide.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Sir Tristrem</i>, st. 2, p. 12.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>The word of Horn wide sprong,</p>
<p>How he was bothe michel and long.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Horn Childe</i>, ap. Rits. <i>Metr. Rom.</i> V. iii. p. 291.</p>
<p>See also the <i>Kyng of Tars</i>, ll. 19, 1007, <i>Emare</i>,
l. 256, <i>Roland and Ferragus</i>, as quoted by Ellis, <i>Ly beaus
Desconus</i>, l. 172, and <i>Chronicle of England</i>,
l. 71.</p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag984" id="textnote984" class="pginternal">984.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>In armes him noman (ne) nam,</i></p>
<p><i>þat he doune sone ne caste.</i></p>
</div>
<p>The same praise is bestowed on Havelok in the French text,
l. 265,—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Deuant eus liuter le fesoient</p>
<p>As plus forz homes q’il sauoient,</p>
<p>Et il trestouz les abatit—</p>
</div>
<p>and it was doubtless in imitation or ridicule of the qualities
attributed to similar heroes, that Chaucer writes of Sir Thopas, “Of
wrastling was ther non his per.” Cant. Tales, l. 13670.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1006" id="textnote1006" class="pginternal">1006.</a> <i>To ben þer at þe parlement.</i> Cf. l. 1178.
If we examine our historical records, we shall find that the only
parliament held at Lincoln was in the year 1300, 28 Edw. I., and
the writs to the <i>Archbishop of York</i>, and other Nobles, both
ecclesiastical and secular, are still extant. The proceedings are
detailed at some length by Robert of Brunne, Vol. <span class="smallroman">II.</span> p. 312, who might have been in Lincoln at
the time, or, at all events, was sufficiently informed of all that took
place, from his residence in the
<span class="pagenum">95</span>
<a id="page95"> </a>
county. If we could suppose that the author of the Romance alluded to
this very parliament, it would reduce the period of the poem’s
composition to a later date, than either the style or the writing of the
MS. will possibly admit of. It is therefore far more probable the writer
here makes use of a poetical, and very pardonable licence, in
transferring the parliament to the chief city of the county in which he
was evidently born, or brought up, without any reference whatever to
historical data.</p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag1022" id="textnote1022" class="pginternal">1022.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>Biforn here fet þanne lay a tre,</i></p>
<p><i>And putten with a mikel ston</i>, &amp;c.</p>
</div>
<p>This game of <i>putting the stone</i>, is of the highest antiquity,
and seems to have been common at one period to the whole of England,
although subsequently confined to the Northern counties, and to
Scotland. Fitzstephen enumerates casting of stones among the amusements
of the Londoners in the 12th century, and Dr Pegge, in a note on the
passage, calls it “a Welch custom.” The same sport is mentioned by
Geoffrey of Monmouth, among the diversions pursued at King Arthur’s
feast, as will appear in a subsequent note (l. 2320). By an edict
of Edward III. the practice of casting stones, wood, and iron, was
forbidden, and the use of the bow substituted, yet this by no means
superseded the former amusement, which was still in common use in the
16th century, as appears from Strutt’s <i>Popular Pastimes</i>, Introd.
pp. xvii, xxxix, and p. 56, sq. In the Highlands this sport
appears to have been longer kept up than in any other part of Britain,
and Pennant, describing their games, writes, “Those retained are,
throwing the <i>putting-stone</i>, or stone of strength (<i>Cloch
neart</i>) as they call it, which occasions an emulation who can throw a
weighty one the farthest.” <i>Tour in Scotl.</i> p. 214. 4to. 1769.
See also <i>Statist. Account of Argyleshire</i>, xi. 287. In the French
Romance of Horn, preserved in MS. Harl. 527, is almost a similar
incident to the one in Havelok, and would nearly amount to a proof, that
Tomas, the writer of the French text of Horn, was an Englishman.</p>
<p>In the Romance of <i>Octovian Imperator</i> it is said of
Florent,</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>At <i>wrestelyng</i>, and at <i>ston castynge</i></p>
<p>He wan the prys, without lesynge;</p>
<p>Ther n’as nother old ne yynge</p>
<p class="indent">So mochell of strength,</p>
<p>That myght the ston to hys <i>but</i> bryng,</p>
<p class="indent">Bi fedeme lengthe.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—l. 895.</p>
<p>It is singular enough, that the circumstance of Havelok’s throwing
the stone, mentioned in the Romance, should have been founded on, or
preserved in, a local tradition, as attested by Robert of Brunne,
p. 26.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Men sais in Lyncoln castelle ligges ȝit a stone,</p>
<p>That Hauelok kast wele forbi euerilkone.</p>
</div>
<p><a href="#texttag1077" id="textnote1077" class="pginternal">1077-1088.</a> <i>The king Athelwald</i>, &amp;c. Comp.
the Fr. text, ll. 354-370.</p>
<span class="pagenum">96</span>
<a id="page96"> </a>
<div class="verse">
<p>Quant Ekenbright le roi fini,</p>
<p>En ma garde sa fille mist;</p>
<p>Vn serement iurer me fist,</p>
<p>Q’au plus fort home le dorroie,</p>
<p>Qe el reaume trouer porroie.</p>
<p>Assez ai quis &amp; demandé,</p>
<p>Tant q’en ai vn fort troué;</p>
<p>Vn valet ai en ma quisine,</p>
<p>A qui ieo dorrai la meschine; &amp;c.</p>
</div>
<p><a href="#texttag1103" id="textnote1103" class="pginternal">1103.</a> <i>After Goldeborw</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the Fr.
l. 377.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Sa niece lur fet amener,</p>
<p>Et a Cuaran esposer;</p>
<p>Pur lui auiler &amp; honir,</p>
<p>La fist la nuit lez lui gesir.</p>
</div>
<p>The French Romance differs here very considerably from the English,
and in the latter, the dream of Argentille, her visit to the hermit, and
the conversation relative to Havelok’s parents, is entirely omitted.</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag1174" id="textnote1174" class="pginternal">1174.</a> This may mean—“He (Havelok) is given to
her, and she has taken (him)”—but this makes <i>yaf</i> and
<i>tok</i> past participles, which they properly are not; or else we
must translate it—“He (Godard) gave them to her, and she took
them,” i.e. the pence. This alone is the grammatical construction, and
it suits the context best; observe, that the words <i>ys</i> and
<i>as</i> are equivalent to <i>es</i> = them. Cf. l. 970. See
Morris; <i>Gen. &amp; Exod.</i>, Pref. p. xviii.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1203" id="textnote1203" class="pginternal">1203.</a> <i>Thanne he komen there</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the
Fr. l. 556.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>A Grimesby s’en alerent;</p>
<p>Mes li prodoms estoit finiz,</p>
<p>Et la Dame q’is out nurriz.</p>
<p>Kelloc sa fille i ont trouée,</p>
<p>Vn marchant l’out esposée.</p>
</div>
<p>The marriage of Kelloc, Grim’s daughter, with a merchant is skilfully
intro­duced in the French, and naturally leads to the mention of
Denmark. The plot of the English story is wholly dissimilar in this
respect.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1247" id="textnote1247" class="pginternal">1247.</a> <i>On the nith</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the Fr. l.
381.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Quant couché furent ambedui,</p>
<p>Cele out grant honte de lui,</p>
<p>Et il assez greindre de li.</p>
<p>As deuz se geut, si se dormi.</p>
<p>Ne voloit pas q’ele veist</p>
<p>La flambe qe de lui issist.</p>
</div>
<p>The voice of the angel is completely an invention of the English
author, and the dream (which is transferred from Argentille to Havelok)
is altogether different in its detail.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1260" id="textnote1260" class="pginternal">1260.</a> <i>He beth heyman</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the Fr. l.
521.</p>
<span class="pagenum">97</span>
<a id="page97"> </a>
<div class="verse">
<p>Il est né de real lignage,</p>
<p>Oncore auera grant heritage.</p>
<p>Grant gent fra vers li encline,</p>
<p>Il serra roi &amp; tu reyne.</p>
</div>
<p>[<a href="#texttag1334" id="textnote1334" class="pginternal">1334.</a> The words <i>euere-il del</i> are corruptly
repeated from line 1330 above. Perhaps we should read <i>wit-uten
were</i>, i.e. without doubt.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1430" id="textnote1430" class="pginternal">1430.</a> <i>Hauede go for him gold ne fe.</i> Cf. l. 44.
So in Laȝamon:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Ne sculde him neoðer gon fore</p>
<p>Gold ne na gærsume, &amp;c.;</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—vol. ii. p. 537.</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag1444" id="textnote1444" class="pginternal">1444.</a> The French text helps but little to supply the
blank. It shows that Havelok and his wife sailed to Denmark, and, on
their arrival, sought out the castle belonging to Sigar, who answers to
the Ubbe of the English version.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1632" id="textnote1632" class="pginternal">1632.</a> <i>A gold ring drow he forth anon</i>, &amp;c.
A similar incident, and in nearly the same words, occurs in Sir
Tristrem.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>A ring he raught him tite,</p>
<p>The porter seyd nought nay,</p>
<p class="indent3">In hand:</p>
<p>He was ful wis, y say,</p>
<p>That first yave yift in land.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—fytte i. st. 57, p. 39.</p>
<p>So also Wyntoun, who relates the subsidy of 40,000 moutons sent from
France to Scotland in 1353, and adds,</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Qwha gyvis swilk gyftyis he is wyse.</p>
</div>
<p>[See also <i>Piers Plowman</i>, Text A. iii. 202.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1646" id="textnote1646" class="pginternal">1646.</a> <i>Hw he was wel of bones</i>, &amp;c. Comp.
the Fr. l. 743.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Gent cors &amp; bele feture,</p>
<p>Lungs braz &amp; grant furcheure</p>
<p>Ententiuement l’esgarda.</p>
</div>
<p>[<a href="#texttag1678" id="textnote1678" class="pginternal">1678.</a> This line has two syllables too little.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1722" id="textnote1722" class="pginternal">1722.</a> <i>Thanne he were set</i>, &amp;c. This is an
amplification of the Fr. l. 677, sq.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Quant fut houre del manger,</p>
<p>Et qe tuz alerent lauer,</p>
<p>Li prodoms a manger s’assist,</p>
<p>Les .iii. valez seeir i fist,</p>
<p>Argentille lez son seignur;</p>
<p>Serui furent a grant honur.</p>
</div>
<p><a href="#texttag1726" id="textnote1726" class="pginternal">1726.</a> <i>Kranes, swannes, veneysun</i>, &amp;c. We
have here the principal constituents of what formed the banquets of our
ancestors. The old Romances abound with descriptions of this nature,
which coincide exactly with the present. See <i>Richard Cœur de
Lion</i>, l. 4221; <i>Guy of Warwick; The Squyr of Lowe Degre</i>,
l. 317; and <i>Morte Arthure</i>, ed. Perry, p. 7.</p>
<span class="pagenum">98</span>
<a id="page98"> </a>
<p>“Wine is common,” says Dr Pegge, speaking of the entertainments of
the 14th century, “both red and white. This article they partly had of
their own growth, and partly by importation from France and Greece.” A
few examples will illustrate this:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>He laid the cloth, and set forth bread,</p>
<p>And also wine, both <i>white and red</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Sir Degore</i>, ap. Ellis, <i>Metr. Rom.</i> V. 3, p. 375.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>And dronke wyn, and eke pyment,</p>
<p><i>Whyt and red</i>, al to talent.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Kyng Alisaunder</i>, l. 4178.</p>
<p>[Cf. <i>Piers Plowman</i>, Text B, at the end of the
<i>Prologue</i>.]</p>
<p>In the <i>Squyr of Lowe Degre</i> is a long list of these wines,
which has received considerable illustration in the curious work of Dr
Henderson.</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag1736" id="textnote1736" class="pginternal">1736.</a> I print <i>kiwing</i>, as in Sir F. Madden’s
edition; but I quite give up the meaning of it, and doubt if it is put
for <i>kirving</i>. The word is obscurely written, and looks like
<i>kilþing</i>, and my impression is that it is miswritten for <i>ilk
þing</i>, the word <i>þe</i> being put for <i>þer</i>, as frequently
elsewhere. We should thus get <i>hwan he haueden þer ilk þing deled</i>,
when they had there distributed every thing. This is, at any rate, the
sense of the passage.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1749" id="textnote1749" class="pginternal">1749.</a> <i>And sende him unto the greyues.</i> In the
French, Havelok is simply sent to an <i>ostel</i>, and the <i>greyve</i>
does not appear in the story.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1806" id="textnote1806" class="pginternal">1806.</a> <i>Hauelok lifte up</i>, &amp;c. In the French,
all the amusing details relative to Robert and Huwe Raven are omitted,
and Havelok is made to retire to a monastery, where he defends himself
by throwing down the stones on his assailants.</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag1826" id="textnote1826" class="pginternal">1826.</a> <i>wolde</i>, offered at, intended to hit,
<i>would</i> have hit.]</p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag1838" id="textnote1838" class="pginternal">1838.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>And shoten on him, so don on bere</i></p>
<p><i>Dogges, that wolden him to-tere<ins class="mycorr" title="text has superfluous closing bracket">. </ins></i></p>
</div>
<p>The same comparison is made use of in the Romance of Horn Childe:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>The Yrise folk about him yode,</p>
<p>As hondes do to bare.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
Rits. <i>Metr. Rom.</i> V. <span class="smallroman">III.</span> p.
289.</p>
<p>See <a href="#textnote2320" class="pginternal">Note on l. 2320</a>.</p>
<p>[<a href="#texttag1914" id="textnote1914" class="pginternal">1914.</a> “Cursed be he who cares! for they deserved it!
What did they? There were they worried.” A mark of interrogation seems
required after <i>dide he</i>.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag1926" id="textnote1926" class="pginternal">1926-1930.</a> <i>Sket cam tiding</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the
Fr. l. 719.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>La nouele vint a chastel,</p>
<p>Au seneschal, qui n’est pas bel,</p>
<p>Qe cil qu’il auoit herbergé</p>
<p>Cinc de ses homes out tué.</p>
</div>
<p>[<a href="#texttag1932" id="textnote1932" class="pginternal">1932.</a> Apparently corrupt. Perhaps <i>is</i> should be
<i>it</i>. “That this strife—as to what it meant.”]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2045" id="textnote2045" class="pginternal">2045.</a> <i>That weren of Kaym kin and Eues.</i> The
odium affixed to
<span class="pagenum">99</span>
<a id="page99"> </a>
the supposed progeny of Cain, and the fables engrafted on it, owe their
origin to the theological opinions of the Middle Ages, which it is not
worth while to trace to their authors. See <i>Beowulf</i>, ed. Thorpe,
p. 8; and <i>Piers Plowman</i>, A. <span class="smallroman">X.</span> 135-156; answering to p. 177 of Whitaker’s
edition. See also the Romance of <i>Kyng Alisaunder</i>:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>And of Sab the duk Mauryn,</p>
<p>He was of <i>Kaymes kunrede</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—l. 1932.</p>
<p>In <i>Ywaine and Gawaine</i>, l. 559, the Giant is called “the karl
of <i>Kaymes kyn</i>,” and so also in a poem printed by Percy, intitled
<i>Little John Nobody</i>, written about the year 1550.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Such caitives count to be come of Cain’s kind.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Anc. Reliq.</i> V. <span class="smallroman">II.</span> p. 130. Ed.
1765.</p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag2076" id="textnote2076" class="pginternal">2076.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>It ne shal no thing ben bitwene</i></p>
<p><i>Thi bour and min, also y wene,</i></p>
<p><i>But a fayr firrene wowe.</i></p>
</div>
<p>These lines will receive some illustration from a passage in Sir
Tristrem, where it is said,</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>A borde he tok oway</p>
<p class="indent">Of her bour.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—p. 114.</p>
<p>On which Sir W. Scott remarks, “The bed-chamber of the queen was
constructed of wooden boards or shingles, of which one could easily be
removed.” This will explain the line which occurs below, 2106, “He stod,
and totede in at a bord.”</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2092" id="textnote2092" class="pginternal">2092.</a> <i>Aboute the middel</i>, &amp;c. In the
French, a person is placed by the Seneschal to watch, who first
discovers the light.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2132" id="textnote2132" class="pginternal">2132.</a> <i>Bi the pappes he leyen naked.</i> “From the
latter end of the 13th to near the 16th century, all ranks, and both
sexes, were universally in the habit of sleeping quite naked. This
custom is often alluded to by Chaucer, Gower, Lydgate, and all our
ancient writers.” Ellis, <span class="smallcaps">Spec. Metr.
Rom.</span> V. <span class="smallroman">I.</span> p. 324,
4th Ed. In the <i>Squyr of Lowe Degre</i> is a remarkable instance of
this fact:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>How she rose, that lady dere,</p>
<p>To take her leue of that squyer;</p>
<p>Al so naked as she was borne</p>
<p>She stod her chambre-dore beforne.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—l. 671.</p>
<p>The custom subsisted both in England and France to a very recent
period, and hence probably was derived the phrase <i>naked-bed</i>,
illustrated so copiously by Archdeacon Nares in his Glossary.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2192" id="textnote2192" class="pginternal">2192.</a> Cf. the French, l. 843.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Ses chapeleins fet demander,</p>
<p>Ses briefs escriure &amp; enseeler;</p>
<p>Par ses messages les manda,</p>
<p>Et pur ses amis enuoia;</p>
<p>Pur ses homes, pur ses parenz;</p>
<p>Mult i assembla granz genz.</p>
</div>
<span class="pagenum">100</span>
<a id="page100"> </a>
<p>[<a href="#texttag2201" id="textnote2201" class="pginternal">2201.</a> Read <i>ne neme</i> = took not, sc. their way,
just as in l. 1207.]</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2240" id="textnote2240" class="pginternal">2240-2265.</a> <i>Lokes, hware he stondes her</i>,
&amp;c. Comp. the Fr. ll. 913-921.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>“Veez ci nostre dreit heir,</p>
<p>Bien en deuom grant ioie aueir.”</p>
<p>Tut primerain se desafubla,</p>
<p>Par deuant lui s’agenuilla;</p>
<p>Sis homs deuint, si li iura</p>
<p>Qe leaument le seruira.</p>
<p>Li autre sont apres alé,</p>
<p>Chescuns de bone volenté;</p>
<p>Tuit si home sont deuenu.</p>
</div>
<p class="spacer"> </p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag2314" id="textnote2314" class="pginternal">2314.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>Vbbe dubbede him to knith,</i></p>
<p><i>With a swerd ful swithe brith.</i></p>
</div>
<p>So likewise in the Fr. l. 928, <i>A cheualier l’out adubbé</i>. The
ceremony of knighthood is described with greater minuteness in the
Romance of <i>Ly beaus Desconus</i>, l. 73; and see <i>Kyng
Horn</i>, ed. Lumby, ll. 495-504.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2320" id="textnote2320" class="pginternal">2320.</a> <i>Hwan he was king, ther mouthe men se</i>,
&amp;c. Ritson has justly remarked, Notes to <i>Ywaine and Gawaine</i>,
l. 15, that the elaborate description of Arthur’s feast at
Carlisle, given by Geoffrey of Monmouth, l. ix. c. 12, has served as a
model to all his successors. The original passage stands thus in a fine
MS. of the 13th century, MS. Harl. 3773. fol. 33 <i>b</i>. “Refecti
autem epulis diversos ludos acturi campos extra civitatem adeunt. Tunc
milites simulachra belli scientes <i>equestrem ludum</i> componunt,
mulieribus ab edito murorum aspicientibus. Alii <i>cum cestibus</i>,
alii <i>cum hastis</i>, alii <i>gravium lapidum jactu</i>, alii <i>cum
facis</i>, [<i>saxis</i>, Edd.] alii <i>cum aleis</i>, diversisque alii
alteriusmodi jocis contendentes.” In the translation of this description
by Wace we approach still nearer to the imitation of the Romance before
us.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>A plusurs iuis se departirent,</p>
<p>Li vns alerent <i>buhurder</i>,</p>
<p>E lur ignels cheuals mustrer,</p>
<p>Li altre alerent <i>eskermir</i>,</p>
<p>V <i>pere geter</i>, v <i>saillir</i>;</p>
<p>Tels i-aueit ki <i>darz lanconent</i>,</p>
<p>E tels i-aueit ki <i>lutouent</i>:</p>
<p>Chescon del gru [geu?] s’entremetait</p>
<p>Dunt entremettre se saueit.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
MS. Reg. 13. A. xxi.</p>
<p>The parallel versions, from the French, of Laȝamon, Robert of
Gloucester, and Robert of Brunne, may be read in Mr Ellis’s <i>Specimens
of Early English Poets</i>. At the feast of Olimpias, described in the
Romance of <i>Kyng Alisaunder</i>, we obtain an additional
imitation.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Withoute theo toun was mury,</p>
<p>Was reised ther al maner pley;</p>
<span class="pagenum">101</span>
<a id="page101"> </a>
<p>There was knyghtis <i>turnyng</i>,</p>
<p>There was maidenes carolyng,</p>
<p>There was champions <i>skyrmyng</i>,</p>
<p>Of heom and of other <i>wrastlyng</i>,</p>
<p>Of liouns chas, of <i>beore baityng</i>,</p>
<p>And <i>bay of bor</i>, of <i>bole slatyng</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—l. 193. Cf. l. 1045.</p>
<p>Some additional illustrations on each of the amusements named in our
text may not be unacceptable:</p>
<p class="inset1">
1. <i>Buttinge with sharpe speres.</i> This is tilting, or justing,
expressed in Wace by <i>buhurder</i>. See Strutt’s <i>Sports and
Pastimes</i>, p. 96, sq. 108.</p>
<p class="inset1">
2. <i>Skirming with taleuaces.</i> This is described more at large by
Wace, in his account of the feast of Cassibelaunus. Cf. <i>Laȝamon</i>,
v. i. p. 347; l. 8144. In Strutt’s <i>Sports and
Pastimes</i> is a representation of this game, taken from MS. Bodl. 264,
illuminated between 1338 and 1344, in which the form of the
<i>talevas</i> is accurately defined. It appears to have been pursued to
such an excess, as to require the interference of the crown, for in 1286
an edict was issued by Edward I. prohibiting all persons
<i>Eskirmer au bokeler</i>. This, however, had only a temporary effect
in restraining it, and in later times, under the appellation of <i>sword
and buckler play</i>, it again became universally popular.</p>
<p class="inset1">
3. <i>Wrastling with laddes, puttinge of ston.</i> See the notes on ll.
984 and 1022.</p>
<p class="inset1">
4. <i>Harping and piping.</i> This requires no illustration.</p>
<p class="inset1">
5. <i>Leyk of mine, of hasard ok.</i> Among the games mentioned at the
marriage of Gawain, in the Fabliau of <i>Le Chevalier à l’Epée</i>, we
have:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Cil Chevalier jeuent as tables,</p>
<p>Et as eschés de l’autre part,</p>
<p>O à la <i>mine</i>, o à <i>hazart</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="inset1 continue">
Le Grand, in his note on this passage, T. i. p. 57, Ed. 1779, writes:
“Le Hasard était une sorte de jeu de dez. Je ne connais point la
<i>Mine</i>; j’ai trouvé seulement ailleurs un passage qui prouve que ce
jeu était tres-dangereux, et qu’on pouvait s’y ruiner en peu de tems.”
It appears however from the Fabliau of <i>Du Prestre et des deuz
Ribaus</i>, to have been certainly a species of <i>Tables</i>, or
<i>Backgammon</i>, and to have been played with dice, on a board called
<i>Minete</i>. The only passage we recollect in which any further detail
of this game is given, is that of Wace, in the account of Arthur’s
feast, Harl. MS. 6508, and MS. Cott. Vit. A. x., but it must be
remarked, that the older copy 13 A. xxi. does not contain it, nor is it
found in the translations of Laȝamon, or Robert of Gloucester.</p>
<p class="inset1">
6. <i>Romanz reding.</i> See Sir W. Scott’s note on Sir Tristrem,
p. 290, [p. 306, ed. 1811]; and the Dissertations of Percy,
Ritson, and Ellis.</p>
<p class="floathead">7.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>Ther mouthe men se the boles beyte,</i></p>
<p><i>And the bores, with hundes teyte.</i></p>
</div>
<p class="inset1 continue">
Cf. ll. 1838, 2438. Both these diversions are mentioned by Lucianus, in
his inedited tract <i>De laude Cestriæ</i>, MS. Bodl. 672, who is
supposed by
<span class="pagenum">102</span>
<a id="page102"> </a>
Tanner to have written about <span class="smallroman">A.D.</span>
1100, but who must probably be placed near half a century later. They
formed also part of the amusements of the Londoners in the 12th century,
as we learn from Fitzstephen, p. 77, and are noticed in the passage
above quoted from the Romance of <i>Kyng Alisaunder</i>. In later times,
particularly during the 16th century, these cruel practices were in the
highest estimation, as we learn from Holinshed, Stowe, Laneham, &amp;c.
See Strutt’s <i>Sports and Pastimes</i>, p. 192, and the plate from
MS. Reg. 2. B. vii. Also Pegge’s Dissertation on Bull-baiting,
inserted in Vol. ii. of Archæologia.</p>
<p class="inset1">
8. <i>Ther mouthe men se hw Grim greu.</i> If this is to be understood
of scenic representation (and we can scarcely view it in any other
light), it will present one of the earliest instances on record of any
attempt to represent an historical event, or to depart from the
religious performances, which until a much later period were the chief,
and almost only, efforts towards the formation of the drama. Of course,
the words of the writer must be understood to refer to the period in
which he lived, i.e. according to our supposition, about the end of Hen.
III’s reign, or beginning of Edw. I. See Le Grand’s notes to the
<i>Lai de Courtois</i>, V. i. p. 329, and Strutt’s <i>Sports
and Pastimes</i>, B. 3, ch. 2.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2344" id="textnote2344" class="pginternal">2344.</a> <i>The feste fourti dawes sat.</i> Cf. l. 2950.
This is borrowed also from Geoffrey, and is the usual term of duration
fixed in the Romances.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Fourty dayes hy helden feste,</p>
<p>Ryche, ryall, and oneste.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Octouian Imperator</i>, l. 73.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Fourty dayes leste the feste.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Launfal</i>, l. 631.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>And certaynly, as the story sayes,</p>
<p>The revell lasted forty dayes.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Squyr of Lowe Degre</i>, l. 1113.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2384" id="textnote2384" class="pginternal">2384.</a> The French story here differs wholly from the
English. Instead of the encounter of Robert and Godard, and the cruel
punishment inflicted on the latter, in the French is a regular battle
between the forces of Havelok and Hodulf (Godard). A single combat takes
place between the two leaders, in which Hodulf is slain.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2450" id="textnote2450" class="pginternal">2450.</a> Cf. ll. 2505 and 2822. This appears to have
been a common, but barbarous, method in former times of leading traitors
or malefactors to execution. Thus in the Romance of Kyng Alisaunder, the
treatment of the murderers of Darius is described:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>He dude quyk harnesche hors,</p>
<p>And sette theron heore cors,</p>
<p>Hyndeforth they seten, saun faile;</p>
<p>In heore hand they hulden theo tailes.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—l. 4708.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2461" id="textnote2461" class="pginternal">2461.</a> We find a similar proverb in the <i>Historie de
Melusine, tirée des Chroniques de Poitou</i>, &amp;c. 12mo. Par. 1698,
in which (at p. 72) Thierry, Duke of Bretagne, says to
Raimondin;— “Vous autorisez par votre silence <i>notre
Proverbe</i>, qui dit, <i>Qu’un vieux peché fait nouvelle
vergogne</i>.”</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2513" id="textnote2513" class="pginternal">2513.</a> <i>Sket was seysed</i>, &amp;c. Comp. the Fr.
l. 971.</p>
<span class="pagenum">103</span>
<a id="page103"> </a>
<div class="verse">
<p>Apres cest fet, ad receu</p>
<p>Le regne q’a son piere fu.</p>
</div>
<p class="spacer"> </p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag2516" id="textnote2516" class="pginternal">2516.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>And the king ful sone it yaf</i></p>
<p><i>Vbbe in the hond, wit a fayr staf.</i></p>
</div>
<p>So in <i>Sir Tristrem</i>:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Rohant he yaf <i>the wand</i>,</p>
<p>And bad him sitte him bi,</p>
<p class="indent3">That fre;</p>
<p>‘Rohant lord mak y</p>
<p>To held this lond of me.’</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—fytte i. st. 83; p. 52.</p>
<p>The editor is clearly mistaken in explaining the <i>wand</i> to be a
<i>truncheon</i>, or <i>symbol of power</i>. For the custom of giving
seisin or investiture <i>per fustim</i>, and <i>per baculum</i>, see
Madox’s <i>Formul. Anglican.</i> pref. p. ix. and Spelman, Gloss.
in v. <i>Investire</i>, and <i>Traditio</i>. The same usage existed
in France, <i>par rain et par baton</i>.</p>
<p class="floathead"><a href="#texttag2521" id="textnote2521" class="pginternal">2521.</a></p>
<div class="verse">
<p>——<i>of monekes blake</i></p>
<p><i>A priorie to seruen inne ay.</i></p>
</div>
<p>The allusion here may be made either to the Abbey of Wellow, in
Grimsby, which was a monastery of <i>Black Canons</i>, said to have been
built about <span class="smallroman">A.D.</span> 1110, or (what is
more probable) to the Augustine Friary of Black Monks, which is stated
in the <i>Monumental Antiquities of Grimsby</i>, by the Rev. G. Oliver,
to have been “founded <i>about</i> the year 1280,” p. 110. No
notice of it occurs in Tanner till the year 1304. Pat. 33 Edw. I.
Some old walls of this edifice, which was dissolved in 1543, still
remain, and the site is still called “The Friars.” If the connection
between this foundation and the one recorded in the poem be considered
valid, the date of the composition must be referred to <i>rather</i> a
later period than we wish to admit.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2530" id="textnote2530" class="pginternal">2530.</a> The French supplies what is here omitted, viz.
that Havelok sails to England by the persuasion of his wife.</p>
<p>[Indeed, ll. 979-1006 of the French text may serve to fill up the
evident gap in the story; a translation of the passage is added, to shew
this more clearly.</p>
<table class="verse" data-summary="parallel texts">
<colgroup><col>
<col class="half">
</colgroup><tbody><tr>
<td>
<p>Quant Haueloc est rois pussanz,</p>
<p>Le regne tint plus de .iiii. anz;</p>
<p>Merueillos tresor i auna.</p>
<p>Argentille li commanda</p>
<p>Qu’il passast en Engleterre</p>
<p>Pur son heritage conquerre,</p>
<p>Dont son oncle l’out engettée,</p>
<p>[Et] A grant tort desheritée.</p>
</td>
<td>
<p>When Havelok is a mighty king,</p>
<p>He reigned more than 4 years,</p>
<p>Marvellous treasure he amassed.</p>
<p>Argentille (Goldborough) bade him</p>
<p>Pass into England</p>
<p>To conquer her heritage,</p>
<p>Whence her uncle had cast her out,</p>
<p>And very wrongly disinherited her.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
<p>Li rois li dist qu’il fera</p>
<p>Ceo qu’ele li comandera.</p>
<p>Sa nauie fet a-turner,</p>
<span class="pagenum">104</span>
<a id="page104"> </a>
<p>Ses genz &amp; ses ostz mander.</p>
<p>En mier se met quant orré a,</p>
<p>Et la reyne od lui mena.</p>
<p>Quatre vinz &amp; quatre cenz</p>
<p>Out Haueloc, pleines de genz.</p>
<p>Tant out nagé &amp; siglé,</p>
<p>Q’en Carleflure est ariué.</p>
<p>Sur le hauene se herbergerent,</p>
<p>Par le pais viande quierent.</p>
</td>
<td>
<p>The king told her that he would do</p>
<p>That which she should command him.</p>
<p>He got ready his fleet,</p>
<p>And sent for his men and his hosts.</p>
<p>He puts to sea when he has prayed,</p>
<p>And took the queen with him.</p>
<p>Four score and four hundred (ships)</p>
<p>Had Havelok, full of men.</p>
<p>So far has he steered and sailed</p>
<p>That he has arrived at Carleflure.</p>
<p>Hard by the haven they abode,</p>
<p>And sought food in the country round.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
<p>Puis enuoia li noble rois,</p>
<p>Par le consail de ses Danois,</p>
<p>A Alsi qu’il li rendist</p>
<p>La terre qe tint Ekenbright,</p>
<p>Q’a sa niece fut donée,</p>
<p>Dont il l’out desheritée;</p>
</td>
<td>
<p>Then sent the noble king,</p>
<p>By the advice of his Danes,</p>
<p>To Alsi (Godrich)—that he should restore to him</p>
<p>The land that Ekenbright (Athelwold) held,</p>
<p>Which was given to his niece,</p>
<p>And of which he had deprived her.</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
<p>Et, si rendre n’el voleit,</p>
<p>Mande qu’il le purchaceroit.</p>
<p>Av roi uindrent li messager—</p>
</td>
<td>
<p>And, if he would not give it up,</p>
<p>He sends word that he will take it.</p>
<p>To the king came the messengers.]</p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>The remainder of the French poem altogether differs in its detail
from the English.</p>
<p><a href="#texttag2927" id="textnote2927" class="pginternal">2927.</a> <i>Hire that was ful swete in bedde.</i><ins class="mycorr" title="mismatched closing bracket in original">]
</ins>Among Kelly’s Scotch Proverbs, p. 290, we find: “<i>Sweet in
the bed</i>, and sweir up in the morning, was never a good housewife;”
and in a ballad of the last century quoted by Laing, the editor of that
highly curious collection, the <i>Select pieces of Ancient Popular
Poetry of Scotland</i>, we meet with the same expression:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>A Clown is a Clown both at home and abroad,</p>
<p>When a Rake he is comely, and <i>sweet in his bed</i>.</p>
</div>
<p>[<a href="#texttag2990" id="textnote2990" class="pginternal">2990.</a> The last word is written <i>thit</i> in the
MS., but, as it rimes to <i>rith</i>, we should suppose <i>tiht</i> to
be the word meant. <i>Thit</i> cannot be explained, but <i>tiht</i> (or
perhaps <i>tith</i>, according to our scribe’s spelling) is the
pp. of a verb signifying <i>to purpose</i>, which is the exact
meaning required. Cf.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p class="indent"> “And y to turne to þee have <i>tiȝt</i>;”</p>
<p>i.e.  “I have resolved to turn to thee.”</p>
</div>
<p><i>Political, Religious, and Love Poems</i>; ed. Furnivall, 1866;
p. 177.]</p>
</div>
<div class="glossary">
<span class="pagenum">105</span>
<a id="page105"> </a>
<h2> <a id="glossary">GLOSSARIAL INDEX.</a></h2>
<hr class="small">
<h4>ABBREVIATIONS.</h4>
<p class="editor">
Barb. Barbour’s Bruce. —Chauc. Chaucer. —Doug. Gawin
Douglas’s Transl. of the Æneid. —Ellis, M. R. Ellis’s
Specimens of Metrical Romances. —Gl. Glossary. —Jam.
Jamieson’s Dictionary. —Laȝam. Laȝamon’s Transl. of Wace (ed.
Madden). —Lynds. Sir D. Lyndsay’s Works. —N.E. Northern
English. —Percy, A. R. Percy’s Reliques of Ancient English Poetry.
—P. Plowm. Piers Plowman. —R. Br. Robert of
Brunne. —R. Gl. Robert of Gloucester, ed. Hearne (2nd ed.
1810). —Rits. A. S. Ritson’s Ancient Songs. —Rits.
M. R. Ritson’s Metrical Romances. —Sc. Scotch, Scotland.
—Sir Tr. Sir Tristrem. —Wall. Wallace. —Web. Weber’s
Metrical Romances. —Wilb. Wilbraham’s Cheshire Glossary.
—Wynt. Wyntoun’s Chronicle. —B. Lat. Barbarous Latin.
—Belg. Belgic. —Fr. French. —Isl. Islandic.
—Lat. Latin. —S. Saxon. —Sibb. Sibbald’s Chronicle of
Scottish Poetry. —<ins class="mycorr" title="always ‘Su.-G.’ or ‘Su.-Goth.’ in text">Su. G. </ins> Suio-Gothic. —Teut.
Teutonic. —<i>q.v.</i> Quod vide. —The Romances separately
cited are sufficiently indicated by the Titles. The numbers refer to the
line of the Poem.</p>
<p class="editor">
It may be useful to add that the names of the Romances edited by Ritson
are— vol. i. Ywaine and Gawin; Launfal. —vol. ii.
Lybeaus Disconus; King Horn; King of Tars; Emare; Sir Orpheo; Chronicle
of England. —vol. iii. Le bone Florence; Erle of Tolous; Squyr of
Lowe Degre; Knight of Curtesy. Those edited by Weber are—
vol. i. Kyng Alisaunder; Sir Cleges; Lai-le-freine. —vol. ii.
Richard Cœur de Lion; Ipomydon; Amis and Amiloun. —vol. iii. Seuyn
Sages; Octouian; Sir Amadas; Hunting of the Hare. Beowulf and the Codex
Exoniensis are quoted from Thorpe’s editions.</p>

Th.</p>

<p class="spacer"> </p>
<a class="glosslink pginternal" href="#glossary" id="gloss_A">A</a>
<p class="letter">
A, 610, 936. Apparently an error of the scribe for <i>Al</i>, but
perhaps written as pronounced. N.E. and Sc. <i>aw</i>. V. Jam.</p>
<p>A before a <i>noun</i> is commonly a corruption of the S. <i>on</i>,
as proved clearly by the examples in Tyrwhitt’s Gl., Jam., and Gl.
Lynds. <i>Adoun</i>, q.v. is an exception. <i>A-two</i>, 1413, 2643.
<i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_On" class="pginternal">On</a>.</p>
<p>Aboven, <i>prep.</i> S. above, 1700.</p>
<p>Abouten, <i>prep.</i> S. [<i>on-bútan</i>] about, 521, 670, 1010,
&amp;c. <i>Abuten</i>, 2429.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Adoun">Adoun</a>, <i>adv.</i> S.
down, 567. <i>Adune</i>, 2735. <i>Doun</i>, 901, 925, &amp;c.
<i>Dun</i>, 888, 927. <i>Dune</i>, 1815, 2656. A.S. <i>of-dúne</i>.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Adrad">Adrad</a>, <i>part.
pa.</i> S. afraid, 278, 1048, 1163, 1682, 2304. <i>Adradde</i>, 1787.
<i>Adred</i>, 1258. <i>Odrat</i>, 1153. Sir Tr. p. 174;
K. Horn, 124. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Dred" class="pginternal">Dred</a>.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Agen">Agen</a>, <i>prep.</i> S.
[<i>on-gean</i>] against, 1792. <i>Ageyn</i>, 493, 569, 2024, &amp;c.
<i>Ageynes</i>, 2153, 2270, &amp;c. <i>Ayen</i>, 489, 1210, 2799.
<i>Yen</i>, 2271. <i>Ageyn</i>, toward, 451, 1696, 1947;
<span class="pagenum">106</span>
<a id="page106"> </a>
opposite to, 1809; upon, on, 1828. <i>Ayen</i>, towards, 1207. <i>Ageyn
him go</i>, 934, opposite him, so as to bear an equal weight. <i>Ageyn
hire</i>, 1106, at her approach. <i>Ageyn þe lith</i>, 2141, opposed to
the light, on which the light shines. V. R. Gl., R. Br.,
Chauc. &amp;c.</p>
<p>Ageyn, <i>adv.</i> S. again, 2426.</p>
<p>Al, <i>adv.</i> S. wholly, entirely, 34, 70, 139, <ins class="mycorr" title="printed as shown: ‘al’ occurs once in 203">203</ins>,
&amp;c.</p>
<p>Al, <i>adj.</i> S. all, <ins class="mycorr" title="printed as shown: ‘al’ occurs once in 203">203</ins>,
264, &amp;c.; every one, 104; every part, 224; <i>plu.</i> alle, 2, 150,
&amp;c.</p>
<p>Albidene, <i>adv.</i> <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Bidene" class="pginternal">Bidene</a>.</p>
<p>Als, Also, <ins class="mycorr" title="printed as shown: error for two words ‘Al so’?">Also</ins>, <i>conj.</i> S. [<i>eal-swá</i>] as,
like, so, 306, 319, &amp;c. <i>Als</i>, 1912, as if. <i>Al so foles</i>,
like fools, 2100. <i>Als</i> is merely the abbreviation of <i>Al so</i>;
and the modern as is again shortened from <i>als</i>. In Laȝamon it is
often written <i>alse</i>, as in l. 4953.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>And he hæfde a swithe god wif</p>
<p>&amp; he heo leouede <i>alse</i> his lif.</p>
</div>
<p class="continue">Cf. Havelok, l. 1663. <i>Als</i> and <i>Also</i>
are used indifferently, and universally by the old English and Scotch
poets.</p>
<p>Alþer-beste, <i>adj.</i> S. best of all, 182, 720, 1040, 1197, 2415.
<i>Alþer-lest</i>, <i>Alþer-leste</i>, 1978, 2666, least of all. It is
the gen. c. pl. of <i>Alle</i>, joined to an adj. in the superl. degree,
and is extensively employed. <i>Alre-leofust</i>, <i>Alre-heudest</i>,
<i>Alre-kenest</i>, Laȝamon, <i>Althe-werste</i>, K. Horn, MS.
<i>Alder-best</i>, <i>Alder-most</i>, R. Br. <i>Alther-best</i>,
<i>Alther-formest</i>, &amp;c. Web. <i>Alther-furste</i>,
<i>Alther-next</i>, <i>Alther-last</i>, Rits. M. R.
<i>Alder-first</i>, <i>Alder-last</i>, <i>Alder-levest</i>, Chauc.
<i>Alder-liefest</i>, Shakesp.</p>
<p>Amideward, <i>prep.</i> S. in the midst, 872. <i>Amiddewart</i>,
K. Horn, 556. <i>Amydward</i>, K. Alisaund. 690. <i>A
mydward</i>, Ly Beaus Desc. 852. <i>Amydwart</i>, Doug. Virg. 137,
35.</p>
<p>An. <i>conj.</i> S. and, 29, 359, &amp;c. So used by Laȝamon, and
still in Somersetsh. V. Jennings. <i>Ant</i>, 36, 557, K. Horn, 9,
&amp;c.</p>
<p>And, <i>conj.</i> <ins class="mycorr" title="printed in italics">if</ins>, 2862.</p>
<p>Andelong, <i>adv.</i> S. lengthways, i.e. from the head to the tail,
2822.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Ovyrtwart and <i>endelang</i></p>
<p>With strenges of wyr the stones hang.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>R. Cœur de Lion</i>, 2649.</p>
<p class="continue">Chauc. <i>endelong</i>, C. T. 1993.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Anilepi">Anilepi</a>,
<i>adj.</i> S. [<i>ánlepig</i>] one, a single, 2107. <i>Onlepi</i>,
1094. In the very curious collection of poems in MS. Digb. 86 (written
in the Lincolnshire dialect, temp. Edw. I.) we meet with this
somewhat rare word:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>A! quod the vox, ich wille the telle,</p>
<p><i>On alpi</i> word ich lie nelle.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Of the vox and of the wolf</i> (Rel. Ant. ii. 275).</p>
<p class="continue">It occurs also in the Ormulum.</p>
<p>Anoþer, <i>adj.</i> S. <i>Al another</i>, 1395, in a different way,
on another project.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Ah al hit iwruth <i>on other</i></p>
<p>Sone ther after.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Laȝamon</i>, l. 21005.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Ac Florice thought <i>al another</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Flor. and Blaunchefl.</i> ap. Ellis, M. R. V. 3, p. 125, ed. 1803.
(Cf. Horn, ed. Lumby, p. 52, l. 32.)</p>
<p>Anuye, <i>v.</i> Fr. to trouble, weary, 1735; R. Gl., K. Alisaund.
876; Chauc. Melibeus. <i>Noye</i>, Lynds. Gl. q.v.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Are">Are</a>, <i>adj.</i> S.
former, 27. Cf. are, <i>adv.</i>, Sir Tr. p. 32; Rits. M. R.,
Web., R. Gl., R. Br., Minot. p. 31. <i>Air</i>,
<i>Ayr</i>, Sc. V. Jam. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Er" class="pginternal">Er</a>, <a href="#gloss_Or" class="pginternal">Or</a>.</p>
<p>Aren, <i>1 and 3 p. pl.</i> S. are, 619, 1321, &amp;c. <i>Arn</i>,
Chauc.</p>
<p>Arke, <i>n.</i> S. Lat. a chest <i>or</i> coffer, 2018. R. Br.,
Jam.</p>
<p>Armes, <i>n. pl.</i> Lat. arms, armor, 2605, 2613, 2925.</p>
<p>Arum for Arm, 1982, 2408.</p>
<span class="pagenum">107</span>
<a id="page107"> </a>
<p>Arwe, S. [<i>earg</i>] timid, 2115. Alter the punctuation, and
read—</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>He calde boþe arwe me<i>n</i> and kene,</p>
<p>Knithes and serganz swiþe sleie.</p>
<p>“Arwe or ferefulle. <i>Timidus.</i>”</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
Prompt. Parv.</p>
<p class="continue">
Cf. Stille, q.v.</p>
<p>As for Has, 1174.</p>
<p>Asayleden, <i>pa. t. pl.</i> Fr. assailed, 1862.</p>
<p>Asken, <i>n. pl.</i> S. ashes, 2841. <i>Aske</i>, R. Gl.
<i>Askes</i>, R. Br. <i>Ashen</i>, Chauc. <i>Assis</i>, Doug.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Astirte">Astirte</a>, <i>pa.
t.</i> leaped, 893. <i>Astert</i>, King’s Quair, ap. Jam. <i>See</i>
<a href="#gloss_Stirt" class="pginternal">Stirt</a>.</p>
<p>At, <i>prep.</i> S. of <i>or</i> to, 1387. Yw. and Gaw. (Rits.) 963.
Still existing in Scotland.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_At-sitte">At-sitte</a>,
<i>v.</i> S. contradict, oppose, 2200. It corresponds with the term
<i>with-sitten</i>, 1683. In R. Gl. it is used synonymously with
<i>at-stonde</i>.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>For ther nas so god knygt non no-wer a-boute France,</p>
<p>That in joustes scholde <i>at-sitte</i> the dynt of ys lance.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
—p. 137.</p>
<p class="continue"><i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Sat" class="pginternal">Sat</a>.</p>
<p>Aucte, Auchte, Auhte, Authe, <i>n.</i> S. possessions, 531, 1223,
1410, 2215.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>And alle the <i>æhten</i> of mine londe.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Laȝamon</i>, l. 25173.</p>
<p><i>Aughtte</i>, K. Alisaund. 6884. <i>Aucht</i>, Doug. Virg. 72, 4;
Lynds. Gl.</p>
<p>Aucte, Auht, Auhte, <i>v. imp.</i> (originally <i>pa. t.</i> of Aw,
or Owe) S. [<i>ágan</i>, <i>áhte</i>] ought, 2173, 2787, 2800.
<i>Aught</i>, Sir Tr. p. 44. <i>Ohte</i>, K. Horn, 418.
<i>Aght</i>, Yw. and Gaw. 3229. <i>Aute</i>, R. Gl. <i>Aught</i>,

<p><ins class="mycorr" title="alphabetization may be intentional">Aute, Awcte</ins>, (<i>pa. t.</i> of the same verb),
possessed, 207, 743. <i>Aught</i>, Sir Tr. p. 182. Ly Beaus Desc.
1027. <i>Oght</i>, Le bone Flor. 650. <i>Auht</i>, R. Br.
p. 126; Wynt., Lynds. Gl.</p>
<p>Aueden. <i>See</i> <a class="error pginternal" href="#gloss_Hauen" title="form of ‘Hauen’">Haueden</a>.</p>
<p>Aunlaz, <i>n.</i> Anelace, 2554. “A kind of knife or dagger, usually
worn at the girdle.” Tyrw. note on Chauc. l. 359. So in Matth.
Paris, “Genus cultelli, quod vulgariter <i>Anelacius</i> dicitur.” V.
Gl. in voc. and Todd’s Gl. to Illustr. of Chauc. In <i>Sir Gawan and Sir
Galoran</i>, ii. 4, an <i>anlas</i> signifies a sharp spike fixed in the
chanfron of a horse. Probably from the Francic <i>Anelaz</i>,
<i>Analeze</i>. V. Jam.</p>
<p>Auter, <i>n.</i> Fr. Lat. altar, 389, 1386, 2373. Sir Tr. p. 61,
Octovian, 1312, R. Br., Chauc. <i>Awter</i>, Barb.</p>
<p>Ax, <i>n.</i> S. axe, 1776, 1894.</p>
<p>Ay, <i>adv.</i> S. ever, aye, always, 159, 946, 1201, &amp;c.
<i>Ae</i>, Sc. V. Jam.</p>

<p><a id="gloss_Ayther">Ayþer</a>, <i>pron.</i>

<p>Barfot, <i>adj.</i> S. barefoot, 862.</p>
<p>Barnage, <i>n.</i> Fr. barons or noblemen collectively, baronage,

<p>Barre, <i>n.</i> Fr. bar of a door, 1794, 1811, 1827. Synonymous with

<p>Baþe, <i>adj.</i> S. both, 1336, 2543. <i>Bethe</i>, 694, 1680.</p>
<p>Be. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Ben" class="pginternal">Ben</a>.</p>

<p><a id="gloss_Bede">Bede</a>, <i>n.</i> S.
prayer, 1385.</p>
<p>Bede, <i>v.</i> S. to order, to bid, 668, 2193, 2396; to offer, 1665,

<p>Beite, Beyte, <i>v.</i> to bait, to set dogs on, 1840, 2330, 2440.

<p><a id="gloss_Ben">Ben</a>, <i>v.</i> S. to be,
19, 905, 1006, &amp;c. <i>Ben</i>, <i>pr. t. pl.</i> are, 1787, 2559.

<p><a id="gloss_Bere">Bere</a>, <i>n.</i> S. bear,
573, 1838, 1840, 2448.</p>
<p>Bere, Beren, <i>v.</i> S. to bear, to carry, 581, 762, 805.
<i>Ber</i>, 2557; <i>Bar</i>, <i>pa. t.</i> bore, 557, 815, 877.
<i>Bere</i>, 974. <i>Beres</i>, <i>pr. t. pl.</i> bear. 2323.</p>
<p>Bermen, <i>n. pl.</i> S. bar-men, porters to a kitchen, 868, 876,
885. The only author in which this term has been found is Laȝamon, in
the following passages:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Vs selve we habbet cokes,</p>
<p class="indent">to quecchen to cuchene,</p>
<p>Vs sulue we habbet <i>bermen</i>,</p>
<p class="indent">&amp; birles inowe. —l. 3315.</p>
<p class="stanza">
Weoren in þeos kinges cuchene</p>

<p class="letter">
Dam, <i>n.</i> 2468, here used in a reproachful sense, but apparently

pl.</i> Danish men, 2689, 2945, &amp;c. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Denshe" class="pginternal">Denshe</a>.</p>
<p>Datheit, <i>interj.</i> 296, 300, 926, 1125, 1887, 1914, 2047, 2447,
2511. <i>Datheyt</i>, 1799, 1995, 2604, 2757. An interjection or
imprecation, derived from the Fr. <i>Deshait</i>, <i>dehait</i>,
<i>dehet</i>, explained by Barbazan and Roquefort, <i>affliction</i>,
<i>malheur</i>; [from the O.F. <i>hait</i>, pleasure]. It may be
considered equivalent to Cursed! Ill betide! In the old Fabliaux it is
used often in this sense:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Fils à putain, fet-il, lechiere,</p>
<p>Vo jouglerie m’est trop chiere,</p>
<p><i>Dehait</i> qui vous i aporta,</p>
<p>Par mon chief il le comparra.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>De S. Pierre et du Jougleor</i>, 381.</p>
<p class="continue">The term was very early engrafted on the Saxon
phraseology. Thus in the <i>Disputation of Ane Hule and a
Niȝtingale</i>, l. 99.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p><i>Dahet</i> habbe that ilke best,</p>
<p>That fuleth his owe nest!</p>
</div>
<span class="pagenum">115</span>
<a id="page115"> </a>
<p class="continue">It occurs also frequently in the Old English
Romances. <i>See</i> Sir Tristr. pp. 111, 191; Horn Childe, ap.
Rits. V. 3, p. 290; Amis and Amil. 1569; Sevyn Sages, 2395;
R. Brunne, where it is printed by Hearne <i>Dayet</i>. To this
word, in all probability, we are indebted for the modern imprecation of
<i>Dase you!</i> <i>Dise you!</i> <i>Dash you!</i> still preserved in
many counties, and in Scotland. V. Jam. Suppl. v. <i>Dash you.</i></p>

<p>Deuel, <i>n.</i> S. devil, 446, 496, 1188. <i>Deueles</i>,
<i>g. c.</i> devil’s, 1409.</p>
<p>Deus. This is undoubtedly the vocative case of the Lat. <i>Deus</i>,
used as an interjection, 1312, 1650, 1930, 2096, 2114. “Its use was the
same in French as in English. Thus in King Horn:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Euuers Deu en sun quer a fait grant clamur,</p>
<p>Ohi, <i>Deus!</i> fait il, ki es uerrai creatur,</p>
<p>Par ki deuise, &amp;c.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Harl.</i> MS. 527, f. 66 b. c. 2.</p>
<p class="continue">It was probably introduced into the English
language by the Normans, and its pronunciation remained the same as in
the French.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>And gradde ‘as armes,’ for <i>Douce</i> Mahons!</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>K. Alisaunder</i>, 3674.</p>
<p class="continue">It is curious to remark, that we have here the
evident and simple etymology of the modern exclamation <i>Deuce!</i> for
the derivation of which even the best and latest Lexicographers have
sent us to the <i>Dusii</i> of St Augustine, the <i>Dues</i> of the
Gothic nations, <i>Diis</i> of the Persians, <i>Teus</i> of the
Armoricans, &amp;c. Thomson very justly adds, that all these words,
‘seem, like dæmon, to have been once used in a good sense,’ and in fact
are probably all corruptions of the same root. Cf. R. Brunne,
p. 254, and Gl. in v. <i>Deus</i>. For the first suggestion of
this derivation the Editor is indebted to Mr Will. Nicol.” —M.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Deye">Deye</a>, <i>v.</i> S. to
die, 840. <i>Deide</i>, <i>pa. t. pl.</i> died, 402.</p>
<p>Dide, Diden, Dides. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Do" class="pginternal">Do</a>.</p>
<p>Dike, <i>n.</i> S. ditch, 2435. <i>Dikes</i>, <i>pl.</i> 1923. N.E.
and Sc., V. Jam. and Brockett.</p>
<p>Dine, <i>n.</i> S. din, noise, 1860, 1868.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Dinge">Dinge</a>, <i>v.</i> S. to
strike, scourge, beat, 215, 2329. <i>Dong</i>, <i>pa. t.</i> struck,
1147. <i>Dungen</i>, <i>part. pa.</i> beaten, or scourged, 227. Sc. and

<span class="pagenum">116</span>
<a id="page116"> </a>
<p>Dint, <i>n.</i> S. blow, stroke, 1807, 1817, 1969, &amp;c.
<i>Dent</i>, Sir Tr. p. 92; Chauc. <i>Dynt</i>, R. Br.
<i>Dintes</i>, <i>pl.</i> 1437, 1862, 2665. <i>Duntes</i>, K. Horn,
865. <i>Dentys</i>, Rits. M. R. <i>Dyntes</i>, R. Gl.
<i>Dintes</i>, Minot, p. 23; V. Gl. Lynds.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Do">Do</a>, Don, <i>v.</i> S. The
various uses of this verb in English and Scotch, in an auxiliary,
active, and passive sense, have been pointed out by Tyrwhitt, Essay on
Vers. of Chauc. Note (37), Chalmers, Gl. Lynds. and Jamieson. It
signifies: to do, <i>facere</i>, 117, 528, 1191; to cause,
<i>efficere</i>, 611; <i>do casten</i>, 519; <i>do hem fle</i>, 2600, to
put or place (used with <i>in</i> or <i>on</i>), 535, 577, &amp;c.
<i>Dones on</i> = don es on = do them on, put them on (<i>see</i>

<p>Dubbe, <i>v.</i> Fr. S. to dub, create a knight, 2042.
<i>Dubbede</i>, <i>pa. t.</i> dubbed, 2314. <i>Dubban to ridere</i>,
Chron. Sax. An. 1085, [11086]. <i>To cnihte hine dubben</i>, Laȝam.
l. 22497. “Hickes, Hearne, Gl. R. Gl., and Tyrwhitt, Gl.
Chauc., all refer the word to the Saxon root, which primarily signified
<i>to strike</i>, the same as the Isl. <i>at dubba</i>. Todd on the
contrary, Gl. Illustr. Chauc., thinks this questionable, and refers to
Barbazan’s Gl. in v. <i>Adouber</i>, which is there derived from
the Lat. <i>adaptare</i>. Du Cange and Dr Merrick give it also a Latin
origin, from <i>Adoptare</i>, and by corruption <i>Adobare</i>.”
—M. The etymology is discussed in Wedgwood, s.v. <i>Dub.</i>
<i>See</i> <a href="#textnote2314" class="pginternal">Note on l. 2314</a>.</p>

<p>For, <i>prep.</i> S. <i>For to</i> is prefixed to the inf. of verbs
in the same manner as the Fr. <i>pour</i>, or Sp. <i>por</i>. It is so
used in all the old writers, and in the vulgar translation of the
Scriptures, and is still preserved in the North of England. Cf. 17,
&amp;c. <i>For</i> = on account of, 1670. Sir Tr. p. 62.</p>

spike rising from the back. There were two kinds, viz. the
<i>glaive-gisarme</i>, with a sabre-blade and spike; and the
<i>bill-gisarme</i>, in shape of a hedging-bill with a spike.” Godwin’s

<p>Giue. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Yeue" class="pginternal">Yeue</a>.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Giue">Giue</a>, <i>n.</i> S. gift,
2880. <i>Gyue</i>, 357. <i>Yeft</i>, 2336.</p>
<span class="pagenum">122</span>
<a id="page122"> </a>
<p>Giueled, piled up, 814. [The O.Fr. <i>gavelé</i> means piled up,
heaped together. To <i>gavel</i> corn (<i>see</i> Halliwell) is to put
it into heaps, and a <i>gavel</i> is a heap of corn. But this may very
well be derived from <i>gable</i>, since a heap takes the shape of a
peaked end of a house; and the O.Fr. term is probably originally
Teutonic, and connected, as <i>gable</i> is, with Mœso-Goth.
<i>gibla</i>, a pinnacle, with which compare German <i>giebel</i>, Du.
<i>gevel</i>, and hence our word would be taken from a verb
<i>givelen</i>, to pile up. The fish in Havelok’s basket would be what
the Dutch call <i>gevelvormig</i>, or formed like a gable, or like the
peaked end of a <i>stack</i> of hay or corn, whence the author’s
expression—<i>giueled als a stac</i>, piled up in the shape of a
stack. Other explanations are <i>flayed</i>, from Du. <i>villen</i>, to
flay; or <i>filed</i>, ranged in rows upon a stick, where <i>stick</i>
is represented by <i>stac</i>. But the latter supposition would require
the reading <i>on</i> rather than <i>als</i>; not to mention the fact
that if fish are carried <i>in a pannier</i> they would not resemble
fish carried <i>on a stick</i>. Nor is it quite satisfactory to say that
<i>giueled</i> is put for <i>gefilled</i>, filled; for this is not
elucidated by the expression <i>als a stac</i>, any more than the
explanation <i>flayed</i> is. <i>Gable</i> is Icel. <i>gafl</i>, Sw.
<i>gafvel</i>, Dan. <i>gavl</i>, Du. <i>gevel</i>, Ger. <i>giebel</i>,
<i>gipfel</i>, &amp;c. Its forked shape seems to give rise to Ger.
<i>gabel</i>, Sw. <i>gaffel</i>, a fork; respecting which set of words
see <i>Gaff</i> in Wedgwood.]</p>
<p>Gladlike, <i>adv.</i> S. gladly, 805, 906, 1760.</p>

<p>Goddot, Goddoth, <i>interj.</i> god wot! 606, 642, 796, 909, 1656,
2543; cf. 2527. It is formed probably in the same manner as
<i>Goddil</i>, for God’s will, in Yorksh. and Lanc. V. Craven dialect,
and View of Lanc. dialect, 1770, 8vo. The word before us appears to have
been limited to Lincolnshire or Lancashire, and does not appear in the
Glossaries. Other instances are in the <i>Cursor Mundi</i>, MS. Cott.
Vesp. F. iii. fol. 87<i>b</i>, and in MS. Cott. Galba E. ix.
fol. 61. It also occurs in a translation of a French Fabliau, written in
the reign of Edw. I.</p>

<p><a id="gloss_Greyue">Greyue</a>, <i>n.</i> S.

<i>g. c.</i> greave’s, 1749. <i>Greyues</i>, <i>pl.</i> 266. V.
Spelm. in v. <i>Grafio</i>, and Hickes, Diss. Epist. p. 21, n.
p. 151.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Grim">Grim</a>, <i>adj.</i> S.
cruel, savage, fierce, 155, 680, 2398, 2655, 2761. R. Br., Rits.
M. R. <i>See</i> Beowulf, l. 204.</p>
<p>Grim, <i>n.</i> [smut, dirt, 2497. The explanation is that Godard, on
being flayed, did not bear his sentence as one of rank and blood would
have done, but began to roar out as if he were mere <i>dirt</i> or
<i>mud</i>, i.e. one of the dregs of the common herd. This curious
expression is ascertained to have the meaning here
<span class="pagenum">124</span>
<a id="page124"> </a>
assigned to it by observing (1) that <i>grim</i> and <i>gore</i> must be
substantives, and (2) that they must be of like signification; but
chiefly by comparing the line with others similar to it. Now the
context, in the couplet following, repeats that “men might hear him
roar, that <i>foul vile</i> wretch, a mile off;” and in l. 682,
Godard calls Grim “<i>a foul dirt</i>, a thrall, and a churl.” The
author clearly uses <i>dirt</i> and <i>churl</i> as synonyms. The word
<i>grim</i> is the Danish <i>grim</i>, soot, lampblack, smut, dirt,
answering to the English <i>grime</i>; see <i>grime</i> in Atkinson’s
Glossary of the Cleveland dialect. <i>Gore</i> is the A.S. <i>gór</i>,
wet mud, or clotted blood, in the latter of which senses it is still
used. See “<i>Gore. Limus</i>” in Prompt. Parv., and Way’s note.]</p>
<p>Grip, <i>n.</i> griffin, 572. Web. <i>Graip</i>, Sc., V. Jam. The
plural <i>gripes</i> is in Laȝam. l. 28062, and K. Alisaund.
4880. Swed. <i>grip</i>.</p>
<p>Grip, <i>n.</i> S. [<i>græp</i>] ditch, trench, 2102. <i>Gripes</i>,
<i>pl.</i> 1924. V. Jam. in v. <i>Grape</i>; and Skinner, v.
<i>Groop</i>. Cf. Swed. <i>grop</i>.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Gripen">Gripen</a>, <i>pr. t.
pl.</i> S. gripe, grasp, 1790. <i>Gripeth</i>, <i>imp.</i> gripe ye,
1882. <i>Grop</i>, <i>pa. t.</i> grasped, 1776, 1871, 1890, &amp;c.</p>
<p>Grith, <i>n.</i> S. peace, 61, 511. <i>Grith-sergeans</i>, 267, legal
officers to preserve the peace. These must not be confounded with the
<i>Justitiarii Pacis</i> established in the beginning of Edw. III.
reign, and called <i>Gardiani Pacis</i>. V. Spelm. in v. Cf. Icel.
<i>grið</i>.</p>
<p>Grom, <i>n.</i> male child, youth, 790; young man, 2472. Belgic
<i>grom</i> has the same sense of <i>boy</i>. Cf. Icel. <i>gromr</i>,
homuncio. So in <i>Sir Degore</i>, A. iv.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>He lyft up the shete anone</p>
<p>And loked upon the lytle <i>grome.</i></p>

<p class="continue">It generally elsewhere signifies <i>lad</i>,
<i>page</i>.</p>
<p>Gronge, <i>n.</i> Fr. grange, 764. [Halliwell says that, in
<i>Lincolnshire</i>, a lone farm-house is still called a <i>grange</i>.
In old English it is sometimes spelt <i>graunge</i>, which comes near
the form here used. Cf. Fr. <i>grange</i>; Ital. <i>grangia</i>
(Florio), a country-farm.]</p>

<p><a id="gloss_Grundlike">Grundlike</a>,

<i>wayte</i>, <i>lake</i> into <i>late</i> (R. Hood, <span class="smallroman">I.</span> 106), &amp;c., or <i>vice versâ</i>. <i>See</i>

<p>Lenge, <i>n.</i> the fish called <i>ling</i>, 832. [<i>Asellus
longus</i>, or <i>Islandicus</i>, Ray.] It was a common dish formerly.
Thus we have <i>Lynge in jelly</i>, in Archb. Nevil’s Feast, 6 Edw.
IV., and <i>Lyng in foyle</i>, in Warham’s Feast, 1504. <i>See</i>
Pegge’s <i>Form of Cury</i>, p. 177, 184, and MS. Sloane, 1986.</p>

<p>Lette, <i>v.</i> S. [<i>lǽtan</i>, <i>lettan</i>] to hinder, retard,

stopped, stayed, 2447, 2500. <i>Leten</i>, <i>pa. t. pl.</i> stopped,

<p>Leue, <i>n.</i> S. leave, 1387, 1626, 2952, &amp;c.</p>
<p>Leue, <i>adj.</i> <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Lef" class="pginternal">Lef</a>.</p>
<p>Leue, <i>v.</i> S. [<i>lýfan</i>] <i>imp. s.</i> grant, 334, 406,
2807. K. Horn, 465, MS.; R. Gl., Erle of Tol. 365. Guy of
Warw. ap. Ellis, M. R. V. 2, p. 77, where it is
misprinted <i>lene</i>. It is very frequently used in the old Engl.
Metrical Lives of the Saints, MS. Laud, 108. [The true distinction
between <i>leue</i> and <i>lene</i> is, that the former is the A.S.
<i>lýfan</i>, G. <i>erlauben</i> = grant in the sense of <i>allow</i>,
<i>permit</i>, and is invariably intransitive; whilst <i>lene</i> is the
A.S. <i>lænan</i>, G. <i>leihen</i> = grant in the sense of <i>give</i>.
The confusion between the senses of <i>grant</i> has led to confusion
between <i>lene</i> and <i>leue</i>, and in at least five passages of
Chaucer (C. T. 7226, 13613; Tro. ii. 1212, iii. 56, v. 1749,
ed. Tyrwhitt) many editions wrongly have <i>lene</i>. In the last three
instances Tyrwhitt rightly prints <i>leve</i>, but unnecessarily
corrects himself in his Glossary. I regret to add that I have
thrice made a similar mistake. In Piers Plowman, A. v. 263, and in
Pierce the Ploughman’s Crede, ll. 366 and 573, for <i>lene</i> read
<i>leue</i>. Halliwell’s remark, that “the [former] editor of Havelok
absurdly prints <i>leue</i>” is founded upon the same misconception, and
he is wrong in his censure. <i>See</i> the use of <i>lefe</i> in the
Ormulum, ed. White.]</p>
<p>Leued, <i>pa. t.</i> S. left, 225.</p>
<p>Leuedi, <i>n.</i> S. lady, 171, &amp;c. <i>Leuedyes</i>, <i>pl.</i>
239. V. Hickes, Diss. Ep. p. 52, n.</p>
<p>Leuere. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Lef" class="pginternal">Lef</a>.</p>
<p>Leues, <i>pr. t. 3 p.</i> S. believes, 1781, 2105. From
<i>lefan</i>.</p>
<p>Leuin, <i>n.</i> S. lightning, 2690. R. Br. p. 174. Yw. and Gaw.

<p>Lith, <i>n.</i> S. alleviation, comfort, peace, 1338. <i>Lyþe</i>,

occurs in Laȝam. l. 7242. Sir Tr. p. 43, 82. R. Cœur
de L. 2480, and Emare, 348, from the v. <i>líðian</i>, alleviare.
Cf. Icel. <i>lið</i>, sometimes used to mean <i>help</i>. See
<i>Leathe</i> in Atkinson’s Cleveland Glossary.</p>
<p>Lith, <i>n.</i> S. 2515. This word is explained by Ritson
<i>plains</i>, by Hearne <i>tenements</i>, and by Jamieson a
<i>ridge</i> or <i>ascent</i>. Its real signification seems unknown, but
may be conjectured from the following passages.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>No asked he lond no <i>lithe</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Sir Tristr.</i> p. 101.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Ther wille not be went, ne lete ther lond ne <i>lith</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>R. Brunne</i>, p. 194.</p>
<p class="continue">
where it answers to the Fr. Ne volent lesser tere ne
<i>tenement</i>.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Who schall us now geve londes or <i>lythe</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Le Bone Flor.</i> 841.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Here I gif Schir Galeron, quod Gaynour, withouten ony gile,</p>
<p>Al the londis and the <i>lithis</i> fro laver to layre.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Sir Gaw. and Sir Gal.</i> ii. 27.</p>
<p class="continue">[<i>See</i> Glossary to <i>William of Palerne</i>,
s.v. <i>Lud.</i>]</p>
<p>Lithes, <i>n. pl.</i> S. the extreme points of the toes, or

<p>Liþes, <i>imp. pl.</i> S. listen, 1400, 2204. <i>Lyþes</i>, 2576. The
verb in the Sax. is <i>hlystan</i>, but in Su.-G. <i>lyda</i>, and Isl.
<i>hlyda</i>, which approaches nearer to the form in the poem. So also
in K. Horn, 2, <i>wilen lithe</i>, MS.; R. Br. p. 93;
R. Hood, <span class="smallroman">I.</span> p. 2; Minot,
p. 1. Still used in Sc. and N.E. V. Jam. and Brockett.</p>
<p>Littene, <i>part. pa.</i> [or <i>inf.</i>?] 2701. “<i>Qu.</i> cut in
pieces, from the same root as to <i>lith</i>, divide the joints. V. Jam.
Suppl.” —M. [Or it may mean disgraced, wounded, defeated. Cf.
Su.-Goth. <i>lyta</i>, to wound; Icel. <i>lyta</i>, to disgrace; Sw.
<i>lyte</i>, a defect, <i>litt</i>, deformed; Dan. <i>lyde</i>, a
blemish.]</p>

<p>Loken, Lokene, <i>part. pa.</i> S. fastened, locked, closed, 429,

<span class="pagenum">136</span>
<a id="page136"> </a>

nithes</i>, 2048. <i>O worde</i>, 1349, in the world (<i>see</i>
<a href="#gloss_Werd" class="pginternal">Werd</a>). <i>O mani wise</i>, 1713, in many a
manner. <i>On gamen</i>, 1716, in sport. <i>On lesse hwile</i>, 1830, in
less time. <i>O bok</i>, 2307, 2311, on the book. <i>Wel o bon</i>,
2355, 2525, 2571, strong of body. <i>Iuele o bone</i>, 2505, lean. <i>On
hunting</i>, 2382. <i>O stede</i>, 2549, on steed. <i>Up-o the
dogges</i>, 2596, on the dogs. From these examples, added to those which
occur in every Glossary, it is evident the Sax. prep. <i>On</i> was
subsequently corrupted to <i>O</i> and <i>A.</i> <i>See</i> Tyrwh. and
Jam. <i>A nycht</i> in Barb. xix. 657, explained by the latter
<i>one</i> night, is according to the above rule <i>In the night</i>, as
confirmed by l. 1251. Sir Tr. pp. 47, 114. R. Glouc.</p>

<p>Parred, <i>part. pa.</i> confined, fastened in, barred in, 2439. We
have met with this word only in one instance, where Ritson leaves it
unexplained.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Yn al this [tyme] was sir Ywayn</p>
<p>Ful straitly <i>parred</i> with mekil payn.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Yw. and Gaw.</i> 3227.</p>
<p class="continue">[It is undoubtedly equivalent to O.E.
<i>sperre</i>, or <i>spere</i>. Halliwell, s.v. <i>Parred</i>, quotes
“ȝe are <i>parred</i> in . . . ȝe are so <i>spered</i> in.”
So, too, the Ital. <i>sbarra</i> is the Fr. <i>barre</i>. Cf. A.S.
<i>sparran</i>, O.N. <i>sperra</i>, Sc. <i>spar</i>. Hence the
derivation of <i>park</i>, O.E. <i>parrock</i>, an enclosure.]</p>

<p>Prud, <i>adj.</i> S. proud, 302.</p>
<p>Pulten, <i>pa. t. pl.</i> so reads the MS. l. 1023, instead of
<i>putten</i>. Both have the same signification. So in the Romance of
<i>Rob. of Cecyle</i>, Harl. MS. 1701, f. 94, c. 1, <i>pulte</i>
occurs for <i>put</i>, placed, and <i>pylt</i> in R. Cœur
de L. 4085; pelte, Sir Tr. p. 95. In the <i>imp.</i>
<i>Pult</i>
<span class="pagenum">138</span>
<a id="page138"> </a>
for <i>put</i>, <i>place</i>, is used in <i>Hending the Hende</i>, MS.
Digb. 86. In the signification of <i>drove forward</i>, which is nearer
to the sense we require, we find <i>pylte</i> in K. Horn, 1433, and
R. Glouc. Hence the Engl. word <i>pelt</i>. <i>See</i> Putten. Cf.
<i>Pult</i> in Gl. to <i>Will. of Palerne</i>.</p>
<p>Pund, <i>n. pl.</i> S. pounds, 1633.</p>
<p>Put, <i>n.</i> cast, throw, 1055. <i>But</i>, 1040.</p>
<p>Putten, <i>v.</i> to cast, throw, propel forward, 1033, 1044.
<i>Puten</i>, 1051. <i>Putte</i>, <i>pa. t.</i> cast, 1052.
<i>Putten</i>, <i>pa. t. pl.</i> cast, threw, 1023, 1031, 1844. From the
Fr. <i>bouter</i>, Teut. <i>buitten</i>, or Belg. <i>botten</i>, to
drive or propel forward, or, as others suggest, from the Br.
<i>pwtiaw</i>, which has the same meaning, or Isl. <i>potta</i>. From
the same root are derived both <i>Put</i> and <i>But</i>. Thus to
<i>butt</i> in Sc. is to drive at a stone in curling, and to <i>put</i>
in Yorksh. is to push with the horns. In the passage before us it is
applied to a particular game, formerly in great repute. <i>See</i>
<a href="#textnote1022" class="pginternal">Note on l. 1022</a>. Cf. Ramsay’s Poems, ii.
106. The word is still retained in the North, and Sc. V. Jam. and
Brockett. See <i>But</i> and <i>Pulten</i>.</p>
<p>Putting, Puttinge, <i>n.</i> casting, 1042, 1057, 2324.</p>
<p>Pyment, <i>n.</i> <ins class="mycorr" title="text unchanged: usual form is ‘B. Lat.’">B.L.</ins> spiced wine, 1728. <i>See</i>
<a href="#textnote1726" class="pginternal">Note on l. 1726</a>.</p>

<p><a id="gloss_Qui">Qui</a>. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Hwi" class="pginternal">Hwi</a>.</p>
<p>Quic, Quik, <i>adj.</i> S. alive, 612, 613, 1405, 2210, 2476,
&amp;c., <i>quik and ded</i>. This is the usual language of the
Inquisitiones post mortem, which commence at the early part of Henry
III. reign. For the usage of the term, <i>see</i> Gl. to Sir Tr.
p. 98. Yw. and Gaw. 668. Chron. of Engl. 762, &amp;c. The word is
preserved in the vulgar version of the Scriptures, and Creed.
<i>Quike</i>, quick, alert, 1348. <i>Al quic wede</i>, 2641. Cf.
l. 2387.</p>

<p>Rang, <i>adj.</i> S. [<i>ranc</i>] perverse, rebellious, 2561.</p>
<p>Rath, <i>n.</i> S. counsel; hence, an adviser, 75. <i>Dat. c.
rathe</i>, in the phrase <i>to rathe</i>, 2542; for the meaning of
which, <i>see</i> <a href="#gloss_Red" class="pginternal">Red</a>.</p>
<p>Raþe, <i>adv.</i> S. speedily, readily, quickly, 358, &amp;c. (In
l. 1335, I prefer considering it as a verb.)</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Rathe">Rathe</a>, <i>v.</i> S.
[<ins class="mycorr" title="text reads ‘raedan’"><i>rædan</i></ins>]
to advise, 1335. A provincial pronunciation of <i>Rede</i>. In
l. 2817, it is still broader, “Yif ye it wilen and ek
<i>rothe</i>.” In the same manner <i>Rode</i> is spelt, and was
undoubtedly pronounced <i>Rothe</i>, Ly Beaus Desc. 425, and
<i>Abode</i> is spelt <i>Abothe</i>, ib. 1118. Cf. ll. 693, 1681, 2585,
of the present poem, in all which instances the <i>d</i> in <i>rede</i>
has the sound of <i>th</i>.</p>
<p>Recke, <i>pr. t. subj.</i> S. may reck, may care, 2047, 2511. Sir Tr.
p. 124, &amp;c.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Red">Red</a>, <i>n.</i> S. advice,
counsel, 180, 518, 826, 1194, 2871, &amp;c. <i>To rede</i>, lit. for a
counsel, i.e. advisable, 118, 693; spelt <i>to rathe</i>, 2542.</p>

<a id="page139"> </a>

<p>Reft, Refte, Reftes. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Reue" class="pginternal">Reue</a>.</p>

<p>Rippe, <i>n.</i> fish-basket, 893. Hence a <i>Rippar</i>, B. Lat.
<i>riparius</i>, is a person who brings fish from the coast to sell in
the interior. V. Spelm. in v. Nares prefers the etymology of
<i>ripa</i>, but without reason. <i>Rip</i> is still provincial for an
osier basket. <i>See</i> Jam. and Moore. So also in a curious Latin and
English Vocabulary, written out by Sire John Mendames, Parson of
Bromenstrope [Broomsthorp, Co. Norf.] in the middle of the 15th cent.,
and now preserved in the valuable MSS. library of T. W. Coke, Esq.
<i>Cophinus</i> is explained <i>A beryng lepe</i>, or <i>ryppe</i>,
terms still retained in the county. Jam. gives Icel. <i>hrip</i>, a
basket.</p>

<p>Shides, <i>n. pl.</i> S. It here expresses pieces of wood cleft at
the end, 917. In Doug. Virg. <i>Schide</i> signifies a billet of wood,
223, 10;
<span class="pagenum">142</span>
<a id="page142"> </a>
or a chip, splinter, 207, 8. So in <i>Rauf Coilzear</i>, st. 39,
Schaftes of schene wode they scheueride in <i>schides</i>. So also in
P. Pl. The word is preserved in Lanc. This custom of skinning eels
by inserting the head in a cleft stick, is still practised, we are
informed, in the fish markets.</p>

<p>Simenels, <i>n. pl.</i> Fr. 779, a finer sort of bread, “q. a

minus quam Wastellum. It elsewhere appears to be a sort of cake, or
cracknel. So in the <i>Crieries de Paris</i>, v. 163, Chaudes tartes et
<i>siminiaus</i>. V. Nares in v.</p>
<p>Sinne, <i>n.</i> S. fault, 1976. <i>Ne for loue ne for sinne</i>,

may be elsewhere found:</p>

<p><a id="gloss_Sire">Sire</a>, Syre, <i>n.</i>
Fr. The term in ll. 310, 1229, is used not only to express respect, but
command. A parallel passage is in R. Cœur de L. 2247. It
simply means <i>Sir</i>, ll. 909, 2009.</p>
<p>Site, <i>v.</i> S. to sit, 2809. <i>Sittes</i>, <i>pr. t. 2 p.</i>

<p>Sket, <i>adv.</i> quickly, soon, 1926, 1960, 2303, 2493, 2513, 2574,

K. Alisaund. 3047; R. Cœur de L. 806; Rom. of Merlin, ap.
Ellis, M. R. V. i. p. 228. [Icel. <i>skjótt</i>, quickly,

<p>Span-newe, <i>adj.</i> quite new, 968. This is the earliest instance
on record of the use of this word. For its disputed etymology see Jam.,
Nares, Todd’s Johns., and Thoms. Etymons; but especially Wedgwood’s
Etym. Dict. <i>Span</i> = chip; <i>Span-new</i>, chip-new. A.S.
<i>spón</i>. It occurs in Chauc. Troil. iii. 1671.</p>

<p>Spusing, <i>n.</i> S. espousals, marriage, 1164, 1177, 2886.</p>
<p>Stac, <i>n.</i> S. 814. [This I believe to mean simply a stack, or
heap, like the Dan. <i>stak</i>, Sw. <i>stack</i>. I add Sir F. Madden’s
note in the edition of 1828.] A stack, or, more properly,
<i>stick</i> of fish, a term applied to eels when strung on a row, ‘sic
dicta, quod trajecta vimine (quod <i>stic</i> dicimus) connectebantur.’
<i>Spelm.</i> A <i>stica</i> consisted of 25 eels, and 10
<i>Sticæ</i> made a <i>Binde</i>. Glanv. lib. 2, c. 9.</p>
<p>Stalworþi, Stalworþe, Stalwrthe, adj. S. strong, valiant, courageous,
24, 904, 1027, &amp;c. <i>Stalworþeste</i>, <i>sup.</i> 25.</p>
<p>Stan-ded, <i>adj.</i> S. dead as a stone, completely dead, 1815.
<i>Stille als a ston</i>, 928. Cf. K. of Tars, 549; Erle of Tol. 754;
Launfal, 357. <i>See</i> Gl. to <i>Partenay</i>.</p>
<p>Star, <i>n.</i> Icel. a species of sedge, 939. Icel. <i>stör</i>; Sw.

<p><a id="gloss_Stareden">Stareden</a>, <i>pt.
t. pl.</i> 1037. <i>Probably miswritten for</i> Stradden, contended. Cf.
Su.-Goth. and Sw. <i>strida</i>, to contend.</p>

<p><a id="gloss_Teyte">Teyte</a>, <i>adj.</i> S.
1841, 2331. [Explained “lively” by Coleridge, Stratmann, and Morris, as
if from Icel. <i>teitr</i>, hilaris. This I believe to be completely
wrong. The word occurs in Allit. Poems, ed. Morris, B. 871, with
reference to <i>tight</i> lasses, and in l. 1841 of Havelok we have
a reference to <i>tight</i> lads. In l. 2331 it may also mean
<i>flawless</i>, staunch. “<i>Theet</i>, <i>adj.</i> water-tight. O.N.
<i>þiettr</i> or <i>þéttr</i>, densus, solidus. O.Sw. <i>thæter</i>, Sw.
Dial, <i>tjett</i> or <i>tjætt</i>, Dan. <i>tætt</i>, Germ.
<i>dicht</i>. Ihre gives . . . . <i>ett tätt fat</i>, a
flawless vessel. ‘<i>Thyht</i>, hool fro brekynge, not brokyn.
<i>Integer</i>, <i>solidus</i>. Prompt. Parv.’” Atkinson’s Glossary of
the Cleveland dialect.]</p>

accepted.]

<p>Þertekene, 2878. [Coleridge’s Glossarial Index has “Thertekene = mark
thereto. A.S. <i>tácnian</i>.” But this is a very awkward phrase, and I
should prefer to suppose <i>þer-tekene</i> = by the token, i.e. in
token. <i>Tekene</i> answers to the Sw. <i>tecken</i>, a token; and
<i>þer</i> is found as a prefix in <i>P. Plowman</i> in the phr.
<i>þer-while</i> = <i>þe while</i>, i.e. in the time that. The only
difficulty is that <i>þer</i> is properly feminine (A.S. <i>þære</i>),
whilst <i>tecken</i> in Sw. and <i>tácen</i> in A.S. are neuter. <i>In
tokne</i> (= in token) occurs in Shoreham’s poems, ed. Wright, 131.]</p>

<p>Thit, <i>pp.</i> 2990. [The rime shews that the <i>i</i> is long;
and, whether
<span class="pagenum">148</span>
<a id="page148"> </a>
the <i>th</i> is sounded like <i>t</i>, or (which is more likely) the
word should have been written <i>tiht</i> or <i>tith</i>, we may be
tolerably confident that it is equivalent to the O.E. <i>tight</i> or
<i>tiȝt</i>, a pp. signifying <i>intended</i>, <i>purposed</i>,
<i>designed</i>, which is the exact sense here required. Stratmann gives
five instances of it, of which one is— “To brewe the Crystene
mennys banys Hy hadden <i>tyght</i>;” Octovian, 1476.]</p>

<p>Totede, <i>pa. t.</i> peeped, looked, 2106. This verb is thrice found
in P. Ploughman’s Crede, ll. 142, 168, 339. Although it would
appear a rare word from its not appearing in Hearne, Ritson, or Weber,
yet in later times it, occurs often, and is instanced by Jamieson from
Patten’s Account of Somerset’s Expedicion, p. 53, and by Nares from
Hall, Latimer, Spenser, and Fairfax. It also occurs four times in the
<i>Ancren Riwle</i>, ed. Morton, 1853. In Sc. it is pronounced
<i>Tete</i>, which is derived by Jam. from the same stock as Su.-G.
<i>titt-a</i>, explained by Ihre, “Per transennam veluti videre, ut
solent curiosi, aut post tegmina latentes.” V. the authorities quoted,
Todd’s Johns. and Wilbr. Gl. [Cf. Sw. <i>titta</i>; Dan. <i>titte</i>,
to peep; Dan. <i>tittelege</i>, to play at bopeep.]</p>
