L. Forest, <i>Si quis cetum</i>. In Cotgr. also we find “<i>Tumbe</i>,
the great Sea-Dragon, or Quadriver; also the Gurnard, called so at
Roan.” [But the Sw. <i>tumlare</i>, a porpoise, <i>lit.</i> a tumbler,
suggests that the name may be given from its <i>tumbling</i> or
<i>rolling</i>. The Dan. <i>tumler</i>, however, is a dolphin.]</p>
<p>Tun. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Toun" class="pginternal">Toun</a>.</p>
<p>Turues, <i>n. pl.</i> S. turf, peat, 939. Chauc. C. T. 10109. V.
Spelm. in v. and Jennings’ Somersetsh. Gl.</p>
<p>Twel <i>for</i> Twelve, 2455.</p>
<a class="glosslink pginternal" href="#glossary" id="gloss_U">U</a>
<p class="letter">
Ueneysun, <i>n.</i> Fr. venison, 1726.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Vmbestonde">Vmbestonde</a>,
<i>adv.</i> S. for a while, formerly, 2297.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>&amp; heo seilede<i>n</i> forth,</p>
<p>þæt inne sæ heo comen,</p>
<p>þa <i>vmbe stunde</i></p>
<p>ne sæge heo noht of londe.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Laȝam.</i> l. 11967.</p>
<p class="continue">It is equivalent to <i>umbe-while</i> or
<i>vmwhile</i>, Sc. <i>umquhile</i>. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Stunde" class="pginternal">Stunde</a>.</p>
<p>Umbistode, <i>pa. t.</i> S. stood around, 1875. <i>See</i> <ins class="mycorr" title="no Glossary entry">Bistode</ins>, <a href="#gloss_Stonden" class="pginternal">Stonden</a>.</p>
<p>Vn-bi-yeden, <i>pa. t. pl.</i> S. surrounded, 1842. <i>See</i>
<a href="#gloss_Yede" class="pginternal">Yede</a>.</p>
<p>Vnblithe, <i>adj.</i> S. unhappy, 141. Sir Tr. p. 171.</p>
<p>Unbounden, <i>pa. t. pl.</i> S. unbound, 601.</p>
<p>Underfong, <i>pa. t.</i> S. understood, 115. This sense of the verb
is not found elsewhere. It is in the present poem synonymous with
<i>Understod</i> (as Lat. <i>accipere</i>, <i>percipere</i>).</p>
<p>Understonde, <i>v.</i> S. to receive, 2814. <i>Understod</i>, <i>pa.
t.</i> received, 1760. <i>Understonde</i>, <i>pr. subj.</i> receive,
1159. So in K. Horn, 245, ed. Rits.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Horn child thou <i>vnderstond</i>,</p>
<p>Tech him of harpe and song.</p>
</div>
<p class="continue">where the MS. Laud 108 reads <i>vnderfonge</i>.
See Lumby’s ed. l. 239.</p>
<p>Unker, <i>pron. g. c. dual.</i> S. of you two, 1882.</p>
<p>Vnkeueleden, <i>pa. t. pl.</i> S. ungagged, 601. <i>See</i> <a href="#gloss_Keuel" class="pginternal">Keuel</a>.</p>
<p>Unkyndelike, <i>adv.</i> S. unsuitably, 1250.</p>
<p>Vnornelike, <i>adj.</i> S. basely, or degradingly, 1941. The only
word in the Sax. remaining to which it can be referred, is
<i>unornlic</i>, tritus, Jos. 9. 5. The following instances also
approach the same stock:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Ne speke y nout with Horne,</p>
<p>Nis he nout so <i>vnorne.</i></p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>K. Horn</i>, 337.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Mi stefne is bold &amp; noȝt <i>vnorne</i>,</p>
<p>Ho is ilich one grete horne,</p>
<p>&amp; þin is ilich one pipe.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Hule and Niȝtingale</i>, l. 317.</p>
<p class="continue">[Ihre shews that Icel. and Su.-Goth. <i>orna</i>
mean to acquire vital heat, to grow warm. Hence <i>unorne</i> means
unfervent, spiritless, feeble, old. Thus, in the <i>Hule and
Niȝtingale</i> it means <i>feeble</i>, <i>weak</i>; in Jos. 9. 5,
it is used of <i>old, worn-out</i> shoes. In the Ormulum, <i>unnorne</i>
occurs frequently, in the sense
<span class="pagenum">151</span>
<a id="page151"> </a>
of <i>poor</i>, <i>mean</i>, <i>feeble</i>; see ll. 827, 3668; also
<i>unnornelig</i>, meaning <i>meanly</i>, <i>humbly</i>,
<i>obscurely</i>, in ll. 3750, 4858, 7525, 8251.]</p>
<p>Unride, <i>adj.</i> S. [<i>ungereod, ungerydu</i>] It is here used in
various significations, most of which, however, correspond to the senses
given by Somner. Large, cumbersome (of a garment), 964; unwieldy (of the
bar of a door), 1795; deep, wide (of a wound), 1981, 2673; numerous,
extensive (of the nobility), 2947. <i>Unrideste, sup.</i> deepest,
widest, 1985. In the second sense we find it in Sir Tristr.
p. 167,</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Dartes wel <i>unride</i></p>
<p>Beliagog set gan.</p>
</div>
<p class="continue">And in <i>Guy of Warwick</i>, ap. Ellis, M. R. V.
2, p. 79.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>A targe he had ywrought full well,</p>
<p>Other metal was ther none but steel,</p>
<p class="indent">A mickle and <i>unrede</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="continue">In the fourth sense we have these examples:</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Opon Inglond for to were</p>
<p>With stout ost and <i>unride</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Horn Childe</i>, ap. Rits. M. R. V. 3, p. 283.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>Schir Rannald raugh to the renk ane rout wes <i>unryde</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>Sir Gaw. and Gol.</i> ii. 25.</p>
<div class="verse">
<p>The soudan gederet an ost <i>unryde</i>.</p>
</div>
<p class="author">
<i>K. of Tars</i>, 142.</p>
<p class="continue">Cf. also <i>Sir Guy</i>, Ee. <span class="smallroman">IV.</span> in Garrick’s Collect. ‘Ameraunt drue out a
swerde <i>unryde</i>.’ In the sense of huge, or unwieldy, we may also
understand it in Sir Tr. p. 148, 164; Guy of Warw. ap. Ell.
M. R. V. 2, p. 78; Horn Childe, ap. Rits. V. 3,
p. 295. In R. Brunne, p. 174, it expresses loud, tremendous.
Sir W. Scott and Hearne are both at fault in their Glossaries, and even
Jamieson has done but little to set them right, beyond giving the true
derivation, and then, under the cognate word <i>Unrude</i>, Doug. Virg.
167, 35, &amp;c., errs from pure love of theory.</p>
<p>Vnrith, <i>n.</i> S. injustice, 1369.</p>
<p>Unwrast, Unwraste, <i>adj.</i> S. [<i>unwræste</i>] feeble,
worthless, 2821; rotten, 547. This word occurs in the Saxon Chron.
168, 4 (ed. Thorpe, p. 321), applied to a rotten ship, and
this appears to have been the original meaning. The sense in which it
was subsequently used may be learnt by comparing Laȝam. ll. 13943,
29609; R. Gl. p. 586; Chron. of Engl. 662, 921; Ly Beaus Desc.
2118 (not explained by Rits.); K. Alisaund. 878; R. Cœur
de L. 872, and Sevyn Sages, 1917. It is not found in Jam. Cf. A.S.
<i>wræst</i>, firm.</p>

<p><a id="gloss_Wawe">Wawe</a>, <i>n.</i> S. wall,
474, 2470. The phrase <i>bith wawe</i>, 474, is also found in Rits.
A. S. p. 46, which is left unexplained by the Editor, and is
badly guessed at by Ellis. By the aid of Moor’s Suffolk Gl. we are
enabled to ascertain the meaning of an expression which is not yet
obsolete. “By the walls.” Dead and not buried. “A’ lie bi’ the walls”
—said, I believe, only of a human subject. [This remark only
applies to l. 474. In ll. 1963, 2470, the phrase refers to the
benches placed round the walls in the great hall, whereon men slept at
night, and sat in council by day.] <i>Wowe</i>, 1963, 2078. Still so
pronounced in Lanc., &amp;c.</p>

<p>Were, should be, 2782. <i>Weren</i>, <i>3 p. pl.</i> were, 156,
&amp;c.</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Weren">Weren</a>, 784. Sir F.
Madden says—Garnett conjectured <i>weirs</i> or dams, from Isl.
<i>ver</i>. [If <i>weren</i> be really a plural noun, I should prefer to
translate it by <i>pools</i>; cf. A.S. <i>wær</i>, Icel. <i>ver</i>,
Su.-Go. <i>wär</i>. Ihre says— “<i>Wär</i>, locus, ubi congregari
amant pisces, ut solent inter brevia et vada. Isl. <i>ver</i>,
<i>fiskaver</i>. A.S. id. unde <i>ver-hurde</i> apud Bens. custos septi
piscatorii, Angl. <i>wier</i>, <i>wear</i>, &amp;c.” See <i>wer</i> in
Stratmann. In this case the line means— “in the sea-pools he often
set them,” and the note on the line (q.v.) is wrong.]</p>
<p><a id="gloss_Werewed">Werewed</a>, <i>part.
pa.</i> S. worried, killed, 1915. [We should probably insert a mark of
interrogation, thus— “Hwat dide he? þore were<i>n</i> he werewed,”
i.e. “What did they effect? There were they slain.” Spelt <i>wirwed</i>,
1921. Cf. Du. <i>worgen</i>, and see Jam. s.v. <i>Wery</i>, and
<i>Worry</i> in Atkinson’s Gl. of Cleveland dialect.]</p>

<p>Wicth, With, <i>adj.</i> courageous, stout, active, 344, 1008, 1064,
1651, 1692, &amp;c. <i>Wicteste</i>, <i>sup.</i> 9. An epithet used
universally by the ancient poets, and to be found in every Gloss. merely

<i>Wight</i>, <i>Wich</i> &amp;c. [Sir F. Madden suggests a derivation
from A.S. <i>hwæt</i> (Icel. <i>hvátr</i>), acute, brave. Wedgwood
suggests Sw. <i>vig</i>, nimble. Cf. Su.-Goth. <i>wig</i>, Icel.

<h3> <a id="names">INDEX OF NAMES TO “HAUELOK.”</a></h3>
<hr class="small">
<p class="editor">
[In this Index, the references under words in large capitals are in
general to the <i>pages</i> of the book; otherwise, the references are
to the <i>lines</i> of the poem.]</p>
<p class="letter">
<span class="smallcaps">Athelwold</span> (<i>spelt</i> Aþelwald, l.
1077), is king of England, and governs wisely, pp. 2, 3;</p>
<div class="inset">
<p>feels he is dying, p. 4;</p>
<p>bequeaths his daughter to the care of Godrich, pp. 6, 7;</p>
<p>dies, p. 8.</p>
<p>Mentioned again in ll. 2709, 2803.)</p>
</div>
<p>Auelok, <i>another spelling of</i> Hauelok, 1395, 1793.</p>
<p class="letter">
<span class="smallcaps">Bernard Brun</span> (i.e. Bernard Brown; so
called in ll. 1751, 1945),</p>
<div class="inset">
<p>provides a supper for Havelok, p. 48;</p>
<p>his house attacked by thieves, p. 49;</p>
<p>fights against them, p. 52;</p>
<p>tells Ubbe how well Havelok fought, p. 54.</p>
</div>
<p><span class="smallcaps">Bertram</span> (<i>named</i> in l.
2898),</p>
<div class="inset">
<p>is cook to the Earl of Cornwall, and employs Havelok, pp. 27, 28;</p>
<p>is made Earl of Cornwall, and marries Levive, Grim’s daughter,
p. 83.</p>
</div>
<p><span class="smallcaps">Birkabeyn</span> (spelt Bircabein, l.
494<ins class="mycorr" title="close parenthesis printed after ‘2296’">);</ins></p>
<div class="inset">
<p><i>gen.</i> Birkabeynes, 2150, 2209, 2296,</p>
<p>is king of Denmark, p. 11;</p>
<p>commends his three children to Godard, p. 12;</p>
<p>dies, p. 13;</p>
<p>his son Havelok’s resemblance to him, p. 60.</p>
</div>
<p class="letter">
Cestre (Chester), 2607, 2859, 2896.</p>
<p>Cornwayle (Cornwall), 178, 2908;</p>
<p class="inset">
Cornwalie, 884.</p>

<div class="inset">

Dauy, seint, 2863.</p>

<div class="inset">

<p>—Henglishe, 2945.</p>
</div>
<p class="letter">
Giffin [<i>Qu.</i> Griffin] Galle, 2029.</p>
<p><span class="smallcaps">Godard</span> (<i>gen.</i> Godardes, l.
2415),</p>
<div class="inset">
<p>is made regent of Denmark, pp. 12, 13;</p>
<p>shuts up Birkabeyn’s children in a castle, p. 13;</p>
<p>kills Swanborow and Helfled, p. 15;</p>
<p>spares Havelok, p. 16;</p>
<p>but afterwards hires Grim to drown Havelok, p. 17;</p>
<p>is attacked by Havelok, p. 67;</p>

<span class="pagenum">158</span>
<a id="page158"> </a>
<p><span class="smallcaps">Godrich</span> (<i>spelt</i> Godrigh, l.
178),</p>
<div class="inset">
<p>is Earl of Cornwall, p. 6;</p>
<p>is made regent of England, pp. 7, 8, 9;</p>
<p>shuts Goldborough up in Dover castle, p. 10;</p>
<p>makes Goldborough marry Havelok, p. 33;</p>
<p>raises an army against Havelok, p. 72;</p>
<p>excites his men, p. 73;</p>
<p>marches to Grimsby, p. 74;</p>
<p>fights with Ubbe, p. 75;</p>
<p>fights with Havelok, pp. 77, 78;</p>
<p>is taken prisoner, p. 78;</p>
<p>taken to Lincoln, and burnt alive, pp. 80, 81.</p>
</div>
<p><span class="smallcaps">Goldeboru</span> (<i>or</i> Goldeborw, l.
2985),</p>
<div class="inset">
<p>is daughter of King Athelwold, p. 4;</p>
<p>is committed to the care of Godrich, pp. 8, 9;</p>
<p>shut up in Dover castle, p. 11;</p>
<p>is sent for to Lincoln, p. 33;</p>
<p>is married to Havelok, p. 36;</p>
<p>hears an angel’s voice, p. 39;</p>
<p>encourages Havelok to go to Denmark, p. 41;</p>
<p>rejoices at Godrich’s death, p. 81;</p>
<p>is queen of England, p. 85.</p>
<p><i>See</i> <a href="#names_havelok" class="pginternal">Havelok</a>.</p>
</div>
<p><span class="smallcaps">Grim</span>, a fisher, is hired by Godard
to drown Havelok, p. 17;</p>
<div class="inset">
<p>discovers Havelok to be the right heir to the crown, p. 19;</p>
<p>takes Havelok over to England, p. 20;</p>
<p>founds Grimsby, p. 23;</p>
<p>sends Havelok to Lincoln, p. 26;</p>
<p>dies, p. 37.</p>
</div>
<p class="editor">
[In l. 2333, there seems to be an allusion to a spectacle, in which the
history of Grim is represented.]</p>
<p>Grimes, <i>gen. c. of</i> Grim, 1343, 1392, 2867.</p>
<p>Grimesbi, 745, 2540, 2579, 2617, 2619;</p>
<p class="inset">
—Grimesby, 1202.</p>

2896.</p>
<p>Gunter (an English earl), 2606.</p>
<p class="letter">
<a class="smallcaps" id="names_havelok">Hauelok</a>, son of king Birkabeyn of Denmark, p.
13;</p>
<div class="inset">
<p>spared by Godard, p. 16;</p>
<p>but given over by him to Grim to be drowned, p. 17;</p>

<p>goes to England, p. 22;</p>
<p>sells fish, p. 25;</p>
<p>works as a porter, p. 27;</p>
<p>puts the stone, p. 31;</p>
<p>marries Goldborough, p. 35;</p>
<p>returns to Grimsby, p. 36;</p>
<p>his dream, p. 39;</p>
<p>returns to Denmark, p. 43;</p>
<p>trades there, p. 44;</p>
<p>is noticed by Ubbe, p. 45;</p>
<p>defends Bernard’s house against thieves, pp. 48-53;</p>
<p>is known to be heir of Denmark by a miraculous light, p. 60;</p>
<p>is dubbed knight by Ubbe, p. 65;</p>
<p>is king of Denmark, p. 66;</p>
<p>defeats Godard, p. 68;</p>
<p>invades England, p. 72;</p>
<p>defeats Godrich, p. 77;</p>
<p>rewards Bertram and others, p. 82;</p>
<p>lives to be a hundred years old, p. 83;</p>
<p>is crowned king of England at London, p. 84;</p>
<p>is king for sixty years, p. 85.</p>

<p>Rokesborw (explained by Prof. Morley to mean Rokeby, but it is surely

<div class="inset">
<p>—Rokesburw, 139.</p>
<p>Roxburgh is spelt <i>Rokesburgh</i> in Walsingham, ed. Riley, i. 340,
&amp;c.</p>
</div>
<p class="letter">
Sathanas (Satan), 1100, 1134, 2512.</p>
<p>Swanborow (Havelok’s sister), 411.</p>
<p class="letter">
<span class="smallcaps">Ubbe</span>, a great Danish lord, p. 44;</p>
<div class="inset">
<p>entertains Havelok, p. 45;</p>
<p>takes him to his castle, p. 57;</p>
<p>does homage to Havelok, p. 63;</p>
<p>dubs him knight, p. 65;</p>
<p>his combat with Godrich, p. 75;</p>
<p>is sorely wounded, p. 76.</p>
</div>
<p class="letter">
Willam Wendut (one of Grim’s sons), 1690, 1881, 1892, 2348, 2632;</p>
<p class="inset">
—Wiliam Wenduth, 1398.</p>
<p>Winchestre, 158, 318.</p>
<p class="letter">
Yerk (York), 1178.</p>
<p>Ynde, India, 1085.</p>

<p> <br> </p>
<h6>JOHN CHILDS AND SON, PRINTERS.</h6>
<div class="endnote">
<h4> <a id="endnote">
Transcriber’s Note on Texts:</a></h4>
<p>This book exists in at least two forms, distinguished by minor
differences in the modern (1868) material. The title page(s), verso and
introduction give no hint that there were any changes or additions;
references to the “former” and “present” edition are to Madden alone
(Roxburghe, 1828) contrasted with Skeat (EETS, 1868). For convenience
the two 1868 variants will be called “preliminary” and “final”. The 1975
reprint used the “final” version.</p>
<h5>Emendations:</h5>
<p class="quotation">
p. 24, <a href="#texttag784" class="pginternal">l. 784</a>. Perhaps we should, however,
read <i>se-weren</i>, and the note on the line (p. 93) may be
wrong. See <i>Weren</i> in the Glossary.</p>
<p>The preliminary version of the Emendations reads:</p>
<p class="quotation">
... Perhaps there should be no stop in the line, and ...</p>
<p>There are no differences in the body text or Note.</p>
<p class="letter">
The last five Emendations— changes to glossary items
<i>Greting</i>, <i>Hal</i>, <i>Shoten</i> (<i>Schoten</i>), <a href="#gloss_Teyte" class="pginternal"><i>Teyte</i></a>, <i>Bise</i>— are missing from the
preliminary version.</p>
<h5>Glossary:</h5>
<p class="quotation">
Datheit, <i>interj.</i> ... [from the O.F. <i>hait</i>, pleasure].</p>
<p>In the preliminary version, the bracketed addition reads “possibly
from <i>haïr</i>, to hate”.</p>
<p class="quotation">
Pastees ... Al of <i>pasteiis</i> beth the walles.</p>
<p>The preliminary version has the apparent error <i>pasteüs</i>.</p>
<p class="quotation">
Weren ...</p>
<p>The preliminary version is printed without brackets but is otherwise
identical.</p>
</div>
